## **Who Is Gary Bencivenga?** 

Gary Bencivenga is one of the world's most respected copywriters. 

Specializing exclusively in direct response for more than 40 years, Gary apprenticed under John Caples at BBDO and David Ogilvy at Ogilvy & Mather, among several other legendary copy masters. These advertising legends shared with him the results of thousands of their split-run tests—a priceless legacy of knowledge of what really works and what doesn't in scientifically measured direct response advertising. 

Gary has created breakthrough campaigns for many of the most successful direct marketers in America, including Phillips Publishing, Boardroom, Rodale, KCI Communications, Prentice Hall, Doubleday, _Time, BusinessWeek, Forbes,_ and Merrill Lynch, among dozens of others. 

In direct mail circles, any copywriter who can consistently beat his clients' control packages roughly one out of four times is considered a star. In head-tohead competition against the best copy talent in America, Gary's winning percentage has remained, over many years, at about 85 percent. 

In other words, in actual orders and cash generated, his work has won more than eight out of every ten times he has been tested against other top copywriting talent. For several major mailers, over many years, he has _never_ lost a split-run test against the strongest copywriting competition in America. 

He is now retired, but keeps his hand in a few direct marketing enterprises in which he is either an owner or a partner.

## **Introduction** 

Now that I am retiring, I want to place in your hands a unique legacy—my collection of the most powerful secrets that consistently boost advertising response. 

These are the closely guarded secrets I've discovered from 40 years and more than a billion dollars worth of scientific direct marketing tests. And when I say "closely guarded," that's not hype. I know because I was the one guarding them! Not once did I share even one of these secrets at an industry conference or luncheon, in an article, or in any other way. Why not? Because my clients and I never wanted to lose our edge by revealing them to the very people we were competing with. 

But now that I'm retiring, I'm spilling everything, holding nothing back. That includes the many techniques and strategies I've invented over the years to ratchet response higher, as well as numerous ingenious "street-smart" tips and "tricks" I learned at the feet of such legendary marketing masters as John Caples; David Ogilvy (and his top direct marketing lieutenants); Max Sackheim; Ted Nicholas; Dan Rosenthal; and a host of veteran copy chiefs, entrepreneurs, and clients at many of the most successful direct marketing companies in America. 

Every insight, every technique, every recommendation is based not on theory but on the precisely tracked results of thousands of split-run tests, the ultimate gold standard of scientific advertising. As you'll see, some of our tests yielded surprising, even shocking, results. Yet the secrets we learned have worked again and again without fail to increase response across a wide range of products and in every direct marketing medium—direct mail, space, radio, TV, the Internet, package inserts, you name it. 

This transcript contains everything in the seminar, but also goes beyond. That's because, as I edited it, surgically removing the irritating "ahs," "urns," and "you knows" that creep into the spoken language of a seminar, I couldn't resist adding numerous additional insights that make the content even clearer and more valuable. 

In all, what I aim to give you here is everything you need to... 

- ... achieve breakthrough results with a consistency that will surprise and delight both you and the people you work for; 

- ... make it much easier to identify and launch products that people crave and will buy with abandon; 

- ... evaluate the copy and design of others with a jeweler's eye that will seldom be fooled; 

- ... see instantly, even before you test, exactly where an ad is weak and how to make it stronger; 

- ... know how to persuade almost anyone in almost any market to intensely desire 

- 12 - 

your product above all others you compete against, so much so that they may never truly feel satisfied with anything else; 

- ... leverage your advertising budget so that each $1 you invest can do the work of $2, $5, $8, and sometimes even $9 or more. 

Most of all, I want to give you the "big picture" of how to understand advertising and its power with a clarity you never experienced, as if you were viewing the landscape—and your market—from a satellite photo, giving you a tremendous "battlefield advantage" over those without such a view. 

As part of this, you'll see how to take breakthrough advertising out of the realm of the occasional and mysterious and turn it into a repeatable system. You'll learn how to make your advertising so strong that it stands out like a lighthouse in a sea of clutter. You'll discover how to make your prospects welcome your advertising and eagerly read it, rather than toss it. You will see how to close the sale—and collect the check!—more effectively than ever before, especially when you apply "the secret of master closers" I'll share. As a result, you will be able to trigger much higher response and revenue virtually on command—and mercifully _without_ hype, falsehoods, sweepstakes, or superficial gimmicks. 

Finally, I want to turn you in the fastest way possible into a marketing master, someone who knows how to _control_ the process of persuasion rather than merely guess at it, someone who knows how to pull the puppet strings of the marketplace, make it dance to your tune, and then have that same marketplace applaud you as its hero. 

For that's what you are when you do your marketing right—a hero, someone who stands for excellence in both your product and the way you present it, someone who enlightens, inspires, and uplifts people by showing them truly superior ways to realize life's great blessings, be they living longer...becoming healthier...retiring richer...raising more-confident children...enjoying financial independence...succeeding in their own business...or achieving any other benefits your products or services make possible. 

Once you possess such marketing mastery, you can grow quite wealthy, and I'll cover the smartest ways I know to do that, as well. You can grow wealthy because selling is one of the world's most highly paid professions. And since advertising is _multiplied salesmanship,_ it gives you enormous leverage to _multiply your income exponentially,_ once you've got a winning ad. And if you follow these principles, you will have wining ads—lots of them. 

You don't need a silver tongue or a golden touch to work such magic. What you need are the secrets you are about to receive. They have made my life so much richer and happier than anything I could have imagined when I sat down at my first job in the copy department at Prentice Hall Publishing some 40 years ago. May they do the same for you. 

Sincerely, Gary Bencivenga

_PAULINE BENCIVENGA:_ Good morning, everyone. Welcome to New York and the beautiful St. Regis Hotel on this lovely spring day. My name is Pauline Bencivenga. But don't worry, I'm not the Bencivenga you'll be hearing from today. 

You each picked up a name tag when you came in this morning, and we ask that you hold onto it because it will be your ID card when you come back tomorrow. 

If any of you have any questions or need assistance, please come to me or one of our helpers. We have T. J. Robinson, Hanna Lee, Nancy Loseke, and Meaghen Lewis. Please feel free to come to us, and hopefully we can help you. 

We also ask that you please turn off your cell phones. As you can see, we are videotaping this, and Gary will tell you something a little special about that, which you'll be pleased to hear. 

Now it is my pleasure to introduce the man who's the most important person in my life, and I think by the end of this seminar, he'll be an important person in your life as well. I'd like you to join me in a warm welcome for my husband, Gary. 

## _(GARY RECEIVES STANDING OVATION)_ 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ My goodness! Thank you so much. That was a wonderful way to greet me. I really should be applauding all of you because, as I looked over the roster of attendees and now see all of you in front of me, I see that we've got the greatest collection of direct marketing talent ever assembled in one room, right here. So I applaud you. I know the DMA and other industry groups have more people at their conventions, but we've got the best right here. 

## _(APPLAUSE)_ 

In fact, we have top direct marketing talent from every continent on the globe, except for the lone, cold exception of Antarctica, where they have more penguins than people. But their whole continent is melting anyway, so they've got bigger things to worry about. 

Thank you, Pauline, for that very nice introduction. 

You know, this marks the 40th year that I've been a copywriter, and the 40th year I'm married to Pauline. There is a connection there. Pauline was the 

**- 16 -** 

inspiration for my greatest headline and offer ever. 

The headline was _'Will you marry me? "_ The offer was _"Two can live as cheaply as one."_ And I got 100 percent response! 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

## **"Let These Four Little Words Guide All Your Marketing—and Your Life"** 

I grew up right across the East River, in a rough and tumble section of Brooklyn called Crown Heights. Believe me, if I could make it from the rough streets of Crown Heights over here to the St. Regis, talking to the likes of you— the preeminent talent in direct marketing today—there's nothing that can stop you from being or doing or achieving anything you want in your life. 

Why this beautiful room? We could have held this seminar anyplace. I wanted it here, in what may be the most elegant meeting room in New York, as a statement—a statement about you, about me, and about the nature of aspiration. 

That statement can be summed up in just four little words, a guiding principle for your career and life: _why not the best?_ 

Why should the best be reserved for others? Maybe this is the Brooklyn in me coming out. The other boroughs have always played second fiddle to Manhattan, and we all know that you can take the guy out of Brooklyn, but you can't take the Brooklyn out of the guy. So maybe I, and perhaps you, too, have been conditioned to think that the best is reserved for other people. 

But that's not so, not in a free society, where we all have the chance to compete on an equal playing field. And we are all from free societies. Always remember that _you_ can achieve the best, _you_ can be among the best, _you_ can create the best products and services in your market, and then _you_ will be showered with the best rewards. So why not strive for the best? 

As Somerset Maugham once said, _"It's a funny thing about life; when you refuse to accept nothing but the best, you very often get it."_ 

That statement is more than a slogan for me. I apply it with my clients in everything we do. There's neither fun nor greatness in mediocrity, so let's take the time to do things right. Let's strive to be the best. 

**- 17 -** 

## **"The Battle Is Won Even Before It Is Engaged"—Sun Tzu** 

Part of achieving the best is having a clear vision of what you'd like to accomplish before each activity—whether it's in your marketing or copywriting or life in general. You want to start by having a very clear picture of what you'd like to experience in any major activity _before_ you plunge in. 

In other words, a breakthrough advertising campaign does not start with the writing of headlines. It does not even start with your research. A breakthrough starts with _your intention to make it so and a clear image of success as it will look and feel to you, as if its already achieved._ 

This is ancient wisdom that holds true in any field of endeavor. For example, in about 400 BC, the military genius Sun Tzu, the great general of China, wrote a treatise called _The Art of War_ This book is still studied in every major military college in the world today. 

Sun Tzu said, "The battle is won even before it is engaged." 

Bill Belichick is a modern student of Sun Tzu. Bill is the man with the highest postseason winning percentage of any head coach in NFL history. He is the only head coach to win three Super Bowl championships within a four-year span. Echoing Sun Tzu, Belichick has said, "The Super Bowl is won long before the players take the field." 

In the same way, the direct mail package becomes the new control _before the writer s fingers touch the keyboard._ The space ad becomes a breakthrough _before the headline is written._ The new product is a breakthrough _before it s launched_ — IF we have the intention to make it so and, before we take action, vividly _picture_ and emotionally _feel_ the success we want to experience in the rehearsal studio of our own mind. 

In similar fashion, the seminar becomes a life-changing experience even before it unfolds, _if we intend to make it so._ 

## **My Vision for This Seminar** 

What, then, is my vision for this seminar, the vision I would love you to experience? 

My vision is that tomorrow—late tomorrow afternoon, when your feet once again touch the sidewalks of New York after our last session together—I would like you to feel lighter than air because you are so excited about the ideas that you've learned here and can't wait to apply them in your life. 

## I don't want this to be a "suntan seminar," where you have a warm glow for

**18 -** 

three days, which then fades away, and you're back to wherever you were before. 

The only way this seminar will be of value in your life is if you make notes of the good ideas you hear and then put them into practice. We'll get into that as we move ahead—exactly how to put these ideas into practice, the habits of winning. 

I like to use sports analogies. Another great football coach, Vince Lombardi, once said—and everybody knows this famous quote—that "winning isn't everything, it's the only thing." 

I know that many of us copywriters face intense pressure when we have to write a winning package. But Lombardi also said a much less well-known quote— that "winning is a habit, and so, unfortunately, is losing." 

One of the things that I'll be talking about is how to develop the habits of a winner in whatever field of life you are engaged. Since most of us here are in marketing, we'll get into how we can apply winning habits to producing breakthrough products and campaigns consistently. 

Now, getting back to the talent that is in this room, imagine how exciting it could be if we all shook hands tomorrow afternoon and formed a marketing corporation where we are all even-steven partners. 

Look at the talent in this room! We have extraordinary copywriters, superstar designers, some of the best list brokers, incredible entrepreneurs who've built megamillion-dollar fortunes in direct marketing, all-star marketing managers— they're all right here. So imagine if we could form a corporation. We would rule the direct marketing world for years if not decades. 

## **How We Should Relate to One Another During This Seminar** 

That's not going to happen, because we all have prior commitments. But I would like to try to capture a little bit of that excitement and feeling for these two days. 

I'd like us all to relate to one another as members of an elite all-star team, all deserving of each other's mutual respect, just as you would be on an all-star team. 

Of course, if you are the all-star team, that means I get to be the coach. Maybe I should be wearing a whistle, and if you misbehave, I'll make you do laps around this beautiful room! 

I invoke this spirit of camaraderie for a reason. We are not here to compare ourselves with anyone else, including me. I want you to understand that the principles I will be sharing are as powerful, reliable, and impersonal as the laws of 

**- 19 -** 

gravity or electricity. They don't depend on either me or you applying them. If you apply them, they will work. If you don't apply them, they won't work. 

As I'll explain later, I was quite a mediocre copywriter for about the first eight or nine years of my career, until I learned the system that I'm going to be sharing with you. It's a system that I've helped refine, with the help of some other very talented direct marketing people. It's not really humility, my saying this. I _really_ was a mediocre copywriter! As you may have guessed if you've read my sales letters about own my services, or the sales letters by other copywriters, as a group, we are not burdened by false modesty. 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

So when I tell you I was mediocre for the first several years of my career, you can believe it. If anything, that might be a stretch on the upside. 

The system I'm going to teach you works beautifully and reliably _irrespective of my personality, yours, or anyone elses._ 

That is important because I ask that you not judge these strategies by what I've done or not done in my life. Look at me not as a rival but as a coach. 

Coach Angelo Dundee, for example, the great boxing trainer, could not last in the ring with a top-ranked heavyweight more than 15 seconds. But he knew how to bring the best out of Muhammad Ali and make him the greatest. 

Look at me in that light, and let me do the same for you. I don't want competitive feelings coming between any of us in what we'll now share. Don't judge these principles by what I may have done or not done. There are entrepreneurs in this room who have done incredible things. But we're not comparing anyone to each other, including me. 

## **The Only Person You Should Ever Compete with Is Yourself** 

The only person that you should ever compete with in any activity is _yourself_ If you could be a little bit better next week than you are this week—you are succeeding. And that's how I view my career and ambition. I try not to compete with anyone else. I say this for two reasons. 

First, if I create in my own mind an intense competition with someone else, I would find myself placing too much of my focus on that person and not on what I 

**- 20 -** 

know how to do. You'll find yourself playing their game, imitating them too much, not focusing on your own strengths. I always want to be the best I can be, and if I can improve 1 percent a week, I'm happy, regardless of what anyone else may be doing. 

Second, you will find that you are far more open to learning and applying these principles if you never let yourself feel defensive about learning from someone else. That can happen if you see yourself as being in competition with the person you want to learn from. In that case, your competitive instincts, your own ego, can secretly, subconsciously disparage what that person can teach you, and you will be less likely to master his or her secrets of success. 

The best way to avoid this is to see me as a coach, which is what I am for you. With these principles, you have the potential to do much greater things than I or anyone else in this room or in this world has ever done, just as Coach Angelo Dundee helped Muhammad Ali be the greatest ever, far greater than himself. With me on this? 

OK, so if I'm the coach, what are the pages from Gary's playbook?

## **Harness the Miracle of "Compound Knowledge"— with 1 Percent Weekly Improvement** 

I'll explain all these principles as I go through the seminar. But let me give you a quick overview. 

One of the things, as I just mentioned, is the 1 percent weekly improvement. You don't have to improve your abilities 50 percent in one week—that's too hard, too high a goal. 

## Just improve 1 percent per week, in whatever you're doing—whether you're

**- 21 -** 

playing the guitar or the piano or trying to be a better copywriter or marketer. If you can increase your ability 1 percent per week, it means that in a year, you're at least 50 percent better than you are today. 

In two years, you're 100 percent better. Over time, that really compounds and you become a superstar. Or if you're starting as a superstar, you become simply incredible at that rate of improvement, and it's very easy to do. 

## **The "Ad-a-Day" Program...and Other Strategies** 

One of the tools for doing that is what I call my ad-a-day program. Every single day, no matter what else you have to get done, read a good ad, or at least a portion of a good ad, so that you're always keeping in your consciousness what great advertising is. We'll get more into that later. 

Also, we'll get into... 

- **The Great Fisherman's Secret,** and how that can help you reel in many more customers than you're catching now. 

- _**The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®**_ —a very reliable system for taking direct response breakthroughs out of the realm of the occasional, haphazard, and unpredictable, and transforming the whole process into a predictable, repeatable method. 

- **i/ The Secret of Master Closers** and how to apply it in your advertising. 

- **The Pareto Principle,** a very important tool for both managers and marketers. In any activity, a very few number of factors will usually yield the lion's share of results. This is normally known as the 80/20 rule. From the 40-year career I've enjoyed and more than $1 billion of scientifically measured direct marketing, I will isolate for you the few secrets of advertising that virtually guarantee—lock in—your consistent and often outrageous success. 

From now on, you won't have to worry about literally hundreds of small or irrelevant bits of advice you may hear and read about advertising. Follow these few critical strategies scrupulously, and you will never go far wrong. These are the factors that will give you the outsized portion of your results—80 percent, 90 percent. All you need to do is these few things right, and your success is as close to assured as humanly possible. 

- **How copywriters, marketers, designers—everyone in the room—can make more money,** and how to use all of the tools that we're talking about not to just achieve great things for others, but also enjoy the rewards yourself. 

- **Manifesting your own desires.** We'll get into this tomorrow. As marketers, 

**2 2 -** 

this is what we are—alchemists of desire. We want to help other people intensify, clarify, and then fulfill their desires. You'll get a tremendous boost in your ability to do this once you know how to unfailingly fulfill your own desires. There are some very interesting and reliable techniques I'll share on that. 

OK, so we're about to get started. Just a couple of housekeeping points. As Pauline mentioned, we are taping the seminar, and all of you will receive videotaped copies. Technically, we won't _give_ them to you, we're going to _license_ them to you. 

## **A Jay Abraham Anecdote** 

Jay Abraham recently gave me a tip on this. By the way, Jay sends all of you his regards. He wishes he could have been here, but he had to be in Malaysia. He extended to me a classic Jay Abraham offer, and you'll all get a kick out of this. He offered me $10,000 if I would rehearse this seminar over the phone with him! 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

I said, "Jay, I can't rehearse over the phone. It's a two-day seminar. I'll get a stiff neck." He quickly came up with a few alternate options. 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

Anyway, he said, "Well then, you have to tape it, so I can eventually get a set of the tapes." And then he told me the right way to do it. 

He said that he used to give away tapes of his own seminars to all attendees. But then he would see them on eBay, being sold for $100 or $500, whatever. This wasn't fair, because attendees might have paid, say, $25,000 to attend a Jay Abraham seminar, and then a week later the same seminar would be for sale on eBay for maybe $500. 

He told me that the way to avoid this is to license everything. Everything you'll receive from us, feel free to use it forever, for your own enjoyment, your own education. But in fairness to me and to all the people who've paid full price to be here, you're not allowed to sell or give away any of these materials to anyone else. My company—Accountable Advertising, Inc.—retains the ownership. You can have them forever, train your staff with them, that's fine. 

**- 23 -** 

Since you'll eventually be getting a set of the videos, you won't have to feverishly take notes for two days. 

What I would recommend, if you do like to take notes, is to jot down the most important points so you can get a head start. We won't be able to produce the videos for a while. It takes a long while to edit the transcript and produce the videotapes of an event like this, at least the way I like to produce them. So you'll want to go home with some good notes that you can put into practice immediately. 

Now, I know there are people who have come from great distances. Curtis Tuck—where's Curtis? 

Hi, Curtis! Curtis deserves the "Overcoming the Greatest Obstacles Award." That's because Curtis works in Saudi Arabia. You have no idea what this man had to go through to get out of the country safely and make his way over here. 

Many others have come great distances, as well. This is not an official break, but I'm going to ask for the next 30 seconds, if you could just look around and, if there's someone you don't know, just introduce yourself so that everybody can feel at home. There are some people who've traveled across whole continents by themselves, and I'd like everybody to feel welcome. So if you could just turn around, and if there's somebody you don't know, please introduce yourself. 

## _(AUDIENCE BUZZES WITH CONVERSATION AND GOOD CHEER AS ALL GREET THEIR NEIGHBORS)_ 

## — — _End of Session #1_ 

**- 2 4 -** 

**==> picture [446 x 246] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [368 x 295] intentionally omitted <==**

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ Wow, this is a friendlier group than I ever imagined! That's very nice. Thank you for welcoming everyone else. Now we're going to resume. 

As a Zen master might say—if you're going to hit a target, you must first _see_ your target. In that spirit, let us raise our most important starting question... 

## **What Is Advertising?** 

I'm going to begin by touching upon some things that are basic. Those of you who are advanced should not be put off by this. 

Let's imagine that we are the world champion—I was about to say Yankees, we New Yorkers have grown so accustomed to it. But I can't say that this year. 

It doesn't matter. Let's imagine we're the world champions of any sport. Even the superstars must rehearse the basics. It's the whole point of spring training, where even the veteran superstars polish up their fundamentals. 

We're going to touch on the core fundamentals very briefly to make sure we're all on the same page together. And then very quickly after that, we'll take it to advanced levels from the foundation that we will now start on together. So what is advertising? 

## **The Most Important Meeting in Advertising History** 

Many people may not realize it, but this month of May marks the anniversary of the birth of modern advertising. 

It was in 1904—May of 1904—that a famous meeting took place between two of the three great founders of modern advertising. The man on the left is Albert Lasker, and his meeting took place with the man in the center—the first brilliant copywriter, John E. Kennedy, in 1904. 

Albert Lasker was the son of a prosperous German immigrant. Albert could have pursued almost any career he wanted, because his father could have funded any ambition. He started out in journalism as a reporter. But his father disapproved, mainly because most of the reporters he knew were heavy drinkers. He urged his son to go into advertising, and Albert agreed, taking a job at the Lord & Thomas ad agency in Chicago in 1898, when he was just 18. 

He started at the bottom, cleaning out spittoons, among other responsibilities. Within a few years he was the owner of the agency. He was such a go-getter. He was great in personal sales presentations. He could charm the birds out of the trees. 

**- 27 -** 

In fact, years later he teamed up with Claude Hopkins, the greatest copywriter ever. Hopkins said of Lasker, "There's nobody who could resist the persuasive power of Albert Lasker when Albert wanted you to do something." 

While Lasker was amazingly persuasive personally, he had no idea of how to translate this personal magnetism into advertising. This bewildered, even tortured, him. On one hand, he could see that the right advertising could skyrocket sales, making a company's founders and key executives wealthy beyond the dreams of avarice. 

On the other hand, he had no idea which specific factors were responsible for such occasional successes nor which other factors caused failure, which was much more common. Why did advertising work for some but not others? What were the critical factors for success, and how could they be harnessed to make success predictable and repeatable? 

This all was a mystery to him, and he determined to find the answer, seeking out the opinions of the leading figures in advertising of his day. 

He searched all over the country for someone who could enlighten him about this, to no avail. Nobody else seemed to know how to systematically uncork the genie from the advertising bottle. 

## **The Canadian Mountie's Secret for Exploding Advertising Response** 

Then one day in May of 1904, Lasker was sitting in the office of the owner of the company, Mr. Thomas. They were talking about business. A clerk brought in a note and handed it to the boss, Mr. Thomas. 

The note read as follows. By the way, it's interesting that modern advertising was launched by the little direct mail letter you're about to see. It came from the man in the middle of the slide, John E. Kennedy, a Canadian Mountie turned copywriter. His note read as follows: 

_"Dear Mr. Thomas,_ 

_I am in the saloon downstairs, and I can tell you what advertising is. I know that you don[ f] t know. It will mean much to me to have you know what it is, and it will mean much to you. If you wish to know what advertising is, send the word 'Yes'down by messenger."_ 

## — _Signed John E. Kennedy_ 

Remember, Kennedy was delivering this note to a captain of industry, the head of the third-largest ad agency in the world, so this took gumption. He must have 

**- 28 -** 

been immensely sure of himself—a trait not uncommon among copywriters! 

Also, please understand that this is not an urban myth. Being a trained journalist, Lasker recorded this event exactly as it happened. He also saved Kennedy's note. 

Upon reading the note, Mr. Thomas asked Lasker, "Have you ever heard of this man?" 

Lasker said no. 

Thomas instantly dismissed it, probably assuming its author was just another crackpot who'd wandered into the saloon downstairs. He told Lasker, "The man must be crazy. I won't waste any time on him." 

But Lasker burned with curiosity. He said, "Let me see him. What have we got to lose?" **The Most Important Three Words in the History of Advertising** 

So Lasker sent word down to Kennedy to come up to his office. Kennedy told Lasker that he'd heard of his reputation and of his quest, how Lasker had been asking all the heads of other agencies, "What is good advertising?" without getting a satisfactory answer. 

"I have the answer," said Kennedy. But first, Kennedy asked what Lasker thought advertising is. Being a journalist, Lasker replied that as close as he could figure it, advertising is news—news about a product. 

Kennedy said, "No, news is a technique of presentation, but advertising is a very different thing. I can give it to you in three words." 

To which Lasker replied, "I am hungry. What are those three words?" 

Then Kennedy uttered the three words that have defined advertising ever since. "Advertising," he said, "is _**salesmanship in print."**_ 

Those three words electrified Lasker. He instantly grasped their profound implications, and those three words quickly became the foundation of modern advertising. Decades later, when David Ogilvy wrote his book, _Ogilvy on Advertising,_ a great book, he said, "There has never been an improvement on that definition." 

Actually, there was a slight improvement, which came after Kennedy, from the third founding father of advertising shown above, the all-time greatest king of copywriters, Claude Hopkins. He observed that since there are many different media, not just "print," it's more accurate to define advertising as _**"multiplied**_ 

**- 2 9 -** 

## _salesmanship"_ 

And this has been the gospel for direct marketers ever since, but not, unfortunately, for many other advertisers. 

## **They Are Guilty of Advertising Malpractice!** 

As I stand in this room, high above 55th Street, we can look out the window, turn our heads east, and see Madison Avenue, just a block down the street. If you walk over to Madison Avenue after our session today, you'll see it's a canyon of tall buildings. Up and down this canyon, and through the canyons of many adjoining streets, there are hundreds of ad agencies, perhaps even a thousand or more within several miles of my voice. Yet an astonishing number of these "advertising experts" do not know or embrace our definition of advertising. 

Why not? Because neither they nor their clients measure the results of their advertising. They are like the lost tribes of Israel, wandering in the desert. They spend millions of their clients' dollars running ads on TV and in magazines, on radio and in newspapers, hopelessly confusing their mission with their medium. That is, not knowing their mission is to sell, they imitate the entertainment they see in each medium. This is why you see so many silly commercials that create absolutely no desire in you whatsoever to own the product advertised. Like the TV shows that surround them, they seek to entertain, not sell. 

If one of these ads wins an award for creativity, or even a chuckle from the copywriter in the next cubicle, their misguided creators feel successful and proud. These people are flagrantly guilty of advertising malpractice, but their clients will never know until they apply the discipline of measurement—how many sales did this ad generate? 

In contrast, those of us who measure the results of our advertising, as we all do in this room, know the value of this historic definition— _advertising is multiplied salesmanship._ Its purpose is to sell, not win awards or applause. 

We want to sell more of our clients' goods, services, and ideas. As a starting point for understanding great advertising, always remember what a great ad is— _a single skillful salesperson able to persuade a thousand or a million people at once._ 

And look at the enormous leverage this gives you! Instead of having to pay a hundred thousand salespeople to knock on a hundred thousand doors one by one, with an ad you can have a single good salesperson close a sale with perhaps a million people _all at the same time!_ This gives you tremendous leverage when it's done right. It builds wealth very rapidly. 

**- 30 -** 

_salesmanship"_ 

And this has been the gospel for direct marketers ever since, but not, unfortunately, for many other advertisers. 

## **They Are Guilty of Advertising Malpractice!** 

As I stand in this room, high above 55th Street, we can look out the window, turn our heads east, and see Madison Avenue, just a block down the street. If you walk over to Madison Avenue after our session today, you'll see it's a canyon of tall buildings. Up and down this canyon, and through the canyons of many adjoining streets, there are hundreds of ad agencies, perhaps even a thousand or more within several miles of my voice. Yet an astonishing number of these "advertising experts" do not know or embrace our definition of advertising. 

Why not? Because neither they nor their clients measure the results of their advertising. They are like the lost tribes of Israel, wandering in the desert. They spend millions of their clients' dollars running ads on TV and in magazines, on radio and in newspapers, hopelessly confusing their mission with their medium. That is, not knowing their mission is to sell, they imitate the entertainment they see in each medium. This is why you see so many silly commercials that create absolutely no desire in you whatsoever to own the product advertised. Like the TV shows that surround them, they seek to entertain, not sell. 

If one of these ads wins an award for creativity, or even a chuckle from the copywriter in the next cubicle, their misguided creators feel successful and proud. These people are flagrantly guilty of advertising malpractice, but their clients will never know until they apply the discipline of measurement—how many sales did this ad generate? 

In contrast, those of us who measure the results of our advertising, as we all do in this room, know the value of this historic definition— _**advertising** is_ _**multiplied salesmanship.**_ Its purpose is to sell, not win awards or applause.

And look at the enormous leverage this gives you! Instead of having to pay a hundred thousand salespeople to knock on a hundred thousand doors one by one, with an ad you can have a single good salesperson close a sale with perhaps a million people _all at the same time!_ This gives you tremendous leverage when it's done right. It builds wealth very rapidly. 

**3 0 -** 

I know that some of you may be thinking, "I already know this. I've heard the Lasker—Kennedy story. And I already know that advertising is multiplied salesmanship." 

_Not so fast!_ Maybe we don't know all this as well as we think. Because even if we know the definition of advertising as multiplied salesmanship, do we really know what salesmanship is? _Ah, now we're about to take this to a new level.._ 

## **What Is Great "Salesmanship" in Today's Over-Marketed World, and How Can You Easily Master It?** 

Since Kennedy, Lasker, and Hopkins ruled the roost 100 years ago, most of us —copywriters, entrepreneurs, all of us in direct marketing—have been coasting on the coattails of the definition of advertising as multiplied salesmanship. 

There's just one problem. Over the last 100 years, "salesmanship" has changed radically. What was effective salesmanship back in the days of Hopkins and Lasker is much different than what works today. And if we don't understand this, you'll doom your advertising results to mediocrity. 

I remember reading books written by salespeople of yesteryear. For example, Elmer Wheeler was a top-selling author of sales books. He advocated, "Sell the sizzle, never the steak." And then there's my favorite title from those years, _Timid Salesmen Have Skinny Kids._ 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

## **Woody Allen's Definition of Hell** 

The approach to salesmanship in earlier days, which many marketers mistakenly emulate and thus cripple their results, was characterized by manipulation and high pressure. It was typified by a life insurance agent cornering somebody, using high-pressure tactics, and giving snappy comebacks to every feeble objection the prospect could raise, until the prospect, weary from the exchange and losing the battle of wits, succumbs and gives his written "OK" on the dotted line (never, we were told, use the word "sign," as people are afraid to _sign_ anything!) 

This high-pressure approach inspired Woody Allen's definition of hell— _"an eternal airline trip seated next to a life insurance agent."_ 

If you saw the movie _Groundhog Day_ and were amused by the mannerisms of the pushy insurance agent, Ned Ryerson, you've got the picture. 

**- 31 -** 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

This style of "salesmanship"—relying on hype and high pressure—no longer works well in direct marketing, assuming it ever did. 

First, we in direct marketing address our messages to 10,000 prospects at once, or a million. We're not in a face-to-face relationship with our prospects, where we can see how our prospects are reacting to what we're saying. At any moment, they can just toss our piece in the garbage or turn the page and dismiss us without our even knowing it. 

In other words, salesmanship changes radically when it moves from the person-to-person type of salesmanship to mass-market salesmanship. And you've got to know how to adjust your _style_ of selling if you want to maximize your response. 

There's a second major difference in what constitutes effective salesmanship in direct marketing today. Because we are all so inundated with advertising, you cannot expect your prospects to be like babes in the woods who've never seen a good sales presentation nor been confronted by high-pressure salesmanship. As a result, our prospects are more savvy about what you're up to and much less inclined to tolerate hype, manipulation, or high pressure. 

Finally, and most important, there is the clutter factor. Today, all day long, our prospects must process and try to avoid sales presentations pulling at them from every direction. Just to get through their busy day, people _are forced_ to habitually and vigorously ignore, avoid, delete, and dismiss marketing messages at every turn. 

And this is a much, much more powerful factor than it ever was 100 years ago or even 50 years ago, when John Caples and David Ogilvy were teaching us how to sell. We face a much different situation today. Because of the tidal wave of marketing that is sweeping over us as prospects, our instinctive, reflexive response is to avoid, dismiss, ignore, and delete it. 

The key question becomes, how do we apply effective salesmanship today, when our prospects are so weary of being sold at every turn? 

If you don't face this question squarely, you'll never know how to achieve consistent breakthrough results. Today, massive clutter is the single greatest barrier to your advertising success. So let's examine what defines "good salesmanship" in such an over-marketed environment. 

**- 3 2 -** 

## **The Only Two Skills You'll Ever Need to Master Salesmanship in Advertising— (1) How to Open the Sale and (2) How to Close the Sale** 

I have the answer for you, and it's very simple. First, let me simplify everything so easily that we can walk out of this room being very good salespeople in print or in person in today's over-marketed world. 

To make this as simple as can be, let's divide all of salesmanship into two parts. The first part is _**opening the** sale_ —starting the conversation with our prospects. The second part is _closing_ _**the** sale_ —coming home with the order. 

These are the two skill categories that encompass all of salesmanship. If you can follow what I recommend in these two areas—opening the sale with your prospects and then closing, taking home the order—you'll be outrageously successful in _"multiplied salesmanship![1 ]_ 

First let's focus the right way, the most effective way, to open the sale. 

## **What You Can Learn from the Amazing True Story of Captain John Rade and His Greatest Secret for Catching Fish** 

To do this, I'm going to start each of these major sections with a story, in this case, a true story about a man named Captain John Rade. He's a master fisherman who has a great secret to teach us about how to catch many more customers than our competitors. 

**==> picture [353 x 226] intentionally omitted <==**

**- 33 -** 

Pauline and I have, for many years, owned a home out in Amagansett. For those of you not from New York, I'll explain where that is. 

Long Island is just east of where we are now in Manhattan. It's 118 miles long and sticks out like a finger into the Atlantic. The very tip of Long Island is one of the world's great fishing grounds. That's because when fish migrate south in the cooler months or north in the warmer months, they must swim around the end of this big finger of land to get where they are going. They have to make their way around the point of Long Island, which is known as Montauk Point. And wherever you have lots of small fish migrating, you have big fish feeding on them, and feeding on the big fish, even bigger fish, and so on. 

As a result of all this, world records for some of the largest fish ever caught of various species have been set by captains who fish out of Montauk. 

As you'd expect, Montauk is a great center of many types of fishing boats— commercial fishing fleets, charter boats for hire by serious sports anglers, as well as the large "party fishing boats" that cater to weekend fishermen. 

It's a very interesting, lively harbor, and if you're ever out toward the end of Long Island, you must see Montauk Point. 

It's also the home port of many legendary fishing captains, including, for example, Frank Mundus, the great white shark hunter who was the real-life inspiration for the character Quint in _Jaws._ For many years, Frank made his living catching great white sharks on rod and reel. That's his idea of fun. 

Famous as he is, Frank Mundus, and every other fishing captain out in Montauk, holds one man in awe. This is the man who towers above everyone in his ability to catch fish, and most will readily admit it. His name is Captain John Rade. 

In fact, the _East Hampton Star,_ the major newspaper of the East End of Long Island, ran a story about him not long ago, entitled "John Rade, Maestro of Rod and Reel." You can see him here with a typical day's catch. Those are striped bass in his plastic bins. 

To understand how good a fisherman this fellow is, you must know that government agencies regulate fishing. To protect this natural resource, they set limits on the number and size of fish that can been taken by any one fisherman. For sports anglers, that number is low, maybe just a few fish per angler, depending on the species. 

For commercial fishermen who earn their living from the sea, the limits are much higher. They can catch hundreds or even thousands of pounds offish per 

**- 3 4 -** 

day, depending on the species. 

I mention this because Captain John Rade is a _commercial_ fisherman, someone who catches fish for a living, to sell to restaurants, fish markets, etc. But he is a very special and rare type of commercial fisherman. He is what they call a "pinhooker," which means he doesn't rely on the usual commercial fishing methods. 

Unlike most commercial fishermen, he uses no giant nets, no long lines with hundreds of baited hooks, no big boats with crews of five or six mates, none of that. He just goes out on in his little put-put motorboat with a rod and reel and hooks his fish one at a time. Yet he manages to come home regularly with hundreds and hundreds of pounds offish. 

To put that in perspective, on a given Saturday in the summer, there's the _Viking Starship,_ which is a big boat, called a party boat, where any weekend angler can come on board for a day's fishing. On a typical Saturday, the _Viking Starship_ may take out 50 to 60 fishermen. John Rade will usually outfish all 50 to 60 fishermen _combined,_ fishing in the same water. 

The newspaper reporter who wrote this story about him asked, "Captain John, how do you do this? This is an astonishing feat, and you do it day in and day out." 

Mind you, this man catches fish not just when they're biting. He will come in almost every day with hundreds of pounds offish, more than 50 to 60 other anglers catch, and he does this through all seasons. 

When asked "How do you do this?" he thought for a moment and said something profoundly important to us as marketers: 

## **"Don't Think Like a Fisherman, Think Like a Fish"** 

He said that he's been a fisherman ever since he was a child, and there's one guiding principle an old-timer once taught him to follow. Captain John Rade says that "when most fishermen go out to fish, they think like fishermen. When I go out, / _think like a fish."_ 

Captain John Rade has devoted his whole life answering this question: _"How can I think more like a fish? "_ 

Now let me ask you—as marketers, how much do we think like marketers and how little do we think like our prospects? You'll see what I mean in a moment. 

There are many parallels here. For example, John Rade will tell you that in a typical bait and tackle shop, fishermen will go in and want to buy lots of fancy, 

**- 35 -** 

feathered lures and other gimcracks. But, he says, most of this stuff is designed to catch fishermen, not fish. Yet fishermen are always chasing these newfangled devices. They waste lots of money chasing fantasies because they are thinking like fishermen, not fish. 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

By contrast, John Rade doesn't spend his time or money looking for magical solutions, investing in the latest fishing fads, or purchasing the hyped-up informational products written by those who make their living catching fishermen and not fish. (Starting to sound familiar?) 

Instead, he patiently studies everything there is to know about the fish he wants to catch—what their life is like, when they're hungry, what they like to eat as the seasons change, what bait is running in the water and attracting them on any given day, where they like to hang out, what makes them bite with abandon, what makes them lay low, how to catch the big ones and avoid the small, etc. 

And the most important thing he studies is the ocean, the environment his quarry swims in. It's the ocean that affects his target more than anything else. The ocean determines the temperature of the water, the currents, the depth at which the fish are swimming, the times they are likely to be hungry, etc. 

By studying all this, by learning exactly how to think and feel like the fish he's trying to catch and knowing exactly what their environment is like at all times, he manages to outfish, by enormous amounts, every other fisherman in Montauk, Long Island's mecca for thousands of ardent, skilled fishermen. 

## **What Can We Learn from This?** 

If we apply this same thinking, as I will now show you, you will take your success in marketing to a whole new level. If you can start thinking more like the fish instead of the fisherman, you will quickly begin to see incredible opportunities to improve your results in attracting and catching your prospects. 

That's what I'll be teaching over these next two days—how to catch a lot more fish by thinking more like the fish and less like the fisherman. 

By the way, I've tried to find a nonsexist way to say "fisherman," but "fisherperson" just doesn't cut it. 

Have you ever heard of Captain Linda Greenlaw? Terrific fishing captain and an award-winning author too. Remember the movie _The Perfect Storm?_ That was a 

**- 3 6 -** 

true story, based on a book of the same name written by Sebastian Junger. Captain Linda Greenlaw and her boat, the _Hannah Bo den,_ were featured in that movie. She was the captain savvy enough to steer clear of the monster storm. In his book, Junger called Greenlaw "one of the best swordfishing captains, period, on the East Coast." 

Anyway, a reporter once asked Captain Linda, "What should I call you? Should I call you a _fisherman?_ Should I call you _afisherwoman?_ Is that a word?" Captain Linda responded, "I'm a fisherman and proud of it. That's what you should call me." So if the word _fisherman_ is good enough for Captain Linda, it's good enough for me. 

## **The Only Two Ways to Get Anything You Want in Life** 

Getting back to my point here, if you can learn to apply John Rade's secret— thinking like the fish and not the fisherman—this one idea will prove immensely valuable to you over the course of your marketing life. 

Why will your rewards be so great? Because persuasion is the single most highly rewarded skill in a free society. And there's a good reason for that. 

Plato, the great philosopher, said that when you see a lot of persuasion taking place, it's a sign of a free society. That's because almost every person on this earth wants to be able to get other people to do what he or she wants. It's how we human beings normally get what we want in life—as social beings, we must get what we want through others. 

And there are only two ways to get somebody to do what you want—by force or persuasion. 

In a free society, we're not allowed to use force. We must use the art of persuasion and allow our reasoning to be freely accepted or rejected by others. That's why we smile in _The Godfather_ movie when the Godfather says... 

## **"I Made Him an Offer He Couldn't Refuse"** 

The irony is that this phrase is typically used for voluntary persuasion, but of course the Godfather was using brute force, as all criminals, tyrants, and bullies prefer to do. 

Thankfully, there are rules in free societies that forbid that. So wherever there's a free society, you know there's a great demand for those who know how to persuade, because it's the only legal way for people to get what they want. 

**- 37 -** 

**==> picture [386 x 255] intentionally omitted <==**

This is why, in free societies, those who know how to persuade are the most richly rewarded. It's a skill everyone must harness to accomplish their goals. It's why salesmanship, in person or in print, is on average the most highly rewarded profession in the free world. 

If you learn how to persuade as I will teach you, your rewards will be enormous. You'll enjoy wealth, independence, fame, security. And you'll have a lot of fun doing it. You can pursue any passion you want. You can have the power to help anyone or any cause dear to your heart. You'll have the almost irresistible power to bring other people to your way of thinking. 

## **Your Biggest Obstacle to Success in Marketing— and the Most Overlooked!** 

But before you can reap all these wonderful rewards, you must learn how to overcome a great hidden obstacle. 

What is this biggest obstacle to being a masterful persuader and marketer? 

It's _not_ what we marketers would normally suspect. It's not poor headlines. It's not weak offers. It's not underperforming mailing lists or other media. Yes, all of these concern us as marketers, and we think about them constantly. But that's the whole point! We are always thinking like fishermen, not like fish. And we must change that. 

What does it mean to start thinking less like fishermen and more like the fish we hope to catch? 

**- 38 -** 

Let's take a cue from Captain John Rade, who first studies the ocean, the environment our fish swim in. 

When you think of the ocean, the environment that our prospects swim in, you see life from a whole different perspective. And you will immediately understand your biggest obstacle to success. 

It can be summed up in a single word, and that word is... 

**==> picture [347 x 203] intentionally omitted <==**

One hundred years ago, when Albert Lasker, Claude Hopkins, and John E. Kennedy were writing, there was little clutter. Hardly anyone knew what advertising was or how to harness its power. Sure, there was advertising, most of it your basic "house for sale" variety. There wasn't anything like the inundation of marketing messages your prospects have to cope with today. 

Even 50 years ago, when David Ogilvy and John Caples wrote their rules of good direct marketing, they didn't address clutter so much, because it wasn't that big a problem. Today it's your single greatest obstacle to success, the very ocean your prospects swim in. I can't emphasize this enough, how addressing this obstacle is your most important starting point for more successful advertising. Today everyone from the corner hot dog man to the president of the United States is trying to market, spin, and jive you at every opportunity. 

A trade magazine in the insurance industry recently conducted a study. They found that the average household is exposed to 3,000 sales messages per day. Every one of us—every man and woman in America—has 3,000 advertising messages beamed at us every day, on TV, in magazines, on the radio, etc. 

You might say, "Well, I don't read or listen to 3,000 messages a day." Of course not! That's because you're behaving just like your prospects, which means 

**3 9 -** 

you're energetically tossing, ignoring, avoiding, dismissing, and deleting virtually all of those carefully crafted messages instantly, just to get through your day. 

Just like your prospects, you're deleting your email with a quick trigger finger. You're letting your magazines and newspapers pile up in a corner of your desk and, when the pile gets too big, will just toss them out unread. 

And I know you're doing this—when the TV commercials run, _you_ run either to the fridge or the bathroom, am I right? 

That's assuming you even tolerate the presence of commercials at all. An article I read recently described how all of America and the rest of the world are now separating into two types of TV viewers—those who watch TV in the normal way, as the shows are broadcast, with the commercials. Then there is a muchfaster-growing group of us who record our favorite programs for viewing at another time, when we zap out or zip past the commercials. 

In this second group of avid commercial avoiders, there is another huge and growing submarket. These are the people who prefer to buy or rent their favorite TV shows on DVD rather than watch when the shows are broadcast. These savvy people save great amounts of time by automatically skipping the commercials. This is the reason why so many TV series—just like movies—are now being issued on DVD _without_ commercial interruption. Check this out for yourself at Amazon—you can now purchase (or rent through Netflix) entire seasons of many of your favorite TV shows and avoid all the usual commercials. 

And did you know that during the Super Bowl, the workers at municipal water companies can tell, without watching any TV in the control rooms, exactly when the Super Bowl commercials are being run? How do they know? They see a spike in water usage during those commercials as the TV audience dashes to the bathroom and flushes all at once! 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

Sure it's funny, but not if you've just paid two million dollars to address this runaway audience! 

Let's get back to my point, that we _must_ think less like fishermen and more like fish. 

As marketers, we sit in our offices and craft our messages meticulously. We spend days, sometimes weeks, polishing the copy, bouncing it back and forth among everyone involved. We fuss and fidget. We hire a designer to make it look 

**- 4 0 -** 

enticing. We sweeten the offer, tweak the copy, double-check our guarantee, second-guess the colors, enhance the premiums—we do all of this and more until we think our precious message is just right. 

Then we lovingly put it out there for our prospects to receive with open arms. And we are totally thinking like marketers, not prospects! We are behaving as if we are oblivious to the fact that our prospects are eagerly tossing, avoiding, dismissing, and deleting our messages with hair-trigger attention spans because they simply can't cope with this barrage of 3,000 marketing messages per day! 

## **As a Marketer, You Assume You're Playing a Friendly Game of Catch with Your Prospects— But They Are Playing Dodgeball!** 

It's as if, as marketers, we assume we're playing a friendly game of catch with our prospects. _But our prospects are playing dodgeball!_ They do _not_ want to read your messages! They don't have time. They are dodging them, deleting them, tossing them, and avoiding them, because they can't possibly review 3,000 messages a day and don't know and don't care that yours may be different. 

Addressing this reality—realizing that the fish we aim to hook swim in an ocean polluted to the brim with marketing messages—is the absolutely necessary foundation for increasing your response. On the flip side, however, once you do pay more attention to this reality, you can increase your response and conversions massively. 

So you'll really get this, let me ask you to take off your marketer's hat and put on your consumer's hat. When you return home from this conference and open your emails, are you going to be thrilled to see 158 new emails piled up? Probably, many of them will have been sent by the other people in this room! 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

Nevertheless, you're just going to go through and delete away feverishly— click, click, click, click. You're not going to pay attention to each of these carefully crafted headlines, offers, and pieces of body copy. Not going to happen! 

It's the same with your direct mail, your magazines, everything else. This is exactly how your prospects are processing your messages right now. 

To better understand the marketing-polluted ocean your prospects swim in, just imagine that as you walk out of this building today, somebody comes up to you 

**- 41 -** 

and asks, "Can you tell me how to get to Grand Central Station?" 

You want to be a helpful citizen, so you try to figure it out, and you give the best directions you can. Then you walk another three feet and somebody else runs up and interrupts you, again asking you for directions, this time to the Bronx Zoo. OK, you try to help that lost soul too. Then this happens again and keeps happening every three or four feet that you walk. 

Well, if you walked five blocks and were interrupted by 100 people in this way, how do you suspect you'd react to the 101st, the 302nd, or the 2,057th? 

After just a small series of such incidents, you'd put up a shield. You'd assume a whole different, hard-shell attitude toward responding to such interruptions. You'd vigorously avoid them. You'd erect a mental barrier, a determined facade that says, "I'm not responding to these interruptions. My first order of business is to avoid them." 

Well, this is exactly how you are reacting to thousands of messages beamed at you, and exactly how your prospects react to _your_ messages. 

It's also why response rates have fallen precipitously in recent years, as the volume and intensity of marketing has exploded. Your prospects know how to blow you off at every opportunity. They _must_ blow you off to get through their day. 

And now you know your big enemy of advertising success. Often in the movies, you'll hear the leading man say, "Our real enemy has not yet presented himself." 

## Well, now you know your real enemy, and its name is _clutter._

You have to find a way to get past this automatic rejection response, to pierce the shield, to breach the fortification, to instantly flash a signal or password, in effect, that gains you entrance, that says, _"No, my message is different."_ And this requires a whole new way of opening the sale. 

## **Let's Review...** 

Where are we then? Satellite photo! 

First, we've said that advertising is _multiplied salesmanship._ OK, we all agree on that. Most of us knew this already. 

But, aha, when we look at what _salesmanship_ means, we realize times have changed radically since the days of Lasker, Kennedy, and Hopkins. Today we must know how to be supersalespeople in a whole new era marked by massive marketing clutter and glut, a problem the founding fathers of modern advertising didn't have to face. 

**- 42 -** 

OK, to rebuild our skill set to succeed in this new environment, we've agreed to break "salesmanship" into two component parts: _opening the sale_ and _closing the sale._ 

_Opening the sale_ has gotten much tougher than ever and is where most marketers are now woefully incompetent, because they're thinking like fishermen, not like fish. That means they're not paying enough attention to the ocean of other marketing messages their prospects must swim through every day, an ocean so polluted to the brim with marketing messages that our prospects must reflexively, energetically, and persistently toss, delete, skip, and avoid your marketing 

messages _as soon as they recognize them as such—just like the person walking on a busy New York street who resents being interrupted every five seconds by someone asking for directions and develops a fierce, hard-shell resistance to such interruptions._ 

## **Wow, This Sounds Pretty Depressing! What's the Answer?** 

OK, now that you thoroughly grasp the problem, it's time for the solution. 

There is a simple five-word master secret that works like a charm in getting around this problem. It penetrates the shield, outflanks clutter, repositions your messages as something other than pesky interruptions, captures your prospects favorable attention, and most effectively opens the sale and achieves breakthrough results even in today's over-marketed world. 

I'll reveal the five-word "secret" in a minute. But first, please notice the last part of the sentence that comes right before the subhead above. I'll repeat it, to set up the solution: 

If your prospects... 

_...reflexively, energetically, and persistently toss, delete, skip, and avoid your marketing messages as soon as they recognize them as such..._ 

...then the first critical principle is to stop making your advertising _look_ like marketing messages! If prospects cross the street to avoid Ned Ryerson, the relentless life insurance agent in _Groundhog Day,_ for heaven's sake, stop dressing and acting like Ned Ryerson! 

You don't want your marketing messages to look like marketing messages! When they do, it causes most of your prospects to instantly flee from you! 

All by itself, this one strategy will rocket your response higher if you apply it as I'll show you now. 

**- 4 3 -** 

I realize this goes against the grain of what many direct marketing books teach. Many of these books reprint control ads that are obvious sales messages. You can spot them as such a block off, just as your prospects can, just like you can see Ned Ryerson coming toward you on Main Street. 

For a time in my career, I emulated these same old examples. But once I discovered the five-word secret I will now share, I started enjoying extraordinarily higher results. It happened when I stopped making my direct mail and space ads look like these "famous control ads" and instead made them look like editorial matter. 

## **Beaten Like a Rented Mule** 

Even today, in some of these famous books, I still see control packages, "hall of fame control packages," that I know weren't even working anymore after they were beaten mercilessly years ago by my packages that employed the principle I'm now sharing. My direct mail packages, written in this new way, beat them like a rented mule. Yet these old-fashioned, obsolete designs and control packages of yesteryear are still being trumpeted as models throughout the industry by those who are behind the times. If you follow the wrong model, you're going to get the wrong results. If you're not doing it already, just test what I'm saying here and you'll see a quantum leap in your results. 

## **OK, Drumroll Please... What Are the Five Magic Words?** 

A minute ago I promised you this... 

_There is a simple five-word master secret that works like a charm in getting around this problem. It penetrates the shield, outflanks clutter, repositions your messages as something other than pesky interruptions, captures your prospects favorable attention, and most effectively opens the sale and achieves breakthrough results even in today s over-marketed world._ 

Are you ready for the five simple words? OK, here they are... 

## **Make Your Advertising Itself Valuable!** 

In recent years, this simple five-word strategy has revolutionized direct marketing. It has come to completely dominate the advertising of the most savvy marketers on the planet. I speak of major marketers like Phillips Publishing, Healthy Directions, Boardroom, Rodale, Agora, and others. 

**- 44 -** 

Our tests of this simple five-word principle— _make your advertising itself valuable_ —shattered every single control package I and my clients have ever tried it on, _without exception!_ 

That means that dozens of previous control packages, the former "kings of the hill," the best direct mail packages ever written by top copywriters to sell scores of products for the savviest, largest, most successful mailers I've ever worked for— every single one of these king-of-the-hill mailings fell like wobbly dominoes, usually losing by overwhelming margins, to this new style of advertising, based on this strategy 

And you needn't take my word for this. Order any of the products offered by today's top mailers, the companies I've mentioned—Phillips, Agora, Rodale, Healthy Directions, Boardroom, Lombardi, Weiss Research, etc. When you buy some of the products of these major league players, you'll start to receive their advertising regularly. You'll be stunned at how this strategy— _make your advertising itself valuable_ —has proven so effective that few competing strategies are even tested against it. 

Among savvy mailers who test numerous approaches in their advertising, this master strategy has won virtually every contest, virtually sweeping the field, so much so that few of these top mailers seldom even test any other advertising style. 

I have noticed a few, very few, savvy mailers _not_ using this strategy, and I don't know if it failed in those cases or if they just haven't tried it yet. I suspect the latter. But among the clients I've worked with, and they include most of the largest mailers, if their carefully tracked advertising tests could be equated to the Super Bowl, the score in favor of this five-word master strategy over more traditional-style advertising would be something like 98 to 0. 

That's how dominant and utterly necessary this strategy has become if you want to beat clutter and get past the instant, hair-trigger rejection of your prospects. If they see your message as _marketing,_ out it goes in a wink. If they see **it as** _**something valuable,**_ it's a keeper. 

This master principle for opening the sale applies to any product and any **— medium you sell in. If you** _**make your advertising itself valuable and by that I mean both look and be_ valuable**_ —you make it much more difficult for your prospect to toss it. Even if it's basically the same headline, same offer, or the same body copy. If it's designed to look like typical sales material, it gets tossed. If it's designed to look and be valuable, you get a hearing. 

***** 

**- 4 5 -** 

## **OK, Exactly How Do You Do It?** 

Now let's see some examples, so you'll fully understand. 

Let's start with space ads. 

When you're doing a space ad, it should look like an article. Period. That is the easiest way to give a space ad the instant appearance of value to your readers. After testing this many times, I have never found an exception, at least not in my own work or anyone else's that I know of. 

In fact, this is something that John Caples and David Ogilvy did talk about even 40-50 years ago. 

Ogilvy conducted a study for his clients. Many of them were not direct response advertisers. They were in packaged goods, so he had to rely on readership studies, not sales, to see what kind of response his ads were getting. 

He found consistently that ads that looked like editorial articles enjoy 500 percent higher readership compared with ads that look like ads. So right off the bat, you get a 500 percent pop in the readership of your advertising just by making it look like a valuable editorial article! 

John Caples ran a split-run test once. Both halves of the test featured exactly the same copy, headline, and offer. The only difference was that one was designed to look like a typical ad, while the other was designed to look like an article in the magazine where it ran, _Reader s Digest_ The one that looked like an article pulled in 81 percent higher sales. 

This makes sense. Why? Because people read newspapers and magazines for the articles, not the ads. Their eyes are habitually trained to scan the headlines of the articles, but not necessarily the headlines of the ads. 

So this is a scientifically proven principle, even more so today when people have a much quicker trigger finger on getting rid of anything that looks like sales messages. 

Let's look at some examples. 

When I worked in the direct response department at Ogilvy's agency, two of our good friends were list brokers, Walter and Joyce Lake. They said, "Gary, can you help us write an ad that we'd like to run in _Direct Marketing Magazine_ to get us business?" 

I wrote this ad for them, and it looked exactly like an article in _Direct Marketing Magazine._ It featured valuable advice on how to pick a list broker and triggered many new leads for them. It was valuable, and prospects cut it out and saved it. That's the secret behind this principle. Make the advertising itself so 

**- 4 6 -** 

**==> picture [465 x 613] intentionally omitted <==**

valuable, people won't want to throw it away. 

I'm going to give you a few more examples of space ads that look too valuable to ignore, because they either look like articles or contain valuable information. Here's one by the master himself, David Ogilvy. 

A lot of people enjoy beer with their oysters during the summer, so Ogilvy ran this Guinness Guide to Oysters. In the ad, he tells why Guinness goes so well with each variety of oyster. It's so superior to the usual "buy our beer" ads because it provides interesting information. If you're an oyster and beer fan, you can't ignore it. You may well conclude, "Maybe I should try Guinness the next time I'm savoring oysters by the shore." 

**==> picture [393 x 470] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [394 x 487] intentionally omitted <==**

Here's another: _"How_ _**to** take_ _**out** STAINS."_ This ad for Rinso soap ran many years ago. It shows the most common stains—grease, oil, grass, lipstick, etc.—and how to remove each with Rinso. This ad made history because it was read and remembered by more people than any other ad ever created for a detergent. It once again proves the astonishing pulling power of giving valuable information right in **the ad—** _**making your advertising itself valuable.**_ I would certainly save this ad if I came upon it in a magazine. As Pauline will attest, I stain everything! 

## _**(LAUGHTER)**_ 

**- 4 9 -** 

Ogilvy said it took him 22 years to write this next ad. He wasn't happy with it, and he didn't run it until he got it right. This can happen when you want to advertise your own services. It's very tricky. Most attempts wind up sounding like braggadocio, which is off-putting to the very people you want to attract. In the end, Ogilvy realized the power of the five-word principle we've been discussing— _**make your advertising itself valuable,**_ and the result is this absolutely brilliant ad. 

**==> picture [437 x 524] intentionally omitted <==**

I worked at the Ogilvy agency when he ran this, which became the foundation for an entire series of ads, each geared to a different group of prospective clients: _How to write financial advertising that sells...how to write travel advertising that sells...how to write food advertising that sells...how to launch new products..._ etc. They were so great because they gave away lots of secrets right in the ad. They worked like a charm, bringing in numerous world-famous clients to the agency and helping it grow rapidly into one of the largest agencies in the world. 

As an example of its persuasive power, let's look at the subhead of this ad. Just imagine for a minute that you own a company. You're not happy with the ads being created by your ad agency. You have no idea if your ad agency even knows what it's doing, let alone how to sell your product effectively. 

One morning you're reading your _Wall Street Journal_ and suddenly come upon this bombshell. The headline catches your eye. And you read this devastatingly effective subhead... 

_"Ogilvy & Mather has created over $1,300,000,000 worth of advertising, and spent $4,500,000 tracking the results._ 

_"Here, with all the dogmatism of brevity, are 38 of the things we have learned/[1 ]_ 

You continue reading and discover all these great tips about how to create better advertising. And they make so much sense. Can you imagine how antsy you start to feel about your current agency, which now seems so incompetent by comparison? If you were the head of a large company spending millions on advertising, you'd probably want to contact David Ogilvy. Many did. 

By the way, it's ironic, but many creative people who _don't_ work in direct response can be resistant to Ogilvy's teachings about advertising that sells. 

Why? They yearn to be _creative_ and to do their own thing. As a result, it was always a challenge, even for Ogilvy himself, to make sure that his writers and art directors were following his guidelines. 

For example, when I joined the Ogilvy agency, I was told by my copy chief to be sure to read Claude Hopkins's book _Scientific Advertising._ My copy chief told me that right in the introduction of this book, our boss, Mr. Ogilvy, wrote, "Nobody, at any level, should be allowed to have anything to do with advertising until he has read this book seven times." 

I was young—just 25—and ambitious. I wanted to be a good employee. So I immediately dashed to the company library to borrow this book, hoping there was at least one copy left on the shelf, as there were many new employees recently 

**- 51 -** 

hired. _Good luck_ —there were several! I reverently took a copy from the shelf, slowly opened the cover and was shocked. The little card in the pocket inside the book revealed that the last person to borrow this book from the company library had done so _several years ago!_ And the same was true of the other copies! 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

So even at a big agency where you have a master like David Ogilvy urging every one of his employees to read Hopkins, even then, not many do. I reported my surprising library experience to my copy chief, who confided it was a universal problem. He said it bewildered and frustrated him no end that most people in advertising simply don't feel as if they have to study their craft. He said it would be as if our medical schools were turning out doctors who couldn't tell you where to locate the spine. _Mystifying!_ 

Let's move on. We'll start going more quickly through these. You can see that they all embody our five-word master principle for opening the sale— _make your advertising itself valuable._ 

## **This Next Ad Is Densely Packed with 6,540 Words. Yet It Pulled a Mind-Boggling 10,000 Responses from a Single Insertion** 

## Here is a superb example: _"What Everybody Ought to Know About This Stock And Bond Business"_

This ad ran in the _New York Times._ It contains 6,540 words. The offer is buried deep in the body copy, and there isn't even a coupon. Yet it pulled a mind-boggling 10,000 responses from a single insertion. 

When people tell you, as they often will, that nobody will read long copy or that long copy doesn't sell, just show them this ad and tell them it pulled 10,000 responses from just a single run. You can build a very large business when you can produce 10,000 responses from a single ad in a single newspaper. 

Best of all, this ad beautifully illustrates the awesome response-generating power of our master five-word strategy for opening the sale— _make your advertising itself valuable._ 

This principle is timeless. I've illustrated it with some older ads. Now let me show you one that I tore out of _New York_ magazine just the other day. This is an ad I've seen running all over... 

**- 52 -** 

**==> picture [460 x 590] intentionally omitted <==**

The headline is _**"Better than Botox?"**_ It sells an antiaging cosmetic. It doesn't look exactly like an article, but it has that editorial feel and seems to offer the reader information of value.
