Notice this deft touch—the question mark after the headline. That little question mark greatly expands the strength you can pack into the words that precede it. If you were to state just a flat-out claim _"Better than Botox,"_ you'd have to back up that promise with much stronger proof. The question mark gives you a lot more flexibility. 

**==> picture [375 x 502] intentionally omitted <==**

**- 54 -** 

Paradoxically, or so it might seem to those who don't understand the principle we're using here of mimicking an actual editorial article, the question mark does _not_ weaken the headline. It strengthens it, because that's how a magazine would run an article on the subject, as if to say, "Could this new discovery actually be better than Botox? Let's look at the evidence." 

## **How to Apply This Master Strategy in Direct Mail** 

OK, let's move on to direct mail. 

How do we apply this awesomely potent five-word strategy— _**make your** advertising itself valuable_ —in direct mail? 

The number one way that you can be virtually guaranteed to open the sale in direct mail is to send your message in a FedEx envelope. That instantly communicates importance and value. 

Obviously this is quite expensive. But in certain high-dollar situations, it's the smartest, most responsive thing you can do. Your prospect _must_ open a FedEx envelope. It might be a lawsuit, a large check, a letter from a regulatory agency, a signed contract—who knows? You have to open it. 

For example, Pauline and I are involved in raising money for disabled children. Whenever we want to send a proposal to a potential donor, asking for a major gift, we always send it in a FedEx envelope. Nothing gets opened faster or more reliably. Whenever I would send a proposals to a potential new client, I would always underscore its importance by sending it via FedEx. 

**==> picture [430 x 222] intentionally omitted <==**

Let's review—satellite photo! 

Advertising is multiplied salesmanship. 

Salesmanship consists of two parts, knowing how to open the sale and then knowing how to close it. 

Right now, we're still in the first of these two parts, how to open the sale, how to start the conversation. 

Because of the overwhelming number of marketing messages we all face daily, we know that most people instantly tend to toss, dismiss, delete, ignore, avoid, flee from, and otherwise reject messages that instantly communicate that they are sales pitches. So, as a first rule, we want our messages to look, feel, and actually be valuable to our prospects. In direct mail, your best way to do that is with a FedEx envelope. 

## **Don't Spill Your Candy in the Lobby** 

The next best way, if your price point won't permit FedEx, is to use a personal letter, one that _looks_ like a personal letter. This one, for example, doesn't look or feel like junk mail that's been printed up in the millions. Neither does it "spill its candy in the lobby," that is, reveal who it's from in the upper left and thus risk instant rejection when the recipient suspects, _"Aha! A sales pitch! Out it goes!"_ 

**==> picture [453 x 206] intentionally omitted <==**

- 5 6 - 

Now, you might be thinking, as I encourage you to use FedEx envelopes and personal letters, _"Gee, we really can *t afford either Both are way beyond our price point."_ 

But could you be thinking too much like a fisherman and not enough like a fish? 

If your prospects are telling you, "You will always have my full attention above anything else if you send me a FedEx package," might it not be worthwhile, at least as a brainstorming exercise, to see if you can devise a product or service with margins large enough to use such an incredibly valuable delivery vehicle? 

Hear me on this—delivery vehicles that are guaranteed to be opened are much rarer than products or services that your prospects will happily pay you huge money for! 

In other words, a delivery vehicle with a guaranteed "open me" response is so rare and valuable, you _must_ consider developing products to take advantage of it. A guaranteed-reliable distribution channel is too good to waste! 

Otherwise, you're still thinking like the fisherman and not like the fish. It would be like saying, "My fishing pole is too small to catch those big fish breaking water over there. So we'll just let them go." Get a new fishing pole, for heaven's sake! 

In other words, don't adjust your marketing universe to fit the small size of your current pricing. That's working backward and thinking like the fisherman! Consider adjusting at least some of your product line to catch the biggest, richest, and most lucrative opportunities out there. An underused distribution channel, such as FedEx, is one such opportunity too good to pass up. 

## **Your $25 Million Opportunity** 

Let me break it down. If you have a list of 100,000 good customers, there may be at least 5,000 people who, if they're sent the right sales pitch in a FedEx envelope, will tomorrow send you a check for $5,000. 

That's $25 million dollars—for a single effort! A 5 percent response rate is too much to expect? Maybe not, but OK, let's say you pull just 1 percent. That's still $5 million for a single effort. 

Doesn't that make it worth your while to create a product that sells for $5,000 to some upscale portion of your audience and have the most powerful copy you can put together delivered in a FedEx envelope? If you don't, you may be passing up an easy $5 million to $25 million opportunity, or even a lot more if your list is larger or you can come up with an even more attractive product or service for the 

**- 57 -** 

highest end of your market. Don't dismiss such huge opportunities just because _"it's not what we normally do."_ 

The same is true for using personalized letters. Don't adjust your opportunities to capture higher response because you think a certain delivery method, such as personalized letters, is "too expensive" to cover your current price point. Think up a product with much larger margins that can easily cover the cost if it works. 

**==> picture [241 x 249] intentionally omitted <==**

## **The Cracker Jack Secret for Boosting Your Direct Mail Response** 

Another way to do make your direct mail valuable—too valuable to toss—is to apply what I call the "Cracker Jack Secret." 

Nobody in the history of the world, as far as anyone can tell, has ever thrown away a Cracker Jack box without first rummaging to get the little prize inside, even though it's worth maybe half a penny. 

We can apply the same secret to direct mail, and I don't mean giving away a plastic trinket or something gimmicky. 

The best freebie you can give away in a direct mail package is _information of great interest and value to your client._ 

For example, I once was retained to write a direct mail package for a newsletter published for survivalists, people who don't like to live near major cities in case of a nuclear or biological terror attack, widespread anarchy, civil unrest, etc. There's a strong segment of such people out there. As you can see, the 

**- 58 -** 

headline of my direct mail package announced, _**"Survival Checklist Enclosed."**_ This was a huge winner. 

**==> picture [422 x 248] intentionally omitted <==**

The people in this market are really into checklists—how many cans of tuna fish do I need to store in my basement, how much water, etc., to survive the coming chaos. If you're a person who feels passionate about this subject, you simply cannot throw this mailing away. It's too valuable. 

That's the secret, that's the goal here, _to make your advertising too valuable to throw away._ In time, after much testing, we in the industry found that the most effective ways to accomplish this strategy was with formats we came to call magalogs and bookalogs. 

In these formats, you may devote as high as 50 percent of your selling space to provide great information, reserving maybe 50 percent to sell. That's not counting the order form and premium pages. 

That's not a bad ratio to shoot for, and it means that your advertising will be filled with information too valuable for your best prospects to throw away. To be truly valuable to your market, your giveaway information must not be sales hype. It must not be fluff pieces or old-hat information your market already knows or will find boring. It must be the most compelling, fascinating, irresistibly interesting information you can find on the subject, at least as interesting and compelling as an article on that subject that you might find in a current issue of a real magazine. 

- 5 9 - 

**==> picture [481 x 567] intentionally omitted <==**

## **I Knew This Would Be a Blockbuster** 

For example—is Ed Elliot in the room? Hi, Ed! So good to see you! We are very privileged to have Ed here. Today he's head of creative services at Healthy Directions, but many years ago when he was a designer, he was the coinventor of 

**6 0 -** 

the magalog. 

Ed, imagine if you had patented that somehow or copyrighted it? Every magalog ever since would owe you a royalty! Of course, a design format is impossible to copyright. Anyway, Ed and a copywriter named Jim Rutz were the first to come up with this format, which came to be called a "magalog." When I saw what they had done, designing a direct mail piece to resemble a magazine, I knew it would be a blockbuster. 

You see, in space, we were always able to emulate editorial articles, just by making an ad look like the articles in the magazine where it would run. 

But a direct mail package had always consisted of an envelope, a letter, a reply card, and maybe a brochure. I could never figure out how to capture an "advertorial" look in a direct mail package, because direct mail was predominantly the realm of the personal letter. 

But Ed and Jim realized that we can make a direct mail piece look like a _magazine_ and give it greater perceived value. 

After I saw what Ed and Jim had done, I couldn't wait to try my own version. I got the chance with Vicki Moffitt at KCI. Is Vicki here? Hi, Vicki, how are you! And this piece, featuring the _**"Hottest Investment Opportunities of the Year,"**_ was a blockbuster. 

I made Richard Band look like "Man of the Year" on _Time._ I intentionally emulated _Time's_ red border. Vicki, you got a phone call from lawyers at _Time_ saying, "What are you doing? That's our red color, and it's copyrighted." We said, "What do you mean that's your color? You can't copyright a color!" They said, "Well, our small army of lawyers says we can. You don't want to fight us over this, do you?" We asked, "How do you feel about blue?" They said fine, they don't own blue. In our next mailing, we made the border blue, and it worked almost as well. 

We weren't too worried about the color change, because the perceived value was in the overall feeling of the piece, including the headline _**"Hottest Investment Opportunities of the Year."**_ 

To pay off the headline, right in the mailing, Richard Band, the editor, described some of these great investment opportunities, such as his pick of the top growth stocks for the year ahead, a way to make a small killing in ultrasafe U.S. treasuries, terrific investments in real estate, collectibles, etc. The basic premise was, take a look right now at some of our fantastic investment ideas. If you like what you see here, get the full details on these and many more by trying a riskfree subscription. It was a tremendous success. 

**- 61 -** 

Here's another one I wrote, this one for Boardroom, around the same time. Where's Brian Kurtz? Hi, Brian! 

I've done many packages with Brian and the good people at Boardroom. Before I wrote my package, this was the control for their flagship publication, _Bottom Line/Personal_ The headline is _**"Bills it's OK to pay late."**_ 

**==> picture [429 x 168] intentionally omitted <==**

It's a traditional, conventional #10 package, very strong in its day. It features a classic Boardroom-style headline, _**"Bills it's OK to pay late"**_ You've seen many such Boardroom headlines over the years, bullet-style headlines you just can't resist. The letter inside continues with such bullets, which Boardroom calls _"fascinations."_ 

OK, so that was the control. And this had beaten many, many controls before it, by Gene Schwartz, Mel Martin, and many other famous copywriters. So in this #10 direct mail package, you had the all-time champion, the number one winner in the Darwinian evolution of constant testing up to that moment, the very best, stateof-the-art copywriting ever produced by Boardroom, one of the world's largest and savviest mailers. 

Now compare that to the next package, which I wrote for Boardroom introducing to its market the five-word strategy I advocate— _**make your advertising itself valuable**_ —and not just a sales pitch. 

It's _**The Little Black Book of Secrets.**_ Right on the cover, you can see that there are scores of these little secrets you're going to discover _right in this booklet!_ 

In other words, you don't have to send away for a product to get the immediate gratification of satisfying your curiosity. We're going to tell you, right in the booklet (so how can you throw it away?)... 

**6 2 -** 

**==> picture [401 x 455] intentionally omitted <==**

- The safest seat on an airplane and the scary reason why 

- If you win the lottery, do this _immediately_ to protect your winning ticket 

- World's most effective hiccup cure 

- Secrets of enjoying much better sex 

- 10 simple secrets of how to get rich 

- 5 signs of a good stock 

- What it means if you have cold hands, etc. 

And all of these very intriguing secrets were printed right on the front cover. You know that you could go right into this book and discover the secrets behind these provocative bullets. 

- 63 - 

Unlike the old school of advertising, it's not clobbering you with a big-promise headline, which you know you can experience _only_ if you buy or commit to something. 

Instead, to open the sale with perceived value, it visually says, "Right now, open this booklet and you'll receive some really interesting and valuable information." When you do read these valuable pieces inside the booklet, you are told that these are just a few of the hundreds upon hundreds of additional tips and secrets you'll receive when you try a risk-free subscription to the publication, _Bottom Line/Personal_ 

This is the state of the art of our knowledge today in terms of getting highest response. _**You must give something away that's valuable.**_ You will almost always outpull a former control that does not use this secret. 

## **This Beat the Control Package by 133 Percent!** 

Boardroom uses an index system in testing all its advertising. The current control, as a benchmark, is always indexed at 100, and then other packages get an index based on their performance compared with the control. For example, a package that indexes at "90" performed 10 percent below the control. 

So let's take the very powerful mailing that had been Boardroom's control, the #10 package I just mentioned, featuring _**"Bills it's OK to pay late."**_ This was the king-of-the-hill mailing, the control, written by a great copywriter, someone who had beaten previous strong packages by greats like Gene Schwartz, Mel Martin, and many others. 

Yet _**The Little Black Book of Secrets**_ smashed this previous, king-of-the-hill control with a 233 index, meaning it brought in 133 percent higher response. 

That's the kind of difference this strategy makes because clutter, and the resulting hair-trigger response to _toss, toss, toss_ your sales pieces when they look like sales pitches, is now universal. 

But how can you toss _**The Little Black Book of** Secrets?_ Who knows, maybe it's personal about me—maybe it's _my_ black book! I've got to investigate. So you take a longer look and, before you know it, you see these other intriguing secrets and now they've sunk their little hooks into you, and you can't escape. 

For Rodale, I wrote another package, this one for a book on home remedies. 

This follows the same principle. The advertising itself is loaded with goodies. Stop a toothache with two cloves, page 26. Heal a burn with milk, page 17. Take the sting out of a bee bite with meat tenderizer, page 25. You can't throw these tips away. 

**6 4 -** 

**==> picture [351 x 478] intentionally omitted <==**

As you can see, we apply this principle again and again, in endless variety... 

**- 65 -** 

**==> picture [370 x 470] intentionally omitted <==**

When Rodale offered a weight-loss book, I asked if we could borrow the masthead of their sister publication, _Prevention,_ because it would lend much greater credibility to the piece, which then made our direct mail piece look much more valuable. Also, I gave an overview of this weight-loss plan on page 8 of the piece, which led to the headline on the front cover. 

**6 6 -** 

**==> picture [372 x 475] intentionally omitted <==**

For Phillips, I created this MD's digest, filled with news of medical breakthroughs, for arthritis, pain, heart disease, and so on. All of these breakthroughs are described right in the mailing. If there's a certain supplement that you should take for arthritis, it's named in the piece. But if you want to know the proper dosage, when to take it, and how to combine with other foods and supplements, that's all in the free premium you'll receive with your no-risk free trial of the newsletter. 

In other words, when you're writing these pieces, you have to hold something back to motivate readers to buy. You can't just give away all your good information. Use a healthy portion of it, such as an absolutely essential piece of 

**- 6 7 -** 

information on how to make a given remedy work, to hold back for your premium, so that you incite great motivation to buy to get the rest of the information. 

## Here's another example for Rodale. The title on the cover promises _"Foods_ _**That Heal"**_

**==> picture [399 x 464] intentionally omitted <==**

Notice that this starts to look more like a paperback book, one you might come across in a drugstore. By not filling the cover with lots of screaming bullets, we make it look more valuable. 

Here's another, this one for Phillips' _Mutual Fund Investing_ newsletter. As many financial magazines feature at the beginning of each year, the cover story is a forecast of the best and worst mutual funds of the next twelve months. To mutual 

- 6 8 - 

fund investors, this is catnip. They could not throw it away. _**We are making the** advertising itself too valuable to toss._ 

**==> picture [526 x 562] intentionally omitted <==**

## **The Trojan Horse Welcome Package** 

This next one is really intriguing. In fact, if I was still actively copywriting, I wouldn't tell you this secret, because it's too good. I would keep it just for my clients. 

But now that I'm retired, I can spill all. This is something that I don't think anyone else has ever talked about, because even if you're a direct marketing professional, if you were to receive this, you probably wouldn't even realize the strategy it's using. 

I call this the _Trojan Horse Welcome Package._ Here's how I came up with it... 

I was thinking that we want our promotions to both _look_ and actually _be_ quite valuable. What other formats have high perceived value? 

I realized that most of our prospects are avid mail order buyers. That's why their names are on our mailing lists. If they're on the list, it shows that they've bought things before. 

I'm a very active mail order shopper. Often I will send away for something, and when it arrives maybe three or four weeks later, my mind is on something else. At first I don't even remember sending for the particular item, especially if it's an informational product, and especially if it comes in a package or from a source I don't immediately recognize. So I'll have to open it. 

I reasoned that this must be a common experience for most mail order buyers. If I receive something that looks like I might have ordered it, I must open it. I wouldn't want to take a chance throwing _that_ out! 

So I created this next piece to look just like a premium booklet, something you would receive if you had subscribed to a publication and this was your welcome gift. 

To me, as long as you are truthful in what you're putting out there, you are under no legal or ethical mandate to make your advertising look like advertising, only to be tossed. Salespeople are not obligated to dress like Ned Ryerson. Make your advertising look like something valuable, and make it _be_ something valuable. 

In this case, the piece looks like a premium, just like the free book you receive after you subscribe. 

This cover is entitled _**The Solid Gold Treasury of Inside Information.**_ The subhead says, "A one-volume gold mine of the best secrets ever published in _Bottom Line,_ America's #1 magazine of inside information." 

## Then below that, a second subhead says, "Your free Welcome Bonus from _Bottom Line Personal!_

**- 70 -** 

**==> picture [469 x 545] intentionally omitted <==**

Welcome bonus? The recipient may well wonder, "Did I send for this? Did I subscribe to something?" Better check this out. 

When you get inside the package, the copy says something like "We'd like to welcome you to the _Bottom Line_ family and show you our best ideas, free of charge. If you like them, we invite you to subscribe and receive many more." 

**- 7 1 -** 

So, yes it is a welcome gift, one that arrives to welcome you to the family, without any subscription having been required. And the interior is designed to look like a beautiful premium, filled with wonderful free information, looking for all the world just like the kind of bonus you normally have to pay for and receive only after you've signed up. 

Sneaky? Some would make the accusation. But what on earth is wrong with giving away a beautiful, free Welcome Bonus as a "come-join-us" gift _without_ requiring any money or commitment first? To me it's in the best tradition of free sampling, something that is not only ethical but very consumer friendly. 

So that's the _**Trojan Horse Welcome Package.**_ Believe me, this has worked exceptionally well. In fact, I cannot remember an initial test where this did not beat the previous control. Naturally, nothing lasts forever, and eventually, after being mailed in large quantities, this piece, like all controls, started to fatigue and eventually lost to another fresh-faced format. But it will almost always give you a big boost in response the first time you try it. 

Here is another example of that same principle. It's very subtle—doesn't scream lots of bullets, it just says, _**"Your Special Report Enclosed"**_ 

**==> picture [470 x 264] intentionally omitted <==**

Since this was first mailed, it's been imitated a lot, so now it doesn't seem like such a breakthrough. But look at the inside, at how the "premium look-alike" feeling has been carried throughout. Having a design that goes all the way with this idea gives you a big edge over other mailers who don't go to such pains to 

**- 72 -** 

create a consistent editorial-looking design for their direct mail. 

The inside of the package is on glossy stock, just like a premium that you might get when you subscribe. 

Notice that the overleaf says, _**"Your Special Report Enclosed...And thank** you!"_ Doesn't that sound as if you have subscribed, that this is a welcome gift? 

**==> picture [480 x 510] intentionally omitted <==**

This is what you're allowing the reader to assume, and why I confess it is shamelessly clever, though I believe totally ethical, as you're not saying anything untrue. The illusion continues beneath the overleaf, where the reader encounters a glossy front cover that looks for all the world like a premium booklet. This editorial look continues throughout the piece. How can you toss this out? 

**==> picture [446 x 499] intentionally omitted <==**

Critically important in this strategy—you must pay off the "thank you" mentioned atop the overleaf. 

You'll note that in the letter below this headline, we thank the reader for taking a few minutes now to review the 38 great tips inside the report on how to get Washington out of your wallet and off your back. We tell the reader that we want to make it extremely rewarding for him to give you a little time right now to demonstrate the quality of our investment advice. If he likes these ideas, he can try a no-risk subscription to discover many more. It all flows smooth as silk and is a wonderful way to open the sale. 

Again, these are all different melodies on the same theme—the best way to open the sale is to... 

## Make Your Advertising Itself Valuable, Too Valuable to Throw Away! 

I'm going to wrap up on this soon. 

Before I get to this next example, let me say a word about the names we use to describe these formats. 

When Vicki Moffitt and I did our first magalog together, we didn't have a way to describe this new style format. Jim Rutz and Ed Elliott should have named it. They invented it. But at first it didn't have a name. Vicki asked me, "What should we call this format? The production people need a name." 

I remembered hearing the word "magalog" used many years before to describe something that featured articles and a magazine-style look but also sold merchandise, like a catalog. So I said to Vicki, "Why don't we call it a 

'magalog'?" Rather ugly word, but that's how this format got named. 

And there followed a parade of ugly names. Exploiting this principle of making the advertising look valuable, we created little paperback books and named them bookalogs. And, perhaps the ugliest name of all, a mailing that mimics a newsletter issue became an "issue-log." Yes, in our unbridled attempts to sell, we are also massacring the English language. 

Let me show you one of the first issue-logs. It's designed to sell a newsletter, which makes a newsletter look-alike very appealing, because it's sent to people who love newsletters. And of course it embodies our master principle of looking like, and actually containing, very valuable information instead of mere advertising. 

**- 7 5 -** 

**==> picture [514 x 681] intentionally omitted <==**

And let me tell you an interesting story about these next examples. 

**==> picture [451 x 423] intentionally omitted <==**

When Pauline and I bought a beautiful old home in Garden City, the man who came to insure the home said, "Wow, this is a gorgeous home. What do you do for a living?" 

After many years in the business, I've learned never to say, "I write direct mail." Most people haven't a clue about what that means. So they'll respond with something like "Oh, you mean like ads in the _Pennysaver_ newspaper, that kind of thing?" 

## Then I'd say, "No, I don't have anything to do with _Penny savers."_

"Oh, you print up those little cents-off coupons then?" 

**- 77 -** 

"No, no. Nothing to do with cents-off coupons. I write direct mail offering magazines, newsletters, books, other informational products, and many other kinds of services." 

"Oh, I see. You write newsletters?" 

"No. Let's just forget it!" 

I learned to say something else instead, which every person in business understands. In fact, if any of you copywriters are looking for business, this is a great way to describe what you do. It instantly makes your questioner believe he has stumbled across someone he's been seeking for years. 

When someone asks what you do, just say, "You know how every business likes to get new business?" 

Your questioner's eyes will light up and he'll say, "Sure!" 

Then you say, "Well, it's my job to know everything there is to know about how to get new business." 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

And this is what I said to the insurance agent who asked me what I do. 

As soon as the words escaped my mouth, he said, "I'm always looking for new business. Can you get new business for me?" 

I had a gleam in my eye as well, because Pauline and I had just bought this big home, and it came with a big mortgage. I reasoned that perhaps I could create a powerful direct mail campaign for this local insurance agent. Then, if it works, I could sell it to other insurance agents all across the country. 

The samples I've been showing you so far were all written for large mass marketers, such as Phillips, KCI, Boardroom, Rodale, and so on. 

But you can make a ton of money creating campaigns for local advertisers and then selling basically that same campaign hundreds or even thousands of times across the country to other local advertisers. _Create a marketing template, like a software program, for a given industry, and you can make a fortune._ 

In fact, there are some people in this room doing this very thing. One fellow who ran a very successful pizza parlor now sells off-the-shelf marketing campaigns to other pizza parlors. We have a dentist with us who teaches other dentists how to market their services to make a lot more money. There are financial planners who do the same. 

**- 78 -** 

So the gleam in my eye was because I was looking a lot further than this one agent. I asked him, "What is the market you'd most like to crack?" He replied, "Physicians. I could sell them malpractice insurance all day long." 

I said, "I'll make a deal with you. I'll create a very strong advertising campaign for you. You won't pay me anything for it. But when it works, I want you to give me a testimonial and serve as a reference, which will help me market this across the country to other insurance agents who want to build their business with a similarly effective marketing campaign." 

He said, "That's very fair. I'll go for that." 

Copywriters, this is a great way to build your wealth. Once you do establish a format, a medium, and a message that work for somebody locally, you can package that and sell it to a thousand similar practitioners or small businesses across the country. 

Let's say you develop a highly successful program for real estate agents. You can then sell it for, say, $5,000 to other agents. If you do that in each of 1,000 communities, you've just made yourself $5 million for a single piece of copy The leverage is tremendous. What's more, you can also offer annual updates and new campaigns to keep this river of revenue pouring into your bank account like a perpetually flooding Mississippi. 

This was my plan for my new insurance broker friend. I gave him a great mailing, a takeoff on the Gary Halbert "dollar-attached-to-the-top-of-the-letter" mailing. By the way, is Gary here? There's Gary. Hi, Gary, how are you? 

I felt funny imitating Gary's letter so blatantly, so I changed it. I had the agent attach a $2 bill to the top of the letter. This was extremely eye-catching because most people don't realize that there is a legal $2 bill minted by the U.S. government, and when you see one, it's fascinating. The copy said that, yes, this $2 bill is 100 percent real, and so is the extraordinary offer that comes with it. 

It was really powerful. I also invited his physician prospects to sign up for a newsletter I created for this insurance agent. I named it the _Wealth Letter for Physicians._ 

The only problem with my plan—and it was a big one—was that this agent didn't listen to my instructions. The letter was a six-pager, with lots of good copy Very persuasive. But without my knowledge, he printed his entire letterhead— name, address, logo, phone numbers, partner list, the whole works—at the top of _every_ one of the six pages! So it no longer looked like a six page letter. It looked like six different one-page letters, all stuffed in the same envelope. It looked totally bizarre. 

**- 7 9 -** 

When I saw the printed samples, I asked, "Why on earth did you do this?" He replied that he wanted his name, his company, and contact information in front of the prospect at all times, on every page of the letter. I told him this made no sense, that nobody sends a letter like that. He should toss these letters out and print them over the right way. This way won't work. He should make it look like a normal letter he might send to a friend or business colleague, with just one letterhead, not _six!_ 

He said, "The printing is already paid for, and I'm going to send it out. If you're right and it doesn't work, I'll print it up again the way you suggest." 

I said, "Look, I've put in a lot of work on this, and you're getting a free ride. I want you to do this right." 

But he didn't listen, which is a problem when you're dealing with people who are not professional direct marketers. They often think they can improve on what you recommend, and they're almost always way off base. 

Naturally, his mailing didn't work. I asked, "OK, so when are you going to reprint and mail the letter the correct way?" 

He replied, "Your letter didn't work. Why should I invest any more money in it?" 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

That's the frustration you can run into when you're dealing with local people who are inexperienced marketers. However, on the Internet, you can make it a lot easier for them. You can take care of all the important details yourself, make it much more foolproof. Your client won't have to get involved with direct mail production, the renting of offline mailing lists, knowledge of postage regulations, all the unusual pitfalls that can lead an inexperienced marketer astray. 

And please don't let my experience with this agent discourage you from trying to sell your copy to local business people in dire need of sound marketing. The opportunity there is tremendous. Who wouldn't want to sell the same piece of copy that's working many, many times over? This could be a fabulous annuity for you. 

Once a local real estate broker finds out, "Gee, you really do bring in great leads for me, both sellers and buyers," he or she will want to spend another $5,000 with you next year to update it. And if you build up to 1,000 people paying you $5,000 a year, you have a $5 million renewable yearly income, just from updating 

**- 8 0 -** 

a few good pieces of copy. 

## **The Secret of the Wise Old Salesman** 

Let me wrap up this session with the secret of the wise old salesman, which will reinforce the idea I just raised, about marketing on the Internet. 

One of the best salesmen I ever worked for was a top producer for Merrill Lynch. He's rich and retired now, but when he worked, his office was not far from this building. 

He territory was midtown Manhattan, a happy hunting ground for a stock broker who knows how to sell. And, boy, could this guy sell. 

When I asked what made him so successful, he told me that his secret was twofold. First, he'd never put heavy pressure on anybody to buy anything. Second, he was pleasantly and incredibly persistent. 

He said it would often take him an average of nine phone calls before he could connect with somebody to share a moneymaking idea. 

This never bothered him. He considered it his competitive advantage. While other brokers would give up after three or four attempts to speak with a wealthy executive, he would phone an average of nine times. 

## **"All the Universe Bows to Persistence"** 

He would get a list of top executives, and these Manhattan woods are full of them. Then he would call and, when he managed to get past the gatekeeper, would tell his prospect that he's an expert in using a wide variety of investments to lower their taxes and keep a higher portion of their income. The first time or two, the prospect would usually blow him off. But he would just call back again the following month. After about nine such calls, he would often hit pay dirt. Such pleasant, pressure-free persistence has an almost magical way of melting people's resistance. _All the universe bows to persistence._ 

He called it hunting the elephant because he was going after elephant-sized clients, people with massive amounts of wealth. And once they became his clients, they would reward him with exceptional commissions. So it was worth it to him to be pleasantly persistent. 

That's how he became one of Merrill Lynch's top producers. 

Today, you don't have to use telemarketing the way my client, Ron Goldberg, did. In many cases, you can't, thanks to the Do Not Call list. 

## Even if you could, it's much more efficient to do it via the Internet. You can do

**- 81 -** 

it through an ezine where you're offering valuable information. 

Remember, that's the key of this whole session— _make your advertising itself valuable._ And you'll find no smarter way to do that than with the right kind of ezine. 

## What Is "the Right Kind of Ezine"? 

By now you _have_ to know the answer, or you've been asleep all morning. 

Above all you want an ezine prized by your readers _because it contains so much valuable information, not just relentless sales pitches I_ 

If you're not using a high-value, fantastic-content ezine that your prospects rave about amongst themselves, you're missing one of the most powerful marketing tools ever conceived. Such an ezine gives you the "pleasant persistence" of the wise old salesman, without the cost and inconvenience of sending out printed offline newsletters...and without the "annoyance" factor of telephone marketing. 

Most of the ezines I get, and I subscribe to scores of them, commit the same critical mistake. They sell too much. Before they realize it, they've become characterized in their subscribers' minds as _nonstop sales pitches._ Every issue is somehow going to be tied to a teleseminar, a new product, or the best new way to get rich, build your business, or who-knows-what-else ever to come down the pike. By not giving away enough truly valuable, interesting information, they don't get opened anymore, even if the recipients never get around to removing their names from the subscriber list. 

I have signed up for scores of ezines but read only a tiny handful—those that offer truly valuable information with just an occasional sales pitch. All the others I keep getting, in the hope that maybe sometime I'll have the time to read them. But I delete them by the dozen, unopened, because I suspect they're just more hamhanded, hyped-up attempts to sell me something, not deliver truly valuable content, and I don't want to waste my time. 

Before, I said that in off-line direct mail, the ratio I like to use in magalogs, bookalogs, and other long-copy formats is about 50 percent great giveaway information, and 50 percent sales copy. 

However, online that ratio changes. If you try to sell with 50 percent of your copy, in my view, you will be pegged as someone whose ezines sell too much and should be deleted. We are all looking for excuses to delete every email we can. Don't make the mistake of giving your prospects that "delete-me-fast" excuse! Make it too valuable to delete. Fill your ezines with maybe 90 percent of awesome 

**- 82 -** 

content and 10 percent or less of selling. 

People are so weary of opening emails just to be sold something. In my own ezine, I give away even more than 90 percent. For one thing, I'm not an affiliate for anyone, and I don't push other people's products. There's nothing wrong with doing that, except that it increases your ratio of selling-versus-valuable content, and that is not a good thing. Choose your sales opportunities judiciously and infrequently, and deliver truly great content the rest of the time, and your ezines will be opened. 

As I say, I don't sell others' products, because I'm retired now. 

But when I launched my ezine a year and a half ago, I did have in mind that I would someday like to host a seminar like this, my farewell seminar, one I won't ever repeat. 

I created my ezine— _Bencivenga Bullets_ —where I give away almost 100 percent of valuable information and almost never sell anything. I say almost never because a few times I mentioned, ever so softly, that I would be doing a seminar for a limited number of people. 

Because I sold nothing up until that moment when I just mentioned this seminar—not even a sales pitch, just a mention—readers started beating my door down to register. It was as if I flicked a lure into a virgin pond of ravenously hungry fish. 

Uh-oh, now I'm in trouble. I realize that I've just called all of you fish, and I really don't regard you that way, you must understand. How will I talk my way out of _this_ now? 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

Well, I have found that every analogy, if pushed too far, will break down, so let's say that you're not fish at all, just hungry customers. 

The point I'm making, of course, is that your ezines, even more than your offline advertising, must overflow with honest-to-goodness value for your market. I can guarantee that almost all of you, if not all, are selling too much and offering too little by way of real value in your ezines. Change that and you will prosper mightily online. 

Time to wrap up and repeat the most important lesson you will ever hear on how to open the sale. Build your advertising around these five magic words— _**Make your advertising itself valuable!**_ Do this in every medium you use—direct 

- 83 - 

mail, space, off-line newsletters and ezines, and any other advertising medium that the future may bring us. Make it something that your prospect finds too valuable to toss, delete, avoid, or ignore. 

That's how to open the sale. In our next session, we'll move on to the smartest ways to close the sale. 

## — — _End of Session #2_ 

**- 8 4 -** 

**==> picture [469 x 211] intentionally omitted <==**

_GARYBENCIVENGA:_ Let's review—satellite photo! 

Advertising is multiplied salesmanship. The two parts of salesmanship are (1) how to open the sale and (2) how to close the sale. 

Next we saw that the best way to open the sale is to _**make your advertising itself valuable**_ to your best prospects, so they will welcome and embrace it, not toss it away as "just another sales pitch," an annoying interruption in their busy day. 

Now we're going to get more advanced. 

**==> picture [331 x 230] intentionally omitted <==**

• . y . ? * ^ - , . , - . ^ ^ ^ ... •..,-,. ,••:.•.-•••.>>••••..••-....^v,.-j»^.;..'-->'<";'V.-<'.-••.'••••-•-".-5.•.--;•-• •.•:~->.':- _•'••-:•••••• :•.,••.••••;•:••.••_ . -• •.•.••.-.•..•«<>-,-,•-•; • . v . ' . T ^ * ^ s : « . v : W ^ ' 

Look at this famous painting of the Tower of Babel. It symbolizes where advertising is today. As I mentioned, there may be a thousand ad agencies within a couple of miles of where we are right now. If you were to ask the head of each one, _"What is good advertising? "_ you might get a thousand different answers. 

I used to work on Madison Avenue and was totally confused about what great advertising is or how to create it. But later in my copywriting career it all came together for me, when I went to work at a small direct response ad agency in New Rochelle, New York. 

In time, I became a copy chief at this small but extraordinarily talented ad agency headed by copywriting legend Dan Rosenthal. The business did very well, and we had to hire other writers. I and my partners wanted to be sure that those who came to work for us were not confused and knew how to sell in print. 

- 89 - 

As copy chief, I had to come up with a way to teach copywriters a system that would produce breakthrough advertising repeatedly and consistently. 

I didn't have my thinking as well formulated as today, about how to think like the prospect and not like a marketer, as I described this morning. 

But with the help of others, I put together a training system that turned even young, wet-behind-the-ears copywriters and trainees into highly skilled and even masterful copywriters. 

Since then I have tinkered with the system to make it even better, more reliable. This is the system that I want to teach you now. I call it... 

## **The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®** 

How many people here would like to know a very simple four-part formula for knowing how to be in perfect sync with your best prospects? How many would be interested if this same four-point formula would allow anybody in your organization, whether a designer, writer, marketing manager, anyone, to make extremely valuable contributions to your advertising? And what if this same fourpart formula could make it simple for you, with mathematical precision, to grade any direct marketing ad and be very close to how it's going to pull—how many would like to know that? 

## _(LAUGHTER, AS AUDIENCE SHOWS INTEREST)_ 

OK, I expected that response. That's because I'm already using this _Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®_ with you! 

Notice I didn't say _an_ you. All persuasion begins with the person you're trying to help or sell something to or serve. And it all starts with _what that person wants._ 

So many times I've seen marketers go wrong in trying to sell something because they want to sell it, not because anyone wants to buy it! Again, that's thinking like a marketer, not like a prospect. 

The great Dale Carnegie, author of _How to Win Friends and Influence People,_ once said that he loves strawberry shortcake. He would hate the thought of eating worms. But when he goes fishing, if he puts strawberry shortcake on the end of his line, the fish won't bite. They prefer worms. So we always have to gear our efforts to _what our prospects want to buy, not what we like ourselves or what we'd like to sell._ 

- 9 0 - 

**==> picture [289 x 214] intentionally omitted <==**

Let me illustrate this point with some great advice from one of the most persuasive men of the 20th century. His name was Bernard Baruch. 

Many people here may have heard of him. He was a great financier, a Wall Street titan. He was also an adviser to six presidents. He possessed a unique ability to persuade people. 

You can imagine that on Wall Street and in Washington, there are egos so large that they have trouble fitting through the front door. Yet Bernard Baruch could walk into a room full of big egos, all with different points of view, determined to get their way, and usually in a short time, sometimes 20 minutes, a half hour later, you might see everyone walk out of that meeting and say, "Bernard Baruch has the solution, and we are all in agreement." He was famous for this. 

Near the end of his life, somebody asked him, "Mr. Baruch, what is your secret? How are you so persuasive with so many different kinds of people? Is it something the rest of us can learn?" 

He responded by giving the questioner his 12-word secret, which I will share with you now. It is the heart of all persuasion, whether in print, in person, or in any other medium. 

## **This Profound Twelve-Word Sentence Is the Foundation of All Persuasion** 

That sentence, which all marketers should memorize and live by, is... _Find out what people want, then show them how to get it._ 

## That's how he would persuade the big egos in the room. He would sit down

- 9 1 - 

with each person and sincerely probe to find out "What is it you really want; what would you most like to get out of this deal?" 

He would ask the same of each key person. Then he would involve everyone in working together to find a mutual solution that would give the parties most if not all of what they wanted. 

This is the foundation of all persuasion. _**Find out what people want, then** show them how to get it._ 

And that's where our four-part formula for persuasion will begin, with what your market wants. 

Before we get into this, I should say that this will _not_ address, at least not at this point, how to write or design great ads. We will get into that later. The most important point that we want to understand now is how to create a _selling proposition_ for any product that is in perfect sync with what your prospects want. 

One reason so many new products blow up on the launchpad is because we choose to sell something because we want to sell it, not because anyone wants to buy it. 

Now, you'd think that this would be obvious, but advertising, like any other field, can get caught up in a lot of jargon—psychographics, demographics, relationship marketing, etc. I have found it much more helpful to define marketing opportunities in this way... 

## **Problems Are Markets** 

When I say problems, I mean the whole panoply of consumer desires— problems, wants, needs—I'm lumping them all together as "problems," as a shorthand convenience. 

I know some people in selling and advertising may say, "Never sell people what they _need._ Always sell what they _want."_ To me that's a semantic difference. Last week, my mom fainted. We took her to the hospital, and she needed a pacemaker put in. Thank goodness, she's fine now. When we visited her in Winthrop Hospital, there were other people in the cardiology wing, lined up in beds, scheduled to have their chests cut open and their hearts and arteries worked on—not because they _wanted_ it but _needed_ it. 

These heart operations will cost a great deal of money, pain, time, and inconvenience. But the beds are filled and the waiting list long, not because people _want_ these operations, but because they _need_ them. There's nothing wrong with selling into people's "needs." Either wants or needs can be powerful 

- 9 2 - 

motivators in the right hands. 

But, as I say, just as a shorthand, we're going to refer to everybody's wants, concerns, desires and needs, etc., simply as _problems._ A master salesman once gave me this simple yet powerful bit of advice: "No problem, no sale." We want to find the biggest problems of those we wish to serve. 

I take an aspirin only if I have a headache! You can be the most brilliant copywriter in the world, but you're not going to convince me to take an aspirin if I don't have a headache. 

That's how you have to think about your prospects. You must identify those with the greatest problems that you can solve. Don't waste your time trying to offer something to people you must educate before they even realize they have a problem. You'll waste a lot of time and money doing that. Instead, you want to sell to those who are not only aware of their problem, but are screaming in pain over it and are desperate for a solution. 

If you want to know what the best markets are, think of it that way: _problems_ are markets. The bigger, more extensive and more painful the problem, the bigger your opportunity. 

## **"I Take an Aspirin When I Have a Headache, Not Because I Want a Relationship with My Druggist"** 

Demographics are not markets. I never bought an aspirin because I'm a member of a demographic group. Neither do I buy just because I'm in a relationship, or might want one, with a given company. I take an aspirin when I have a _headache,_ not because I want a relationship with my druggist! Once I have a headache, I might be more inclined to buy from my friend Wayne, the friendly pharmacist in my hometown of Garden City. But I've got to have the headache first. 

In short, I don't buy anything unless I have a want or desire. Again you may think, this is so obvious. Who doesn't know this? 

Surprisingly, it's not so obvious, not when we spend so much of our lives as marketers and forget to see the world through our prospects' eyes. 

## **A Classic Example Is the Ford Edsel** 

In the 1950s, one of the greatest failures in marketing history was named after Edsel Ford, the only child of Henry Ford. 

Back then, the Ford company had a big problem in the middle of its product line. Ever since the days of the Model T, many people would buy a Ford as their 

- 9 3 - 

first, entry-level car. Years later, Ford came out with the Lincoln Continental, a car you might buy when you started doing well in your career. Remember, this was before the invasion of high-end foreign cars from Mercedes, BMW, Lexus, etc. So Ford was strong on both its low-end and high-end offerings. 

But in the middle part of the market, Ford was losing a lot of sales to General Motors, which had many different models in the middle range. 

The bright lights at Ford, including a lot of business and marketing MBAs and other brilliant executives, said, "We've got to solve this problem." The Edsel was the answer to this problem. 

They introduced the Edsel and spent $350 million in 1950s money—which has got to be worth at least 10 times that in today's money. So they spent the equivalent of an incredible $3.5 billion launching this car, only to discover that nobody wanted it. 

Why not? Because automobile buyers weren't waking up in the morning saying, "My goodness, I'm so worried about the middle of Ford's product line! I don't know how I'm going to get through another day until I solve this problem." 

You see, the "problem" that Ford tried to solve was its own, not the customer's. Yes, it was a big problem inside the Ford building. Outside the Ford building, nobody cared. 

This is a classic example of how even the best and brightest can fall into this trap of trying to sell something because they want to sell it, not because people want to buy it. 

**==> picture [231 x 169] intentionally omitted <==**

OK, let's get to the formula that will prevent this and make sure you have a maximum probability of marketing your goods and services successfully, _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®-_ 

- 9 4 - 

**==> picture [372 x 278] intentionally omitted <==**

Here's the formula in its most basic form. It starts with the prospect's _**problem,**_ want, or desire. That's where your marketing message should be anchored. 

As a marketer, you must then make a _**promise**_ to solve that problem, fulfill that want or desire. 

Then you must provide _**proof**_ that you can solve the problem in a way that's believable. And then you want to offer an attractive proposition for acting now. Add those four up and you have _**persuasion.**_ 

Before we covered how to open the sale—by making the advertising itself valuable. This buys you a hearing. 

Now we are addressing the four-step formula your message should follow to _**close the sale.**_ This puts the money in your bank account. 

And it's so simple. Make sure that you start with what somebody wants, that you promise to solve their problem, that you prove that you can deliver on what you're promising, and then make an irresistible proposition. _Sale closed!_ 

But, as Emeril would say, we can kick it up a notch and be super-persuasive. 

You'll really get outstanding results if you solve an _**urgent problem...** if_ your promise is _**unique**_ (not the same promise shouted by countless others)...if you make your promise utterly believable with _**unquestionable proof...** and_ then offer a _**user-friendly proposition**_ that makes it almost irresistible to act now. 

- 9 5 - 

**==> picture [376 x 280] intentionally omitted <==**

## **Why This Formula Is So Important** 

This is so important because we have to remember that in multiplied salesmanship, we really don't have much power. People often ask, "What are the most powerful words in advertising" or "What are the most powerful headlines and offers?" 

Again, this is thinking like a marketer. You have no power. If you're talking to a million people at once, each one of those people has the total power to toss your ad, ignore it, run to the fridge—anything he or she would rather do. 

You have no direct power in the situation, save one. And that is the power to _anticipate_ what they are likely to think at each point of the sale, from the moment your ad crosses their consciousness. 

We are completing the picture of what good salesmanship means. First, we open the sale with something of value, so that people aren't tossing our message away immediately. And now we have the other key components—the four-part formula of _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®_ —to close the sale. 

In essence, if we think about salesmanship, it's really the science of anticipating the prospect's objections. And there are five universal objections to every sale... 

- 9 6 - 

## **The 5 Universal Objections— aka as the "5** _**Exits of Escape"**_ 

## **1. No time** 

## **2. No interest 3. No perceived difference 4. No belief** 

## **5. No decision** 

Let's review the five universal objections that every salesperson encounters, whether they're selling in print or in person. 

First, when we're trying to open the sale, start the conversation, our prospects are feeling, "I have no time. I'm just too busy. I don't want to enter a sales dialogue right now." 

OK, we anticipate and then counter that objection with the five-word strategy I mentioned earlier— _**make your advertising itself valuable,**_ otherwise known as the Cracker Jack secret, of always having something so interesting and valuable in your advertising that it prevents your prospect from tossing it. 

If we do our job right on that score, the prospect's inner dialogue changes to "I have no time. I'm too busy—oh, wait a minute, a little black book. Well, gee, I can't throw that away...I'll look it over." 

So the "no time" objection is taken care of. 

Then, as we start closing the sale that we've now opened, the remaining four objections will be taken care of by _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®._ 

Objection number two, "no interest," is countered if your headline addresses an urgent problem in the prospect's life. 

Objection number three, "no difference," is countered by your unique promise, which sets you apart from all the others offering a solution. 

**- 97 -** 

Objection number four, "no belief," is countered by your unquestionable proof. Objection number five, "no decision," arises because your prospect fears making a decision, wants to "think it over," isn't sure about being able to afford the price, worries about making a mistake, wonders about your guarantee, or has any other uncertainties. All these should be countered by your "user-friendly proposition," which addresses each concern in turn and closes with a powerful reason for acting now. 

You can see that what we're doing as skilled salespeople-in-print is so much more sophisticated and effective in today's world than the Ned Ryerson approach of yesteryear—the manipulative, high-pressure techniques recommended in books like _The Sale Begins When the Customer Says No._ 

We _must_ be more sophisticated and consumer-friendly because we are selling in a multiplied way, where we're not even on the scene to communicate personally with our prospects or adjust our sales pitch according to their reactions. So we must plan instead. We must anticipate each of their feelings, each of their five instinctive objections, and then close off their avenues of escape. 

## **The Five Major Exits Your Prospects Escape Through** 

Yes, these five major objections are, in effect, the five major exits of escape that your prospects will always run for. And your job is to lock the door at each exit so that your prospects stay in the room until they make a definite decision either to buy or not. 

**==> picture [331 x 244] intentionally omitted <==**

**- 98 -** 

"I'm too busy to deal with this now...Oh, wait a minute, there's a neat Cracker Jack prize in this direct mail package. This is something too valuable to throw away. 

"OK, well let me just get that and then be on my way, because I have no interest in buying anything...Well, no, this really is addressing an urgent problem or want or concern in my life. 

"Alright, this does sound interesting, but it's probably just another sales pitch, no different from the rest...Oh, hold on, here's a _unique_ promise. I never saw that one before. 

"Yeah, but it's probably just hype...Wow, look at this proof. This is amazing. 

"Well, let me think it over and then decide...Gee, the proposition says no, I can't think it over. I'll get all the goodies only if I act by a certain date or only if I'm one of the first 1,000, etc." 

Do you see what's happening? We are anticipating and then closing off all five major avenues of escape that normally let our prospects wiggle away from us and cause us to lose vast numbers of sales. If we don't construct our messages to lock these five exits, it's as if we're fishing with a net that has five gaping holes for our fish to escape through. 

## **So Simple a Child Can Do It!** 

This process is so simple that even a child can do it. I'll show you how right now. 

Let's say that you and I are kids, maybe eight or nine years old. We have just been let off for the summer, and we're not going to camp. We're good buddies, we live next door to each other, and we're sitting on your front porch wondering, "What are we going to do for the summer?" You say, "It would be nice to make some money! Maybe a lemonade stand?" I say, "That's so yesterday. Nobody buys lemonade today. They're all on Atkins in this town. What else have you got?" 

Finally, lightning strikes. One of us says, "The lake is very popular with fishermen, especially over the summer. Why don't we try to sell them worms?" 

We know where to dig up the worms because every autumn the sanitation department dumps tons of leaves collected from the streets into a park on the side of the lake, and this becomes a giant breeding ground for worms. 

We can dig up hundreds of worms over there, so our product is free. Here's what we'll do—we'll dig up some worms on Friday, and then, on Saturday morning when many of the fishermen come to the lake to launch their boats, we'll 

- - 9 9 

sell them boxes of worms. We'll put the worms in little boxes we can buy at the bakery and sell the fishermen worms. 

Of course, we need an advertising campaign now. Just being kids, we hire Gary Bencivenga to do the art, and this is the pathetic layout he creates. 

**==> picture [206 x 245] intentionally omitted <==**

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

We put out our little sign. It just says, "Worms," and we sell a fair number of boxes. We make a few bucks. As basic as it is, it's probably a better ad than half of the Fortune 500 are running on TV! 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

Have you ever noticed that you often can't even tell what they are trying to sell you on TV? You say to yourself, "What was that about?" And that's if you're even paying attention! 

So we kids are not doing too badly with our basic sign. But we suspect we could do better. 

- 100 - 

We know that Johnny's dad works on Madison Avenue, so we ambush him on the way home from his train. We say, "Mr. Jones, we're trying to build a little business here for the summer. Here's our ad. Can you make it better?" 

He says, "I like your entrepreneurial spirit. Let me take a look. It's good, sound advertising to sometimes just say what you're selling flat out, but usually it's better if you can put a benefit in your headline, a benefit your prospects will like." 

So we go back home to work with our crayons or hire Gary again, and once again, you can see that the resulting artwork is just stellar. 

## The benefit-driven headline we come up with is _"Worms that catch fish."_

## **Worms that catch fish!** 

**==> picture [203 x 78] intentionally omitted <==**

We intercept Johnny's dad the next day to see what he thinks, and he's quite impressed. He says, "That's the ticket!" 

On Saturday morning, we go down to the lake again, and lo and behold, we do see a nice increase in sales. In one respect, we're thrilled because we now have a big benefit ad campaign, which even many modern marketers would say is pretty much state of the art. Can't be improved. 

But we notice that quite a few fishermen looked at our sign, smiled at our business spirit, but didn't buy. We can't figure out why. 

- 101 - 

We want to sell even more, and one of our friends tells us that Patricia's mom is a great saleswoman. We take our ad campaign to her on Monday. We ask, "Mrs. Smith, can you help us? We thought this was such a great headline, and it did increase our sales. But there were still lots of fishermen who saw our sign, just smiled, but didn't buy. How come? What else could we do?" 

She tells us, "You're in luck, kids, because I just got back from Gary Bencivenga's seminar, and I have to tell you, your campaign is good but could be a lot better." 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

She says, "You're missing two other steps. First, you're missing strong, believable proof." 

"What do you mean, proof?" 

"What you have on your sign, _'Worms that catch fish,'_ does have a big benefit, but it's still just a claim. Today people are bombarded by thousands of claims every day, full of benefits, but people just blow them off. They're like raindrops off a duck's back. Your headline may have been good in the old days of advertising, when there were very few ads offering big benefits, but not today, when there's so much clutter and we're hit with promises from every direction. You have to build _proof_ into your promise or else people will dismiss it right off." 

We ask, "How do we prove that these worms catch fish?" 

She says, "You find proof with _deeper research._ Where do you get your worms?" 

We tell her, and she says, "I know the place you're talking about. There are some old-time fishermen who dig up their worms in the same place. Why don't you ask them for help. Those old-timers know the lake better than anybody." 

That's what we decide to do. We visit the old-timers and tell them we're trying to get a little business going for the summer. "Can you guys tell us why _do_ you dig up your worms here?" 

One of the old-timers says, "You remind me of my grandkids, and I admire your entrepreneurial spirit. Let me tell you about these worms. You see, fish, like most animals and even people, are wary of things they don't know. If something is unfamiliar to them or smells unusual, they back off. 

"But these worms are what these fish eat all the time. Every time it rains, many of the worms on this big hillside get washed down into the lake and give 

- 102 - 

these fish a feast. The fish in this lake know exactly what these worms taste and smell like. So they're not wary of these worms. This is what's familiar to them. It's their usual diet. I've tried a lot of different baits, but I and my buddies found that we always caught more fish using the worms we find here." 

"I and my fishing buddies have a bunch of photos of fish we've caught in this lake. The photos are back at our clubhouse. If you want, you can borrow them and use them in your little campaign." 

Naturally, we get very excited about this. We work up a new sign for next Saturday, one that now incorporates proof, _a reason why._ 

This makes a tremendous difference, and we see a quantum leap in our persuasive power and our sales... 

## **Local worms catch more fish!** 

**==> picture [238 x 240] intentionally omitted <==**

## Our sign says, _"Local worms catch more fish!"_

And now we notice that when the fishermen come down to the lake and see our sign, they come right over and ask, _"Hey, kids, what's this all about? "_ They are intrigued by this headline because it beckons with _a reason why,_ a powerful proof of our promise. 

- 103 - 

Our sales patter, or if we were writing an ad, our body copy, becomes just what our research revealed. "The fish in this lake are wary of the smell, appearance, and taste of bait they're not used to, _but not these worms!_ These fish love these local worms because this is what they normally eat. Just look at the photos of the fish caught with these worms. We got these pictures from the old-times who use the same worms that we're selling you, from the same place, to catch these big beautiful fish. They told us that there's no better bait if you want to catch fish in this lake." 

We now see a tremendous difference in selling power between a headline of _"Worms that catch fish "_ —just a bald claim—and _"Local worms catch more fish,"_ which has built-in proof. 

Much of direct marketing, even today, is at the "claim only" level. And because so many prospects use "no belief" as their preferred exit of escape, this is where we can usually get our biggest increases in response, by strengthening our proof, by closing off the "no belief" exit of escape. 

Look around, look at your own ads, and you'll be shocked to see that unproven promises dominate advertising. But if promises were gold, politicians would be fabulously wealthy, and we'd all love them. We hold them in disregard because their promises, without proof, are so cheap. As advertisers, we are viewed in similar fashion, unless we back up our promises with _strong, believable proof._ 

Getting back to our campaign, we kids see that this inclusion of strong proof in our headline brings a dramatic increase in sales. We excitedly go back to Patricia's mom with the news. You remember Patricia's mom. She came to our seminar. 

We tell her that, boy oh boy, did we see a boost in response from putting proof in our headline! 

She tells us that's great, but we forgot something else, the last step—to have a user-friendly proposition, a good offer to close the sale. 

We're old pros ourselves now, so we say, sure, we can do that! And here is the result... 

- 104 - 

## **Local worms catch more fish!** 

Only $2 per box of a dozen; Buy 3 boxes for just $5 and gel free secret map of lake's best fishing holes 

**==> picture [258 x 196] intentionally omitted <==**

## **At Last—a Killer Ad!** 

Now we have a killer ad: 

_"Local worms catch more fish! (subhead) Only $2.00 per box of a dozen. Buy three boxes for just $5.00 and get free_ 

_secret map of the lake's best fishing holes"_ 

So we've now done a good job of sealing off the five exits prospects normally escape through. 

And you can just picture what's happening down at the lake. Bright and early on Saturday morning, fishermen start showing up near the boat launch. 

They've got their $15,000 bass boats hooked up to their $30,000 SUVs. They've got all kinds of expensive paraphernalia—fish finders, depth gauges, rods, reels, lures, tackle boxes, all kinds of high-tech gadgetry. 

And then they see our sign. Are they going to begrudge us $5.00? _No way!_ Their price resistance will go right out the window when they see those fish photos and hear our sales pitch, one in which we close off all the usual exits of 

- 105 - 

escape—(1) no time, (2) no interest, (3) no difference, (4) no belief, and (5) no decision. 

Notice this is not brilliant copywriting nor eloquent prose. Most ads win or lose _not_ by the eloquence of the writing but the psychology of the sales process that I've just mapped out for you, a process that _anticipates each of the five major sales objections and counters each of them before they can kill the sale._ 

Again, the only power you have in the transaction is anticipating the prospect's next objection, his next move for one of the exits. And that's what this formula does. 

When you apply this formula to your advertising, something else interesting and marvelous happens. Price resistance starts to melt like ice cream in the sun. 

A great salesman once told me that price is a funny thing. When a qualified prospect says, "It's too expensive," what he or she is really saying is that _you haven't given me enough of a believable benefit yet._ 

## **Think of This Apothecary Scale Every Time You Sell Something** 

Every time you are selling something, picture an apothecary scale in your mind. On the left scale, you're going to throw your price. And if that's all you place on the scale, the left side will fall immediately and far outweigh the right side. 

**==> picture [307 x 229] intentionally omitted <==**

- 106 - 

But on the right side of the scale, you're going to place the biggest benefits your product can deliver, and you're going to give them the added weight of belief. 

The bigger the desire you can create, the more unique your promise, the more believable your proof, and the more user-friendly your proposition, when you pile on all those factors, the heavier the right side of that scale is going to be. And that means you can _increase_ the price you place on the other side without tipping the scale against you. 

In our example of the fishermen, we've succeeded in placing a great weight of benefit, desire, proof, and attractive proposition on the right-hand side of the scale. 

That means that we could probably throw a much higher price on the left-hand side and dramatically increase our revenue without disrupting the scale's balance. 

For example, as I mentioned, these fishermen, with their bass boats and paraphernalia, have already invested tens of thousands of dollars in their hobby. But they will justify this by thinking that they do have fun and, after all, put fish on their family's table. Never mind that that by this calculation, the fish probably costs them about $700 a pound. 

No, they don't care about that. They want to catch fish. After investing a small fortune in this hobby before they see our sign, there's no way they're going to begrudge us kids the $5 we ask for our worms. 

In fact, maybe we should double our price to $10. Or maybe we don't even have to dig up worms anymore and should just go into the information marketing business, selling our secret map of the lake's best fishing spots for $100 a pop. Who knows? We're dealing with passionate hobbyists whose desires can easily be enflamed, putting a massive weight on the right-hand side of the scale, allowing many opportunities—limited only by our imaginations and ability to come up with worthy products—to throw big price numbers on the left. 

The rule to remember—as desire goes up, price resistance goes down. The more you create desire with your promise, uniqueness, and proof, the higher price you can charge. The more you pile on the right side of the scale, the more you can pile on the left. 

In fact, you can even assign a value to each component of _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®_ and have an amazingly reliable tool for predicting how well your advertising will do. 

Even in this room, full of very savvy direct marketing people, if we were to hand out ads and ask each of you to rate them, we'd get many different opinions on whether any given ad is good or not. Naturally, we could probably all recognize 

- 107 - 

the "straw man" cases, the ads that really stink. But with most other ads, we'd get wildly different opinions. 

The process I will now explain brings all those opinions much closer together, and, more important, much closer to how the ad will actually perform in the marketplace. If you're a copy chief or an art director or an entrepreneur and you want to know if a given ad is any good, watch how simple this is. 

# **Urgent (10) Problem (15) Unique (10) Promise (15)** 

# **Unquestionable (10) Proof (15)** 

# **- User Friendly (10) Proposition (15)** 

**"Crackerjack Secret" Layout = 20 bonus points** 

As you can see, there are eight terms in the formula. Four of the terms begin with the letter _P._ And four begin with the letter _U._ 

Whenever you must judge a piece of copy, award the ad 15 points if it successfully meets a criterion beginning with a _P,_ and 10 points if it successfully meets a criterion beginning with a _U._ 

In other words, if the ad, and especially the headline or first several sentences of the body copy, directly address a prospect's _problem_ (want or concern), award it 15 points. If that problem (want or concern) is an _urgent_ one, award it another 10 points. 

If the headline does _not_ directly address a problem (want or concern) of your main prospects, award it nothing. 

And do the same for each of the terms in the formula. 

Is it clearly making a promise? Award it another 15 points. Is it doing it in a _unique_ way? Give it another 10 points. 

Is there solid proof? Give it 15 points. If the proof is exceptionally compelling, strong, and virtually unquestionable in its source, give it another 10 points. 

- 108 - 

Is there a clear-cut proposition to close the sale? Give it 15 points. If the proposition is user-friendly, add another 10. 

And, just to be sure we're surviving the initial screening, where ads are ignored and tossed into the garbage if they look like ads, ask if _**the advertising itself is valuable,**_ if it contains a Cracker Jack prize, not a plastic trinket but usually a piece of information or enclosed free checklist, booklet, guide, etc., that your market would find impossible to toss, then give it 20 extra bonus points. 

This adds up to 120 points. You can grade the final score just as you would have when I was in college. 

100 or better is an A+. The ad should be a blockbuster. 

Anything above 90 is an A. 

85 to 90 is a B+. 80 to 85 is a B. 75 to 80 is a C+. 70 to 75 is a C. 65 to 70 is a D. 

Below 65 is an F. 

This is not rocket science. Is the ad solving a problem? Is it an urgent problem? Go through the whole list, and you'll quickly see that most of the ads in any magazine would get very poor grades. 

Just ask these simple questions because these are the questions your prospects are asking as they encounter anything that you present to them. 

Again, this does not teach you to write with the brilliance of Shakespeare or the eloquence of Clarence Darrow. Neither is necessary! You want to _sell,_ not impress with your words. Indeed, if your words are too fancy, you arouse suspicion, just like an overly slick salesman. 

This is the formula to follow if you want to be sure that you're thinking like the fish and not the fisherman, if you want to structure your message to give your prospects exactly what they look for, exactly what will make them buy. 

Anybody can apply this formula, whether you're a copywriter or someone who must judge copy. In a little while, we'll get into how to create products that come with unique promises and bold proof already built into them, which will make it amazingly easy to create blockbuster breakthrough ads. More on this later! 

— — _End of Session #3_ 

- 1 0 9 - 

## Session #4: 

## The Secret of Master Closers... and How to Apply It to Direct Response Advertising

**in -** 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ Now we're going to get more advanced. Now we're going to get into _The Secret of Master Closers_ and how to apply it to direct response advertising. 

Satellite photo! Advertising is multiplied salesmanship. What is salesmanship? It's changed because of the tremendous clutter. Captain John Rade gave us a clue about how to outflank the clutter: always think like the fish, not the fisherman. If you do that, your thinking will be aligned with your prospect. 

What's the best way to do that, to both open and close the sale? 

We must address the five universal objections that all groups of prospects will throw at you. 

The first is _"no time."_ OK, you anticipate and prevent that objection by making your advertising _valuable,_ too valuable to throw away, so that your prospect must make the time to read it. That's how to open the sale, to start the conversation. 

How to close the sale? 

You follow the four parts of _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®._ We're making very good progress here. As you see, you don't need to be an eloquent wordsmith. You just have to know the psychology of your prospect at each step, and you do that by following _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®-_ Now let's turn to... 

## **The Secret of Master Closers and How to Apply It to Direct Response Advertising** 

I started my advertising career at Prentice Hall, a publishing house in New Jersey, selling their books by mail order. It was a long commute from Long Island, but I took the job to break into the industry. I had to break in somewhere but secretly wanted to get into the big time in New York. I envisioned myself writing glamorous TV commercials. But, as time would tell, they have never let me out of the direct response department!
