Prentice Hall gave me an excellent apprenticeship, but I yearned to work on Madison Avenue, at a major ad agency. I took a succession of jobs at small agencies, always in the direct response department, and eventually got to work with John Caples at BBDO and then with David Ogilvy at Ogilvy & Mather. 

When I say "with David Ogilvy," I must tell you that he was the head of a giant ad agency and didn't even know I existed. Nevertheless, when I went to work at his agency, I adopted him as my mentor, without his knowledge, of course. But 

- 113 - 

I studied everything he wrote and worked under a copy chief who did work with David personally. 

I did encounter Mr. Ogilvy once on the elevator. I refrained from introducing myself because it was 10:30 and I was inexcusably late for work. I wanted so much, in that fleeting moment, to blurt out, "You're my hero, you're my mentor!" But then, if he asked, I would have had to admit, "Uh, yes, I am an hour and a half late. Please don't fire me! I need this job." 

He was known as a martinet and didn't like people coming in late. So I chose the better part of valor and remained silent. Years later, after I had left his employ, when he saw one of my ads, he wrote me to say what a good ad it was, and I was on top of the world. I'll show you that ad in a minute. 

Anyway, I was at Ogilvy & Mather Direct and feeling on top of the world, thinking I knew so much about effective direct response advertising and wondering, "What should I do next?" 

I thought, "Maybe I should go out on my own, become a freelancer and make a lot more money." I had heard that freelancers can earn a high income. 

## **The Letter That Changed My Life and Raised Me from Mediocrity to Success in Direct Response Copywriting** 

I was about to do that when I received a phone call from an executive recruiter. She said that there's this small direct response agency up in New Rochelle, New York, a suburb about 45 minutes northeast of here. 

She said "They're looking for somebody who knows Claude Hopkins, has studied John Caples, and knows the Ogilvy style of copywriting. You fit the bill." I said, "I'm really thinking of going out on my own as a freelancer." 

But she didn't take no for an answer. She said, "You should at least talk with these guys. Listen to this letter that they've sent me." 

Then, over the phone, she read me a letter written by Dan Rosenthal, the head of this small agency and a brilliant copywriter I would eventually learn so much from. 

In the letter, Dan said that he ran an ad agency that has taken salesmanship in advertising to a degree that nobody else in the world has ever done. He said that in our advertising, we sell just like master salespeople. He said that we study master salespeople and apply their methods to direct marketing. We worship Claude Hopkins and Albert Lasker and Johnny Kennedy. We have virtually memorized 

- 114 - 

their books. 

I was beginning to think that this was some sort of strange cult! 

And I cut the recruiter short by saying, "Well, look, all of this is nice, but I'm at Ogilvy & Mather now. I've got a great job in the direct response division of the most elite and respected agency in the entire world. Why would I leave here only to go to this tiny little ad agency, run out of a three-bedroom apartment? Why would I do that? I'm at the top of the advertising world here. If I do anything, I'd like to go out on my own." 

She said, "Let me finish the letter." Then she read the next paragraph, which did catch my attention. It said, "Just as with top salespeople, we pay our copywriters on commission. In fact, our top senior copywriter now is making [in today's money—I've done the math] $750,000 a year." 

To which I responded, "Maybe I _should_ see these people, who knows?" 

The recruiter added, "You'll start [again, in today's money] at $250,000 a year as your base, just like a salesperson, and then you can start building up your commissions from there. The sky's the limit if you know how to sell." 

I figured, at the very least and even if this job offer doesn't work out, maybe I could get some freelance work from them and start my freelance career. 

But when I met with Dan, I saw we were a great match, and he needed someone full time, not a freelancer. So I joined the firm, which was named Callas, Powell, Rosenthal & Bloch—catchy name, wouldn't you say? 

A few years later, another key employee came on board, an outstanding chief administrator who became the company president. Her name was Alyse Chobanian. So at one point the board of directors proposed that the agency's name be changed to reflect all the key partners, which meant that our name would become Callas, Powell, Rosenthal, Bloch, Chobanian & Bencivenga. Just rolls right off the tongue, doesn't it? Alyse and I said thanks for the offer, but the name would be impossibly long. 

I found that the entire firm, under Dan's guidance, was passionately devoted to salesmanship. For my part, I brought many of the little tricks and techniques of effective copywriting, which I'll share with you this afternoon, that I learned from terrific creative directors and copy chiefs I had worked under, including people like Ogilvy and his key lieutenants. 

They knew so much from running thousands of different split-run ads and had little tricks for boosting the response at every step. I brought this street-smart copywriting knowledge to the system that they already had in place, an approach 

- 115 - 

to copy writing based on everything taught by Claude Hopkins. 

Together we created a tremendously responsive style of advertising, as well as a confidence—perhaps cockiness would be more accurate—that we could beat just about anyone else's ads. Actually, in almost all cases, we could. 

We got so cocky that we ran an ad _guaranteeing_ that we could beat other people's best ads. We ran this ad to get new business after we were staffed up with new copywriters. 

As you can imagine, this ad blew the minds of many direct response advertisers, especially other direct response ad agencies. We were so cocky, probably more so than we should have been. But we were young, in our twenties, and were so passionate about what we were doing, almost a cultlike following of Ogilvy, Hopkins, and these other advertising legends. We just lived and breathed great headlines and copy. We worked 60 to 70 hours a week just trying to beat other people's control packages and learning from each other. It was wonderful fun. 

Notice that in this ad, we weren't just saying that we'd guarantee to beat your best ad. We went a lot further. Here's what we said: 

**"We'll guarantee to outpull your best ad by at least 10% in a split-run test.** 

## **"If we don't pull at least 10% more responses, you won't owe us a penny for any work we've done—creative or production."** 

And here's the part that really blew people's minds.... 

## **"We'll even refund half your media expense, to cover our half of the test."** 

In other words, it would cost an advertiser _absolutely nothing_ to test the pulling power of our copy. And if we lose, we'll cover half the cost of the media, even if it's a full page ad in the _New York Times_ or _Wall Street Journal!_ That would be an extremely punishing result if we lost, on top of all the creative and production expenses we would have lost. So this was an amazingly gutsy offer. 

As with anyone young and cocky, on some days, prior to running this ad, we'd feel spasms of nervousness. Are we nuts? What if it's some major company, a Merrill Lynch or somebody, how could we take the financial risk of guaranteeing perhaps a very expensive test? 

But by next morning we'd feel cocky again and, by acting bravely, each of us reassured one another. We did know that almost every time we were up against somebody else, our ads and packages would win very easily by large margins. 

## 1 1 6 -

- 

**==> picture [492 x 666] intentionally omitted <==**

Before this campaign broke, in one of our spasms of nervousness, we decided to run away on a company retreat, down to the Bahamas, to get serious and figure out how we would answer all the questions that would surely be raised by this campaign. 

For example, how would we count the responses, to be sure we weren't getting cheated or that the results would not be tampered with? Should all the responses go to a bank lockbox? We dreamed up all kinds of crazy scenarios and tried to figure out how we could respond to them. 

We held our breaths, ran the ad, and... 

## **A Most Interesting Thing Happened!** 

We received lots of responses, several of them from major, blue-chip direct response advertisers that any ad agency of any size would _love_ to have as clients. Astonishingly, not a single one of them cared about taking us up on our split-run test offer or our guarantee! 

Every one of them said, forget the test. Running an offer like that, you sound like you know what you're doing. Let's get started right away. We need help with this project. 

We were dumbfounded by this. And we got quite a few clients from running that ad. The ad was also a great insurance policy to keep in our back pocket. If we ever lost a major account, and that can happen no matter how good you are because companies change hands and new managements sweep with a new broom, we knew we had an ad we could run at any time and generate new business. 

But we never had to. As we took on more clients, word of our exceptional results spread through the industry, and we had much more work offered to us than we could possibly handle. 

## **How Did We Achieve Such Exceptional Results?** 

After leaving Callas, Powell and refining Dan Rosenthal's approach further, I came to call this method of marketing, _**Proof Element Marketing®.**_ 

It consists of taking one key component of _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®_ and emphasizing it beyond the normal to leverage response beyond the normal. 

Let's review _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®._ Remember, there are four basic parts to it: 

- 118 - 

**1. You start with someone's** _**problem (want, desire, need, or concern).**_ 

_**2.**_ **You make** _**a promise**_ **aimed squarely at that problem.** 

**3. You** _**prove**_ **that your promise is totally believable and not just more hype, like everyone else is using.** 

**4. You offer an irresistible** _**proposition.**_ 

Having studied at the feet of Hopkins, Caples, Reeves, and Ogilvy, and having learned a great deal from master salespeople we had also studied in many different industries, and from running scores of split-run tests, we found that the real magic in boosting response can be found in the third element of this formula, _**proof.**_ 

We found that if we focused more on _proof_ 'than anything else, in every part of our advertising, from headline to footnotes, we would almost always pop our response way up. **The Secret of Master Closers— Master Closers Sell on Proof** 

Especially from studying master salespeople in many fields, we learned that master closers usually sell on _proof._ 

Anyone can make promises. Anybody can say, "I can solve your problem." 

Prospects know this and don't want to waste their time with claims based on hot air. So they instantly dismiss unproven claims, especially in today's massively cluttered marketing environment, where all our prospects are looking for a quick excuse to blow you off. 

Remember this rule: the sale gets _opened_ by making your advertising valuable. But it gets closed with _proof_ 

Strong, utterly believable, unquestionable _**proof**_ is the absolutely essential element your advertising must have to close the sale, yet it's usually the one factor most deficient in advertising, even ads from veteran copywriters and marketers. 

## **Nobody Buys Without Belief** 

It follows that if belief—based on strong proof—is the narrow tollgate that your prospect must pass through in order to send you money at the _end_ of your selling process, you will have much greater success if you build belief—using strong proof—at the _start_ of your selling process as well. That sets you up to close the highest number of sales, _starting_ your presentation with strong proof, not adding it later as an afterthought or subordinate element. 

## Of all the four parts of _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®,_ this is the part

- 1 1 9 - 

that gets you the most leverage because it's the one that most advertisers overlook. 

For entrepreneurs, copywriters, salespeople, _everyone_ in the marketing process, making big promises is the most fun, and this is where their attention goes. It's much harder, less glamorous, less exciting to methodically build your advertising around—and anchor your products, your headlines, your guarantees, your entire product development and marketing effort deeply within— _rock-solid proof._ 

That's not uppermost of marketers' minds because they're thinking like fishermen, not fish. And it's why so much of your advertising is underperforming. 

For the fish, your prospects, nothing will happen unless your promise is _believed._ So you might as well try to lock in _both_ a big promise _and_ powerful proof right in your headline, together, joined at the hip. 

Remember, in this age of clutter, people are very astute about marketing, much more so than you give them credit for. 

You cannot aim 3,000 marketing messages a day at people without them getting savvy about what's BS and what rings true. 

## Your prospects _know_ that bona fide proof is the most difficult part of

persuasion to manufacture. It is the element, along with an absence of hype, that most convinces them that a message can be believed and trusted, that it is not BS. So your prospects know that believable proof is the most rare—and therefore most valuable—element of persuasion. 

## **In Advertising, Strong Proof Is as Rare —and Valuable!—as Gold** 

Why is gold valuable? Because it's hard to manufacture. It's a lot harder to manufacture than silver. So gold is about 60 times more valuable than silver. 

In our equation, proof is worth much more than any other element. Why? Because it's the hardest to manufacture. 

All four elements are essential, of course. You can't have a good ad without solving someone's problem or making a strong promise or offering an attractive proposition. All those are necessary, as well. 

But the one step that's overlooked more than any other by most marketers, because it seems so boring, is building believability with _proof._ 

"Proof? Yeah, we've got the testimonials, a guarantee, no problem." 

No, those things are merely par for the course today. They are the bare minimum that you must provide if you want outsize response. 

- 120 - 

Do you want to raise the bar—and your response—much higher? Then work harder than you've ever worked in your life to build your proof. Follow these core principles of _Proof Element Marketing®..._ 

**==> picture [349 x 260] intentionally omitted <==**

_Proof Element Marketing®_ starts with the premise that without belief, nobody buys. And the higher the level of belief, the higher your response. If you make compelling proof the alpha and omega of your advertising, you'll get the highest response. The strongest ads of all pile layer upon layer of compelling proof. 

I know that some of you copywriters and marketers have come to learn some magic words that you can just sprinkle on your copy, like sugar on your morning cereal, to make your response go way up. Unfortunately, that's not how it works. Politicians have tried doing just that for too many years. Do we believe our politicians? No way, _because they offer no proof!_ 

Likewise, many advertisers try to jack up response merely by jacking up the promise. But that, in fact, works against you because people flee from hype and unproven sales pitches. So if you merely crank up the hype, you push down your 

- 121 - 

response. The higher you crank the hype, the more you flag your message as an unbelievable sales pitch, which people avoid like the measles. You're shooting yourself in the foot. 

## **So Then, What Are the Most Effective Ways to Build Belief? Let's Look at Some Powerful Examples** 

Let me now run through some of the best ways I've found to execute what I'm talking about— _the greatest secrets of building belief._ 

## **Proof Element #1: The Demonstration** 

Claude Hopkins once said, "No argument in the world can ever compare with one dramatic demonstration." 

Let me cite a famous example. Everyone here has ridden in an Otis elevator. You know the experience. We crowd into the elevator, everybody stares straight ahead or at their shoe tops, not wanting to make eye contact with anyone in such close quarters. 

The next time you're in that situation, you might look around and see if you can spot a brass sign that says it's an Otis elevator. 

Otis did not invent the elevator. What Otis invented was a way for elevators to stop themselves when the cables broke. 

Back in the 1800s, if people were on an elevator when the cables snapped, they had a disappointingly short lifespan—about four or five flights and that was it. 

Naturally people were afraid of riding elevators because of this. If the rope cables broke, they would plunge to their death. And these accidents always made front-page news, frightening people away from elevators even more. 

As a result, the vast majority of office buildings and residential apartment houses in the U.S. built before the 1850s were, at most, three or four stories high. They didn't build higher floors because nobody would rent the higher floors. Everyone was afraid to ride on elevators, and this fear limited the vertical height of all construction. So cities like New York, during their great growth spurts, had to expand _horizontally,_ moving outward from their centers. 

But a single demonstration changed this, and the world has never been the same. Here's what happened. 

Otis invented an automatic braking system whereby, even if the cables suddenly snapped, the elevator would drop only a few inches, and then a 

- 122 - 

mechanism would kick in and automatically stop the elevator from falling any farther. 

Otis was thrilled. Visions of booming sales and great wealth danced in his head. He announced his invention to the world and waited for the orders to pour in. He waited...and waited. Nothing happened. 

People so feared elevators that their universal reaction was, in effect, "Yeah, sure, you've got a great system. Good for you. I still don't trust elevators. I'll never ride in one, because I don't want to take a chance that it will plunge while I'm in it, leaving me dead in the basement under a pile of debris." 

Nothing Otis said seemed able to make any impact on the deeply ingrained fear that people had of elevators. Sales languished, and he didn't know what to do. 

In 1855, he had an idea. That year, New York was the site of something called the Crystal Palace Exposition, the 19th-century equivalent of today's world's fair. 

Right along the midway, where thousands would stroll, he set up a demonstration. He had a big contraption set up where he would ride in an opensided elevator and then have his assistant cut the rope cables with an axe. 

This was such a famous demonstration that it was immortalized in artwork. That's Otis with the top hat in hand, suspended in the air, as his assistant stood on the platform above him, axe at the ready. 

**==> picture [387 x 283] intentionally omitted <==**

As thousands gaped below, Otis would say, "Ready?" And his crew would hoist the elevator, with him in it, way up. Then he'd yell out, "Cut the cables!" His his assistant would swing his axe and whack one rope cable, and the crowd would gasp. Otis would say, "Wait, I'm still held up by one cable." And then the assistant would go over to the other side and whack the other cable. The crowds really gasped at this, for he would surely plunge to his death! 

But the elevator would drop a few inches and then stop securely. And Otis would proclaim, "Do you see what I've invented? An automatic braking system for elevators. From now on, you never have to worry about riding on an elevator— _as long as it's an Otis elevator!"_ 

That demonstration captured the public's imagination. It made headlines across the country. From that day forward, the public lost its fear of riding in elevators, _as long as they were Otis elevators._ 

And this changed the world, because it enabled New York and other major cities to start expanding _vertically,_ not just horizontally, with the skyscrapers we now take for granted. This one demonstration revolutionized the architecture of cities all across the world. Now developers could build buildings more than four stories high because the public no longer feared elevators. Such is the power of a single demonstration. 

## **Ron Popeil Has Lived off This for Decades** 

You know Ron Popeil. You have seen him many times on late-night TV, in infomercials, demonstrating his slicers and dicers, his coffeemakers, hair-in-a-can, pocket fishing rod, chicken roasters, and everything else. He's not a brilliant, eloquent writer. But he _is_ a brilliant salesman who makes fortunes by demonstrating his products while talking about their benefits. It's a formula you can adapt to any product in any advertising medium if you put your mind to it. 

You can be the best copywriter or salesperson in the world. God may have blessed you with a silver tongue and golden voice. But you will never be as persuasive as a powerful demonstration. No copywriter, no silver-tongued salesperson, could have ever convinced the public in the 1800s to start riding in elevators. You simply could not achieve that verbally. But a good demonstration could—and did. As a marketer, it's one of your most potent weapons. 

## **The Greatest Ad in Direct Marketing History?** 

Another example of that principle is perhaps the greatest ad in direct marketing history. Its headline: _**Do You Make These Mistakes in English?**_ 

## 1 2 4 -

_-_ 

**==> picture [449 x 554] intentionally omitted <==**

Right in the first paragraph, it rattles off several grammatical gaffes that many people make, making themselves seem less intelligent than they are. 

If you are a bit uncertain about your command of grammar, and who isn't, and if you read these common mistakes and realize you commit them yourself, you are 

- 125 - 

already half convinced—in the very first paragraph!—that you're a prime candidate for this course. 

This ad was so strong that it was the control for 40 years! _Talk about good advertising!_ Today it's hard to write a control package or ad that runs for even a year or two. That was the control for 40 years, and nobody, even the best copywriters of that age, could beat it. That's how good that ad is. 

## **"Takes a Licking and Keeps on Ticking"** 

I remember an ad campaign from when I was young. Maybe some of you my age will remember it too. It was for Timex watches. The spokesman was the well- 

## 1 2 6 -

- 

known news announcer and TV personality, John Cameron Swayze. In each commercial, he would perform a dramatic torture test on a Timex watch and then see if it was still running. 

For example, in one commercial, he would strap the watch to the propeller of a motorboat, watch the boat zoom around a lake, and then say, "Let's look at the watch." Watch in hand, he'd triumphantly proclaim, "Look at that, it takes a licking and keeps on ticking." These demonstrations were so powerful, I still remember them vividly 50 years after they ran! 

Here is a print ad that did the same thing. Remember, after World War II, it was revolutionary for a mass-market watch to work after being underwater. In this ad, they demonstrated the point by strapping Timex watches to the backs of turtles and photographing them underwater. 

On the strength of these demonstrations, Timex watches went from an unknown entity to becoming America's largest-selling watch and remains so to this day—all thanks to a good product and the power of demonstrations. 

**==> picture [390 x 366] intentionally omitted <==**

Here is another, for a course on learning how to draw. I confess that I should take this course! You've seen my artwork. On the left is the _"before."_ On the right is the _"after,"_ the result of just one hour of instruction. Very effective ad. 

In summation, remember what the King of Copywriters, Claude Hopkins, recommended: _**"No argument in the world can ever compare with one dramatic demonstration."**_ 

Always try to think of ways that you can demonstrate what your product or service can do for your prospect, and you may well find the strongest ad you can run. 

## **Quick Review** 

Satellite photo! 

We're looking at the most effective ways to close the sale. 

## Your overall strategy for doing that is the four-part _Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®._

Within the four parts of this equation, the factor that is most frequently weak and overlooked is _proof._ This is where your competition in the marketplace is most vulnerable and, therefore, where you get your greatest leverage for boosting response and capturing market share, because this is where copywriters and marketers are the laziest. 

Proof—by its presence or absence—is also the shorthand way your prospects can instantly detect whether you're trying to BS them or you're the real deal. Proof is the consumer's most reliable screening strategy, an absolutely necessary one because we are all so inundated with marketing messages. 

You must understand, you must have it deeply ingrained in your consciousness, that your prospects will instantly look, after they have been enticed by your promise, for the proof of what you are claiming. You'll enjoy far superior results if you create headlines and body copy with both your strong promise and your dramatic proof _joined at the hip._ 

Your prospects are looking for proof because they know that proof is the rarest element of persuasion, and what is rare is most prized. You are exactly the same way. If you were going to hire someone—to manage your company, fix your roof, run a department—would you do so based on a stream of mere promises and unsupported claims? 

Or would you look for some proof behind the promises? 

Your prospects are no different. Give them credit for being as smart as you. 

- 128 - 

Many advertising and sales books over the years have advised you to talk to the emotional side of your prospect, to crank up the emotion very high. We found in all of our testing that it's just the opposite. When you immediately start hurling unsupported emotional claims at your prospects, they suspect you're trying to manipulate them. They are very smart—at least as smart as you and I are. 

For example, if I had tried to sell you on coming to this seminar by saying, "With my copywriting secrets, you will get rich, you can have your own business, you can retire a multimillionaire, blah, blah, blah," your reaction is, "Yeah, sure. You sound like every other info-marketer." 

But if I describe my proof, that I have, for example, scores of control packages that have won against the work of America's best copywriters, and my clients are all verifying this, ah, then you begin to think maybe there _is_ something special you can learn from me. You will fill in your own emotional reasons for paying attention if I give you the proof of what I claim. See how it works? 

So proof is the most prized element of persuasion that all your prospects are looking for in your ads, and if you take the time to put it in or, even better, enhance your products themselves so that dramatic, powerful proof is built right into them, as we'll see later, then you can get rich in this business. 

OK, let's move on with another in this series of the best ways to put proof in your advertising. We've covered the demonstration. Now let's look at another... 

**==> picture [241 x 204] intentionally omitted <==**

Take Domino's Pizza. Its founder, Tom Monaghan, was a college student. Just to make some money, he partnered with a friend and opened a pizza joint near his campus. His friend quit, moved away, and Tom bought out his share. He hit upon this theme to promote his pizza: _fresh, hot pizza delivered in 30 minutes or less,_ 

_-_ 129 - 

_guaranteed._ Very strong proof element. 

Notice what he didn't talk about. Nothing about Mama's sauce recipe from the old country. Nothing about extra-crispy crust. Nothing about the usual benefits other pizza stores normally feature in their ads. 

His thought was that when people are hungry for pizza, they don't give a pepperoni slice about Mama's sauce from the old country. What they want is pizza, and they want it fast. 

So he came up with a unique and very strong proof element. Based on that proof element, his business exploded. Within a number of years, he went from college student to one of the world's richest men. 

His business grew from that one little off-campus pizza joint to a dominant nationwide pizza chain. His advertising was so successful that he went on to capture, on average, 25 to 30 percent of the market share _of all pizza sold in 50 major metropolitan markets in the U.S._ 

All thanks to the rocket fuel of an incredibly potent proof element. 

That's how influential proof elements are. You marketers in the audience, I urge you to focus on this when you are putting your products together. Build your proof elements in from the ground up. 

I'll show you how to do this later in the afternoon. I call it to your attention now because once your product is established, your copywriter will usually be reluctant to ask you to make major changes to your product or offer. Too much retooling, too big a risk, too much inconvenience, or most often— _too tight a deadline!_ As a result, your campaigns will lack some of the most powerful opportunities to boost response. Your marketing effort will overlook many 

opportunities to present mighty proof elements that could have been built in at the beginning very easily, as you'll see later. 

Short of retooling your product, there are some things both marketers and copywriters can do even after the product is established. For example, you can always get creative—and more persuasive—with your guarantee, as did Tom Monaghan. 

Here's another example. This is a direct mail package I wrote for one of Rodale's books... 

- 130 - 

**==> picture [387 x 200] intentionally omitted <==**

This was a book on men's sexual health. They told me that in their book club, one of their previous books had enjoyed a record-high response rate from their book club readers. The title of that older book was _How to Drive a Woman Wild in Bed._ 

I asked, "Well, if that earlier book is no longer around, and it had been such a big winner, would you mind if I use that proven title as the headline of my ad?" They said, "Sure, why not?" 

This direct mail piece went to lists of young men interested in sexual health. It proved to be a blockbuster and ran for many years. 

Sindy Berner, is Sindy here? Hi, Sindy! How are you? Sindy Berner was the marketing manager in charge of that book at the time. You see, all this time you thought those nice people out at Rodale were just raking their organic tomatoes and growing their zucchini, and now you discover they've been selling these sexy book titles! You should know what goes on out there in the country! 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

Anyway, this direct mail package, with the headline _"How to Drive a Woman Wild in Bed"_ was super-successful. Another reason for its success, and this is the point I was building toward, is its creative guarantee. 

Rodale has a very generous guarantee with its books. "Send no money now. Try it for 21 days. If you don't like it, send it back and pay nothing, owe nothing. And we'll even pay your return postage." Then, right in the mailing, as a very powerful proof element that has raised their response rate across the board at Rodale, they print a return postage label. The recipients of the direct mail piece 

- 131 - 

can see, right in the mailing, this return label. On the spot, they can cut it out and know that if for any reason they don't like the book they're about to order, they already have the return label they can slap back on the shipping carton and conveniently return the book to Rodale. 

This is a great guarantee, and I took it a step further. Instead of using the usua language for this Rodale guarantee, you know, try the book for 21 days. If you're not happy with it for any reason, send it back...and so on, I promised our young studs a guarantee in language that enflamed their desire. 

**==> picture [371 x 511] intentionally omitted <==**

In case you can't read it, here's what it says... 

— _"GUARANTEED 21 incredible days of the most erotic, thrilling sex of_ — _your life or you pay nothing... You and your lover must begin to experience the most erotic, passionate, heart-pounding sex of your lives, or you may send the book back and pay nothing, owe nothing, and be under no obligation of any kind whatsoever..."_ 

In the letter, the body copy was even more descriptive and enticing about the 21 days of mind-boggling sexual excitement that was theirs for the asking... 

Based on the reactions of other guys and their lovers once they've peeked inside this extraordinary volume, I know that if you will simply look at _SEX, A MAN'S GUIDE,_ free for 21 days, I won't be able to pry it out of your hands with a crowbar. Even if I could, your lover would _never_ let you give it up. _NEVER!_ So here's my risk-free proposition. Actually, it's an invitation for you and your partner to enjoy 21 days' worth of the most stimulating, erotic sex of your lives, completely free of cost, risk, or obligation. 

Let me send you, for your 21-day free preview, a copy of _SEX, A MAN'S GUIDE._ Look it over and let your eyes be opened to sex secrets you never even knew _existed,_ let alone tried. All I can say is, get ready for 21 days' worth of flaming passion and sexual heaven on earth, as you discover how to... 

- ... drive a woman wild in bed... 

- ... have you and your lover enthusiastically share and then play out each other's most erotically charged fantasies... 

- ... scale new peaks of prolonged sexual ecstasy as you both linger "on the edge" of mutual, explosive orgasm for mind-shattering sexual pleasure you've never known... 

- ... and then fulfill yourselves again and again as you both enjoy _multiple_ orgasms. 

Experience all this for three straight weeks before you send any money. Don't even _think_ about sending money until after you've tried these sex secrets yourself for 21 days. Then and only then will you decide if this book is one of the greatest investments you've ever made in your own fulfillment and sexual pleasure, as well as your lover's. 

If, after 21 days of the most passionate and exquisitely pleasurable sex of your life, you somehow decide that you _don't_ want to keep _SEX, A MAN'S GUIDE_ for any reason whatsoever, just send it back and pay nothing, owe nothing. 

Even if the covers are dog-eared and the pages are underlined with red ink, _it doesn't matter!_ Just send the book back and pay nothing, owe nothing. 

That's how confident we are you're going to love this _Guide._ With interest in sex as high as it is among us guys, we're getting a huge response to this announcement. We simply couldn't afford to make an offer like this if these books came boomeranging back to us once they leave our hands. 

We'd be awash in red ink and my boss would be in my face real quick about a 21day-don't-pay-a-dime-upfront deal. The truth is, these books are flying out of our stockroom like boxes of chocolate on Valentine's Day, and _very few_ ever come back! 

- 133 

## (Geez, maybe _that's_ why so many women seem to be smiling more these days!)

In any case, why not go ahead and dare to become a master lover, the kind of man who knows how to drive a woman wild in bed. You will _not_ be dissatisfied, and you can be absolutely sure that neither will she —we _guarantee it!_ 

So why not trust your impulse on this one and take a look at _SEX, A MAN'S GUIDE,_ free for 21 days. Just return the enclosed Free-Preview Reply Card, and we'll send you _SEX, A MAN'S GUIDE_ for your 21-day free preview. 

The sooner you mail back the Free-Preview Reply Card, the sooner you'll start to experience the most erotic three weeks of your life. You'll soon have your lover purring with passion as you've never seen her before...and you'll both start experiencing new heights of sexual excitation neither of you imagined could exist. 

You have nothing to lose and a lifetime of more arousing, more intense, more thrilling, more frequent, and more passionate sex to gain. And that's not a bad deal in any man's book! I invite you to return the Free-Preview Reply Card now, while it's conveniently at hand. Then get ready to experience a 21-day free trial of the most erotic, explosive, and pleasurable sex of your life... 

**==> picture [26 x 7] intentionally omitted <==**

In other words, don't just go with your standard guarantee language. Use the guarantee as a mechanism to enhance the benefits of your product. 

Another great guarantee is the "110% Money-Back Guarantee." 

Still another is the "Multiple Benefit Guarantee," where you review the guarantee to review all the reader benefits: "You must experience A. You are guaranteed to experience B, and so on, or you get your money back." 

"Try Us Free" is another option that you can usually find a way to use, even if you're asking for cash up front. That still is valid if you offer a money-back guarantee. 

**==> picture [279 x 206] intentionally omitted <==**

- 134 - 

## **Proof Element #3: The Reason Why** 

OK, time for our next proof element. I'm going to move more rapidly through these. This is _The Reason Why._ 

Remember the campaign we ran as kids, _Worms That Catch Fish?_ We saw that we could engineer a quantum leap in response by changing our headline to _Local Worms Catch More Fish._ That gave us a big increase because we explained, right in our headline, that there is some mechanism or reason why the benefit is true. That's the beauty and power of putting a reason why in your campaigns, especially in your headlines. 

A reporter once asked David Ogilvy, "I understand that you're an adherent of 'reason why' advertising," to which he replied, _"Is there any other kind? "_ To Ogilvy, if you didn't have a reason why your prospect should buy into your promise, you weren't doing your job. 

Here's a good example. Soft drinks are a notoriously difficult product category to break into because most people are loyal to their soft drinks. 

**==> picture [286 x 210] intentionally omitted <==**

The road to soft drink paradise is littered with numerous packaged-goods marketers who would love to have a soft drink that people accept, but they just didn't bring it off. 

Slice used a "reason why" campaign, which is unusual in packaged-goods advertising. Since they're not direct marketers, they don't usually understand or value the benefits of multiplied salesmanship that we're talking about. 

- 135 - 

But this campaign was excellent. It said, _**"Slice, a better tasting soft drink** because it contains 10 percent fruit juice."_ 

Take away that "because" phrase and you have a typical campaign you'd expect from Madison Avenue— _**"Slice, a better tasting soft drink."**_ Pure claim that just rolls right off your back. No reason to believe, no impact, no desire to try this new soda. But when you add _"because it contains 10 percent fruit juice,"_ it ignites a little bit of curiosity in your mind. "Maybe I'll try it. I wonder what that would taste like, a soda mixed with a little bit of fruit juice. Maybe the flavor will be better than the usual soda." 

As a result, in this field notorious for a horrendously high failure rate, Slice went on to capture, very quickly, 7 percent of a $30 billion dollar a year industry on the strength of that one little proof element. That's how valuable that was for them. Today that industry is worth much more, but right out of the gate they founded a $2-billion-a-year business on the back of that little seven-word "because" phrase. 

**==> picture [294 x 187] intentionally omitted <==**

Same thing for Kleenex. To see what a difference this makes for Kleenex, just imagine how weak this headline would be if you merely say, _**"Kleenex towels** absorb 50 percent more."_ 

Pure claim. No belief. No traction. No sale. But include the mechanism, and suddenly you can understand and easily accept the benefit. "I get it. If you give me two plies in the tissue, it soaks up 50 percent more. That's common sense, **so,** sure, I can buy into that." 

**==> picture [427 x 425] intentionally omitted <==**

## My friend Don Hauptman wrote this ad, _**"Speak Spanish like a diplomat."**_

This language course was created by the U.S. State Department to teach newly appointed diplomats to learn Spanish in a hurry. Isn't that a fantastic proof element? You can use almost any words you want to have written this ad, but what really closes the sale is this amazingly persuasive proof element, which Don seized upon so intelligently. 

The same holds true for any kind of special sale. Give _a reason why_ you're running the special sale, and you'll attract a lot more buyers. 

- 137 - 

**==> picture [329 x 245] intentionally omitted <==**

For example, if you hear the words, _**"Half Off Sale,"**_ odds are it makes little impression on you. You probably think it's a gimmick or a come-on, where they may have started with artificially high prices to begin with or have last year's merchandise. You've heard about such sales many times and have unconsciously learned to turn a deaf ear. 

By contrast, if you read an ad that says, _**"Fire Sale! 50% off everything because of our recent fire** "_ now you're intrigued because if the department store is emptying out its entire warehouse because of a recent fire, you believe that it really is a half-price sale. You might think, "I'm looking for a new TV, so who cares if the box may be a little smoky." 

## **Proof Element #4: Specifics** 

Here's another powerful proof element— _specifics._ Let me show you one of the most successful headlines ever written. 

I believe that the great copywriter Fairfax Cone was the author of this headline for a space ad selling a round-the-world cruise. 

In those days, a round-the-world voyage could easily take an entire year. Imagine going on vacation at sea for a _year,_ far away from your office, your home, your mail? Affluent people did this back then, but this cruise offered something special, a world cruise in less than four months, which was much more manageable for most people. Here's the headline... 

- 138 - 

## **"Take only 110 days, spend just $11,033 for this 26,000-mile world cruise to 21 fascinating ports in 14 famed countries. Shore excursions abroad included."** 

I love this headline because it's full of specifics. Yes, it's unusually long, but it outpulled every other ad ever done for a cruise line. This was the most successful headline in the history of cruises. 

If anybody ever tells you that long headlines don't pull, tell them to go take a cruise! This headline has 25 words, and it worked like gangbusters. Naturally, this doesn't mean that _any_ 25-word headline is going to work automatically. But if you have a strong headline, one that's filled with specific information of potential interest to your prospects, don't be afraid to express it in however many words it takes. 

Another way to take advantage of the power of specifics is to single out the problem that you can solve for somebody. For example, take the headline of this lovely little editorial ad: _**''Relief from RINGING in the EARS."**_ If you have this specific problem, this headline seems to call out your name. 

**==> picture [263 x 288] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [447 x 326] intentionally omitted <==**

Here's an insert I wrote almost 30 years ago. It's the one on the left, and on the right, an updated version that's still in the take-one racks at supermarkets and other stores today. 

The headline is _**"Free Gift for Mothers Only."**_ That very targeted, specific headline has been the control for about 30 years now. It's a colossal, long-running control ad that's sold a ton of subscriptions. 

I actually think that the updated version, by someone else, is an improvement over the first one I wrote. In fact, this shows the evolution of design. 

This is a very important point. I know that some of you, as I show you these examples of magalogs, bookalogs, and other formats that make your advertising itself look valuable, I know that you're thinking, perhaps like a marketer, "We know this already. We're doing magalogs. We're doing bookalogs. We're up to speed on this already." 

## Not so. Remember, the rule is _**make your advertising itself valuable.**_

And value is in the eye of the beholder. Just because you're throwing bullets on a cover that resembles a magazine does _**not**_ necessarily make your content valuable! This is a mistake made by so many, many marketers—you may be 

- 140 - 

_imitating the form of value but lacking its substance._ Just because you're "doing a magalog" doesn't mean that you're delivering truly valuable information to your market. Just because your layout may use "magazine-style" layout doesn't mean your prospects are _seeing_ your piece as inherently valuable, which means new, fresh, and interesting. 

It must both _**look**_ and _**be**_ valuable. The form and the substance must both communicate value, and you can play with both those variables to find your most powerful mix. 

For example, take another look at the _Parents_ insert that I wrote 30 years ago, the one on the left, as well as the newer one on the right. 

If you're thinking like a copywriter—the fisherman and not the fish—you might easily assume that the one on the left, with all its great bullets, would sell more. Not so today. The original one sells hard, with lots of strong bullets. But the new one is much cleaner, with much less copy, which makes it look _more_ like a real magazine and thus communicates more value. 

This is what I've been seeing in the evolution of magalogs and bookalogs lately. Yes, you can cram lots of bullets on the cover, just as you would on an oldfashioned direct mail envelope package. 

But the more you do, the more it starts to flag itself as looking more like advertising and less like a real magazine, and it thus starts to look _less_ valuable, because of your "great sales copy"! If you stop selling so hard, you can often gain more sales because your piece looks more like a real magazine and results in higher perceived value. 

I'll show you more examples of this surprising finding later, where the more copy we took _off_ 'the front cover, even though they were great bullets, the higher response would go because the cover started to look _more_ like a clean, modern magazine cover. One big idea is often worth more than a string of multiple benefits. But sometimes not. You've got to test both, and I'll show you examples of both styles later. 

OK, let's look at a few more examples of how you can bring proof into your advertising with strong specifics... 

- 141 - 

**==> picture [404 x 279] intentionally omitted <==**

In this headline for the _Medical Hotline_ newsletter, my favorite word is "under-publicized," which hit a raw nerve in the health market. We all know that there are so many medical breakthroughs for major diseases, and we never hear about them in the media. I like the 62 number, very specific, and the subhead is full of good proof elements too. This piece worked like gangbusters. As I recall, it outpulled the control by 250 percent. 

This next one was for Phillips Publishing. It names the seven biggest threats to your health after you pass age 50 and the breakthrough treatments and remedies that prevent or reverse each. It, too, worked very well, beat the former control, and remained the control for a long time. 

- 142 - 

**==> picture [439 x 523] intentionally omitted <==**

## **Proof Element #5:** 

## **The Expose** 

Another way to boost your response with strong proof is to create your piece to look like an expose. This next direct mail package was a blockbuster that I wrote for Phillips called " _**The Great Vitamin Hoax."**_ 

_-_ 143 - 

**==> picture [427 x 511] intentionally omitted <==**

This was such a breakthrough because the editor of the newsletter, Dr. Julian Whitaker, is a well-known champion of vitamins and supplements. After we had saturated the mails for years with packages in which Julian promoted vitamins, supplements, and other natural remedies, it caught everyone's attention big-time to see a headline _"The Great Vitamin Hoax."_ 

Readers immediately wondered, "Wait a minute, you're the guy who's been telling me to take these handfuls of vitamins every day. And now you're saying it's 

- 144 - 

all a hoax?" For this market, this was a very inflammatory headline, especially coming from the doctor who had, over several years, persuaded millions of people to take more vitamins. It was a must read. 

You may wonder, "Sure, it's provocative, because it so strongly _'goes against type.'_ But how can you possibly pay off such a headline?" 

In a word, easily. 

We just have Julian saying the truth—that, yes, he's been a great champion of vitamins and still is, but he can no longer recommend them _wholesale, across the board,_ because too many vitamin manufacturers are taking production shortcuts and cheating buyers of the very health benefits people want from their vitamins. That's the new enemy— _the many charlatans who 've invaded the marketplace._ Subscribe to his newsletter, and Dr. Whitaker will tell you the best brands to buy and those to avoid. 

This is an example of outflanking the competition by repositioning your main benefit, starting to sell something different. For years, this newsletter, Dr. Julian Whitaker's _Health & Healing,_ had dominated the marketplace as a newsletter featuring natural cures and remedies for major diseases. 

He was so successful, that at least a dozen new competitors jumped into the marketplace, imitating the look of his newsletter as well as our advertising. This hurt our response, as our prospects started receiving scores of look-alike promotions just like ours from other doctors, all claiming to be able to recommend similar natural remedies for healing the same diseases. 

With this mailing, we changed the battlefield. We sold something new and different, not just the same old remedies, but an entirely new benefit—protection against all the me-too vitamin manufacturers, many of whom were cheating health-minded consumers. It worked like a charm. 

Bottom line: becoming a consumer champion through an expose is always a good technique to enhance your credibility _and_ create a highly provocative, chainrattling campaign that captures enormous readership and interest from your core market. 

Speaking of that, here is another mailing that does the same thing, in a different way, for a different market—the investor market. 

- 145 - 

**==> picture [348 x 414] intentionally omitted <==**

There are a few words that almost always guarantee readership of your headline. _**"Breakthrough"**_ is one of them. I seem to remember hearing Joe Sugarman, the mail order superstar, once saying that he never had an ad that bombed when it used the word "breakthrough" in the headline. Since hearing that, I used it many times myself. I, too, have never had a bomb with it and enjoyed several big, well, _breakthroughs._ 

Another such word is _"Surprise."_ It's pure catnip in a headline. Many years ago, I wrote the above headline to sell a Phillips publication, _Richard Young s Intelligence Report._ In the year of a presidential election, many investors wonder what a new president will do to the economy and their investments. How should they plan to avoid losses and make profits from the situation? 

This was such a success that to this day, my friends at Phillips trot this headline out every presidential election, and it always works. They update the copy each time, of course, and it always works. 

- 146 - 

They always come up with some surprise to write about in the copy. It's too good a headline to waste. Where's Chris Marett? Hi, Chris! 

Chris Marett is a great manager at Phillips Publishing. Every presidential cycle he sees to it that this old chestnut of a headline works again. It's in the bank waiting to work its magic again in the next presidential election year. 

This next one is one of my all-time favorites... 

**==> picture [355 x 391] intentionally omitted <==**

This sold another financial newsletter, _Mark Skousen s Forecasts & Strategies._ At the time I wrote this piece, investors were getting fed up with all the lies, scandals, greed, hidden financial incentives, and other forms of cheating taking place on Wall Street and in government, all of which was hurting small investors. 

I positioned the editor, Mark Skousen, as the white knight who was taking on the scoundrels, giving his readers the inside information of how not to get conned when investing. It was one of the biggest winners in the history of financial 

- 147 - 

publishing. 

The mailing featured hysterically funny caricatures of the lying stock broker, the lying politician, the lying newsletter guru, the Darth Vader IRS agent, etc. This resonated so well with investors who felt abused by unscrupulous brokers, disgusted with politicians, and shocked to find that people they trusted were cheating them. It's also an example of how you can use humor in advertising, as long as it resonates with your market. 

**==> picture [352 x 157] intentionally omitted <==**

Here's a typical sidebar from inside this mailing—"Lies Politicians Tell." Notice that as he's solemnly pledging to keep his many promises, he's got his fingers crossed. 

Here is another sidebar from the same mailing, this one inspired by my friend Gary Halbert. Is Gary here? Where's Gary? Hi, Gary! Please forgive me if I'm stepping on your toes with this idea, but I have learned a lot from you over the years and must give you credit for this one, as well. 

**==> picture [252 x 186] intentionally omitted <==**

As many of you know, Gary has very effectively used the term "sworn under oath" in his headlines, to enhance credibility and drama. 

In this mailing, I didn't want to say that our financial editor had to swear anything under oath, because that might imply that he was under investigation, like the very scoundrels we were vilifying. 

But our editor, Mark Skousen, is a Washington insider, and I liked the reverse psychology of having him "deny" that he gives illegal "inside information" to small investors, as their champion. What he does is _legally_ give small investors inside information, by tapping his many connections throughout the government and sending his readers advance alerts about developments that will profoundly affect their investments. 

In this sidebar, we have him "admit" that, yes, he did work in the economic wing of the CIA and, yes, does have access to privileged "inside information" throughout Washington but, no, has no qualms whatsoever about sharing his latest warnings, alerts, and other "insider" information with small investors, to give them an edge. It was great "I'm for the little guy" psychology, and it helped induce scores of investors sign up for his service. 

OK, onward in our march of reviewing the most powerful proof elements... 

- 149 - 

**==> picture [354 x 396] intentionally omitted <==**

## **Proof Element #6: Explain the Mechanism** 

Explaining the mechanism, or the _way,_ by which your product delivers its benefits is another powerful way to utilize proof. Doing so makes your benefits more believable, and always remember, _nobody buys without belief._ 

I wrote this piece for another Phillips publication, _Richard Band's Profitable Investing,_ and it, too, was a monster hit. 

Here's the thinking behind it. I have always been taken by the great inspirational writing of the book _The Richest Man in Babylon_ by George S. Clason. It's a little gem of a book on how to grow rich. 

I relied upon several of its key principles to position what Richard Band does, which is to give readers a methodical, reliable way to invest and grow richer each and every year, to compound your wealth systematically until you are, in fact, rich. 

- 150 - 

The mailing explains that Richard doesn't just throw darts at a board to recommend stocks and bonds. It describes Richard's precise and reliable _mechanism_ for screening out risky and overpriced stocks and focusing instead on finding screaming bargains, just like Warren Buffett, the world's most successful investor. When readers can understand the _way_ you do something, they can then understand how you are able to deliver the benefits you claim. This is a very important principle—whenever you can, explain the mechanism by which you make the benefits possible. 

## **Proof Element #7:** 

## **Sell Against Type** 

Another way to capture credibility is to sell against type. We saw this before in the ad I showed for Dr. Julian Whitaker, _**"The Great Vitamin Hoax."**_ That's so arresting because it seems to be against everything that this famous champion of vitamins stands for. It would be like George Bush saying, _**"The Great Iraq War Hoax"**_ or Michael Jordan saying, _**"The Great Chicago Bulls Basketball Hoax."**_ 

This was another very successful mailing for Richard Band, one which played against type. Here we have Richard Band, a conservative financial advisor from New Hampshire, someone beloved by tens of thousands of wealthy Republican investors, saying—quite surprisingly—that he's thrilled about the reelection of Bill Clinton, a Democrat, because he's going to make us all richer than you'd ever dared to hope. Coming from a Bill Clinton partisan, this would not have made a splash. But coming from a conservative, it was startling indeed. 

**==> picture [519 x 262] intentionally omitted <==**

One more example of playing against type: John Dessauer is a financial advisor famous for advocating international mutual funds and international stocks. Here we have him announcing his report _**"The Case AGAINST International** Mutual Funds."_ 

Among those who know him, and our previous mailings made him very well known throughout the investment newsletter community, this headline was a shocker, making legions of investors wonder, "How can this be? This man for years has been urging me to _get into_ international mutual funds. Has he done a 180 on us?" 

Whenever a champion, a strong advocate of something in a given field, says, or seems to say, "Hold on, there's a case against something here," anybody interested in that subject has to pay attention. 

In this case, we paid off the headline the same way as with " _**The Great Vitamin Hoax."**_ Yes, he is a longtime champion of this field, but he can no longer recommend it _wholesale,_ that the sheer growth in popularity that he himself helped bring about in this area has now resulted in many less qualified product offerings. You've now got to be more careful than ever, and thus the rationale for a newsletter on the right way, the safest way, to invest in these very attractive international mutual funds and stocks. Illustrate all these points with lots of examples of what not to do, of the mistakes others are making, and this becomes a potent sales piece. 

Now, with all of these examples, please don't think that if you're not in the financial or health newsletter business, that these ideas can't work for you. We're talking here about human psychology that works in any market. With just a little imagination, you can take any of these concepts and create a blockbuster for your product or service. 

As an example, this innocent looking headline phrase, _**"The** Case Against... "_ has been for me one of the most powerful headlines I've ever found for any product or service. 

Here's the formula: you state _**"The** Case Against..."_ and then fill in the blank with whatever your market is now doing. It virtually guarantees a very high readership every time. 

For example, let's say that after you sent in your money for this seminar, you suddenly came across a mailing or article that said, _**"The** Case Against_ _**Attending** Gary Bencivenga *s Seminar in May."_ 

Could you possibly ignore such a headline? It instantly grabs your emotions because you're already committed to doing something that someone has a "case 

## 1 5 2 -

- 

against" doing. You've got to read it to find out if you're about to do something wrong or dangerous. 

Or, since this seminar is in New York, imagine if you saw a headline that said, _"The Case Against Traveling to New York This May."_ 

If that was your plan, to travel here in May, you'd instantly stop whatever you're doing to find out "Why shouldn't I travel to New York in May? That's what I'm scheduled to do! Is there some terrorist alert I don't know about? What's the problem with going to New York in May?" 

You've got a guaranteed readership that you can then take wherever you want. 

In Dan Rosenthal's ad agency where I once worked, the one I mentioned earlier, the brilliant little agency with the horrendously long name—Callas, Powell, Rosenthal & Bloch—that's where I first came across this _**"Case Against..."**_ headline. 

I'm jumping ahead here, as **I'll** cover this in our section on copywriting later. But while we're on this _**"Case Against..."**_ headline, I have to tell you that this is one of those formula headlines that you can use over and over again, as long as you fill in the blank after the word _**"Against"**_ with something that your prospects are already involved with, are committed to, believe in, or are planning on doing. 

I remember that at Callas, Powell, etc., we had the account for the T. J. Holt investment newsletter. Mr. Holt published a report warning about the overlooked dangers of municipal bonds, otherwise known as "tax exempt bonds," or "tax exempts" for short. 

Someone at the agency at the time, perhaps it was Dan Rosenthal, I don't remember, wrote an ad with the headline _**"The Case Against Tax Exempts."**_ 

Most of us thought this would bomb. It sounded so boring. But the ad was written, so let's test it. 

We felt confident enough that we'd have a winner for the account, because another ad that one of us had written was all about gold stocks, which were hot as a pistol at the time. 

The headline for _that_ ad was something like "7 South African gold stocks poised to soar." 

This was much sexier, and South African gold stocks were rising rapidly at the time. 

If I were to ask all of you, which headline do you think would win, given that gold stocks were so hot, you'd probably guess, as most of us did, that the gold stocks headline would win. 

- 153 - 

But _"The Case Against Tax Exempts"_ beat it massively, as I recall, by perhaps multiple hundreds of percentage points. It was unbelievable. 

A test result like that takes your breath away, especially when most of us were so wrong about it. So we had to figure it out. 

When we did, we learned something very valuable. We came to realize that the number of investors who own tax exempt bonds was enormous. In fact, the bond market is so big, it's _nine times larger_ than the entire stock market! And gold stocks are just a tiny, miniscule sliver of the stock market. So right off the bat, our ad about tax exempts was aimed at a market many, many times larger than the gold stock ad. 

Moreover, not only did so many investors own tax exempt bonds, but they also felt very complacent, safe, and secure about them. They're known as "widows and orphans investments." 

In fact, many people had invested a big part of their life savings in tax exempt bonds, something that virtually nobody does with gold stocks. When this vast market of tax exempt bond holders saw the headline _"The Case Against Tax Exempts,"_ they were absolutely compelled to find out what could be wrong with such a large, important part of their portfolio. They could not ignore such a threat. 

When you have something that can be fit into this headline, _"The Case Against..."_ with a marketplace already committed to however you fill in the blank, you are almost guaranteed to get a high response, provided that you pay off the headline with a strong, hard-hitting presentation that builds a powerful case against, and then describes how the solution can be found in what you're offering. 

Combining techniques here, this strategy is especially effective when you use it with a spokesperson who goes against type. In other words, this _"Case Against Tax Exempts"_ could have been even stronger had it come from someone who was a champion of tax exempt bonds. Then it makes it seem as if someone has had a complete change of mind on the subject. It would be like, _"The Case Against Low-Carb Diets" by Doctor Rodney E. Brice, formerly of the Dr. Robert Atkins Weight Loss Center._ 

## **Proof Element #8: Acknowledging Disbelief** 

OK, I'm coming toward the end of the most persuasive proof elements you can harness in your advertising. 

Acknowledging your reader's disbelief is one of the easiest and most effective. 

- 154 - 

If you have a very strong promise, sometimes the best thing you can do is acknowledge that "Hey, I find this hard to believe myself, but here it is." Or you can use a shorthand where your headline simply says, "Believe it or not...." 

For example, I wrote this for the editor of a mutual fund advisory service. It was a huge hit... 

**==> picture [415 x 330] intentionally omitted <==**

One thing I'd like to point out here is that this is also is a very flexible headline and subhead structure. 

I call it an "umbrella-style" headline, because it lets you fit lots of ideas and benefits under it, which is especially helpful when they're not related. 

You copywriters will appreciate this. Have you ever had to write about something and you just can't sum up the benefits of the product or proposition in a single, magical seven, eight, 12, or even 25 words? 

This headline saves your life in this situation. _"Believe it or_ _**not, I've** discovered an investment that's safer than your bank and offers you all these advantages..."_ 

## And then you just write up a laundry list of all the great advantages that it

- 155 - 

combines. The best thing I've found about headlines like this is that they have great pulling power. I've used these umbrella headlines with many different products across the board—consumer finance, health, investing, moneymaking opportunities, you name it. It always seems to work, usually with a strength you'd never guess. 

And this kind of headline is very easy to write. _**"Announcing a blank that combines all these advantages...'**_ 'You can almost do that in your sleep, yet it's very, very effective. 

But the main point I was making, to keep this on track here, is acknowledging disbelief. When the writer speaking the headline is already bringing skepticism to the foreground, it saves the reader from doing so, and you get a pass, even on a very powerful benefit. The reader thinks, "I would normally find that hard to believe. But the speaker is acknowledging that he or she feels that way too. So maybe I can trust this person. He or she isn't trying to snow me." 

**==> picture [419 x 182] intentionally omitted <==**

Gene Schwartz wrote one of my favorite headlines, which, in a very subtle way, acknowledges disbelief. He did it with the deft touch of phrasing an admittedly outlandish claim in the form of a question. _**"Should any man be given so much sexual power over women?"**_ Provocative, amusing—and deadly effective. 

## **Proof Element #9:** 

## **A Highly Believable Source** 

This is obvious. The more credible your source, the more credible your message. I don't know who wrote this great headline, but it's a dazzler. It pulled in 

- 156 - 

a ton of sales for a silver option dealer. This looks like a joint venture. Chip Wood, the publisher of _Insider Report,_ sent this letter to his own house list, endorsing someone else's offer. Since he is admired and respected by his readers, many of them will follow suit. The headline is extremely believable because Chip is such a trusted source by his readers. 

**==> picture [458 x 257] intentionally omitted <==**

Here's another example of the persuasive power of a highly believable source. 

In fact, when I came upon this ad, my initial thought was that it was poorly designed. 

**==> picture [487 x 211] intentionally omitted <==**

This headline looked quite weak to me. _"I'd advise_ _**you** to subscribe."_ Normally, as a prospect, I'd skip right over it. "Who cares _that you_ advise me to subscribe? _Who the heck are you to advise me about anything? "_ 

But, as an advertising critic wanting to see how bad this ad could get, I kept reading, and I was bowled over. 

_"Who the heck was this? "_ 

Warren Buffett, that's who! Only the most successful investor on the planet... possibly the smartest and most successful investor who's ever walked the earth! 

And what does the greatest investor on earth, someone who, to my knowledge, had never endorsed any newsletter before, have to say about _this_ newsletter? 

"I advise you to subscribe. I read each issue religiously. Anyone interested in investing who doesn't subscribe is making a big mistake." 

Oh, what this newsletter could have done with this testimonial if it was only given a better graphic treatment, including a photo of the master himself. 

When I saw this ad, I picked up the phone and called America's largest investment newsletter publisher, my client, Phillips Publishing. I urged Richard Stanton-Jones, then a key executive at Phillips, to buy this newsletter immediately, if only to get this incredible testimonial. Oh, what hay we could make with this! 

What we have here is a vastly undervalued testimonial, a vastly undervalued marketing asset. Warren Buffett, the greatest investor in the world, is saying, "I advise you to subscribe. You're making a mistake if you don't." You cannot get better than that! Any copywriter who tries to write something could never have the credibility of Warren Buffett. 

But, alas, the newsletter wasn't for sale and the opportunity eluded us. 

## **Proof Element #10:** 

## **Testimonials** 

We can all do a better job of getting great testimonials. To be great, testimonials should have what David Ogilvy called a _"burr of singularity."_ 

That means they should sound rough-edged and real. They shouldn't sound like the polished prose of a copywriter. They should have a spontaneous, singular feel to them, as this adds to their believability. 

For example, this testimonial was written to one of our clients at Callas, Powell, etc. I believe that Dan Rosenthal seized upon it and wrote the ad. I want to give him proper credit. I love the part of the testimonial that says, "Guys like me need guys like you _**and our** wives_ to protect us." A copywriter wouldn't have 

- 158 - 

thought to add that little _**"and our** wives."_ That part lets you know it came from a real guy, with a real wife who looks out for him. 

**==> picture [456 x 175] intentionally omitted <==**

Here is another example. You get the strong feeling that this is a real person who would write in and say, _**"When I've cleared a million dollars, I'll be out to buy you a lunch."**_ You suspect the person is real because the quote sounds so real. It gives you the feeling, _"You just can't make this stuff up!"_ 

**==> picture [494 x 159] intentionally omitted <==**

Here is another technique to use when you're desperate for a good testimonial but don't have one. You can always get one from the owner of the company. Don't laugh. Here's how to do it... 

- 159- 

**==> picture [453 x 289] intentionally omitted <==**

**Proof Element #11:** 

## **Candor** 

I think that my favorite proof element of all is candor. This is one you can have all to yourself because so few of us like to be candid in our advertising. 

**==> picture [441 x 462] intentionally omitted <==**

_JOE POLISH YELLS OUT:_ "I've been trying to teach this to Gary Halbert for years. He just doesn't get it!" 

_GARYBENCIVENGA:_ You'd better watch out, he's sitting right behind you, Joe! This is another ad from my days working with Dan Rosenthal, and I think 

- 161 - 

you'll find it very instructive, as I did. 

## The headline is "2 _reasons why the price of SILVER may rise steeply."_

When I joined the agency, this ad was already running and pulling in tremendous numbers of leads throughout the country. The client was mainly selling silver coins and was making a fortune because inflation was rising at that time and people were buying and hoarding legal-tender silver coins. 

The ad said that two factors—a shortfall in silver production and the rise of inflation—were putting irresistible pressure under silver prices. Very rational copy, filled with excellent facts and proof. The offer was a free booklet, _**"The** Case_ _**for** Silver"_ and people could request a copy to learn more about the opportunity. 

I came along, brilliant copywriter that I thought I was, and said to Dan, "That's kind of a wimpy headline, isn't it? That word 'may' bothers me. Why don't we just say _'2 reasons why the price of SILVER will rise steeply_ '?" 

Let's be sure of ourselves. Let's be strong in our forecast. Let's act as if we really believe in what we're predicting. To say "may" rise steeply just sounds weak. 

We tested my suggestion and by now you already suspect the outcome. The headline with the word "may" greatly outpulled the headline with the word "will." Isn't that counterintuitive? It was for me. Most copywriters would never write the headline with the word "may." They would opt for "will" or some other way to indicate that a price rise is "inevitable." Thinking as a marketer, that may seem stronger. But our tests showed it wasn't even close. 

Why? 

Because our prospects are almost always a lot smarter than we give them credit for. For example, in this case, investors know that no one can predict the future. When you pretend to, when you come on too strong, you lose credibility, which is the main lesson I'm sharing with you this morning. 

Above all, keep your claims credible. Sometimes, intentionally understate them. Don't be afraid to reveal candor, which your prospects and customers will love. You'll see for yourself if you try it, even though it probably goes against your marketing instincts, which normally lead you to hype your promise to the sky. Take a step backward, one that speaks from the heart with candor. You may well be amazed at the results. 

- 162 - 

**==> picture [461 x 224] intentionally omitted <==**

Here is another example of what I'm talking about. When Hume Publishing hired me to sell their investment course, they wanted something really exciting to beat their control package. They had tested many of the country's best direct mail copywriters and had heard, from my clients, that they should also test me. 

## I came back with this concept, and they asked, _**"'Get Rich Slowly'?"**_

"Are you serious, Gary? We think you're losing your touch, because people don't want to get rich _slowly."_ 

I said, "Believe me, you must take the side of the prospect. Trust me on this and just test it. There's so much get-rich-quick hype out there. Let's sell against that. Let's sell this excellent course as one the prospect can trust." 

The copy said, in effect, that if you're looking for a way to get rich overnight, which nobody believes you can do anyway, this is _**not**_ the course for you. But if you'd like to accumulate a million dollars over the course of a normal working lifetime, that _is._ certainly doable. We'll show you how with this simple course. Just put aside maybe $1,000 or $2,000 a year in an IRA, invest it properly as we'll teach you, and you can indeed reach the million-dollar mark someday, thanks to the power of compounding. If you can sock away even more, you'll get there faster. 

This was a monster success. It uses a number of the techniques I've been talking about. _**"Your Special Report Enclosed"**_ indicates something of value inside. The corner card copy, in the upper left, does not spill our candy in the lobby by revealing that we're a publisher trying to sell something. And the subhead uses a quote from our own editor, though you don't know he's our editor yet, which is an extremely powerful testimonial that reinforces the headline. 

- 163 - 

When you get inside the package, the letter attacks the hype that we see all around us about how to get rich quick. We dismiss this as rubbish, saying to the reader, in effect, you know it's not true, I know it's not true. So let's get real. If you have it in mind that you want to make a million dollars someday and you only have a couple of thousand to put away each year, is there really a way you can do it? Here's a course created by a group of self-made millionaires to show you how they did it and how anyone can do it, etc. The whole package was very high in credibility and sold a tremendous number of courses, beating the former control by a huge margin. 

**==> picture [413 x 244] intentionally omitted <==**

Still on candor, here is a headline test that John Caples conducted. He had disappointing results when his client ran the first ad he wrote, which featured the headline _"Fix any part of any car."_ The ad was for a book on auto repair, and mechanics were the market. 

But the ad flopped, even though Caples believed it was well written. He did his own little "mother-in-law survey" with a few mechanics, asking them, "Why wouldn't you respond to this ad? Why wouldn't you buy this book?" 

The mechanics looked the ad over and were suspicious. _"Fix ami part of any car? Come on, you can't be serious!"_ 

They explained that they occasionally see weird cars driving into their stations, cars from Europe, for example, that they have no idea how to fix. They just couldn't believe that a single book on auto repair could possibly be thorough 

- 164 - 

enough to address every part of every car made in the world. The ad was shooting itself in the foot by overclaiming. 

Caples realized they were absolutely right. He went back and tested a new headline, _**"Fix almost any part of almost any car/'**_ and now the book became a hot seller among auto mechanics. 

Again, this seems very counterintuitive. You would think that the second headline, _**"almost any part of almost any car"**_ is weaker. But that's because we're thinking like marketers, not prospects. Marketers want to _push, push, push._ But if you think more like your prospects, who _doubt, doubt, doubt,_ and who look for the quickest excuse, for the slightest evidence of overclaiming, to blow you off, you'll start to be much more careful not to overclaim, not just because it's the right thing to do, but also because, far and away, it's usually the most responsive thing you can do. 

One last example of unusual candor. 

Back at the turn of the last century, the early 1900s, Ernest Shackleton was a great explorer of the South Pole. He would take these expeditions down to the Antarctic. He needed substantial funding for such adventures, so he would go to scientific organizations and ask if they could be a sponsor.
