His sponsors insisted that Shackleton find and take with him a large team of highly respected scientists who could conduct their investigations during the trip, to record what they find among the mammals they'd encounter, the plant specimens, geological observations, and so on. 

Copywriters, imagine what a difficult assignment Shackleton faced! He had to persuade successful scientists and doctors to leave home and hearth; to kiss their wives, children, or sweethearts good-bye, possibly forever; and to quit their cushy jobs in academia to join him on one of the longest, most dangerous, and most uncomfortable journeys imaginable, a trip to the South Pole, through raging storms, freezing cold, and uncharted seas full of ice that could crush a ship's hull like an eggshell in a single day of bad weather. 

And, no matter what happened, there would be no navy, no coast guard, no 911 or rescue team to call upon if they got into trouble. They'd simply perish silently, alone, invisible, unreachable, and as vulnerable as candles in a windstorm on the dark side of the moon. 

Just picture how most of us copywriters would write an ad to bring people in! We'd trumpet all the benefits and, at the end of the ad, in a footnote of mice type, acknowledge that there could be some risks, so please check with your doctor. 

But look at what Shackleton did—with the power of candor... 

- 165 - 

**==> picture [443 x 198] intentionally omitted <==**

How did this brutally candid ad do? 

Shackleton reported that it seemed like half of England wanted to go with him! He said he could not keep up with the responses. 

Why? There is something so incredibly powerful about admitting four or five faults or shortcomings and then, at last, hitting upon the two great benefits— _"honor and recognition in case of success."_ 

Had he started out with the benefits—"Come and be famous with us on this historic expedition"—candidates would immediately pull back. They'd think, "Not so fast. What are you hiding from me? What are the risks? Are you insane for even asking me?" 

But when Shackleton himself bravely brings up all those risks, his candor makes you trust him immediately. And you must trust someone if you're going to let him lead you on a treacherous expedition. 

The psychological secret behind this ad is the _contrast principle._ Psychology professors will sometimes demonstrate to their classes in this way. They have three pails of water at the front of the class, on the teacher's desk. You are asked to put your right hand in a pail of freezing water and your left hand in a pail full of hot water and leave them there a while. 

When you are asked to take both hands out, one has gotten very cold and the other hot, from being in the different pails of water. Then the professor tells you to put both hands in another pail, this one filled with lukewarm water. 

What happens? Your hand that was roasting in the hot water will perceive the lukewarm water as cold, because of the contrast. Likewise, your hand that was freezing in the ice water will perceive the lukewarm water as hot. 

- 166- 

It's an amazing experiment because here you are, the same person with both your hands in the same pail of lukewarm water. Yet one of your hands perceives that lukewarm water as cold, the other as hot. That's the contrast principle. _Your current perception is highly influenced by what has preceded it._ 

You can use it very effectively in advertising, as Shackleton has done masterfully here, by admitting some faults. Here's the pattern of what he did and the reason that his last line of copy has such enormous propulsion. The structure is _shortcoming...shortcoming...shortcoming...shortcoming...shortcoming...honor and recognition in case of success._ And half of England seemed to respond. Candidly admitting faults and shortcomings is one of the most persuasive things you can do. As Ogilvy would say, "The antique dealer who points out a scratch always wins my trust." 

By the way, in addition to the legions of men who answered this ad, five women wanted to go. Shackelton thought about this and realized that with all the men, with the long months of total darkness and so much time on the crew's hands, having mixed genders could trigger a lot of rivalry among the men, so he set sail with men only. Of course, this was in the early 1900s, before many women had the opportunity to be scientists, as they do today. 

## **Proof Element #12: Specialization** 

Specialization is a great proof element, a tool you can use almost any time to make yourself more credible. 

The principle is simple and powerful: if you have a brain tumor, you don't go to your family doctor. You want a good neurosurgeon. If the tumor is on the left side of your brain and has some arcane medical term, you want the specialist within the neurosurgeon community who has most experience with this specific problem. If you come across such a doctor, he won't have to sell you on letting him be your doctor. If he specializes in exactly what you've got, the "sale" is almost closed before you even meet. This is how powerful specialization can be. 

This is what's wrong with department stores now. They try to be everything to everybody in an age of greater and greater specialization. This is a formula for disaster, and it's why almost every month that goes by, you hear about another department store chain going bankrupt. They're trying to be everything to everybody in an age of specialization. 

New York City is a perfect example of how specialization works. There are not many old-fashioned department stores here anymore. There are a few, and for an 

- 167 - 

increasingly small number of people who like to find a little bit of everything all in one place, there will always be a need for an "everything for everybody" department store. 

But today, most stores will have a much greater shot at success by specializing in a narrow niche. That's so much more powerful because it gives you built-in proof as an expert and preferred source within that narrow field. If you're a brain surgeon, you don't have to sell yourself. People who need brain surgery seek you out. You don't have to seek them out. The same psychology, to one degree or another, works in any field. The more you specialize, the more people will seek you out. 

After about 10 years of working in ad agencies, I decided to go out on my own, hang my own shingle. But I was leaving a very high-paying job and felt nervous. 

In fact, I felt so nervous that I created 11 different letterheads, each one featuring a different specialization. I specialized in financial newsletters. I specialized in health newsletters. I specialized in financial and health newsletters. I specialized in direct response advertising for book and record clubs. I specialized in lots of things. 

When a potential new client would ask me to send information about myself, I'd send it on the appropriate letterhead for his business. 

This was before the computer, and I couldn't make these changes using just a keyboard and a laser printer. Pauline will remember, I had to get all these different letterheads printed up, and in our supply cabinet we had all these boxes of different letterheads. 

"What kind of client is this? A stock broker?" Great. I'd pull out the letterhead describing me as a specialist in financial direct response advertising and send him a proposal on that. 

This was all true, mind you. I did have specialized knowledge in each one of these 11 areas, but I wanted that client to see that I'm a specialist in exactly what he or she was looking for. I usually got the job. 

I mentioned before how New York is becoming a city of highly specialized stores. If you get the time while you're here, try to make it over to the new Time Warner Center at Columbus Circle. We walked by there recently, and I saw something that exemplifies this trend toward ever-increasing specialization. There's a store there called "The Pink Store." I'm not sure what they sell, but looking through the window, everything I saw was pink! How about that? A store that specializes exclusively in pink accessories. 

- 168 - 

That's how specialized some of these stores in Manhattan have become. 

But this is not new. It's one of the most powerful long-term trends in marketing. When I was a kid, there was one basic type of sneaker. A popular brand was PF Flyers. All the kids wanted those, and they were a fine all-around pair of sneakers. 

Today things are much more specialized. First off, you don't buy sneakers in just any old shoe store. You buy them in an _athletic shoe store._ 

So shoe stores themselves have subdivided into specializations. When you walk into the athletic shoe store, you don't find one generalized, made-foreverybody, all-purpose sneaker. 

Everything is much more specialized. You've got tennis shoes, basketball shoes, running shoes, hiking shoes, walking shoes, etc. All these varieties are a testament to the sheer marketing power of specialization. 

Let's say you're a young person and you didn't know there are special sneakers for runners. You're in high school and you decide to take up running. You walk into the store and the salesperson asks what you want the shoes for. 

You say you're taking up running. The salesperson says, "That's great. We have just what you need— _runners' shoes"_ 

As soon as he or she utters those words, you're hooked. Now that you know there are specialized shoes for runners, can anything else be good enough for you? Of course not! That's how powerful specialization is. It sells you instantly. Even if the basketball shoes happen to be _the exact same shoe in every way except name,_ you wouldn't believe it. You'd feel better buying the shoes for runners only. 

You can use that to great advantage in your marketing. In fact, when we get into launching your own new products, we'll see how to harness this principle with devastating effectiveness. At this point, just bear in mind that specialization is always among the strongest proof elements in your arsenal. 

## **Great Ads That Have It All** 

We're getting close to our lunch hour, and I'm almost done on this subject. I'd like to wrap up with a couple of ads that have it all, that illustrate the raw power you can harness when you use some of these tools together instead of separately. 

The first, unfortunately, is a masterpiece. I say "unfortunately" because it was used to sell cigarettes. It was written by the great copywriter Rosser Reeves, the man who invented the USP—the idea that every ad should have _a unique selling proposition,_ which was a major breakthrough in advertising history. 

- 169 - 

**==> picture [505 x 692] intentionally omitted <==**

I do believe that the _Persuasion Equation_ I described earlier is an advance on the USP because the USP just talks about having a unique selling proposition, or promise. But because of the overwhelming amount of clutter we must now compete against, and because powerful proof is a shortcut through that clutter, I believe the equation I gave you is a lot more effective than the classic USP, which didn't talk about proof. 

But if he didn't talk about proof much nor include it in the formulation of his USP rule, the great copywriter Rosser Reeves nevertheless understood how important proof is, as you can see from this ad. 

It ran in the 1950s and is a masterpiece. I used the word "unfortunately" in describing it before because it's a real shame to use all of our skills to sell bad products that are going to hurt people or hoodwink them. We should use our expertise to introduce people to truly great products and services that will help them improve their lives. But this is what Rosser Reeves wrote, and I use it as an example of his great craftsmanship, despite the contempt I feel for his product. 

You can see just the principles he harnessed. He announces that there's going to be an article in _Reader s Digest_ confirming everything he says, which itself is a great proof element, as he invokes the most trusted magazine in America, wrapping his message in the goodwill and trust this magazine enjoys with the buying public. 

He also introduces the mechanism, "filtered cigarette smoke," and gives an easy-to-understand illustration down below. The benefit is "better for your health," of great concern to smokers who can't quit. He's hitting on all cylinders here. He even includes a doctor, representing perhaps America's most trusted profession in those days, further explaining the mechanism and underlining the importance and urgency of smoking a filtered cigarette. 

## **How Did This Ad Work?** 

Let me tell you what Rosser Reeves wrote about the results of this ad. 

"With these 130 words of copy, no more, no less, monthly sales, according to Wooten's annual published reports, jumped from 50 million to 60 million, then to 100 million, then to 150 million, all on the strength of this ad. 200 million, and up and up and up. 

"We are not only producing and selling now at factory capacity. We are enlarging the plant to meet consumer demands." 

All from the use of the manipulation of the four factors, _especially proof,_ that I've mapped out for you in _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®._ 

_-_ 171 - 

It's almost that these four elements of this equation are like puppet strings. Once you know how to use them, especially proof, you can virtually make the marketplace behave the way you want. 

Finally, let me show you an ad of how I brought all of these factors together in a mailing. 

**==> picture [440 x 182] intentionally omitted <==**

This was an ad for Julian Snyder's newsletter. It was such a big hit, so much more powerful than anything he'd ever used before, that Julian was astonished by its performance. He started minting money once this ran. 

The headline says, _"On page 5 of the enclosed letter, I name a stock with spectacular profit potential."_ 

So first off, right there in the headline on the envelope, we are using the Cracker Jack secret, revealing that there's a wonderful surprise inside that this market will love—a hot tip on a great stock. You don't have to buy the newsletter to get this tip, just open the envelope now and it's yours. To active stock market investors, this is almost impossible to throw away without opening. So we're effectively countering the first objection, "No time to deal with this right now." 

And here's where the use of proof really gets interesting. The subhead under the headline adds... 

"I think this one will rise even higher than the last one I wrote you about in January, which soared 77% in less than three months. If you don't believe me, just follow this stock in your newspaper and watch it explode." 

What a provocative, confident statement! Who is this guy to make such a boast? 

The caption under his photo tells you that this is no crank or con artist. In fact, he's somebody described as "amazingly, alarmingly accurate" by the _New York_ 

_- Ill -_ 

_Times,_ one of the world's most trusted newspapers. 

If you ever get a testimonial like that, you've got to put it near your claim! Here's the _New York Times_ canonizing Julian Snyder as a guru to be heeded. Put that next to a big, bold promise such as we have here, and it's almost like the _New York Times_ is urging you to take this man's promises seriously. 

On the back side of the envelope, we're not wasting our real estate either. We're using it to sell... 

**==> picture [439 x 184] intentionally omitted <==**

We're filling the back of the outer envelope with lots of other Cracker Jack prizes to be found inside. 

Again, on the letter, we show the reader a copy of the actual direct mail envelope Julian sent previously. Nothing stings an investor like an opportunity missed. Here we are saying, if you didn't act the last time I mailed you a letter like this, wow, did you miss out. Take a look at what we mailed you back in January. If you ignored my advice on that one, for heaven's sake, don't lose out on this next one, which I suspect is going even higher. 

Julian Snyder made a fortune with mailings like this. And he was, many times, just what the _New York Times_ said—"Amazingly, alarmingly accurate." 

This wraps up our discussion this morning. We'll see how to take all of this to an even higher level this afternoon, how to apply these techniques to create new products and sell your services if you're a copywriter or designer. 

- 173 - 

**==> picture [452 x 503] intentionally omitted <==**

## **The Secret of Master Closers** 

The main point I want you to take away from this session, to remember always in your marketing, is _The Secret of Master Closers:_ master closers sell on proof. Strengthening your proof—in support of a big promise—is the most overlooked yet most leveraged and most powerful way to boost your response. Your prospects already know that promises are easy to manufacture, but proof, like gold, is not. They thus view proof as the rarest and most prized part of marketing messages, 

- 174 - 

the rarity that deserves special attention and makes _your_ message stand out from the overwhelming clutter of unsupported promises. 

**==> picture [176 x 18] intentionally omitted <==**

- 175 

**==> picture [473 x 247] intentionally omitted <==**

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ This session is going to be interesting, I promise. It will reveal some secrets that have never been published anywhere as far as I know. It will include an extremely powerful marketing secret I learned from Joseph Segel, the founder of the Franklin Mint. 

Let's start off with... 

## **How to Create Blockbuster Products** 

It's axiomatic in marketing, across virtually all product categories, that up to 90 percent of new products fail, depending on the industry. 

Why is that? Mostly because we have fishermen thinking like fishermen, not like the fish they're trying to catch. We have marketers thinking like the people who launched the Edsel, thinking about their own problems inside their headquarters more than the problems and desires of their market. 

How can we correct that? 

The first rule we must keep uppermost in our minds is that a new product succeeds _not_ because you want to sell it, but because people want to buy it. 

How can we have a _customer-driven_ company that keeps this thought uppermost? 

First, I gave you some clues before, the formula that keeps your internal thinking right in sync with your prospects. That is _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®._ 

During any discussion of a new product, always ask, "Are we solving our prospect's _urgent problem?_ Are we making a _unique promise_ to solve that problem? Are we building in _unquestionable proof,_ the most important step of all? Remember, the biggest way to improve your response is to improve your proof. Combine your bold promise with bold proof and you cannot get a better combination in advertising. And, finally, have we come up with a _user-friendly proposition?'''_ 

When you're evaluating new products, you don't just want to say, "Hey, why don't we sell a fill-in-the-blank?" 

I've been at so many marketing meetings where someone will say something like "We're a well-known financial magazine. We see that lots of people buy financial newsletters. Why don't we launch our own financial newsletter?" This is how bad products get born, only to perish soon after. 

Companies get themselves in trouble with such off-the-cuff marketing thinking. For example, let's say you do have a successful financial magazine. It 

- 179 - 

may be tempting to think "Why not launch our own financial newsletter because investors also buy newsletters?" What's wrong with such thinking? 

First, you're thinking like the fisherman. You're starting something just because you want to sell it, not because anyone wants to buy it or because your market is crying out for it. What urgent problem are you trying to solve, other than your own? 

What's more, you've got to think about your current, main product, your successful financial magazine. What are all your magazine readers to think if you come out with a more private and expensive financial newsletter—that now the good stuff is reserved only for the newsletter people? You may well start to cannibalize your own customer base, unwittingly creating massive dissatisfaction among your magazine's customers. Why aren't they receiving what you're going to be putting into your newsletter? Are they now your poor relations? 

As I say, this sort of thing happens because someone says, "Let's do this or that to make more money," and _not_ because you're listening to a hue and cry arising from the marketplace begging for such a product. It's Ford Motor Company thinking about launching an Edsel just because they'd like to, rather than listening and responding to the genuine desires coming from the marketplace. 

So this _Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®_ will keep your head on straight about this. Your new product evaluation should require all parties involved to answer, _What is the urgent problem we 're solving for our market; what is the unique promise we can make to this market, the promise that clearly differentiates us from everyone else; what is our unquestionable proof that we can deliver on this unique promise; and what is the most compelling, irresistible, user-friendly offer that we can dream up to close the sale on the spot?_ 

And don't let yourself or your people fudge these answers. Imagine that you're a skeptical prospect, and hold their feet to the fire to be sure the answers make sense to you as such a skeptical prospect. 

This is applying the mighty Pareto Principle to your marketing. You know the Pareto Principle, the 80/20 rule, the secret of the vital few and the trivial many. There are very few basic thoughts that you must have in marketing to have it come out right, but these basic tenets are absolutely critical, and they are embedded automatically for you in _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®._ 

## I was reading a book the other day, _2,239 Tested Secrets for Direct Marketing Success._

_A_ good book, but the problem is, I can't remember more than about four or five things when I'm trying to write something. It would be like _"2,239 secrets of_ 

_-_ 180 - 

_the perfect golf swing."_ 

That's why I created this _Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®._ If you bring these four fundamental questions to bear when launching new products or advertising campaigns, you're never going to go far wrong. They will light up like an X-ray machine with four bulbs showing exactly where your copy or art is weak. 

Having been a copy chief, I would have lots of pieces of copy submitted to me by lots of different writers whose styles were different. Sometimes, before I put this system together, I would think, "Gee, something's bothering me about this copy, but I just can't put my finger on what it is." 

This _Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®_ now makes it easy. It doesn't necessarily make it easy to write better copy. I'll have tips on that later. But this does make it remarkably easy to _evaluate_ copy. Instead of wondering, "What the heck is wrong?" just ask... 

- "Are we addressing an urgent problem?" If not, you have more work to do in that quadrant. 

- "Is the promise unique?" Or does this sound like everyone else's advertising? If your headline seems all too familiar and just like so many others out there, you've got more work to do in that quadrant. 

- "Is the proof strong, utterly compelling?" 

- "Is the proposition virtually irresistible?" 

These four simple questions give your entire company a way to discuss the merits of a given piece of copy, the design, the whole package. 

This is so simple that everybody, even your receptionist, can take part in this process to improve your marketing. 

In fact, when I worked at Dan Rosenthal's agency, after I left Ogilvy & Mather, we would give our copy to virtually everyone in the company—the people in the mailroom, the assistants, the receptionist, the designers, everyone. We even asked people to take it home and ask for their spouses' opinions. Two things would invariably happen. 

First, you'd always be surprised at the intelligent commentary you'd get from people you wouldn't normally think of asking. You'd come to realize that they are experts in their own way, expert consumers. We're all good consumers. They will say things like "I didn't understand this part of the ad," or "Over here, I thought you meant something entirely different," or "I didn't believe the claim you're making in this section. Seems quite exaggerated to me." 

Second, you'll also be surprised at how consistent many of the comments are. 

- 181 - 

You'll see right away that several people are all stumbling at the third paragraph or having a problem with a certain visual. 

Spread the copy around, and you'll wind up with far better copy. 

I know that we copywriters don't like the idea of "writing by committee," but that's not what I'm suggesting. The system I recommend is this: distribute your copy far and wide. Get input from as many sources as possible. But the understanding is that you, as the copywriter, will get to decide who and what to listen to. If your client and/or creative director offers input, their views are critical to the approval process, so of course you'll listen to them. But if the receptionist says something that makes no sense to you, you can safely ignore it. 

Just let as broad an audience as possible read your stuff before you mail it or run it in the newspaper. Get input from people throughout your organization on what they think is working. Ask for their views _as a potential consumer,_ not as an advertising critic, because then they'll come up with their own theories, usually off base, such as "The copy is too long, and nobody will read such long copy." 

Just ask, _"Ifyou were interested in this type of product, would this ad be persuasive to you? "_ And you'll find the most amazing and consistent points that come back to you. 

**==> picture [254 x 20] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [39 x 63] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [58 x 22] intentionally omitted <==**

## **ew Product Checklist:** 

**==> picture [18 x 13] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [14 x 9] intentionally omitted <==**

**1. Meets all criteria of** _**Bencivenga Persuasion Equation™**_ 

**==> picture [25 x 49] intentionally omitted <==**

_**2.**_ **Large base of reachable, passionate fans** 

**==> picture [25 x 36] intentionally omitted <==**

**3. Other hot products already in market** 

**==> picture [36 x 38] intentionally omitted <==**

**4. Good margins** 

**5. Repeat business potential** 

**6. Ancillary product sales potential** 

**7. Easily shippable** 

**8. Can we get an exclusive?** 

_m^mmmiMmmmmmm_ 

_-_ 182 - 

## **Let's Now Review My "New Product Checklist" for Launching Blockbusters and Avoiding Disasters** 

## First, as I've said, you should run any idea through _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®._

Imagine if someone in the Ford building had done this? Imagine if just one brave soul among all those MB As and highfalutin executives had merely asked, "Hey, guys, I hate to be the wet blanket in this meeting, but is there any evidence that people actually _want_ this thing? Where is the hue and cry for such a car?" 

Remember, this was in the 1950s, when people were getting tired of these masses of chrome and steel. By contrast, the Volkswagen Beetle got the proposition just right. They had researched the market and found that a new baby boom generation was coming of age, rebellious and disdainful of driving around a huge status symbol that burned up gas like a Sherman tank. 

The ads for Volkswagen, in effect, said, "Hey, gang, here's a cute little car that runs like a top; gets you better than 30 miles per gallon; and, best of all, shows the world that you couldn't care less about status symbols." 

Volkswagen sales exploded, while Edsel sales drove straight off the cliff. One company was in perfect sync with what the market wanted. The other was not. 

For any new product you're thinking of coming out with, make sure you check it against this _Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®_ because you will not only spare yourself from disasters, but also be much better able to build a new product with tremendous appeal right from the ground up. 

Nothing is worse than working on something for six months or a year— everyone working so hard to launch the new product—only to discover that no one wants to buy it. What a downer! 

In fact, for much of my career I refused to work on new products. Luckily I had enough business that I could take on assignments from clients where the only variable was the advertising that I would create. So I required that the product already be a success, as insurance that I wouldn't be wasting any of the few time slots that I had each year to write a new campaign. 

If the product was already a known entity, then my copy could be the only variable on which I'd be judged, and that's what I wanted, because I could control that. I can't control how a company comes up with a new product and if the market will want it or not. With a new product, the copywriter can work his or her heart out, create a brilliant campaign, but if the market rejects the product because it's flawed, you've wasted all that time and effort. That's spoken from experience, 

- 183 - 

of having clients give me a new product to launch, working my heart out, and hearing the marketplace in the end say, "No thanks." 

Some other criteria on my New Product Checklist... 

You want a large base of reachable, passionate fans. Call it a hungry school of fish, which is a good analogy. On the days when Captain John Rade goes out and can easily find a hungry school of fish, there's no better fishing. If you can just put your boat in front of a hungry school of fish, the fishing is fast, furious, and so easy, which is what you want. 

Of course, Captain John knows how to catch fish even when there are no hungry schools in sight. They call him a "meat" fisherman. He's out there not for sport or excitement, but to methodically reel in as many pounds as he can, day in and day out. And he knows how to do it on good days and bad. 

Likewise, we marketers and copywriters want to know how to sell even when conditions aren't perfect. That separates the professionals from the "weekend marketers." 

But, all factors being equal, your ideal is to find a big school of hungry fish and put your boat in front of it. You want a large base of reachable, passionate fans. 

Another criterion—it's a very good thing to see that there are other hot products already in the market. This tells you that your product, if it can deliver the same benefit in a superior, cheaper, or more convenient way, has an excellent chance. It's not good news when there's nobody succeeding with anything similar to your idea. Odds are, it _has_ been tried before and failed. Bad sign. 

Contrary to what many think, you don't want to be a pioneer. Pioneers, as you've heard, are usually found at the side of the road with arrows in their backs. The best products have ample precedents already succeeding. If you can give such an already desired product a new twist, that's your best, most predictable opportunity. Yes, sometimes someone does invent something totally new, and it takes the world by storm. But this is rare. You'll experience many more successes if you look into an already popular product category and figure out how to come up with a superior entry. You don't want to reinvent the wheel. You want to invent one that looks snazzier, rides smoother, lasts longer, etc. 

You also want good margins. In direct response, if your profit margins are razor thin because you're trying to undercut everyone else in price, you'll soon be out of the game, because someone else will soon undercut you. If you've built your product according to _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®,_ your uniqueness and exceptional proof will give you substantial pricing power over your 

- 184 - 

competitors. The greater your uniqueness and proof, the greater your pricing power, and the higher the margins you'll have to work with. 

You also want repeat business potential. We'll get into this tomorrow—how wealth is an income stream. Why go through all the effort to sell one thing when, with the same effort, you can build in an automatic revenue stream from renewals or repeat purchases? 

Quick example. For me, a newsletter is inherently more profitable than a book on the same subject. Why? Because, with the newsletter, lots of readers will renew automatically every year, creating a juicy revenue stream. With a book, you sell one and you're done. If you want to sell another, you must start from zero. It makes you feel like Sisyphus, condemned to ceaselessly push a boulder up a hill only to have it keep rolling back down. 

Similarly, ancillary product sales are very important. When you are thinking about your potential new product, ask yourself if this is a market likely to buy lots of related products. If it's a health newsletter, for example, you can sell a ton of vitamins and other supplements once they've subscribed to the newsletter. One successful new product can easily turn into 10, then 20 or more—an empire! 

All your products, ideally, should be easily shippable if you're in mail order. If it's perishable or heavy, you'll have additional obstacles to overcome. 

Can you get an exclusive? Very important. You don't want to teach your potential competitors the right way to market, only to have them imitate your advertising _and_ be able to sell the same products out from under you. Then you will have lost everything. 

Now let me take this to another level, to reveal... 

## **The Secret I Learned from Joseph Segel About Launching Blockbuster Products** 

## Satellite photo! 

Quick recap: on our New Product Checklist, the first and foremost criterion, and the foundation for all success, is whether the product idea passes the four-part test of _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®._ 

And, as you'll recall, the most important, most leveraged part of that _Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®_ is the third element, powerful proof. Why? If master closers sell on proof and if nobody buys without first believing in you, your presentation must be built on the strongest proof you can possibly present. 

## Now, imagine what a hot seller your new product can be if (1) it passes with

- 185 - 

flying colors all the criteria above, including _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®,_ and (2) rather than relying on a copywriter to add proof to your message, the product has _**powerful proof elements already built right into it!**_ 

When you can find a product like that, the ad almost writes itself and almost closes the deal. 

**==> picture [454 x 545] intentionally omitted <==**

Let me give you some examples from one of the great masters. To do that, I must tell you how I came to know him. 

Remember the ad I showed you earlier, from our cocky young bunch of writers? This ad made quite a splash when we ran it in the _New York Times,_ the _Wall Street Journal,_ and other major newspapers. As you can imagine, we got a large response from a wide variety of businesses, from wacky to wonderful. 

One was a mad chemist who invented a superpotent cesspool cleaner. Given his peculiar personality, we were reluctant to experiment with his product for fear it might blow up our company's plumbing. Another guy sold waterproof watches _packaged in water-filled plastic containers,_ so you could actually see the watch submerged in water inside the box, proving it's waterproof. 

Then there was New York City's "Button Man," so named because he supplied the Garment Center with every kind of button you could imagine. We heard from all manner of businesses we never knew existed, from the ridiculous to the sublime, including some famous Fortune 500 companies. 

The principal owner of our company, Dan Rosenthal, didn't want to go near the Fortune 500 companies. He felt that we should stick with direct marketing clients. He believed that Fortune 500 companies would be too corporate, bureaucratic, political, and ignorant of direct marketing discipline. And if we're guaranteeing our ad, we just didn't want to get stuck in the quicksand of such cultural differences. 

I, on the other hand, was very eager to take on anybody, the bigger the better. But Dan owned most of the company, so his view prevailed. Later I came to realize he was right. It would have been foolish trying to introduce direct marketing in corporate cultures that didn't appreciate or understand it. 

Among all the responses to our ad, there was one I'll never forget. 

I had just gotten back from lunch when I noticed a pink telephone message slip waiting by my phone. It said, "Joseph Segel called." 

I almost fell on the floor. You old-timers in the audience will recognize that name. Joe Segel is the legendary direct marketer who founded the Franklin Mint. He is one of the greatest entrepreneurs America has ever produced, founder of more than 20 different companies in businesses as diverse as publishing, photography, aviation, software, and hospitality. He founded the QVC shopping network. 

His Franklin Mint produced currency for third world countries. They had the silver machinery and the dyes. But their main business was collectibles. To this 

- 187 - 

day, the company sells all manner of collectibles, though Joe sold the business many years ago and is a mega-mega-millionaire. 

Anyway, Joe saw our ad and was about to start another of his new businesses. So he called me up. To me, this was like talking to the god of direct marketing, sitting on his throne atop Mount Olympus. 

He was interested in our young bunch of marketing tigers. He hired us, and I would sometimes meet him at Kennedy Airport to show him our progress before he would jet off to another of his holdings, a five-star hotel in Switzerland. 

He was brilliant, demanding, and a perfectionist, and in time I got to know his approach to marketing and finally understood what made the Franklin Mint so successful. One daffl was explaining to him that we got so many responses to our ad that we were"being selective about which products we'd agree to work on. 

As part of this process, I explained, we were looking for those products with the best proof elements, because good proof elements would make the advertising more successful and make our jobs easier, as well. 

But as good as we thought we may have been, Joseph Segel was way ahead of us. What he did with the Franklin Mint was just amazing. 

If you're a marketer, there is no point that I will tell you at this conference that could have greater profit potential than what I'm about to say. You see... 

_Joseph Segel did not go shopping merely for "new products." When Joseph Segel was on the hunt for a new blockbuster product, he shopped for proof elements!_ 

_In other words, within the product category that he found interesting, he would go looking for the proof elements that, if built into the product from the start, would make the sale very, very easy._ 

Let me give you a couple of examples from the Franklin Mint. 

Here's an ad I just noticed about a month ago, a current ad for the Franklin Mint. Again Joseph Segel, the master, sold the company long ago, but it's still riding on his coattails, on the models he put in place. 

Here is a typical ad from the current Franklin Mint. This is a replica of the Harley-Davidson Heritage Softail Classic motorcycle. 

Let's look at the formula that Joseph Segel set in place. 

When you have a collectibles company, you can create collectibles for almost anything that people are passionate about. Take Harley-Davidson motorcycles, for example. 

- 188 - 

**==> picture [443 x 494] intentionally omitted <==**

Harley-Davidson has an intensely passionate following. If you own a Harley, it's part of your identify, and you might want a replica on your desk. 

This offer reflects the business model that Joe Segel set up. The Franklin Mint can go to a company like Harley-Davidson or any other major organization with a rabid, passionate fan base and say, "We'd like to create a beautiful collectible of your product. We'll sell it and give you a cut of the royalties. You'll have approval over the design and copy, but you know it will be done in good taste because we're the Franklin Mint. 

- 189 - 

"You'll make money from the sale, and you'll also reinforce your brand with your loyal fans." 

After all, what could be better for Harley-Davidson than for their fans to have a little replica of their favorite motorcycle sitting on their desk or mantle? 

Deals like this are a no-brainer for a company like Harley-Davidson because they reinforce the bond they have with their customers. And look what it does for the Franklin Mint. They get to automatically tap into somebody else's incredibly passionate customer base without having to spend a nickel to develop that base. 

And there's something else so beautiful about what Joe Segel did. Not only did he get to tap into other people's passionate customer bases, but he also made his products knockoff-proof. 

How so? Because Harley-Davidson owns the right to its trademark. So let's say that this Franklin Mint ad selling a Harley-Davidson replica is a huge winner, and the knockoff artists start coming out of the woodwork, intent on ripping it off and selling a cheaper version of this replica. 

They can't! Not without Harley-Davidson's permission! And since the Franklin Mint will have secured, I'm sure, an exclusive with Harley-Davidson as part of their joint venture, they have effectively locked out competitors. 

Competing mints that may have larcenous thoughts of coming out with a knockoff product realize, "We can't infringe on Harley's trademark. If we do, we have to face the full force and authority of the United States government." 

Now, wouldn't you like to have the U.S. government keeping knockoff artists out of your territory? I mean, this is great! 

So in one fell swoop, what Joe Segel made possible here is that he gets an exclusive with an authorizing company, agency, or other entity that confers credibility. He taps into the preexisting fanatical fan base in that product category. And he forever locks out competition through the trademark laws. Once an ad runs and it starts pulling in responses, competitors can't breach his defenses. He's got his own "monopoly" within a very passionate base of customers. 

That's the concept. Now let's see how the master himself did it when he was running the company. 

He did it even better. In addition to all the other smart things he did, Joe Segel believed in setting up an automatic revenue stream. 

Let's look at a representative ad from the days when Joseph Segel ran the Franklin Mint... 

- 190 - 

**==> picture [558 x 297] intentionally omitted <==**

Here is a Franklin Mint ad for the 1970s, offering _**The Official Air and Space** Ingot Collection._ 

Joe Segel or someone else from the Franklin Mint might contact the officials at the National Space Institute and say something like "We'd like to honor the heroes of space and air exploration. We'd like to create 100 ingots and have you be the exclusive authorizing agency. Naturally, we'd get an exclusive. When we do this together, we'll pay all the expenses, and when it works, you'll receive a royalty." 

For the National Space Institute, it's a no-brainer. They receive extra money coming in just for authorizing something that's done in good taste and that publicizes the importance of their organizational mission. 

But look at what smart marketing this is. Right off, there are a lot of people who are very passionate about collecting anything to do with space exploration and aviation. The Franklin Mint will be offering such people beautiful silver ingots commemorating the great moments and heroes in a field they love. What a delightful collectible for these passionate fans. 

And his business model makes so much sense. He's taking a raw commodity, silver, and turning it into something special, entitling him to a splendid markup. Let's say that silver may have been selling for $5 an ounce at the time. I don't know how much these little ingots weigh, but let's guess that they're exactly an 

- 191 - 

ounce. That would mean that the Franklin Mint is buying silver at $5 an ounce and reselling it for $25 an ounce, yielding a tidy 500% built-in markup. They've also got an exclusive on this product, making it unique and untouchable by competitors, because nobody else can ever get the endorsement and authorization of the National Space Institute for a similar product. 

And now for the piece de resistance. 

The Franklin Mint includes a magnificent chest for collecting 100 ingots and displaying them to your friends. Now let's imagine you're the customer. You've bought the first ingot and you periodically go over to your display chest to admire it. You love it. Naturally, you can't stop collecting after just one. You buy a second, a third, and you keep on going. 

One month, your spouse asks, "What are you doing here? Every month you're sending money off to the Franklin Mint?" And you would say, "It's fine, honey. This is my hobby; I'm a collector." And of course, once you've gotten four or five ingots displayed in the chest, the pressure builds. Now that you've made such a substantial commitment, you're more determined than ever to complete your set. You're in for serious money already, and you just can't have a magnificent display chest with just five slots filled and 95 that are empty! 

Once you start collecting, something called the Zeigarnik Effect kicks in. 

The Zeigarnik Effect is named after the Russian psychologist who discovered that there is a powerful drive within people to _finish uncompleted tasks,_ sometimes even when it makes no sense to complete the task. The act of completion itself is a powerful, self-generating reward which can override even your own best interests to stop. Your thinking is, _"I've started this now, and I'm committed to finishing it,"_ and you keep on going, sometimes even when it makes more sense to stop. Governments, corporations, and individuals are all prone to exhibit such behavior. 

In this case, the marketing effort, especially that handsome display chest, harnesses this powerful human tendency. _"I started this and, by golly, I'm going to finish what I've started."_ 

Those who do finish will have purchased 100 ingots. So, in effect, this ad sells not just one product at $25, which in today's money would be equivalent to a sale of maybe a couple of hundred dollars. No, this ad didn't make just one $25 sale. It sold 100 coins at $25, or a total sale of $2,500 for those who completed the set, equivalent to maybe a $25,000 sale today per collector who bought them all. 

And this is the formula the Franklin Mint repeated again and again, with scores of different collectibles. In more than one way, the company minted money. 

- 192 - 

Another product might be something like 'The only collection of Norman Rockwell plates ever authorized by the _Saturday Evening Post_ and the Norman Rockwell family." 

Or perhaps the Franklin Mint might go to the royal family of England and obtain their authorization to mint "the only coin collection authorized by the royal family honoring the great monarchs of England." 

## **And Here's the Main Lesson I'm Teaching Here...** 

This marketing method, applied so deftly and profitably by Joseph Segel and The Franklin Mint, can be applied to the marketing of almost anything, not just collectibles. 

_**No matter what you sell, no matter what field you are in, there are hundreds and hundreds of groups, organizations, famous people, owners of unique processes, trusted clubs and community groups, and scores of other entities out there that you can partner with to gain much greater marketing leverage than you could ever hope to wield on your own.**_ 

_**There are scores of groups, organizations, and people with names and reputations already revered by your market and who thus possess amazingly powerful credentialing authority to give you a blockbuster product, one with magnificent built-in proof and a built-in exclusive that will prevent your competitors from knocking off your product once it's successful.**_ 

Examples: 

- _The only automobile antitheft device so effective, it s authorized by the International Association of Chiefs of Police..._ 

- _The only toothpaste endorsed by the American Dental Association..._ 

- _(Using reverse psychology): The only golf ball that flies so far, it's officially banned by both the USGA and the PGA for tournament play..._ 

- _The only cigarette so completely free of tar and nicotine, it's allowed to be advertised on the radio..._ 

- _The only complete golden oldies collection certified by the Rock and Roll Hall of Fame..._ 

- _The only camper s survival kit endorsed by the New York Explorer s Club...and so on._ 

_-_ 193 - 

**==> picture [509 x 270] intentionally omitted <==**

## **A Secret of the World's Most Successful Investor That Also Applies to Marketing** 

There's a strong connection here with the same approach used by many of the world's great investors. 

For example, the most successful investor I've ever read about is Warren Buffett. His track record, believe it or not, is 55 times better than the S&P, and this has made him one of the world's wealthiest men. 

Warren Buffett loves to invest only in companies that have _moats around them —a._ way to protect themselves against invading enemies. 

Whenever you have a success in marketing, you can bet that your competitors are watching. I know that in this room, we all watch what each other is doing. 

With a moat against competition, Buffett feels safer. He feels protected against competitors out to steal his company's lunch money. Remember, though you may not see them, competitors are always out there somewhere, silently watching you, waiting to pounce. As soon as they smell blood in the water—that you've got a success on your hands—they'll race to the scene, like hungry sharks. What can they steal? How can they seize the prize you've captured? That's when you want to know your protection is already in place. 

For example, Warren Buffett was an early major investor in Coca-Cola because there's only one Coca-Cola. He felt that Coke's "moat" consists of its internationally recognized trademark and global distribution system. The 

- 194 - 

universally recognized trademark, perhaps the most recognizable in the world, could never be taken away. And its well-established distribution system would be very difficult to duplicate. He was right on both counts and made a fortune in the stock of Coca-Cola as the company has grown over the years, protected by this doubly effective moat. 

Buffett made another fortune in Gillette, famous for razor blades. Sure, other companies make disposable razor blades, but Gillette was the first dominant player in this gigantic market. Buffett has joked for many years now that he loves waking up each morning realizing that almost half the world needs to use his product again, making him richer with every sunrise. 

Ask most men around the world to name a razor blade, and "Gillette" will be the word that pops from their lips. Such market domination and "top-of-mind" awareness throughout the world is a terrific moat, almost impossible for competitors to breach, certainly not anytime soon. 

Those are the kinds of businesses that Warren Buffett, the world's greatest investor, likes to invest in. They have moats that protect his investment and let him keep making money. You want to do the same in your marketing by building deep moats around your products. 

That's what you do when you have an exclusive on something or when you create or obtain an exclusive alliance, process, or endorsement agreement with an influential authority in your marketplace. _Never build your castle unless you also build your moat, or else you 11 look out from your tower one day and be shocked to find yourself surrounded by a powerful invader._ 

## **Another Key Lesson...** 

Building a deep moat around your business—by virtue of having an exclusive—is one of the most important lessons we can learn from Joe Segel's example. 

Another is this—one of the smartest ways to lock in a high response rate right from the inception of a new product launch is to _**build a powerful proof element right into your product itself and then feature it in your headline.**_ 

This is a very powerful formula for success. Let me give you a current example... 

- 195 - 

**==> picture [416 x 338] intentionally omitted <==**

As soon as I saw the title of this book, I knew it would be a best seller, just based on the title alone. It embodies exactly the formula for success we've just talked about. 

The title is _**French Women Don't Get Fat.**_ Its headline features, with such lovely economy, both the proof element ("French women") and the benefit ("don't get fat"). To see how important that proof element is, try removing it. You're left with a title _**"Don't Get Fat."**_ Nothing there at all! Such a headline would be a dismal failure. 

But put that little proof element back in, and the headline explodes in reader interest. It does everything a great headline should: it combines a strong benefit with powerful proof of that benefit. 

Sure enough, after it was out awhile, this book stormed up the best-seller list and is now one of the hottest sellers across the nation. 

That simple little proof element is so persuasive. Why? If you've ever been to France or Italy, or seen movies of people in Europe, you can't help but notice that the people there _love_ good food, but they're not fat like we Americans are. And it's not because they starve themselves or live on tofu and celery. 

## The French love their quiche, their cream sauces, their croissants, their wine

- 196 - 

and pastries. So this title is very intriguing. "What is their secret?" It thus combines another powerful headline element, curiosity. _"How do they do it? "_ 

Then the subhead reinforces the idea that you're not going to have to sacrifice and starve yourself to lose weight, as so many people assume when they hear about yet another diet plan. Instead, this wonderful subhead promises, _"The Secret of Eating for Pleasure."_ 

Ah, what a beautiful promise for so many American women who've tried and failed so many diets. "I can be slim like French women not by starving and punishing myself, but by _increasing_ my eating pleasure!" What a deft touch, to remove the usual objection to yet another new diet. "I don't want to strain and punish myself on another diet again. It's too hard." 

Study this book title. Memorize it. Follow its example whenever you try to write a headline, by combining a strong promise with a potent proof element. 

Here's another example, not a headline, but a strategy that works like gangbusters... 

**==> picture [296 x 220] intentionally omitted <==**

This is a strategy used by an extremely successful life insurance company in **Japan.** _**Life insurance sold by widows.**_ 

You family men, picture this. You've sent away for a free atlas or a free address book or some other nice gift you saw offered in your favorite magazine. In the evening, after dinner, your doorbell rings. Standing there is a demure Japanese woman, holding a package. She says, "Kind sir, I was sent by my company to deliver your free gift. They've asked me to talk with you for just a 

- 197 - 

few minutes. I hope that is OK, as I am a widow. My husband did not believe in life insurance. I make ends meet by working for the insurance company that sends your gift. I'd like to tell the company that you let me talk with you for just a few minutes, no obligation. May I come in?" 

You'd have to be Simon Legree to slam the door in that struggling widow's face! 

## _[LAUGHTER]_ 

Why is this strategy so compelling? It's because that magical ingredient, _**a powerful proof element,**_ is built right into the presentation. In this case, it is the _foundation_ of the presentation. The presenter is a widow of a husband who died and left her unprotected. That is how to sell with extraordinary effectiveness. 

**==> picture [279 x 304] intentionally omitted <==**

Here is a direct mail package, a magalog, created for KCI by the great copywriter Jim Rutz. 

As you probably know, Lou Rukeyser has been a famous TV personality for decades. He's built a reputation as America's most trusted financial journalist. 

- 198 - 

Realizing this, someone from KCI, the financial newsletter publisher, approached Lou through a mutual friend, Jim Blanchard, to see if he'd be interested in writing a newsletter. Lo and behold, Rukeyser said yes. 

Since Lou and his TV show, _Wall Street Week_ on PBS, had been coming into the living rooms of American investors for 30 years or so every Friday night, he was like a trusted member of millions of households. 

KCI knew the great trust that Rukeyser enjoyed among investors and realized this was a vastly _underutilized marketing asset,_ an awesome proof element they could build a product around. 

I know the folks at KCI, and after this campaign by Jim Rutz was running awhile, I asked, "How did you guys do with it?" 

Brian Smith was in charge of KCI then, and I was working with him on other projects, though not this one. Brian said, "Gary, on our other newsletters we work hard to sell, but this one was so easy. It was like on those tuna boats, where you're in the middle of the school and just hauling fish over the rail as soon as your hook hits the ocean. That's what this was like." 

Mail trucks kept pulling up to the company headquarters with sacks and sacks of envelopes full of orders and checks. This was the first time Lou Rukeyser offered a newsletter, one where he would ask all of his personal contacts on Wall Street for their best investment recommendations. 

So this was an undervalued proof element just waiting to be harnessed, and the results were enormously profitable. This is how you should approach any new product ideas, asking, "How can we harness proof elements that will explode the success of this product?" 

In other words, don't just shop for new products. New products are a dime a dozen. You'll enjoy much greater success when you _shop for proof elements, not just products._ 

**==> picture [209 x 153] intentionally omitted <==**

_-_ 199- 

Satellite photo! 

OK, let's review. Here are two rules to remember. 

_First, a powerful proof element is a blockbuster product waiting to be born._ If you see someone with a great reputation in his or her marketplace or who has a fantastic credential for some other reason, you are looking at a blockbuster product waiting to be born. 

_Second, you can see these opportunities all around you, once you start to look!_ 

We have to remind ourselves to be on the lookout, or else you'll walk right past these diamonds on the street. 

Let me prove it to you. This morning, all of you saw one of these opportunities. Did you realize it? I showed it to you! 

**==> picture [436 x 236] intentionally omitted <==**

Do you remember Captain John Rade? That newspaper article is a mighty proof element, heralding a product waiting to be born! 

As you can see from this article about him, Captain John is getting to an age when his back must hurt from pulling in all these fish. His kind of fishing is tough, physical labor. He might very well be open to another source of income. 

Imagine if someone went to him and said, "Captain John, you've been written up in the newspaper as the greatest rod and reel fisherman on the East End of Long Island, a world-famous fishing capital. We'd like to create a mail order video about your fishing secrets. You'll receive an author's royalty, 12 percent of sales. Yet you won't have to write a word! We'll have someone go out with you 

200 - 

- 

on your boat with a video camera. As you catch these different species of fish, you just talk and tell us your fishing secrets. A lot of fishermen would love to know. If you're open to this and would give us an exclusive on your fishing secrets, we'll take care of all the details, take all the risk, and pay you a royalty if our mail order product works. It won't cost you a penny, yet you could receive very substantial royalties for the rest of your life if our idea works. What do you say?" 

If I were Captain John, that would sound like an almost irresistible proposition to me. If you strike a deal, you would then sell the video and of course be sure to send Captain John his fair royalties. By being scrupulously fair and honest, you'll build a reputation as someone who can be trusted, and many other doors will open for you in the future. Screw people out of their royalties, or in any other way, and you'll screw yourself even more, as your reputation will likewise shut off many opportunities for you in the future. 

Now if you're successful with your first fishing secrets video, you can make other videos, either with Captain John or other famous fishermen who've been recognized as the best in other parts of the country. In other words, you'll apply the same principle— _**you '11 shop for proof elements in different pockets within your market!**_ 

You'll research the field and find other fishermen with fabulous _**underutilized proof elements,**_ such as winners of major fishing tournaments, record-holders of the biggest or most fish caught in a given species, etc. You could even create a whole library of these videos, "Secrets of Master Fishermen," and organize it by species—one video on how to catch striped bass, another on how to catch mako shark, another on how to catch channel catfish, etc. 

There are scores of people out there like Captain John, in all markets, who don't know a thing about mail order marketing, yet are sitting on wildly impressive, _undervalued proof elements,_ just waiting for somebody to come along and cash in on their knowledge and credentials, take care of all the marketing details for them, and then send them royalties on sales. 

And, in the case of Captain John, since he's been written up in the _East Hampton Star_ as the greatest rod and reel fisherman on the East End of Long Island, a mecca of legendary fishing captains, you've got a proof element that nobody can ever steal from you. Sure, when potential competitors see that you're running ads all over for Captain John's fishing secrets, larceny will stir in their hearts, and they'll want to steal your market in any way they can. 

But what can they do? _Only you_ will own the exclusive with Captain John, and _only he_ was written up in the newspaper as the East End's best fisherman. 

- 201 - 

How can they steal any of that? They can't! 

These opportunities, these undervalued proof elements waiting for someone to cash in on their marketing value, are all over, as you'll notice once you start to look for them. 

A few years ago Dr. Andrew Weil, a great practitioner of alternative medicine, was featured on the cover of _Time._ 

**==> picture [428 x 304] intentionally omitted <==**

Dr. Weil was educated at the prestigious Harvard Medical School, so he's got excellent credentials in the conventional medical world. He's definitely not some kook who calls himself a doctor merely because he earned a degree in massage therapy from a correspondence school with a PO box in New Mexico. He's a brilliant and highly respected MD. 

And yet he's _also_ a champion—and relentless researcher—of effective, proven _natural and folk remedies_ that most conventional MDs have no idea about. 

So in the world of physicians, he's a broad-shouldered Atlas who holds up two worlds—the best of _both_ conventional _and_ alternative medical cures. 

Because of this, _Time_ featured a cover story about him. 

When I saw that cover, I begged my clients who publish health newsletters or books to sign him up to write something. Or since he publishes a health newsletter 

- 202 - 

himself, they could acquire his newsletter and pay him to remain as editor. Oh, what we could do with a cover on _Time_ as a credential! He has _no idea_ of the marketing value he's sitting on! 

In effect, _Time_ magazine had just awarded him their "seal of approval" as America's foremost doctor who knows both conventional and alternative healing. 

As it turned out, Dr. Weil wasn't interested in selling his newsletter to a major publisher, so my pleas went unfulfilled. That's a shame, as he didn't know enough about marketing to realize what a gold mine this _Time_ cover is, and I've never seen it in any of his marketing. 

If you watch for these kinds of things, you'll see them almost every couple of weeks. They are like large, undiscovered gold deposits hiding in plain sight and waiting to be mined by someone who recognizes the value of strong proof elements. Most people, even brilliant, accomplished people, don't recognize these marketing opportunities, and so they are wasted. 

Remember, people are inundated by so much marketing, they need to rely on shorthand methods to process it all. If a trusted, independent source like _Time_ proclaims that Andrew Weil is such a big deal in medicine that he deserves his own cover story in _Time,_ that's all the proof many health-conscious people need to conclude, "I should find out what this doctor recommends for various ailments. Maybe one of his natural folk remedies can cure my arthritis or give a ray of hope to my uncle Fred who just learned he has pancreatic cancer. Maybe Dr. Weil can point me toward a cure we are desperate to find for someone we love, a cure from the alternative healing world that even the best conventional MDs know nothing or little about." 

So this _Time_ cover story becomes a convenient shorthand for people, a shorthand that cuts through all the usual need to investigate and evaluate if this man makes sense or not. He's featured on the cover of _Time?_ Shorthand message—he's got to be good, and that's all the proof I need. _Just tell me where I can find out what he says about a disease that's threatening someone in our family and I'm ready to plunk my money down._ 

As I say, these priceless proof elements can come from almost anywhere. Look at what Crest did in its early days. 

Crest was one of the first toothpastes to include fluoride. In 1960 Procter & Gamble, the makers of Crest, won the endorsement of the American Dental Association that Crest does indeed fight cavities. This made an enormous difference in their fortunes, as Crest, on the strength of this proof element, grabbed market share hand over fist. 

203 - 

- 

Today the American Dental Association has endorsed not only Crest, but some 1,300 other products. But in those early days, Crest had this proof element all to itself and ran with it like a burglar. 

For a time, their packaging said something like "The only toothpaste ever endorsed by the American Dental Association." On the strength of this proof element, and advertising that made hay with it, Crest became the number-oneselling toothpaste in the country. 

Once you start looking for these proof elements, they will present themselves to you. 

For example, in _Time_ magazine not long ago, I saw that memory expert Harry Lorraine was described as... 

## **"The Yoda of memory training."** 

Now that's a headline waiting to go somewhere, waiting to sell something. Your headline is already written for you—by _Time_ magazine, which has a lot more credibility than _you_ or / will ever possess! 

Here is one of my favorite direct mail packages. 

**==> picture [454 x 256] intentionally omitted <==**

For many years, I enjoyed an unbroken string of control packages for Hume Publishing's _Successful Investing and Money Management Course._ 

## I seemed to have a knack for selling this course, and even against very stiff

- 204 - 

competition, I was the only one who could beat my own control for about a decade and a half, the entire time I worked on this product. Over that time, against numerous other packages by other top writers, I came up with five or six new control packages, increasing the response each time. 

At one point, although I kept coming up with control after control, I thought I had run out of ideas. I was coming up empty. When that happens, your best remedy is to dig deeper into researching the product, or some aspect of it, to find something you overlooked before. 

That's what I did. I started looking at their board of directors and found that on the board was somebody named Dr. Morton Shulman, a wealthy Canadian who, in addition to contributing his expertise to Hume's investing course, was a medical doctor, a self-made millionaire, and financial book author. 

I investigated further and discovered that he had written a book that was a major best seller called _Anyone Can Make a Million._ 

In its day, it was a runaway best seller, but that was a while ago and the book, to my knowledge, was no longer in print. 

But, hey, a great book title can be a great headline for an ad as well. It's a proven entity, so why let it go to waste? 

I went to Hume and said, "On your board is Morton Shulman. If you give him a little extra royalty, would he allow us to use the title of his book as a headline on our direct mail package? Better yet, could we use his photo as well? Better still, I could interview him by phone, get his story of how he became a successful investor and why he'd now like to help others to get rich through this investment course. What do you think?" 

The people at Hume loved the idea and asked Shulman, who was quite willing to do whatever we asked, as long as he could approve the letter he'd be signing. 

So here we have another perfect example of a fantastic undervalued proof element just waiting to be harnessed to help sell a product. And this one came with a terrific, proven headline from a best-selling book. 

I wrote the letter, Morton Shulman signed it, and it was so persuasive that it just blew the doors off everything else we had ever tested. 

As good as those earlier packages were, this ran one away with sales because it was so loaded with good proof elements. First and foremost, Morton Shulman was a self-made investment multimillionaire. Yes, he's a doctor and he admitted that you might assume that doctors make a lot of money and that's how he got rich. But, no, he really made the big money by learning how to invest. 

- 205 - 

And because he was taken advantage of when he was a fledgling investor—by brokers and others who did not have his best interests at heart—he agreed to join Ron Hume and a group of other self-made millionaires who wish to share their knowledge with new investors starting out. He emphasized that this group sells no investments, only their hard-won knowledge as successful self-made millionaires and how _**anybody can make a million**_ by faithfully following their principles. They will be your mentors. 

Now, this great, proven headline— _**Anyone Can Make a Million—** was_ just sitting there on the shelf, waiting for anyone to come along, blow off the dust, and reuse it. Likewise for Morton Shulman. He was in the background of this product all along, but nobody had asked him to tell his story or lend his credentials to our direct mail effort. These proof elements are what you must look for if you seek outsized response. 

## **The "Hot Hand" Strategy** 

OK, another whole concept for harnessing great proof elements is what I call the _Hot Hand Strategy._ 

I was having so much success helping publishers make a lot of money, I thought that maybe I should publish a newsletter myself. If I were to publish a newsletter, I'd create it to be the perfect product for a copywriter to sell. I'd build it from the ground up to be loaded with promotional potential. For me, as you now know, that means that it would harness awesome proof elements. 

So I decided to create an investment newsletter built strictly on the undervalued, unleveraged proof elements of the best investors in the world, and I would cash in on those proof elements. 

My concept was to interview, each month, the most respected and hottest performing investors, and then feature all their proof elements in my advertising. I would be using them and their proof elements to sell my newsletter, and it would be good for them, too, because they all like publicity. 

- 206 - 

**==> picture [499 x 273] intentionally omitted <==**

You can apply this same approach to virtually any field. Since I was familiar with investing, I created this newsletter, called _Superstars of Investing._ The first fellow I interviewed, Al Frank, had awesome proof elements. He was written up in other publications as "America's top-performing stock-picker of the decade." 

And it followed, as night follows day, of course, that I wrote a headline that jumped on this proof element. _"America's #1 stock-picker of the decade names 'My favorite stocks most likely to double.'"_ 

When you shop for great proof elements as your first step in marketing, the copy writes itself. In this case, I didn't have to strain and get my bowels in a knot to come up with a powerful headline. It was already written for me. The preexisting proof elements did all the hard work: Al Frank was already crowned "America's top-performing stock-picker" by other independent rating services. All I did was interview him and, in so doing, captured _his_ underutilized proof elements to sell _my_ newsletter. 

He was happy to let me do this, because even a brilliant stock-picker like Al Frank had no idea of how to market his services and was quite pleased to capture whatever free publicity he could. It was a win-win for both of us. 

Here's another issue of my newsletter, based on proof elements... 

- 207 - 

**==> picture [503 x 242] intentionally omitted <==**

When you have a product like this that allows you to go out and grab other people's undervalued proof elements, you gain another supreme advantage. It's a very _flexible_ product that lets you shift direction on a dime. 

For example, while I was doing this, gold prices soared and became a red-hot topic of conversation among investors. Great! All I had to do was find the most respected gold bug and interview him. That man's name was James McKeever. No less an authority than Lou Rukeyser had recently described him on national TV as follows: _"In the poker game of newsletter writers, McKeever is the man with the golden arm."_ McKeever's predictions about gold, and the specific gold investments that would profit most, had been the most profitable of any gold specialist, rising some 168 percent in the previous 36 months, as verified by independent rating services. 

Now if just I were hailing McKeever as a guru to heed, readers would dismiss it as hype. "Yeah, sure. You're just blowing smoke, hyping your own claims so I'll be more impressed with your newsletter." But when highly respected, independent, and eminently trusted sources hail this man as the top-performing gold investor at a time when gold is soaring and everyone wants to know how to profit, what do you have? Right—a blockbuster proof element! 

Investors just gobbled up my newsletter, because the proof elements were so strong. 

Here's another... 

- 208 - 

**==> picture [442 x 240] intentionally omitted <==**

I discovered that James Dines had been hailed by the independent _Hulbert s Financial Digest_ as "One of the five greatest investment advisors of all time." 

_Excellent!_ What a great proof element! Now, if I can just get an interview with James Dines, I can cash in on that awesome credential to sell my product. Do you see the idea here? This is what you want to create, a product that is a "shell" that does not rely on _your_ credentials, but that allows you to go out and grab _anyone else's_ proof elements in the field you're covering. And within that field, the greatest authorities, those with fantastic, undervalued proof elements, usually welcome the free publicity you'll give them, because they know so little about marketing. 

In effect, their proof elements are just there for anybody smart enough to create a shell, a product shell, that allows you to go out, interview them, get the best information from these best people, and cash in on all their underutilized proof elements—the people with the highest levels of trust, loyalty, and respect in your market. 

I'm not the only one who realizes this. I know that some of you already do this. Joe Polish is here, and Joe has a great Genius Network where he interviews brilliant marketers each month. I _love_ Joe's interviews and always learn from them. 

Ken McCarthy is here. He has the great System Club, where each month, Ken interviews today's leading Internet marketers. It is _impossible_ to listen to any of Ken's interviews and not learn a host of new tricks and techniques for selling more online. 

- 209 - 

Tomorrow I'll mention some of the resources I find most valuable in marketing, including the best products from several of the marketing gurus here at this conference. 

But what I want you to understand is that this is a very big megaconcept. If you sell information, you must think of which people in your field have blockbuster proof elements. In other words, just like the Franklin Mint, _**don't just** shop for "a new product." Shop for blockbuster proof elements!_ 

Do that and all the rest of your marketing will fall into place so much more easily. 

And, as I mentioned before, such a product, built on the proof elements of others, is very flexible. 

Let's say, for example, that I am creating a direct mail package or space ad to sell my _Superstars of Investing_ newsletter. I could create a very compelling package or ad around any of these monthly interviews. Let's say that I've created a very successful campaign around this theme: subscribe to my newsletter, and **I'll** send you, as your free welcome bonus, our complete interview with Al Frank, independently rated as America's greatest stock-picker. In this interview, he'll reveal the stocks that he believes are most likely to double or triple in the next two to four years, including...(foliowed by enticing descriptions, though not the names, of Al Frank's favorite stocks). 

OK, so let's assume that this offer is working great. But, as with all control packages, after I mail it a lot to investors, it starts to fatigue. No problem! With this type of "shell" product—one that gives me a whole new hot premium, with awesome proof elements and therefore a hot new potential premium _every month,_ I've got 12 interviews a year from which to create a hot new space ad or direct mail campaign. Of course, this would work even better online, where you don't have the long lead time of printing and mailing that direct mail requires. 

So if my campaign for Al Frank, or James Dines, starts to falter, I've got lots of other newsletter issues with superstars of investing, and any one of them can quickly jump into the breach and give me a new control package. 

- 210 - 

**==> picture [442 x 217] intentionally omitted <==**

## **The Al Davis Attitude, Applied to Direct Marketing** 

OK, here's another _very_ powerful strategy. The Oakland Raiders have a reputation as the Hells Angels of football, embodied most infamously in their heyday of about 20 years ago. At that time, they were feared and revered throughout the NFL as a big, bad bunch of dominating players. They got their jollies knocking opponents flat.
