They had muscles out to there and even if they could run around you, they'd rather run _over_ you. They gloried in their rebels' role, this macho, Hells Angels image as the bad-boy pirates of pro football. 

**==> picture [530 x 202] intentionally omitted <==**

The owner of the team, Al Davis, also embodied this attitude and encouraged it among his players. He liked his players to feel like the biggest, baddest dudes in the league, to flaunt a devil-may-care, "we-can-kick-anybody's-butt" attitude. 

One year, the Raiders made it all the way to the Super Bowl. During the pregame hoopla, Al Davis, who was as defiant as his players, held a press conference. 

Reporters usually ask the same questions at these press conferences. One reporter asked Al Davis, "So I guess at the big game, you're gonna take what the defense gives you, huh?" That's the typical strategy in most football games. Your team takes whatever opportunities the defense allows. You normally try to exploit your opponent's vulnerabilities, and this is described as "taking what the defense gives you." 

Well, Al Davis glared at this reporter and shot back a line that I think every copywriter and marketer should always remember. He said... 

## **"We don't take what the defense gives us. We take what we want."** 

This was a very macho response, but as copywriters, we can learn volumes from it. Let me explain... 

I can't tell you how many times I've had a successful mailing or ad because I just didn't take what the client gave me. 

I said well, how can we improve this? What you have is a bit dry, a little academic. I'm not going to just accept this as the best I can promote. 

How can we enhance this product or offer to "take what we want?" 

In other words, if I had a magic wand and a blank check, and did _not_ just accept the product and offer on the table, what could we create that would make our market swoon with excitement? Let's imagine what our most exciting product, offer, and premium could possibly be and then see if we can in fact create it." 

If you're a copywriter and you're dissatisfied, bored, or otherwise unimpressed with the product you've been asked to sell, the worst mistake you can make is to say, "OK, well, I guess I've got to make the best of this hash." Don't think that way! At the very least—even if you can't change the basic product, which you often can!—you can almost always make dramatic improvements in the offer and premium. 

Start off like Al Davis. Imagine that you don't have to take what's given and, instead, _can take whatever you want!_ 

## Open your mind, turn on the faucets of your thinking, and ask your client to

- 212 - 

brainstorm about how you can vastly improve the attractiveness of what you're presenting to your consumer. 

What would it take to make it _irresistible?_ Here's my favorite way to do this... 

## **The Premium Is Your Most Potent and Flexible Secret Weapon** 

One of the very first secrets I learned from my first copy chief was that if your premium is unique and strong enough, people will often _buy the product just to get the premium._ This was an astonishing revelation, and time and time again I've found it to be true. 

It sounds ridiculous, I know, but if you can come up with a dazzling premium, one that enflames your prospects' desire, they will often buy the product just to get their hands on that red-hot premium. They will justify the purchase with whatever rationalization they choose, just to quench the desire you've created for the free premium. 

This insight gives you a tremendous new flexibility to sell anything, because while you might not have much ability to change the product, you can almost always create whatever blockbuster premium you desire and start your ad by selling _that!_ 

In effect, you can reverse the usual psychology of describing the product first and foremost and then the premium as an afterthought. You can, if you do it right, focus on this fantastic premium first and then sell the product, perhaps not as an "afterthought," but as the second item to feature in your ad. If the product is halfway decent, many of your prospects will agree to buy it _to justify the sheer lust they harbor for the free premium you 're offering._ 

This enables you to take the Al Davis attitude to new heights in your advertising, because it means that you never have to "take what you're given" and instead can "take what you want," because you are usually free to suggest whatever premium you can imagine. 

This is why your premium can be your most important secret weapon. Let me give you an example... 

A financial newsletter published in Switzerland once hired me to sell subscriptions. 

I reviewed their newsletter issues and frankly found it rather dry—boring stuff about international economic trends, currency valuations, import-export figures, etc. _B-o-r-i-n-g,_ at least to me and, I suspected, the mass market of American 

- 213 - 

investors. The publisher was quite entrepreneurial, so rather than decline the assignment outright, I told him of my reservations and said he must make his publication more relevant to the needs and interests of American investors. He was all ears and promised he would. But couldn't we get started promoting the newsletter _now_ with an exciting offer, while he revamped his editorial coverage? 

We put our heads together and applied the Al Davis technique. 

Let's _take what we want._ What would make American investors jump on an offer from a newsletter published in Switzerland? 

This was in the early 1980s, when, if you were well-to-do, the IRS was taking much more of your income. The highest bracket at the time was not 36 percent. It was 90 percent! Moreover, the litigation boom was under way, and many people felt the need to keep their assets private from lawsuit-happy lawyers. 

On top of all this, inflation was rampant and gold was rising sharply. So my client came up with this offer... 

**==> picture [473 x 203] intentionally omitted <==**

This offer was mostly a premium sell, and the premium was something that our prospects went absolutely wild for. 

In the letter, the editor said, "We're a newsletter based in Switzerland, with relationships with all the major Swiss banks. To show you how easy private banking can be, to show how easily you can protect your assets against greedy lawyers, vindictive ex-spouses, and all others who may want to grab your assets, we'll get you started with your own private numbered Swiss bank account, and we'll even deposit an ingot of pure gold in it to get you started. There's absolutely 

- 214 - 

nothing illegal about it, as we'd never advise you to do anything illegal. This is all free, our gift to you for sampling our newsletter. So don't send us any money except for the newsletter itself. I will set up, on your behalf, your own secret numbered Swiss bank account, and I'll put your first little drop of gold bullion in for you, then send you all the details on how you can keep it going if you so desire." 

This thing just went through the roof. It was so much stronger than if we had just tried to sell the newsletter itself, about dry economic trends and currency exchange rates. Legions of people wanted to get that secret bank account opened, and they responded in droves. 

This is all related to what I call... 

## **The "Magic Wand" Technique** 

Never be limited by what you're given. Always start your assignments by imagining that you have a magic wand. You can wave this magic wand at any time to imagine a product or premium that your market will go wild for. That's how to start your assignment, by imagining the most exciting possibilities that will drive your market wild. 

This cuts across all fields. I don't care if you're a dentist or someone who sells roofing. Whatever it is, these techniques don't work just for the clients I'm describing. They work for any product, any service, in any market. 

For example, let's say that you sell golf clubs. 

You might think, "I could never get an endorsement from Tiger Woods." OK, you probably couldn't. But look at this... 

**==> picture [339 x 215] intentionally omitted <==**

Here's a paperback book you can buy for a couple of bucks, featuring Tiger Woods' best tips on how to improve your golf game. You can package it with your golf clubs and give it away as a premium, thus, in effect, having Tiger Woods help you sell your golf clubs for a tiny fraction of what you'd have to pay him to be your spokesman, assuming he'd even agree to be your spokesman, which he probably wouldn't unless you could pay him millions. This is a way to "hire" him for pennies on the dollar. 

Usually you can buy a book like this in volume directly from the publisher. Here's how. Just look up the publisher's name on one of the book's early pages. Contact the company and ask for the "special sales department." Or sometimes it's called the "premium and special sales department." If you tell the person who answers that you'd like to buy the Tiger Woods book in bulk to give it away as a premium, they'll be happy to accommodate, and you won't even need Tiger Woods' permission. The publisher owns the rights to the book, and they are authorized to let you buy it as a premium. 

If you buy it in bulk to give it away as a premium, you have, in effect, hired Tiger Woods for mere pennies to help you sell your golf clubs. Indeed, you can normally get 40 or 50 percent off a book like this when you buy it in bulk to use it as a premium. 

In this way, you can package Tiger Woods' tips with your golf clubs and have all of his status, all of his prestige, not to mention his universally recognized image on the book cover, all of it working to help you sell your product. This is very easy to do. 

You might wonder, what if Tiger Woods complains and says, "I never endorsed your golf clubs"? Well, first, this has never happened to me when using this strategy. Tiger Woods, like all other authors, wants to sell books too. Second, you're not saying anywhere in your copy that he's endorsing your clubs. That would be a no-no. You're just giving his book away as a premium, and you've gotten permission from his publisher to do so. 

Nobody's ever challenged me on this or had a problem with it. You can attach anybody famous to your product in this way, as long as they've written a book. You can, in this way, enhance your offer with their image and all their personal glow that then rubs off on what you're selling. 

As always, you should have a good product and treat your customers well, because if you start doing something fraudulent, someone at the publisher or in the government will get very upset, pull the plug, blow the whistle, and start putting you out of business. 

- 216 - 

Run a good, clean operation and serve your customers well, and this strategy can let you hire the most famous people in your market for a very small price. And when you do give away their book, always remember to romance it in the body copy by writing very enticing bullets about what's revealed in the book. That will spark your prospects' interest in both the premium and, by extension, your product, as they spend more time reading the copy not only about the book but about your product as well. 

So this is all part of this Al Davis attitude of _taking not just what's given, but taking what you want._ Always, always, always start your assignment by letting your imagination roam and seeing what you could possibly do to sweeten your offer with something that will make your market go wild. 

## **How to Name Your Product** 

Claude Hopkins gave some good rules for naming a product. He liked the product name to have a benefit. For example, my own company's name, Accountable Advertising, Inc. 

When I went out on my own, I was given advice from the famous copywriter and ad agency boss, Len Reiss, who succeeded Vic Schwab at the Schwab & Beatty agency. 

Len told me I should use my own name when I hung out my shingle, as clients always like dealing with the person whose name is on the front door. 

But I wasn't known at that time and "Bencivenga" is a hard name to pronounce if you're not used to it, so I followed Hopkins' advice and came up with my company name, which we still have to this day. Direct response is a very _accountable_ form of advertising, so that's what I named my company. 

_Kleenex_ is a nice name. It has the concept of _clean_ right in the name. 

I love the name _Swiffer,_ the dust picker-upper that reaches under the coffee table and catches all the dust bunnies. 

_Mr. Clean_ is a great product name. 

_Fat Blaster, Wonder Bread, Mr. Peanut, Vein Away_ —great names. 

Recently, while driving, we passed a dentist's office in the Hamptons where we live and his sign said, _Gentle Dental._ 

## **The True Story of "Painless Parker"** 

Speaking of dentists, have you ever heard of "Painless Parker"? 

## Back in the early 1900s, there lived a famous dentist and flamboyant pitchman

- 217 - 

named Edgar R. R. "Painless" Parker. He looked and dressed like Colonel Sanders and behaved like P. T. Barnum. 

He wore a top hat and a necklace made of teeth he pulled from his patients' heads. To attract legions of patients, if you can imagine this, he crisscrossed the countryside with a traveling-circus-road-show-and-dental-clinic. 

He would travel from town to town with his circus-road-show-and-dentalclinic in tow, proclaiming _"Painless Dentistry!"_ 

He'd set up his dentist's chair on the bed of a horse-drawn wagon parked in the center of town and hire a brass band to play as he pulled teeth. The twin purpose of the brass band was to attract a crowd and drown out the moans of patients, who had been plied with whisky and other pain-reducing concoctions. 

For his outrageous antics, he earned the righteous wrath of his fellow dentists. The American Dental Association denounced him as "a menace to the dignity of the profession" and vowed to put an end to his promotions, which attracted legions of patients who flocked to him for "painless dentistry." 

The dental establishment hatched a strategy. They accused him of false advertising since he claimed that his dentistry was _painless,_ and no dentistry could be truly called "painless." If they could legally deny him the right to promote "painless" dentistry, they'd severely undercut demand for his services. _Oh, yeah! The big boys had him now!_ 

But the wily pitchman left them gnashing their teeth. He legally changed his first name to "Painless," and they couldn't do a thing. Emboldened, "Painless Parker" opened a chain of some 30 West Coast dental offices, all featuring his ingenious name, employing over 70 dentists and grossing $3 million per year, a fortune in his day. 

_Never underestimate the power of a good name!_ 

_A_ new product I recently saw is called _Bites._ They're bite-size oatmeal and jam treats from Quaker Oats. What a splendid little name, _Bites._ I read that they're already selling 5 million _Bites_ a day, they're so successful. 

Another name I love is _Blue Blockers,_ the sunglasses that block out the sun's harmful blue rays. This is Joe Sugarman's product, and I'm sure you've seen his great infomercials. By the way, Joe was going to try to make it here but had other commitments. Anything you can find written by Joe is something you should jump on. 

## Another good name: for a car window shade, _Way Cool._

## I also like this one for pieces of weather stripping that you can easily put

- 218 - 

## under your door in the winter to block out drafts— _Draft Dodgers._

If you're well known in your field, you can name your product after your own name, as did Ransom Eli Olds with his _Oldsmobile_ and also his company, Reo. 

Louis Rukeyser did the same thing with his newsletter, _Louis Rukeyser 's Wall Street,_ which quickly became the most successful launch in the history of investment advisory publishing. 

_Health & Healing,_ the newsletter from Healthy Directions, is a great name. 

We saw another one as we were driving past a paint store. The sign said, _Fresh Coat Paint Store,_ and it immediately planted the idea of how nice a room or house looks with a fresh coat of paint. 

## For an oven cleaner— _Easy Off._

I am throwing these out, and you should start your own collection. Make it a game, a hobby, to collect great product names, and they will inspire you to greatness when you need one yourself. 

Now, for a little humor, I'd like to share some bad names that I've collected over the years. 

Pauline's brother Barry, bless him, always wanted to do things his own way. He decided to start a wedding videotaping service. I tried to help him with the name, but he insisted on _Down The Tubes Video._ When brides got his business card, they called someone else. 

## For a steakhouse we saw up in Maine, _The Bum Steer._

## For a luncheonette, _The Greasy Spoon._

Here's one that's interesting. The _Personal Finance Newsletter_ was originally called the _Inflation Survival Letter._ When that publication was founded by Bob Kephart, the _Inflation Survival Letter_ was a great name because inflation was the dominant economic trend. 

But when inflation calmed way down, the company was smart enough to change the name to _Personal Finance,_ which made it less vulnerable to changes in the economy. Had they had stuck with the old name when inflation was no longer a problem, subscriptions would have plummeted. 

This is another of my favorites, seeing as how so many bright lights seem to gather in car companies. When General Motors came out years ago with the Nova car, they did it with great fanfare, promoting it not only in the U.S., but in Spanish speaking countries, as well. But in Spanish, "No va" means "It doesn't go!" General Motors goofed again recently with its new hybrid car. They called it 

- 219 - 

the _High Wire._ The car is supposed to be an example of modern high-tech wizardry, but somehow consumers just remembered the word _haywire._ 

Finally, there was the company that created a pool additive to keep your pool clean. It could have been _Pool Life._ But in a triumph of cleverness over common sense, they eliminated the middle 'L' and combined the two words. The result was _Poolife._ One consumer's reaction: "I don't want anything named 'poo' going into my family pool!" 

## — _End of Session_ #5— 

- 220 - 

**==> picture [486 x 216] intentionally omitted <==**

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ With the ideas that you've been given now, you already know so much more than many of the best people in traditional direct marketing. These concepts are very advanced, based on many, many thousands of split-run tests. 

Now let's get into some street smart copywriting secrets. When I was a young copywriter, looking to advance myself, I wanted to work with the best copy masters in the city. That's one great thing about New York. Anything you want to learn, there's a master at hand who can teach you, _if you_ can get under his or her wing. There are also so many opportunities to learn from the great people here. You can go to a lecture by a brilliant psychiatrist on Monday, a talk by a famous artist on a Tuesday, and so on. It's a great city. It brings together many of the best people in their professions from throughout the world. I always considered it a blessing to live here and was determined not let such assets go to waste. 

At one point I was aiming to get into the same agency as John Caples and, later, with David Ogilvy. Somewhere along the line, I remember wanting to land a job at the direct response agency of Tom Collins. 

Tom Collins is a fantastic, legendary copywriter and creative director. I believe he's still around. I think he writes a column in one of the trade magazines. Brilliant copywriter! 

I heard of an opening in his copy department and arranged an interview. This was early in my career, before I knew what I was doing, and I was not that good a copywriter, I assure you. 

The master looked at my sample book and wasn't impressed. As he was reviewing my samples, looking for any glimmer of promise and finding none, I sat there, looking around his office. I noticed a little sign on his desk, carved in wood. It said... 

## **"Advertising Is Hard"** 

And it _is_ hard if you don't have a system to follow! That's why I gave you the guidelines to follow with _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®_ and in the steps that I want to show you now. Their entire purpose is— _how can we make it easier?_ 

First, what's so hard about advertising? 

For a copywriter, hard is having your own copywriting practice and no clients to pay your bills. 

Hard is not having a systematic approach to winners. As you'll see, we're getting a much more systematic approach of what's likely to work and what's not likely. 

- 225 - 

It starts with _The Persuasion Equation®._ What is your prospect's problem, want, or desire? What is your bold promise and strong proof? What is your irresistible proposition, your offer. If you can answer these questions, you're doing very well, even before you write a single word. 

With this four-cornered foundation, you will at last have a system of what your advertising is supposed to be doing. This is the strong, square foundation you must lay under your building if it's to stand straight and tall. This is how you must be thinking in order to have control packages, and once you start thinking this way, you'll find that control packages are no longer haphazard events and strokes of luck, but a much more predictable, systematic process. 

Without such a system, advertising can be hard indeed. 

Hard is also having a deadlines looming and nothing that pops into your head to write about. 

Hard is having to sell a me-too product in an overcrowded category. 

Hard is having to compete with some of the people in this room. That's _really_ hard, and something I've always tried to avoid. There's not a lot of gratification in going up against many of the great copywriters in this room, and I won't name them because I'm sure to leave somebody out. But you can surmise from the guest list whom I'm talking about. They have never wanted to write against me and I certainly have never wanted to write against them, because there are so many weakling ads out there that we can all beat up and take their lunch money. 

It makes no sense to compete against the best. One of us will lose and have wasted a lot of time. With so little good advertising to go around, life is just a lot easier when you seek out great products with weak advertising. That's where you can make your biggest contribution, not against products with brilliant advertising. By definition, in such a situation, your contribution is likely to be marginal. 

## **Secrets of Making Your Copywriting Success Much Easier** 

I believe in always looking for ways to make things easier because nobody likes to stay with "hard" very long. You don't want to burn yourself out. As the country music song "The Winner" by Shel Silverstein says, you don't want to spend your life "pickin' fights with thunderstorms and chargin' into trees." 

I've been in this business a long time, 40 years, and I still love it and always will. One of the reasons I've been able to do this so long is that I've run my race as a long-distance runner, not a sprinter. If you make your craft so difficult by making everything a breathless, do-or-die, high-pressure dash to the finish line, 

- 226 - 

putting an immense strain on yourself, you're turning yourself into a sprinter who's going to burn out much sooner than you should. And you will not be as successful as someone who is more relaxed and always brings his best game to the field of play, without undue pressure. 

If you take this steady-as-she-goes approach, the longer you stay in the game, the smarter you get, and the more successful you become. So anything that keeps you in the game longer is helping you. Anything that makes you dread your work and want to get out and find another field is hurting you. 

You see, we do mental work, which gives us a great edge over athletes. 

Athletes are in peak _physical_ shape when their physical assets are at their best, which means when they're young, in their early- to mid-twenties. 

But then their physical assets start to decline, and it's rare to see a world-class athlete at age 35 or 40. But with us, who have intellectual assets that we can build, you can keep doing that until you lose your mind someday or die. You get better and better and better into your thirties, forties, fifties, and beyond. 

So give yourself some breathing room to always like your work enough that you're not getting burned out, because your greatest asset will be your growing base of knowledge over time. It's just like building your wealth by letting your money earn interest, then letting that sum compound with more interest, and so on. That's the surest way to build wealth. Likewise with your knowledge. It's the power of compound knowledge that allows you to get smarter and smarter and more successful with every passing year. 

## **How to Get All the New Clients You Can Handle** 

For you designers, these same principles apply. 

As many people in this room know, and as I have certainly experienced, in direct marketing, _control packages_ are the coin of the realm. When you start developing a reputation as someone who produces control packages and your name is on winning packages, this makes you extremely attractive to potential clients. 

I always remember when I first wanted to go out on my own as a freelancer. I was working at Ogilvy & Mather, in the direct response division. At that time, one of the consultants we used was the great Dick Benson, a big bear of a man with a cranky demeanor. 

He was a prickly, cantankerous guy who never minced his words. And he always seemed to have this grumpy look on his face. But he was brilliant. He really knew his direct marketing. He wasn't a copywriter, but more of a direct 

- 227 - 

marketing strategist and entrepreneur who knew more about direct marketing math, methods, offers, and history than anyone else I ever met. 

One day when he was in our offices doing some consulting with the head of our direct marketing division, I waited in ambush outside the conference room. When he emerged, I nervously approached him, introduced myself, and said, "I'm thinking about going out on my own as a freelancer. You consult with many of the biggest firms in the industry Do you think you could recommend me to any of your clients?" 

He scowled, look down at me, and asked, "How many controls do you have?" I said, "Well, I've got some packages in test now that look pretty good and..." He cut me short, again asking, "No, how many controls do you have _now?"_ Truth be told, I didn't have even one. I said, "Well, right now I'm working on something that I think will..." 

He cut me off at the knees again, stopping our conversation short with "No, you come back to when you have some controls under your belt. Then, maybe, after I see them, I might consider recommending you. Clear?" 

"Yes, sir." 

## **New Business Tip #1: Control Packages Are the Coin of the Realm** 

Dick always cut right to the chase, and his comments are as true today as ever. This is a very accountable business. Everyone in our industry judges everyone else on the basis of results. If you want your phone to ring, you must show results, and for a copywriter, that means control packages. 

Somebody once asked Napoleon, "How do you chose your generals? They are so brilliant." He replied, "I _don't_ chose them. They are chosen for me by the god of battles." Likewise with direct marketers. Whoever wins gets more opportunity. 

If you're just starting out, you may wonder, as I did, "How do I _get_ control packages?" 

First and foremost, you must follow this next recommendation... 

## **New Business Tip #2:** 

## **Know What Control Packages Look Like...and Imitate Them** 

To hit the target, you must see the target. To develop a killer instinct for creating control packages, you must first study the control packages that are out 

- 228 - 

there. You should read and reread them until you know them almost by heart. The headlines and central themes you should know _completely_ by heart. 

Picasso said that every great artist begins by imitating the works of the greats who came before, just as he did. If you look at his early paintings, you'll see that he produced the most magnificent paintings in a classical style. Later, he found his own voice and changed the art world forever. But first, he learned the masters' way of doing things. 

After saturating your mind with the masterpieces of others, you will start to imitate them automatically. In time, you will develop your own voice, bring your own flair for creativity, as Picasso did in his field. But the first essential step is to study how the masters crafted their masterpieces and imitate them in your own work. 

Notice I'm not advocating blind theft and plagiarism. You want to imitate creatively, seeing what principles are at work and then applying those same principles creatively to your own work. 

You don't want to steal entire ads word for word, for several reasons. 

First, with current campaigns and those protected by copyright, it's illegal. 

Second, if you steal from others flagrantly and word for word, you'll be labeled as a lazy writer who just steals others' work and doesn't try to figure out what's unique to the product at hand. 

Third, you'll shortchange not only your client and product, but also yourself, as you're not growing and learning if all you aspire to be is a human Xerox machine. 

And fourth, it won't likely work, as each product and situation is a little different. 

You want to study a proven control package and figure out why it's working and then apply what you've learned to your own assignment, always careful to acknowledge and accommodate the differences between your assignment and the control you're imitating. 

Where do you find the best control packages to imitate? 

Buy products from the best direct marketers out there—Agora, Boardroom, KCI, Lombardi, Phillips, Rodale. Buy their products, subscribe to their publications, and you will get loaded up with state-of-the-art advertising. Your mailbox will be overflowing with great examples of direct marketing very soon. Study those packages, observe when they change, and you'll be state of the art in knowing what's working now. 

- 229 - 

And here's another little tip—enjoy the process of buying something you want. When you feel the desire to own something, enjoy that desire. Ponder and experience it fully. This is exactly the feeling you want to inspire in others, so you should be on intimate terms with it. 

Indulge yourself often. Consider it part of your research. I'm involved with marketing gourmet foods, luscious chocolates, for example. Do you think I'm eating heavenly chocolate just for pleasure? Uh-uh. I am doing research! You should do the same. Indulge your desire to buy, and remember what those experiences feel like, so you can create similar desires in others. 

I even created a folder long ago, and it was one of the best things I ever did to further my ability to write control ads. The file tab on the folder just said, "Made me buy." Into this folder I threw all the ads that persuaded me to buy something. It quickly became one of the greatest educational resources of my life, and this will be true for any copywriter who does likewise. If you understand how it feels to desire, to anticipate, to crave the benefits of a product or service yourself, you can much more easily recreate that feeling in others. This is how to do it. Save the ads that made you buy and reread them often. 

## **New Business Tip #3: Apply the Strategies We've Talked About Today** 

Especially _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®..."Cr?ick£V)?ick_ Secret"... and _Proof Element Marketing®._ They will give you an enormous advantage over those who are not using them and thereby greatly improve your odds of gaining control packages. 

For example, when I started applying the technique of making my advertising look and actually be valuable, I beat so many controls that didn't yet have a clue as to what I was doing. They had better copy than I did much of the time. But even if the advertising I was competing against had, say, a superior headline, if it looked like junk mail while my package looked like a valuable little book or report, I would invariably win. 

A design that communicates value will trump even a superior headline in a design that looks like an ad, and this principle enabled me to win far more contests than any great headline, as is usually the advice you hear in advertising. "Headlines are 80 percent of the sale." That's the conventional wisdom. 

Don't get me wrong, I strive to write the strongest headlines I can come up with, and usually write 25 to 30 before I choose the best. That's how important they are. But _even more important_ is having a format, a design, that instantly 

- 230 - 

communicates value and does _not_ communicate "sales pitch." 

In effect, your design is an even more important headline than your headline! I hate to admit this as a writer. I know the designers have always played second fiddle to us copywriters. But it's really the design now that is ruling the roost in direct marketing. It has been for a number of years because it's the design that will communicate value in your direct mail and ads much more quickly than a headline. 

Look at what we saw before with space ads. John Caples tested the exact same headline and body copy two different ways in the _Reader's Digest._ Version No. 1 was an ad that looked like an ad. Version No. 2 was the same ad designed to resemble an article. And Version No. 2 pulled in 81 percent higher sales. 

Think about that for a moment. It means that the right design instantly puts you 81 percent ahead. The wrong one puts you 81 percent in the hole, _regardless of what your headline says!_ 

In David Ogilvy's tests, he found a 500 percent increase in readership of ads that look like articles versus those that resemble ads. These remarkable results are purely in the realm of design. Nothing to do with copywriting. Many of the successes I've enjoyed have been due to knowing these tests and applying them religiously. I am not about to give up an 81 percent advantage to anyone. Luckily, many of my competitors are. They are still following the old models, of letting their mail look like sales pitches. I say fine and invariably have beaten them. 

Many of my biggest breakthroughs enlisted the help of world-class designers, like Ed Elliott and Rob Davis. I would say to them, "Let's make this look valuable. Let's make it look like a real paperback book that you might pick up in a drugstore rack or at Barnes & Noble." Or "Let's make it look like the cover of a real magazine." And when they had that instruction and ran with it, I was the 

beneficiary because I made a lot of royalties from the winning packages. But the big difference, at least in opening the sale, was in design. Then I just had to do a bang-up job of closing the sale, using the guidance of _The Persuasion Equation®,_ putting overemphasis on proof, and I'd have another big winner. When you know what you're doing, when you apply these principles against competitors who don't know about them, it's like taking candy from a baby. 

## **New Business Tip #4: Prowl the Jungle for the Weak and Wounded** 

## OK, if control packages are what make you famous and in great demand

- 231 - 

among direct marketers, how do you get started? What if you are where I was when I approached Mr. Grizzly Bear, Dick Benson, asking if he could recommend me even though I had no controls to speak of? 

One relatively easy way is to seek out weak competition rather than putting on the gloves competing against the top-ranked heavyweights. First build up your track record against weaker foes, and they are everywhere. You can find them especially among smaller advertisers who often have extremely weak ads. 

When you meet with them, ask what they are trying to achieve. "How can I help you?" 

They'll tell you about their controls or other advertising campaigns that need help. Review them to see if you can find one you think you can beat. If you do see one that doesn't use some of these principles we've talked about, you've got an excellent shot because these are proven principles—making it look valuable, enhancing your proof, etc. Plus, you can use some of the headlines we're going to go through in a little while, and these, too, will give you a monster edge against your competition. Just plug these elements in, and you usually will win. 

## **New Business Tip #5: Avoid Marketing "Challenges"** 

You don't want to sell ice to Eskimos or sand in the desert. I know, it sounds like such a flattering compliment when somebody says, _"He or she could sell ice to Eskimos."_ You don't want to sell ice to Eskimos because they don't need it. And even if you manage to be so persuasive that you pull it off, there's not going to be much repeat business. 

What I'm saying here is that you don't want to overestimate the value of what a copywriter or art director can do. If the product or the psychology isn't right, according to what we said before, if you don't have a great promise that you can fulfill and you can't prove it and the offer stinks, then your copy is not going to help that much. Those fundamentals of persuasion have to be in place. 

Rather than becoming the hero who, by dint of your beautiful words, is going to save the day, just remember how almost nobody would ride in an elevator until they saw that demonstration that the elevator held its place after the rope cables were severed. Know that and you can see the limitation of words. Demonstrations are much more powerful. But in any case, you don't want to accept "marketing challenges," because you're probably going to fail. 

- 232 - 

## **New Business Tip #6:** 

## **Go Gunning for Fatigued Controls... Even Mighty Lions Grow Old and Toothless** 

If you know when a well-known control package is growing weak, it can be an excellent chance for you to build your reputation. This takes a little bit of finesse and timing because the client will know it's growing weaker, too, and is likely already working hard to replace it. 

But if your timing is just right, you can start to build your reputation as a giant-killer by defeating a package that was once a great lion of the jungle, one that ate up all competitors for two or three years. 

This is like the fighter who gets into the ring at just the right moment against a famous champion who's taken too many shots to the head and kidney. He's ready to fall, to lose to a strong new challenger, who will instantly gain fame as the upstart who toppled the champion. 

Of course, then _you_ became the writer to beat, and that's when the pressure will ratchet up quite a bit to hold your place in the spotlight. 

For example, let's say there was a great Gene Schwartz package and that 15 writers before you couldn't beat it. Well, the truth is that each of those other 15 writers wore down Gene's package just a little bit. Every time Gene's package was mailed, it got more exposure and grew just a little weaker. Every order it pulled in meant that that there was one less person out in the marketplace left to respond to Gene's particular message. So, after each mailing, the pool of remaining prospects for that message keeps growing smaller. After being mailed to the point of exhaustion, almost any control becomes ready to be beaten. If you can try to figure out when to take on such an aging champ, you can quickly make a name for yourself or expand your growing reputation. 

## **New Business Tip #7: Double Your Odds of Winning—by Creating Two Versions, One "High-Probability" Test and One Bold New Concept** 

This is an excellent tip that has allowed me to capture many, many control packages. 

I hate to give a client just one package. I like to give two. I separate the two as follows. I like to have one that plays all the probabilities and a second that takes off in a whole new direction. Let me explain what I mean. 

- 233 - 

All the rules, guidelines, and secrets that I've been speaking about are designed to give you "house odds." What do I mean? Year after year casinos make tons of money. And year after year gamblers on average lose tons of money. The only difference is a slight advantage of the odds in favor of the house. That's what these secrets are designed to give you—greater "house odds." Actually, they are increasing your odds to a much greater degree than casinos enjoy, but this is the main idea. You are slanting the odds as much as you can in your favor. 

But even when you do that, there's no guarantee, as we all know. Sure, somebody can come up with a headline or approach from out of left field and it happens to beat your package that has done everything "by the book." That can and does happen. _But not often._ Just as in a casino, yes, it's possible for someone to bet on "red 17" a dozen times in a row, and win all 12 times and break the house. But that's highly unlikely. 

Sure, anybody can win, but if you follow the recommendations that I'm giving you, you raise your house odds dramatically by making your ad look valuable, by using lots of proof, by doing all the things that we've talked about. You will be very hard to beat if you do these things. 

So whenever I've been awarded an assignment, my first goal is to create one version that includes everything that I know that gives me house odds. I would create one version that, in effect, plays it safe with all of these things in place—big benefit headline, bold proof, proven offer, irresistible premiums, etc. 

And you could pretty much bet the ranch on that this would maximize my odds of having outstanding results. 

But in your research, or in brainstorming with your client or other writers, certain ideas may come up that nobody's ever done before. By definition, I would shy away from such interesting but unproven concepts in my first version, the "high-probability version," precisely because there is no track record of success there, no precedent I can count on. 

Yet it would be a shame not to try something so boldly original. And that's the purpose of my second version—to test something totally new, striking, and different from everything else our prospects have seen. 

Often, much of the interior of the ad or package will be identical to my first version—same body copy, subheads, etc. But in this wide-open, swing-for-thefences version, I'd be looking to belt a grandslam homerun off the center field bleachers—really take a rip, a swing at something which, if it connects, whacks the ball out of the park, that doubles or triples response and profits. Yes, because it's so new, it's higher risk. But that's OK, because our bottom line is already 

- 234 - 

protected by the high-probability version, which is a much safer, surer bet. 

In my experience, the high-probability copy almost always wins, precisely because it lines up so many pretested concepts and thus such high odds in our favor. 

But you never know. Every now and then, the wild-stab package wins, and when it does, it's a blockbuster. It will often have long legs, run for years, and be a sheer gusher of profits because the marketplace has never seen anything like it. It's such a big breakthrough because it does, in fact, break the rules and stands out as something so different among all the sameness in the mail. 

Always give your client this same opportunity—one version that is as close as you can get to a lead-pipe cinch, then a second that tries something new, perhaps a new offer, much higher price, radically impressive guarantee, highly provocative new premium, anything. 

By the same token, if you are the client, always be testing new things that may seem out of the normal orbit of your habitual practice, and you'll increase your odds of coming up with a major breakthrough. 

## **New Business Tip #8: Transfer Winning Ideas from Other Fields** 

Don't just read and respond to the advertising in your own field. Look at advertising from many fields, and if you see any of it repeating, you know it's a winner. You can often adapt a winning ad's formula, concept, headline, or offer to your ad and transfer all that dynamite to your campaign. 

There are legions of direct marketers out there spending millions of dollars testing everything under the sun. Keep an eye on them, and you'll be able to benefit immensely from their tests without spending any money yourself. **New Business Tip #9: Hang Around the Gym** 

If you're a copywriter and you're trying to move up in the pecking order of freelancers retained by the major direct marketing companies, here's what you should do. At first, when they have no experience with you, they are not likely to let you take a stab at their control package right out of the gate unless you have control packages for others. 

But they might well take a chance with you on something less critical. Maybe they'll let you do a renewal series or a package insert. Remember when you were in high school, playing pickup games of basketball at the gym? You'd see a game 

- 235 - 

already in progress on the main court. You might want to get into the game, but both sides already have all the players they need. So you just shoot baskets off to the side by yourself. You hang around the gym. And what happens? Sooner or later, one of the players in the full-court game twists an ankle or has to leave for some reason. The other players look around, turn to you, and say, "Hey, you in the blue sweatshirt, wanna get in the game?" Just because you're there, you'll get asked when there's a shortage of major players. 

They key to being asked to do something bigger and better is to do a bang-up job on the little assignments you started on. It may not be on the flagship publication or product, but they'll remember you when you turn in a great effort. 

And that's the approach you should take on all your assignments. Go all out and give your client tremendous service, more than they ever dreamed of getting on such a project. It's the same as they say in the theater, _"There are no small roles, only small actors."_ How often have you seen a character actor or role player steal the scene? They did it by putting so much into that little appearance. Do the same thing as a copywriter. You don't have to wait until you're given center stage. Be a star on the little jobs, and your client may well conclude that you may well be a star on a bigger assignment. 

## **New Business Tip #10: Be a Lifeguard** 

Every client has a drowning product. And they will usually invest much more time and worry on this particular product than they should. They should probably just cut their losses on a weak product and put all their efforts behind the strong. 

But this is hard to do. It's painful to acknowledge failure, because it means that everything invested so far is lost. This is the Zeigarnik Effect in action, the tendency to be far more concerned about something unfinished, and a resulting desire to want to complete the job, even when it's not in your best self-interest and you should be putting your efforts elsewhere for much greater profit. You stubbornly keep pushing and refuse to give up. 

You can benefit from this tendency. You can volunteer as the lifeguard who shows up with a life preserver under your arm, an idea for rescuing this drowning puppy. If you succeed, you'll be a hero, and they'll reward you with lots of other business. This is something of a high-risk strategy, as most of these drowning products can't swim and deserve to go under. But every now and then, there's a decent product and the advertising is awful. That's when you can be a hero to a client, and they'll never forget you for it. 

- 236 - 

## **New Business Tip #11: Specialize!** 

We've talked about specializing. It gives you so many advantages. 

First, it allows you to develop much deeper knowledge within a given market, and this gives you a great edge in writing copy. Over time, you will know what turns this market on, what turns it off, the catch phrases, the underlying assumptions, its current concerns, past disappointments, the competing products, the unfulfilled needs, etc. All this is invaluable. 

Second, specializing bestows great credibility upon you in attracting clients. You are automatically perceived as knowing more than someone who tries to be "everything to everybody," just as a neurosurgeon is automatically perceived to be more knowledgeable about the brain than your family doctor. 

Third, by specializing in one field, you'll be exposed to other players in that same field and come into more networking and joint venture opportunities. 

And fourth, because you know your market so well, you can be among the first to jump on major shifts and new opportunities in that market, getting a head start on others who have spread themselves thin. 

Within direct marketing, you might want to just specialize in a couple of fields to gain these benefits in each market. 

Tomorrow we will talk more about the nature of desire and how we can all be alchemists of desire. We'll see how, no matter how much we try, we humans never totally fulfill our desires, because our desires are infinite. As soon as we fulfill one, another rushes to take its place. And there is a pattern to this process, a pattern that allows you to predict the next desire most likely to emerge once your customer fulfills a desire. That pattern is one of _greater and greater specialization._ 

As I say, we'll get into this a lot more deeply tomorrow. But for now, know that you can make a fortune in marketing by anticipating "the next big thing" in any market, _which is almost always a further specialization within that market._ 

Let's say for example that you sold vitamins in the 1970s, when the most popular vitamins were the one-size-fits-all, one-a-day multiple vitamins. Had you known this principle I'm sharing, _that markets keep subdividing by getting more and more specialized,_ you could have easily predicted that if these one-size-fitsall, one-a-day multiple vitamins were popular, you could subdivide that market by offering a more specialized product, say, vitamins _just for men,_ and another product, vitamins _just for women._ You could have also predicted that you could 

- 237 - 

successfully offer supplements for various conditions—a supplement for _arthritis,_ another for _energy,_ another for _lowering cholesterol,_ and so on. You could anticipate the success of such new products, each one of which could endow you with a fortune, by knowing that every market keeps developing new desires by getting further and further specialized, and it's a never-ending process, a repeating pattern you can exploit by knowing about it and anticipating the next level of specialization. 

And look at the built-in power that such specialization brings to the marketing process. Health seekers may contentedly take their general multiple vitamins for years. But as soon as they hear that there's a new and improved formulaybr _men only_ and another _for women only,_ this alone makes many of them want the new, improved, more specialized version for their gender or health problem. 

For example, if you're a runner, could you ever again be satisfied with generalpurpose _sneakers_ once specialized _runners shoes_ hit the market? Of course not, even if _runners shoes_ happen to be virtually identical to plain old ordinary sneakers. The mere fact of specialization creates a powerful, mystical, irresistible desire within you to own the proper specialized sneakers for your favorite sport. That's how powerful and predictable this process is, and it can make you a fortune again and again. 

It works in every product category, for every service business. It's so powerful, we'll devote a fair amount of time to it tomorrow, when we get into how you can be a master alchemist of desire. But I also had to bring it up here because we're talking about how to get new business, and specialization is such an outstanding way to attract new business no matter what your product or service. 

The more you specialize, the more quickly your prospects will want to jump on what you offer if it matches the level of specialization they're looking for. And even if they weren't looking for it, the fact that you've taken their area of interest to a new level of specialization will make many prospects unhappy accepting anything less. 

## **New Business Tip #12: Write Articles and Give Speeches in Your Field of Specialization** 

This is a great tip for any business, whether you have a product you're selling, a local service business, anything. It gets back to that main concept of _**making your advertising itself valuable,**_ so it's welcomed, not spurned on contact. 

- 238 - 

## **New Business Tip #13:** 

## **License a Winning Campaign to Local Markets** 

This can be a very interesting and lucrative concept that we talked about before. I used my insurance agent as an example, the man who came to sell us insurance for our new home. 

You can license a winning campaign to local markets in a wide variety of fields. For example, let's say that you have a friend who's a real estate broker. She's heard you're a copywriter and would love it if you would write a letter to help her secure house listings. 

You can tell her, "Sure, I can do that." You go to Amazon and buy some books by superstar real estate brokers who reveal their secrets for a pittance, the price of their books. 

You research what works, then craft a simple letter or space ad for your local paper, and it works like a charm in getting listings for your friend. 

Well, you now have a product you can sell to brokers in other towns all across the country! And be sure to get a glowing testimonial from your friend. Then you can license your campaign by zip code or any other way you want to divide up territories, and sell the same piece of copy over and over. In other words, you'll give an exclusive to someone within a given zip code or town, so competitors won't be running the same ad. That makes it more appealing—"You can be the first in your area to have exclusive rights to this campaign." 

Next year, you can create a new letter or brochure or newsletter for this same market and have ongoing repeat business from all your customers across the country. 

Just as soon as you create that first great little business-getting campaign for your real estate broker friend, and after you get a testimonial from her and start rolling out your product, it's almost as if you have a franchise to sell to others, your own mini-McDonald's. 

You've got a great proven piece of advertising that you can now sell to real estate brokers all across the country. And there are thousands upon thousands, most of whom haven't the faintest clue about how to use a local ad campaign to get new listings. 

Bringing powerful direct response advertising to such a market, where the marketing competition is so inept, is so much easier than competing against the people in this room, where you're competing at a major league level against the brightest stars in the world of direct marketing. 

- 239 - 

When you compete in these markets where they have no clue about what effective marketing is, boy, you are not just the biggest fish in the pond, you are a _shark_ among goldfish! You will dominate from the top of the food chain. 

It's as if you have a franchise that you can sell cookie-cutter fashion across the country. Notice that I'm saying _"as if"_ because you don't want to call it a "franchise." Don't use that word! Call it a license agreement because calling something a "franchise" invites a horrendous level of federal government inspection and paperwork. You're not franchising, you're licensing. That's the concept. One successful formula can be multiplied out 50, 100, 1,000 times over. I know some people in this room do this already, with great success. Dr. Tom Orent does this in dentistry. Joe Polish does it brilliantly in carpet cleaning. It's a very good way to build a large business. You become the marketing expert within an industry and you license the same winning campaigns over and over. This is a great way to leverage your wealth. 

## **New Business Tip #14 Don't Act Hungry** 

Until this seminar, I've never given speeches. 

If you're looking for business, if you're looking to establish yourself, yes, giving a talk can be an excellent business builder. 

But if you're someone with a reputation for being booked up long in advance, as I was lucky enough to be after I started having control packages, you don't want to "ruin your image" by giving talks and thus appearing as if you're beating the bushes for work. 

I would never give a speech, because most people assume that if you're up there on the podium to give a talk, you're doing it to stimulate business. And for a writer, someone whose time can easily be booked up, it seems as if you don't have enough assignments to fill your schedule, and this becomes a _reverse_ proof element. It demonstrates that you're not booked up. 

If you're an executive from an ad agency or some other firm that shares the workload among an army of people, that's different. Giving a talk helps generate business for your firm, and that's understood. But if you're a lone practitioner, with only so many hours in the day, any perception that you're out there beating the bushes can be interpreted to mean that you're not booked up. _Why not?_ 

I suppose that if you _are_ looking for business, the best choice is to proceed cautiously. Try to strike the right balance. You certainly must avoid giving speeches _too often._ At least that's my view. Other people might contend that there's 

- 240 - 

nothing wrong with speaking and networking constantly, if nothing else, to enjoy the camaraderie of your colleagues in your industry. I avoided all such engagements like the plague because I did not want anyone to think I was there trying to promote myself. I had the reputation that _"Gary s booked up two years in advance,"_ and I was. This in itself became a strong proof element about my services, another factor in making people want to hire me even more. For a writer or designer, being booked up is a powerful proof element. 

## **New Business Tip #15 Don't Compete on Price** 

Whether you're a copywriter or someone in _any_ service business, always remember that _clients don't want cheap._ They want what works. They understand that the most expensive copy is the copy that doesn't work. 

Most of us copywriters are not great negotiators. We're sensitive, poetic souls. The clients we work with are seasoned businesspeople. Just as a matter of course they'll say, _"Ouch!"_ when you quote your price. 

I learned this when I realized how poor a negotiator I was. I sent away for a bunch of Nightingale-Conant tapes on negotiating. One of the first things the tapes advised was, whenever someone quotes a price, regardless of whether it's high or low, say some form of _"ouch!"_ Communicate a feeling that this is more than you desired to spend. 

I'm not sure this is always correct advice, but it's what some negotiating experts recommend. And if you notice, it's how some clients always respond to whatever price you quote. 

Obviously, this is a technique you can use yourself whenever you are dealing with a vendor. Whatever price they say, you can respond with _"Ooooh, that much?"_ 

Unless the person quoting the price is already giving his best price, or a shrewd negotiator himself, he'll immediately feel like saying, _"Oh, don't worry. I'll do it for less."_ 

Most people want to please their clients, so this is a natural, almost automatic response we have to this _"ouch!"_ technique. _"Ican do better!"_ 

Many smart businesspeople know this and similar techniques, but we poetic, creative souls do not. Before long, we find ourselves asking the client, "I really want to make you happy. How much can I pay you to work for you?" 

- 241 - 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

But as I say, if you're on the buying side, you can use this _"ouch!"_ technique yourself. I didn't have the nerve to try it until once when we were doing some home renovations. A supplier quoted a fairly high price. And I said, _"Ooooh!"_ holding my chin and frowning, as if I just learned that I needed heart surgery or had lost $ 10,000 in the stock market. 

I was astonished when he immediately replied that he could do better. Give him a chance. He immediately cut the price and included a lot of extras for no additional money. 

I discovered that this _"ouch!"_ technique worked instantly. It was amazing. I also realized that this is why my clients have been saying _"ouch!"_ all these years. I had always tried to accommodate them. 

But in time I realized that what they really want is to _"touch bottom,"_ to know that you're not going to give a better price. They had to try, as a test, to see if you'd do it for less. But once they firmly touch bottom and know you're not going lower, they're happy. They just won't show it. 

My ultimate solution to all this negotiating back-and-forth became easy when I was very much in demand. I would just quote a high price, but not astronomically high, plus a royalty that would let me participate in the success of the project. Then I was firm and universally consistent with this rate for everyone in the same market. 

My favorite negotiating technique became not to negotiate at all. "This is the standard price I charge everyone," which was true. "If I'm not right for you, I understand, and there are people who will do this important job for less. But there's a good reason I ask this amount, a reason that benefits you, and that's to give me ample time to do your job right. For example, let me describe my process for giving you a major winner..." 

And then I'd go into the special, very caring, and thorough approach I would take with their work, describing all the research I'd do, some of the ideas I think could work, the past successes that I think could guide us to a blockbuster, and so on. 

Once their mouths were watering for these benefits, for all the attention I would lavish on their assignment, and once they understood I would not bend in my price, they almost always decided to retain me. 

For most clients, like most consumers, a high price is a proof element of value. 

- 242 - 

By charging too little, or by being willing to be beaten down on your price, you actually scare away your most valuable prospects because they will assume that if you are truly excellent and in demand in your field, _you will hold your ground on price._ It shows you are in high demand and don't need to cut your price to mollify an insistent prospect. Your best prospects will respect and desire you more when you hold your ground on price. _So don t chase your best prospects away in a misguided attempt to appease the cheapskates, who will never likely be satisfied anyway!_ 

## **New Business Tip #15 Follow the Work** 

When I first started out, I never thought the demand for my copywriting would be mostly in newsletters—financial and health newsletters. 

But those were the assignments that kept coming my way, so I did them. There was a mortgage to pay, a client base to build, and I did these assignments just as I've done every assignment I ever took—with the best effort I could possibly give. 

Forty years later, and I've still been working largely in that field. So sometimes the thing that you think you want to do is not what your market will affirm. 

Again, we all think like marketers. You may want to sell a certain thing or provide a certain service, but it's not really what your prospects want to buy at that moment. So you have to be flexible. 

Maybe your prospects have products in different fields that you have never thought about, but they can be great marketing opportunities, especially if the other good copywriters haven't found out about such fertile fields yet. **How to Write Proposals That Land the Job** 

The following is true if you are a copywriter, designer, or anybody trying to win an assignment. To write a blockbuster proposal letter, just follow the outline of _The Persuasion Equation®,_ with one exception. Here's what I mean... 

First, talk with your prospective client, get a lot of information, and you'll see what his or her problems are. But, and here's the exception, when you're writing your proposal letter, never call their greatest want a _"problem,"_ even though it really is. 

Nobody likes to confess a "problem" to a stranger. They'll feel defensive about it. They'll happily use terms like "goals," "challenges," and so on, but steer clear 

- 243 

- 

of the word "problem." Nevertheless, that's the sore spot you're probing for, just like a doctor examining a new patient. 

Once you've uncovered the sore spot, or "challenge," then it's easy to start writing your cover letter. You start with the usual pleasantries, such as, "I enjoyed our meeting last Thursday and learning about your goals for... Let me briefly review what I perceive to be your strongest desires for this project..." 

And then you _just feed back_ what you've heard your client tell you. 

"From what you've shared with me, your aim is to...(fill in the blank). This is obviously important to you because...(fill in the blank). Other aspects of this assignment of concern to you to you are...(fill in the blank)." 

And so on, until you feed back to your prospective client the most important feelings and desires he or she shared with you. 

Believe me, your prospective clients will start to _fall in love_ with you when you do this! They will be so appreciative that you showed them the respect of listening and can repeat their greatest wants for their projects. You will go straight to the head of the line of candidates. 

Great salespeople do this all the time. They _let you_ tell _them_ how you should be sold! Let me give you an example, a story you will enjoy. 

## **The Brilliant Closing Technique of "America's Greatest Salesman"** 

Years ago, on the _Tonight Show with Johnny Carson,_ I saw the man who was billed as America's greatest salesman. His name was Fred Herman. 

Before the commercial break, Johnny piqued everyone's interest by saying, "Don't go away! After the break, we'll meet America's greatest salesman, and he's going to tell us how to sell anything." 

Fred Herman boomed onto the stage, a big, jolly man. You liked him just by looking at him. 

After a little banter, Johnny Carson said to him, "Well, you're supposed to be such a good salesman, sell me something." 

Fred said, "What do you want me to sell you?" 

Mind you, this could have been a setup, where they rehearsed the whole thing. Or it could have been spontaneous; I don't know. It doesn't matter. The principles Fred illustrated are prefect for selling anything. 

In those days, guests would smoke on the _Tonight Show,_ and Johnny Carson always had an ashtray on his desk. He said to Fred, "All right, sell me this ashtray." 

- 244 - 

I don't have a tape of the show, so I'm going to quote here from my own memory, and I remember it well. Just listen to how Fred handled this... 

First he said, "Well, I'm not sure if I can sell you this, Johnny, but let me see." Fred then took possession of the ashtray, and Johnny Carson watched his ashtray move over to Fred's lap. Johnny also started noticing the ash that had started to form on his cigarette and realized he had no place to flick it. 

Fred Herman said, "Well, Johnny, I don't know if this ashtray's right for you, but why might you want to own an ashtray like this?" 

Johnny replied, again noticing his lengthening ash, "Well, I like to smoke. I'm on the air, and I have to put my ashes someplace." 

Fred nodded, as if to say, "Good reason." Then he asked, "And in addition to that?" 

You see, it was Fred's belief that people usually give the expected answer to the first question of why something is important to them. But they reveal their _real_ reason when you ask the second question, _"And in addition to that? "_ 

Johnny replied, "Well, I'll tell you. The most important reason why I keep an ashtray on my desk is because many of my guests like to smoke. Before they come out, they know they're going to be in front of millions of people, and they're nervous, even the biggest stars. For those who smoke, I like them to see an ashtray. Just having it here invites them to smoke and makes them feel more relaxed. That's great because I get a better interview, a livelier interplay with my guests, and the audience gets a more entertaining show." 

Now, old Fred is just listening to all of this. Notice that he hasn't tried to sell a thing yet. He has simply asked two good "opening up" questions that got his prospect to open up and talk about what he wants most. After that simple step, Fred just listens, all ears. What he has done is to cleverly _elicit from his prospect exactly what it will take to close the sale before he tries to sell._ 

Now in possession of this critical information—Johnny's own reasons for wanting the ashtray—Fred said, "That sounds pretty important, Johnny." 

Johnny agreed, "Yes, it really is," realizing he had just reinforced in his own mind the most important reasons for wanting such a product and the very desirable benefits it makes possible. 

"Well, Johnny," said Fred, "considering how important this ashtray is to you, how much would you pay for it?" 

Johnny replied, "Oh, I don't know...25 cents." 

## And Fred said, "For that price, I'll let you have it," and, cradling the ashtray

- 245 - 

like a bar of gold, gently handed it back to Johnny, as the audience burst into applause. 

Johnny, of course, did one of his famous double takes, where he looks off to the left in utter bewilderment, as if to ask, _"How did I just buy my own ashtray? "_ 

What can we learn from this? How does it relate to writing proposals that land the job? 

The key, as I said, whether you're selling in person or in a proposal is to first elicit from your prospects exactly what it will take to close them—the reasons they want this product or service. 

Your prospects will give you their reasons for buying if you ask. That's why I say that Fred Herman's little demonstration with Johnny Carson, whether it was a rehearsed setup or spontaneous, was one of the most instructive sales presentations I ever saw. 

## **Perhaps the Worst Sales Presentation in the History of the World** 

Indeed, to appreciate this great lesson—that you must elicit your prospect's reasons for wanting a product _before_ you talk about your solution—let me tell you about possibly the worst presentation in the history of the world, one that I was involved in when I was the creative director of Dan Rosenthal's ad agency. 

Remember how I told you that we ran an ad announcing a direct marketing agency that will guarantee to beat your best ad. We were all copywriters, you know, Claude Hopkins fanatics, and we knew how to sell in print. But selling in person has its own set of dynamics, which I knew nothing about. 

The agency sent me, and other writers, out to meet prospective new clients who responded to our ad. 

This was years before I was to see Fred Herman's presentation on the _Tonight Show,_ which, with brilliant simplicity, showed how to elicit a prospect's greatest wants before trying to sell anything. 

At this earlier time in my career, I didn't have a clue about such a strategy. 

I just blundered ahead. I and someone else from our agency set up a meeting with a potential client. This man ran a collection agency and thought that a direct mail ad agency like ours might help him write better collection letters. 

At first, he kept us waiting for about a half hour, which wasn't a promising sign. Finally, an assistant ushered us into his big office. After a few pleasantries, he threw his pen down, folded his arms, and said, "OK, sell me." 

- 246 - 

"OK, mister," I thought. "I'm going to sell like crazy," and I proceeded to tell him, with great enthusiasm, all about direct marketing, how it's so measurable, and how we love Claude Hopkins, whom he of course never heard of. I just went on and on about the benefits of direct marketing, our methods, the successes of our other clients, our key people, and how we work. I chased my tail like this for about half an hour, telling him all the things I thought would impress him. He could not have cared less! 

Then he had a phone call. After a few grunts, he hung up the phone, looked at his watch, looked at us, and said, "All right, let me think about this, and I'll get back to you guys." Then he stood up, shook our hands, and showed us to the door. 

_"Uh...what just happened? "_ we wondered as we rode down the elevator, suddenly shell-shocked by our unceremonious dismissal. It's as if, with the flick of a finger, we had been flushed away, and I had no clue of what I had done wrong. 

Looking back, I now know that I committed the worst mistake possible. I should have done exactly what canny old Fred did. When my prospect threw down his pen and said, "OK, sell me," I should have said with a friendly smile, "Well, I don't know that I can sell you. But may I ask you a couple of questions first? Why are we here? What motivated you to respond to our _ad?...And in addition to that? "_ 

This would have gotten him talking about _his_ wants, _his_ needs, _his_ reasons for wanting better collection letters, and that in turn would have given me the right ammunition to use when it came my turn to speak—or write my proposal. 

Whenever you meet with a prospective new client, you want to be like a doctor. You want to ask permission to examine your patient, take their temperature, and diagnose their health before you prescribe a remedy. 

Selling that way is much more professional—and _much_ more effective. It's also much easier. To your great relief, your clients will do all the heavy lifting, telling you exactly how to close them, telling you their most painful symptoms, _IF_ you will only ask. 

They _want_ to talk about their problems, wants, and needs. The worst thing you can do is what I did—talk too much at the beginning of your conversation and deprive your prospect of the opportunity to bond with you and simultaneously give you all the ammunition you'll need to close. It's the worst mistake you can make in selling. 

If you are willing to listen, they will tell you everything you need to sell them, just as Johnny Carson told Fred everything he needed to know to close that sale of that ashtray. Your customers, your prospects will do the same thing. So that's the main rule... 

- 247 - 

## **Never Start Selling Until You Know What the Customer Is Buying!** 

Once you know what the customer is looking to buy, including the benefits that the customer hopes to enjoy, you have all the right ammunition for your proposal. 

Remember, this section is all about how to write proposals that land the job. Let's go back to my main point here.... 

The secret of writing a great proposal is to write a letter along the lines of _The Persuasion Equation®:_ (1) Urgent Problem (only you're not going to use the word "problem")... (2) Unique Promise...(3) Unquestionable Proof..and (4) UserFriendly Proposition. 

So after you've met with your potential client, you will write in your proposal, in effect, "Thanks for the meeting...Here's my understanding of what you are looking for..." And then you feed back exactly every headache, want, and concern that he or she described to you. 

## **They Will Love You for This!** 

Potential new clients will love you for feeding back their wants and concerns, even before they hear the solution. It's so nice when somebody really listens to you. It shows they've paid you the respect and exhibited the professionalism of _listening to you carefully!_ 

That is irresistibly flattering! So many people go home to their families who don't listen. Their teenagers don't listen. Their spouses don't listen. Maybe even their dogs don't listen. But you did! _"How wonderful!"_ they think. _"What a splendid person you must be, someone I can easily work with."_ 

When you write your proposal, you start off by demonstrating, in effect, that "I've heard you, and I want to make the situation better." 

Then, after you review, virtually in laundry list fashion, every one of their wants and concerns, you write a new subhead in your proposal, "How Can I (or We) Help You?" 

You then go into what you believe you can do for them, being sure to cite your proof elements at every step. 

Then, finally, another subhead that says "How We Will Work Together." 

In this section of your letter, you describe how you work, what you charge, when you can start, finishing with the benefits of reaching an agreement now, so the work can be scheduled and they can take this immediate step toward reaching 

- 248 - 

the goals described in the first section of your proposal. 

Just follow that _Persuasion Equation®,_ and you'll be amazed at how people respond, at how your closing ratio will soar. Problem, promise, proof, proposition. That's how we all think when we're considering a purchase. Let your proposals be perfectly in sync with your prospects, and they will want to follow you. They will feel you understand them, have the ability to solve their problems, and this is what they want. 

## A couple of quick points in setting your prices—

## **Your Strongest Negotiating Posture Is to Not Negotiate on Price** 

As I said before, clients are generally better negotiators than writers. I found that my strongest negotiating stance was to not negotiate at all. Just take it off the table and you can save yourself and your client a lot of grief and haggling. 

"This is what I charge for this type of project." If they challenge me on it or want a better rate, I tell them that I am not willing to do it for a different rate, for several reasons. 

First and foremost, for their benefit, I want the time to do this important project right. "I'm going to be doing a lot for you, including the following (then you recite all the wonderful things you plan to do for this client). When I work on your assignment, I want to be thinking of all the ways to enhance your response, not feeling that I was hammered down on price. In the end, that's not good for either of us." 

Second, I don't deviate from my existing pricing because all my other clients already pay this same rate, and I don't wish to reopen negotiations with them. I have a very successful, well-established client base. I'm not about to put my relationships with my current clients in jeopardy by giving someone new a better rate than they are entitled to. I value my current clients and treat them right. They have paid the price I am quoting to you. They continue to pay this price, and it would not be fair if I start to cut prices for some clients but not for others. 

As in most cases when a prospective client balks at a price and insists on paying less, the best strategy is to hold firm and keep selling _benefits, benefits, benefits_ of what you intend to do for him, the time and effort you will put in. Resistance on price is merely evidence that you haven't sold the benefits enough yet. 

There's also the downside you must consider when you are tempted to yield. Giving in on price will often destroy your chances to close the client. Giving in 

- 249 -
