convinces your prospect that he hasn't touched bottom yet, making him fight all the harder for further price reductions. The more you give, the more you show him that you do not have the confidence—or enough demand for your services in the marketplace—to hold firm in your pricing, both of which can be ruinous to the sale. 

After all, you will have to be firm _somewhere_ in such a negotiation. You're not going to let him hammer you down into working for nothing. So why not be firm at the price you originally set and not less? Doctors don't negotiate with patients. Attorneys and dentists don't. Why do you feel that you must? It's just a sign of insecurity. 

If your prospective client continues to urge you to give him a special break and swears he will tell no one, don't believe it. He may be sincere, but people innocently forget, within days if not hours, their most solemn pledge to keep a secret. 

Always remember that everyone in our business talks with everyone else. People change jobs from one client to another. Word invariably gets around. Assume that no one will keep secrets, because they won't. 

As soon as your other clients do learn that they're paying you a higher rate than one of their competitors, they may well want to renegotiate _their_ price, and you'll be caught in a downward spiral of renegotiation. 

The solution, at least what worked for me—be consistent with all your clients. Set a fair price and stick to your guns. Don't punish your good clients and reward the price shoppers by allowing your pricing to be dictated by the most aggressive hagglers. If you do, you'll be rewarding the hagglers and bullies and punishing your best, most supportive clients, who will rightfully feel resentful if you take advantage of their good nature by giving better deals to the hagglers. You will force them to become hagglers too, and it will be your own fault. Set a fair price, stick to your guns, and realize that your services are not for everyone. 

## **Insist on the Time to Do the Job Right** 

This is another part of the negotiation when you're accepting a new client. 

In your eagerness to land the job, don't allow yourself to be pressured into an impossible deadline, thinking you can wiggle out later. Your initial promises to your client establish his or her expectation level. Even though most people will pressure you to hurry up and do their work, they will respect your desire to do the job right and work it into your schedule. That's their highest priority, to get the job done right. 

- 2 5 0 - 

If, at the outset of your relationship, you agree to a schedule that's utterly impossible to meet, you've automatically guaranteed that your client will be unhappy eventually, either because you couldn't keep to your optimistic schedule or because you did so by cutting corners. Either way you will disappoint your client. 

This is sometimes hard to avoid, because at the start of your project, you're full of enthusiasm and optimism, and this can easily seduce you into making promises you can't keep. 

## **The "Double-Plus-10-Percent Rule"** 

How can you keep yourself—or someone you're hiring—from agreeing to a deadline that's impossible? 

Follow the _"double-plus-10-percent rule."_ That is, to get a more accurate gauge on when a project will _really_ get done, take your initial—and invariably optimistic—time estimate, double it, then add an additional 10 percent. As hard as it is to face such a lengthy time estimate, this rule will give a much more precise view of the actual time it will take. Try it and see for yourself. It's mystifying in its precision, a great tool. 

## **Earn Your Client's Respect First...Love Will Follow** 

This was a secret that David Ogilvy taught us. Neither love, nor friendship, nor reputation, nor family ties, nor memories of sunny afternoons on a golf course with your client—none of these things will save you if your campaign goes down in flames. Feel free to skip all such activities that don't contribute directly to creating a powerhouse campaign for your client, as this is the only basis on which you will eventually be judged. Your mission is to earn respect and security _through your results,_ not by friendship, as friendship will vanish quickly as soon as your campaign flops. Put your focus—and your dynamite—into your work, and let your results win your security. 

## **Don't Lust After New Business at the Expense of Present Clients** 

This has always been one of my pet peeves with ad agencies, at least with some of the large ones where I've worked. We were often instructed to drop what we were doing for our present clients and focus our energies on speculative campaigns to woo a new client. As a result, the work of present clients was delayed, delegated to junior people, and quickly pushed through without much scrutiny, while our most senior people spent long hours developing speculative 

- 251 - 

## campaigns to show a potential _new_ client what we could do for _them._

I always felt that this was akin to forcing existing clients to subsidize the work of other businesses they knew nothing about! It's insane but is still a common practice at far too many ad agencies. **Thorough Research—the Bedrock of Breakthrough Advertising** 

Now let me turn to one of my favorite topics, one that has been responsible for more breakthroughs than any other single factor in advertising: _thorough research._ 

Remember this morning with our example of the kids who were off for the summer? They wanted to know how to create a great ad campaign for their worms. 

## And somebody who had attended this seminar told them, _"Research is the foundation."_

## **Cast a Wide Net** 

Let me give a couple of pointers on research. 

First, cast a wide net. When you're researching something, you shouldn't start writing _anything_ yet, not even a headline. I've made this mistake many times myself. I thought at the very beginning of the assignment that I had a great idea for a headline. I would write it down and feel I was making great progress by coming up with research that supported this idea. 

But the problem with doing this is that once you think you've got your headline, you tend to notice only the research that supports the direction your headline is pushing you in. That's a mistake, because in the research stage, you want to review everything with an open mind, not through the lens of a preselected headline concept. 

If during the research stage, you think you've got a great idea for a headline, sure, write it down, put it aside for later, but don't think for one moment that your headline problem is solved. Because then you'll subconsciously start shutting down your research process and start looking only for those research points that buttress your headline. In fact, other ideas will start to seem threatening to your headline, and you'll tend to resist them. Just write it down and tell yourself that this is one of many headlines that you'll be coming up with before you're through. _Never select your main headline concept until all your research is finished,_ otherwise you'll miss a great many opportunities. 

- 252 - 

In the research stage you want to be wide open to anything that you might possibly discover. Depending on the product, you have many avenues to research. If it's something sold by salespeople, you want to interview star salespeople because they know how to sell that product. 

You want to interview the product manager. You want to interview the head of the company or division. You want to interview the customers, especially those who love the product. You want to study the product literature. You want to load up on competitive products and their literature, their direct mail packages, and so on. At the outset of taking on an assignment, I would always advise my client to back up the truck. _"You cannot send me too much information."_ 

Very often you'll find a nugget of research in some place where you never expected to find it, and it can give you the kernel of a blockbuster ad. So be very thorough in your research, and don't start writing until after your research is complete. 

This is an important point, especially if you want to avoid writer's block, because thorough research is the best cure for writer's block, which is another reason you must separate the research and writing process into different stages, and I'll explain more about this later. 

For now, it's just important to know that the first stage of writing is pure research. You're not looking to solve any problem just yet. You're not looking to start writing beautiful or persuasive words. The key in this first stage is to be wide open. And that's easy. As much as you're tempted to get started writing, you shouldn't start yet. At this stage, keep it easy, simple. Just stick to reading interesting material and talking with interesting people about the product. That's what research should be—as easy as reading, talking with people, and taking thorough notes. Relax and have a good time with it. You don't want to put that pressure on yourself of solving the problem immediately. You're just looking to find out how and why other people like this product or don't like it, and how it could be improved. 

There are some questions you always want to ask during the research process. Why is this product made the way it is? Why is it better than alternative solutions? Who uses it? Who are the 20 percent who buy 80 percent of the product? What are the hot buttons of the market? What is the lingo of those who use it, their favorite buzz words? What are their main problems? 

John Carlton is so good at doing this. Where's John Carlton? John is very good at this. If he's writing about golfers, he'll pick up their lingo, their slang, the problems and frustrations they experience out on the greens. When other golfers 

- 253 - 

read the ad, they say, "This is written by a golfer who knows where I come from, what I'm feeling." And that's very important in any field, to know the feelings and buzz words that your prospects use. 

Among the most important aspects of your research, you want to look for the factors that fill the slots of _The Persuasion Equation®._ 

We want to find, in all our research, what _**urgent problems**_ this product is designed to solve. If it's more than one problem, just make a whole list and decide later, or even test to find out, which one is the most important to your prospects. 

Ask yourself, in all your research, what can you find to make a _**unique promise?**_ **What** _**unquestionable proof elements**_ can you dig up to make your case airtight, as if to a jury? That's a biggie! Whenever you're doing your research, you must make your top priority be the identification of dramatic proof elements that will let your prospects see that magical combination of a _**powerful promise**_ sandwiched together with _**compelling proof**_ And of course, you want to identify a persuasive, _**user-friendly proposition**_ to enable someone to decide to take action immediately. This is one reason you want to research the most successful ads of your competition, to see which offers and prices have worked best for them. 

You want to keep researching until you've researched everything that can conceivably help you write great copy. Then keep researching some more until you find this magical combination we've talked about— _**a truly powerful promise joined at the hip with compelling proof**_ For example, be inspired by the fantastic book title we discussed earlier, _French Women Don't Get Fat: The Secret of Eating for Pleasure)._ 

That short title explodes with a great promise (don't get fat) with a compelling proof element, that French women, who eat for sensuous pleasure, are slim. All in that one little title. It's a beautiful piece of marketing. You don't have to have yours be that short. But you do want to emerge from your research with that powerful combination— _**a truly powerful promise joined at the hip with compelling proof**_ 

Always remember that the proof is like the gold that your prospects look for. We all have very sensitive BS meters. If you're just going to crank out a lot of adjectives about how "super fantastic" something is, it's just going to get thrown away. But unless you're a total fraud and willing to risk getting arrested and sent away to Club Fed, you can't invent proof elements, and your prospects know it. So that's what they're looking for in your advertising. 

You also want to be looking for bullets. I have a great bullet collection at home. Some day I'll share some in my online ezine, _Bencivenga Bullets._ 

## You, too, should start a collection, a little folder, full of great bullets that are

- 254 - 

very intriguing and provoke wild curiosity. You can then take those bullets from other people's ads and use their same framework to encapsulate the information about your product that you've turned up in your research. 

Throughout your research, you're also looking for great elements to put in your body copy as well as potential headline material, as well as for anything you find simply interesting. If _you_ find it interesting, other people will too. 

Sometimes you'll come across something that will be in a totally different category than what you normally look for, whether you were looking for big fat promises or strong proof elements. Yet, even though this item of research supports neither your promise nor your proof, you still find it intensely interesting. Be sure to make a note of it anyway, and you can often find some way to work it into your copy later. _If it's very interesting, don't overlook it!_ You can almost always find a way to harness that human interest later on, when you start your writing process. 

## **Your Goal—Gather at Least Seven Times More Information Than You Can Possibly Use** 

Your goal in all your research is to gather at least seven times more information than you can possibly use. This was something that John Caples personally said to me. He said that's the advice he got from his copy chief many, many years ago. 

When I worked at BBDO, in the direct response division, John Caples was not a copy chief. He was a copywriter, a very nice guy, someone revered throughout the advertising community and a goodwill ambassador for the agency. He was his own one-man department. 

He was even on a different floor than we copywriters were. Nevertheless, he would occasionally visit our copywriter bullpen, look over our copy, hear our ideas, and help us with his advice. A wonderful man. 

## **How I Organize My Notes** 

Here's a basic way I write up my research notes. I just type up lots of notes in the sequence that I find them. No particular order, just a new paragraph for each new thought or bit of research that I come across, plus the source where I found it, which is critical for verification and documentation later. 

For example, if it's a book, I'll read the entire book, every single page of the book. In fact, I learned this approach from Gene Schwartz. 

## Back when I was given my first assignment for Rodale Books, they told me

- 255 - 

that Gene had written the control for this book. I didn't want to look at his promotion at first, as I didn't want to be overly influenced by what he had done or _depressed_ by what he had done, because he usually wrote such great copy and I had to compete with it. 

After I had finished my research and first draft, I finally took a look at his bullets. I thought, "Just to be thorough, let me see what Gene came up with." Then I did get depressed because he had done a much better job of research. He was phenomenal in his research. 

I realized that the only chance I might have to beat Gene's package was to figure out what he had done to get his fantastic bullets. With most of his bullets, he would describe the secret in a tantalizing way and then say, referring to the book from which it came, _see page 77_ or _seepage 132_ or whatever. 

So, whatever pages his bullets came from, I would turn to those pages and see how he extracted such powerful bullets from information I had glossed over. 

That's how I learned what he did, and you can do the same. As I recommended earlier, just order the newsletters, books, and other products offered in the great promotional pieces that arrive in your mailbox from Agora, Rodale, Boardroom, Phillips or KCI. Order the book, newsletter, or course; hang on to the advertising; and when the product arrives, review the promotional bullets and then find their source in the book or newsletter to see how the copywriter came up with such powerful bullets. That's the best way to learn how to write killer bullets. 

It's how I learned from Gene, just by mimicking what he had done. I believed that my only chance of beating Gene in a head-to-head test was to outresearch him. That's what I did. I made my piece longer and used the powerful concept I mentioned earlier, making my package contain more valuable information than his package, and also look more valuable. His looked just like a sales pitch. It was a powerful sales pitch, but an obvious sales pitch nonetheless. 

We'll talk about the package I wrote shortly, and I'll show you it to you. It was for the book _New Choices in Natural Healing._ 

And using this twofold strategy—of making the mailing look like a valuable paperback book instead of a sales pitch and including many more specific bullets—I was in fact able to beat Gene's package by a wide margin. 

But, in a way, it was his package too, because I learned his technique just from looking at his bullets and seeing that my bullets were much weaker. So he taught me how to take my research notes. At every step, be looking for powerful material you can turn into tantalizing bullets. 

- 2 5 6 - 

To summarize what he taught me, you have to read every single sentence of a book and then stop and think about it. Read another sentence, stop and think about it. The question you ask of every sentence you read is— _"Is there any possible drop of juice I can squeeze from this orange and turn into marketing enticement in some way? "_ 

Don't let a single paragraph go by without pausing and asking that question. This takes a lot of time, but you can't expect to race along and catch all the latent opportunities for bullets that exist. They don't always leap out at you. You have to ponder each sentence, each paragraph, and ask, _"Is there anything at all I could make of this? "_ 

To accomplish this process, Gene had one way, I have another. After I got to know him personally, Gene told me that he would save time by not typing his notes. Instead, he would just underline in the book he was researching whatever he found interesting or promotionally useful. 

He would then give the underlined book to a transcriber with instructions to type up the underlined passages. The transcriber would type this up in a computer document that Gene could then import into his word processing program. 

That done, he would start massaging and manipulating everything that came out of the book. 

This enabled him to win many battles with lawyers who, upon reviewing the legality of his copy, would admonish Gene, saying, _"You can't say that! You 're making too bold a claim."_ 

Gene would reply, "Check the book. The book I'm writing about says you can just rub your tummy and lose weight. I'm not saying that. The _author_ is, and his right to say it is protected by the First Amendment. My ad copy is simply and accurately telling the reader what is in the book. And if it's in the book, you can't forbid me from putting it in my ad." 

So, according to Gene, if you want to write great copy, first get your author to say the most outrageous things in a book, though they of course should be true. _(LAUGHTER)._ 

They should be outrageous but true. And then you have the freedom to say, "Believe it or not, the author says that you can..." And then you're quoting what the author said. 

Before, I mentioned that Gene and I have different methods for taking our notes. If he were researching a book, he'd underline everything he found useful or interesting. 

- 257 - 

I on the other hand prefer typing up my notes myself or dictating them into a recorder for someone else to type up. I feel that this method is superior than mere underlining because it enables me to add additional thoughts, headline ideas, and my own observations right next to the relevant notes from the original source. It does take longer, but I believe results in many more useful ideas. 

Once my notes are typed up, I then print them out. Then, to organize everything, I read them over and figure out the major topics I'll be covering in my copy. Once I know those, I then categorize each one of my notes into the appropriate category. 

For example, in preparing this material on how to do research, I researched the topic, added my own thoughts, then printed them all out, as you can see below... 

**==> picture [347 x 248] intentionally omitted <==**

Then, as you can see, I would go through the entire list of notes and handwrite, in the left margin, the topic each note relates to. In the example above, my first few entries had to do with an introduction to the topic of research. The next item had to do with sources for my research, and so on. In this way, I organized everything I wanted to say on the topic of research. 

Next I would cut and paste all my notes on, say, "the introduction," and cut and paste them into their own new document. Then, within the introduction, I'd number each thought in terms of its logical sequence. And then I'd have much of the piece already written. My main task at that point would be to string each of the thoughts together with the right language. In this way, as you can see, much of the "writing" actually takes place in the _research_ stage. 

- 258 - 

This process also results in a very logical arrangement of your thoughts. Readership studies have shown that when you arrange your copy blocks in fairly logical sequence, you make your copy very easy to read. That's why you may want to organize it according to _The Persuasion Equation®,_ as this is very close to the way people think: Is this product solving a problem of mine? What is the promise that it will do so? What's the proof? What do I have to pay to acquire it? 

That's a very logical sequence, one that fits perfectly with the way your prospect's mind is likely working. 

By contrast, if you write your copy by putting your offer first, then bounce to the proof of your product's claims, then bounce to the problem the product solves, and back and forth again to the offer, the copy starts to have a very disoriented, jumpy, jarring feeling, making it uncomfortable and difficult to read. 

You want to make your entire copy flow like the "greased chute" that other copywriters have talked about. Each paragraph gently, smoothly flows right into the next one, all the way through to the last period. 

You who approve copy should look for that. You don't want speed bumps. If you're feeling a little speed bump as you're reading the copy, there's something wrong. It should flow like silk. And that's much more likely to happen if the writer has first put all his or her thoughts into a logical sequence. 

It doesn't have to be pure logic, as if it's a philosophical treatise. What you want is a sensible, natural path of readership that seems to take you effortlessly from point one to point two, then to point three, and so on, in a naturally occurring, comfortable progression. 

That's how I organize my notes. 

Before, I mentioned that if there are many notes to be taken, I may choose to dictate them into a recording device. 

Today, if you prefer to dictate your notes instead of type them yourself, you don't have to go to the great expense of having your own personal stenographer. The Internet will come to your rescue. 

A service I've used is called Copytalk.com. You can call them up or join their service online and use your telephone to dictate your notes. I have the deluxe service, which currently is about a hundred dollars a month, including toll-free calling. For this sum, you can dictate an unlimited amount of words. It's like having your own stenographer available 24/7 and paying a "salary" of just $25 a week. 

## What they do is they take your recording and send it over to India, where

- 259 - 

people type it up and email it right back to you. It's usually quite accurate. 

Copytalk started as an email service. If you want to respond to your email but don't want to take the time to type messages yourself, say if you're on the road, you can call Copytalk, dictate your email, and tell them where to send it, and it's done. If on the other hand you want to proofread it for accuracy first, you can tell them to send it to _you,_ and that's what they'll do. You proofread it, make any corrections, then send it out yourself. 

Usually they'll send your dictated messages back the very same day, maybe next morning at the latest. Almost all are accurate, requiring very few corrections. 

As of now, they let you dictate only for about eight minutes at a time, because so many writers, of all types, started using this as their dictation service, not as an email response service. 

So you just dictate in eight-minute segments. What I do is dictate into a recorder and let it run for eight-minute lengths. This way, I'll have to make a few phone calls if I have a lot of research I'm dictating. Nevertheless, it saves a lot of keystrokes. 

(NOTE: At the seminar, a member of the audience recommended a service which has proven even superior, called CyberSecretaries at www.youdictate.com. What's so good about this service is that there's no minimum monthly fee. You pay a paltry amount per word that you dictate. Also, their transcribers, at least now, are located in the United States, not India, so they have an even better understanding of American English. With their system, you can also hit a "pause" button as you're dictating, so you can mentally compose your next sentence. This will make you much more productive.) 

Speaking of productivity, let me now reveal my favorite... 

## **Copywriter Productivity Secrets** 

The best productivity secret I've ever learned anywhere is called the "Pareto Principle," or the "80/20 Rule." 

To illustrate this, let me tell you about one of the most successful executives that I've ever known. He had a sign over his desk, _"Is this leading me to my main goal?"_ 

Dozens of times a day he'd catch himself in an idle conversation or a meeting that wasn't going anywhere. He'd see that sign and think, "I've got to get back to the 20 percent of my activities that yield 80 percent of my value to my company." He tried to get everybody in the company thinking the same way. 

- 260 - 

The universality of this 80/20 Rule is astounding. It applies everywhere in our world. 20 percent of the authors sell 80 percent of the books. 20 percent of the customers generate 80 percent of your sales. 20 percent of your carpet gets 80 percent of the wear. 20 percent of your wardrobe gets 80 percent of your use. 20 percent of your customers give you 80 percent of your headaches. 

Now it may not always be exactly 20/80. Sometimes the effect is even more concentrated, such as 10/90 or 15/85. Sometimes it's less concentrated, say, 30/70. But it's all the same concept— _"The Rule of the Vital Few and the Trivial Many."_ 

That is, in any activity there are a few vital factors that will give you outsized results compared to all the other factors involved. And if you look for the 20 percent of your activities that are resulting in 80 percent of your success in life, you will be amazed at how you can then cut out so much of the remaining 80 percent of your activities, switch that time into the 20 percent that really counts, and you will leverage your success enormously. 

## **Copywriter Productivity Secret #1 The Pareto Principle in Action** 

Let me give you an example. For me, as a writer, the most profitable use of my time is to write. That's what generates money. When I'm not writing, I'm not generating money. 

I learned this secret long ago from Frank Bettger, the great life insurance agent who wrote the book _How I Raised Myself From Failure to Success in Selling._ At one point in his career, he discovered the Pareto Principle and started applying it. He didn't know it was called that, but he did realize that the only time he really made any money was when he was _in front of a prospect selling insurance._ 

So he started seeing how much time he wasted in meetings, phone calls, chitchat with other agents, paperwork, and other non-income-producing activities. 

To his astonishment, he found that a huge portion of every working day was being frittered away on the 80 percent of his activities that do _not_ produce income. He vowed to cut back on those activities and strive to put himself in front of hot prospects a much bigger percentage of his time. As day follows night, his income soared, just exploded. 

## **Copywriter Productivity Secret #2** 

## **My Three-Hour Rule** 

As a writer, I feel the best thing I can always spend my time doing is writing. I have developed what I call my three-hour rule. 

- 261 - 

From the moment I get up in the morning, my goal is to log at least three solid hours doing the 20 percent that has yielded 80 percent of success in my life— writing. If I'm on a roll and manage to accomplish six or even eight hours of writing, so much the better. But the minimum _must_ be three. Seven days a week. 

For me, that's where all the payoff is. Yes, it's so easy to be seduced by answering email, going through all the papers on your desk, clearing away the magazines and junk mail, attending meetings, planning new trips, and so on. 

If you stop to analyze it, you'll be surprised at how many distractions are tugging at your sleeve at all times to pull you away from your critical 20 percent. 

Once you become conscious of this, you realize how important it is to relentlessly keep getting back on track. I believe that if you have a quiet, uninterrupted three hours a day to throw at any project, you can accomplish almost anything. 

Even now, as I am retiring, I'm a grumpy bear if the end of the day has come and I haven't even logged a half hour of my three hours that I intend to log every day until I die. Yes, even though I'm retiring, I will still write, not for clients but for myself, mainly to my _Bullets_ readers or on other personal projects. 

## **Copywriter Productivity Secret #3 The Power of Focus** 

This is another very powerful secret of copywriter productivity—focus. Focus is all about specializing in what you do best. It applies to copywriters and companies alike. Let me cite a famous example... 

What a tragedy it was when IBM felt it was so strong in mainframes that, when other types of computers came along, IBM tried to be everything to everybody in the computer business. That's not possible, not even for a giant company like IBM. The copywriter or company that tries to be everything to everybody is doomed to being nothing special to anybody. 

Indeed, based on the rather arrogant assumption that it could dominate every little nook, niche, and nuance of the computer industry, IBM lost untold billions of dollars. They should have stuck to their knitting—mainframes, branching out from that bastion of strength. 

Many, many companies make the same mistake. They think they will broaden out into an area where they're really not sure what they're doing, or they'll acquire a company that they really have no business acquiring, thinking that they can produce superior results because they're such whizbangs at management and know 

- 262 - 

how to market. 

This is a common fallacy because it misses a third factor needed for success, one that's all too often overlooked. 

Yes, it's critical to have good management. And, yes, it's essential to know how to wield powerful marketing. But success in business is a three-legged stool. 

The first two are not enough because you also need _in-depth knowledge of that specific industry._ This, too, is critical. For example, I've never played golf in my life. As good as I might think I am as a copywriter, if I want to write an ad about golf, I've got a steep learning curve to overcome before I can even begin to think about writing with John Carlton on golf. I don't know a thing about golf, except that it involves a long club, a little white ball, and seems to frustrate the bejesus out of grown men and women. 

But John is already a golfer. He plays many of the country's great courses with his golfing buddies. And he's terrific at writing about golf, one of the areas that he specializes in. It really wouldn't make sense for me to try to write an ad about golf when I haven't played a day of golf. I'd be at an immediate disadvantage against someone like John who has an intimate knowledge of the game; shares the love and enthusiasm that fellow golfers feel for the sport; and intimately knows their likes, desires, beliefs, passions, and irritations. 

The same is true of every business and industry. Often you hear of companies that merge and two years later they're unmerging. You hear of so many corporations that think they can run anything because they know management and marketing, _yet they don't have the specific knowledge of that particular field._ Two years after they've taken their misguided plunge, you read about them in the trade press, retreating, cutting back, downsizing, selling off what they acquired, retreating from the field of battle, licking their wounds. 

Likewise I advise you, if you're thinking of getting into businesses that are far afield from your company's knowledge base, _beware!_ It's a very dangerous thing. If you don't know the ins and outs of that specific business, what's worked, what's flopped, the products that have sold well consistently over the past 50 years, the bombs that have blown up in people's faces, the key contacts, the best vendors, the main players, the industry mavens who share a common core of assumptions and understandings that you haven't learned yet, watch out. Your new venture is a balloon in search of a pin. 

-263 - 

## **Copywriter Productivity Secret #4** 

## **My "Time" Budget** 

Here's how I budget my time on an assignment: 40 percent research, 40 percent writing, and 20 percent polishing. 

Believe it or not, for one of these heavy-duty bookalogs or magalogs where I'm giving away a ton of valuable information right in the mailing, it will take me a three-month cycle. That has always proven to be OK for me because I have always wanted to outperform anybody I'm going up against. All factors being equal, I've found that the best way to win is to just put in more time on a project. More time for superior research, superior headlines, superior copy thanks to endless polishing, superior premiums, and a superior offer. If you devote the time to be superior in all those aspects, more often than not, your results will be superior to your competition, which in my case has always been other copywriters. 

When I was a younger copywriter, most of the direct mail that was out there consisted of two-page letters. Wanting to compete and win against those two-page letters, I reasoned that if I write a four-page letter and fill it up with really good copy points, I will be able to outsell the two-page letter. And it almost always worked that way. 

Then a lot of other people started making their letters longer. And I would always keep upping the ante, as I was one of the leading freelancers for many years. If the trend jumped to eight-page letters, I went to a 12-pager. I remember the first time I went to twelve pages. My client thought I was crazy. But the 12pager won. 

Those of us, mostly my clients and I, who saw this happening, wondered, "How far can this go?" 

Well, Sindy Berner at Rodale remembers when my magalog was about 40 pages shorter than the actual book that I was selling. _(LAUGHTER)_ The book was 120 pages and my magalog was 84 pages. Actually, the magalog was better than the book. _(LAUGHTER)_ 

But it shows you that when you're talking to an interested marketplace and you're giving lots of good information away, there's almost no limit to what people will eagerly devour. The truth is, I don't know where this long-term trend to longer and longer copy will end. Offline, it costs a lot of money to produce these very long packages. Eighty-four pages takes a fair a bit of color work and money to produce. So if you wanted to double that to 168 pages, that gets quite expensive offline. But online there's no limit. Online you have the world's cheapest printing press hooked up to the world's cheapest post office. So there's almost no limit. 

- 264 - 

But, as always, the information must be truly interesting and valuable to your target audience. 

And this is why I believe that the future of direct marketing is mostly online— because the expenses of marketing have become so low online. We have lots of problems, of course, with spam, but as I was saying before, if you can make your ezines and other communications valuable and content rich so that people love to open them, you'll be golden. 

This is the main principle, making your first contact, your advertising, and every ezine you write truly valuable, not just a sales pitch. 

Your communications must always be truly valuable if you want them to get opened, and "truly valuable" is a moving target, depending on what your prospects want more information about at any given moment. 

When you do eventually get to your sales pitch, you'll get a much higher response because the built-in value gets you a much higher open rate and readership. You never want to become known as an ezine that's always pitching something to buy every day. 

A very good example of how to do it right, in many different styles, is Agora Publishing. If you sign up for _Early to Rise_ or their other ezines, you'll see a very effective online marketing strategy. 

I've heard rumors of the amounts of money that Agora is making from their online customers, and I wouldn't even repeat them, they sound so astounding, the amount of money I've heard they are making online. But if you look at their ezines and sign up for a few, you'll see why. They have brilliant copy, interspersed with lots of valuable little tips, coming to you regularly on a wide range of subjects of interest to you. And they do it really well. So do yourself a favor and subscribe to some of Agora's online subscriptions. 

## **Copywriter Productivity Secret #5 Deepak Chopra's Secret: Regularity of Schedule Vastly Increases Your Productivity** 

Here's another copywriter productivity secret. Deepak Chopra, the well-known doctor and best-selling author from India, has many good ideas on how to live a more balanced and productive life. 

When I was talking with him once, he said that regularity of schedule vastly improves your productivity. At first I didn't believe this because I have always been a believer in _the productivity of momentum._ 

_-_ 265 - 

That is, if I'm on a roll, even if it's 11 o'clock at night, I have always wanted to keep my momentum rolling, even if that means I keep writing until 2 or 3 in the morning. 

_If it's flowing, keep it going! So what if I sleep late the next day?_ 

And that's how I worked for much of my career. But if you work this way, sporadically working in binges at different hours, I now believe it does more harm than good to your productivity. That is what I learned from Deepak Chopra. 

If you write at different times and change the times when you go to sleep according to what you feel like doing on any given day, you throw off your daily rhythm. If you don't have a time in the day when you regularly do your writing, you never get to establish a daily rhythm. 

The whole universe runs on a rhythm. The sun rises every 24 hours, like clockwork. The planets, the galaxies, the birds of the air, the grain of the field, the flowers of the forest, the fish of the sea, the seasons of the year, the tides of the ocean—all flow in a harmony of regular rhythms, resulting in a sort of frictionless flow that, in my view, produces the highest productivity and least resistance of all. 

I tried writing Deepak's way, at a regular, preset time every day, and going to sleep at the same time each night, and it made a world of difference in my productivity. Try it yourself, and I bet you'll experience the same delightful result. 

Don't bounce all over the lot with your daily schedule. It never lets your mind establish a rhythm, a regular time when all of your powers, all of your subconscious abilities and creativity know it's their regular time to show up and shine. Even though you might have good momentum on that one night when you said, _"Routine be damned; I'm on a roll and I'm going with it,"_ this will probably throw you off for several days after, when you'll notice that you are much _less_ productive than you usually are. 

Now if you're a night owl who writes every night between 8:00 p.m. and 3:00 a.m., that's fine. You're in a regular rhythm and that's the main point. 

But if you try to start writing on most days at, say, 8:00 a.m., then, as you're feeling pressured by an approaching deadline, start staying up until 3:00 a.m., your usual rhythm at 8:00 in the morning will be shot. You're being erratic in your schedule and this is counterproductive. 

This is what Deepak Chopra, a master at tapping one's inner potential told me, and it worked like a charm for me, even though I thought my original way would prove better. 

## Instead of being highly irregular in my working schedule, I started to go to bed

- 266 - 

early every night, no later than 10:00, and rising early, usually by 5:00 or even earlier, without even setting an alarm clock. 

Indeed, I had heard it said that one hour of sleep before midnight is worth two after midnight. I don't know if this is true for everyone, but once I heard it, I started going to bed early, and I did find I needed less sleep and got into an amazingly productive writing rhythm, getting up early every morning, going right to the computer, and logging my three hours before doing almost anything else. I started burning up the track with the volume of work I produced that way. 

I found that for me, the morning time is the most productive of all. Copy just pours out of me as if from a faucet, usually with great clarity of thinking, and all so effortlessly, once I fell into the regular rhythm of doing this every morning. 

## **Copywriter Productivity Secret #6 How to Gain Six to Eight Extra Hours of Productivity Every Day!** 

I have made what follows a regular practice, and it has made a tremendous difference for me. First, thanks to Deepak's recommendation, I do my writing at the same time of day, first thing in the morning. 

Next, and this is the key that will explode your productivity, just before going to bed at night, review the material you're working on. I say to myself, "Great subconscious mind, I don't want to work on this too hard, so just figure out an answer for me by morning." 

And by the morning I'm brimming over with ideas that are perfect for the project. I didn't lose any sleep over it, not at all. I didn't toss and turn or even consciously think about anything regarding the project. 

It's not some major, intense thing you're doing with your subconscious mind. Rather, it's a gentle and clear request. I just say, "Great subconscious mind, you beautiful gift from God, I'd like if you can work this out overnight. Let me wake up with the answer." 

And I usually do! It will pour out like a faucet in the morning, with all the subordinate ideas I need to make it work. 

If you jump on your project first thing in the morning, it's like a computer dump. In effect, you gain six to eight extra hours of productivity, because you're capturing what your subconscious mind figured out during the last six to eight hours of sleep. If you don't capture this precious product upon rising, you lose it. As the day proceeds, your conscious mind takes more and more control of your awareness, and your subconscious recedes to its subterranean world. If you want to 

- 2 6 7 - 

capture all that good stuff that your subconscious has been working on all night, let it talk to you right after you wake up, when your conscious mind has been asleep for several hours, is not so dominant, and the usual barriers to accessing your subconscious mind are not so high. 

This is such a great productivity secret because, in effect, while you're sleeping your subconscious mind is easily handling millions of activities. It's keeping your heart going, regulating millions of cellular transactions and repairs, controlling your breathing and brain waves, watching that every organ is getting whatever it needs, supervising millions and millions of complex tasks, all effortlessly. It _certainly_ can handle an additional little request for a great new headline before morning without waking you up! _(LAUGHTER)_ 

You'll find this an amazingly effective technique. In effect, it's like you just gained another six to eight hours of productivity every single day. You just let your subconscious mind go through lots and lots of permutations and you gain six to eight uninterrupted hours of "work" without really working at all. 

## **Copywriter Productivity Secret #7 Pressure Is Your Enemy, Relaxation Your Friend (Why Athletes and Writers "Choke")** 

Always bear in mind that pressure is your enemy and relaxation your friend. Have you ever thought of what it means when you hear about athletes or writers "choking"? 

What does it mean when a great hitter finds himself in the World Series and can't get a hit to save his life? Maybe he's even been the batting champion all year. But he gets into the World Series when his team needs him most, when the whole world is watching, and on this great stage that will define his reputation for the rest of his life, he strikes out again and again, hits into double plays that erase the runners on base, and starts to stink out the stadium. Fans who cheered him all his life now start to boo, and he'll hear those boos for as long as he lives. It's sad. 

This doesn't mean that he's not trying hard enough. It's just the opposite. With all this pressure, _he s trying too_ hard!_ 

It's not that he doesn't want to succeed in the World Series. He wants to succeed _so much_ that he becomes rigid with conscious control, as if the force of his willpower to succeed is coursing through every muscle. And it is! The only problem is, when you forcefully will your mind and body to achieve, you become rigid. Your desire to perform becomes so intense that you're rigid, not relaxed. And when you're not relaxed, you can't be nearly as flexible, spontaneous, or creative. 

- 268 - 

When most people, whether athletes or writers, face this terrifying situation and find themselves freezing up, their natural instinct is to _try even harder,_ which produces only more of the same rigidity. And for an athlete or writer, that's death because neither your muscles nor your mind will work as freely, loosely, fluidly, or creatively as when you're feeling relaxed. 

So what some experienced athletes have learned, and this is a tip that we can all learn from, is that the most important skill you have when you're in a highpressure situation is to know how to relax. If you can do that, you'll do much better, because the channels to your subconscious mind are open when you're relaxed. 

This is very important, that when you're writing, you don't want to feel rigid. And deadlines will do that to you, make you feel quite pressured and rigid. 

What's the solution? 

First, you should cultivate some method that relaxes you, as this is one of your most important skill sets as a writer. Learn meditation, self-talk, yoga, visualization, Tibetan breathing techniques, _anything_ that reliably makes you feel more relaxed. 

Second, learn to be a better planner. Remember that "double-plus-10-percent rule" I mentioned earlier and use it! For example, when you're taking on an assignment, make sure you secure enough time to do it right. 

Naturally, your client silently suspects that no matter how much time he or she allots, you will succumb to the Term Paper Syndrome (even if I do give him six months, I _know_ he's going to start this in the second week of the fifth month! Who's he kidding?) 

But if they're intelligent clients like the ones I've had the pleasure to work with, they'll try to accommodate the time you say you need. They might not like that you're taking so much time, but most of all they want the job done right, and you must tell them that this requires whatever time it takes to let you do it right. _There is no greater pressure reliever than knowing, weeks before the job is due, that you 've already done all the research, you have a blockbuster headline in the cooler, and you know that much of your writing is already done._ 

Once you do buy yourself a fair amount of time, jump on the assignment quickly. Don't let it turn into the Term Paper Syndrome. Start doing the easy things fast, especially the research. Remember the movie _Patton?_ Imagine you're like General Patton, the way he charged up through Sicily in World War II, literally racing to engage the enemy. He never held territory, preferring to advance 

- 269 - 

rapidly and relentlessly. 

It's the same for you on an assignment. You're not trying to be a perfectionist. You're trying to move this job as fast as you can. Perfection and polishing can come later. The beginning is not the time to be perfect. You want to race as fast as you can, just like General Patton slicing his way up through Italy, to attack, attack, attack. 

## **Copywriter Productivity Secret #8 Resist Perfectionism** 

As I say, in the early stages of an assignment, you want to fiercely resist perfectionism, which will always slow you to a crawl. Another good way to do this, in addition to driving ahead at a Patton-like pace, is to divide the whole assignment into its five separate stages. 

_Step one,_ do your research. Don't even worry about getting brilliant headlines or solving a problem for your prospect. Just quickly dive into research and keep diving every day. This is hard to do, because you already are feeling anxiety about coming up with a winning headline and approach, especially if you're going up against a powerful control package. But don't let your anxiety force you into jumping the gun and starting to write and lock in a headline or direction before your research is finished. Dive into the research and keep diving. 

_Step two,_ organize your notes, as I've said before, into like sections. 

_Step three,_ come up with your main theme and prioritize your sale arguments accordingly. Then, only then, move on to... 

_Step four,_ writing. 

_**And finally, step five,**_ polish, polish, polish. 

When you map out the process in this way, you can see that if you try to come to a perfect polished headline in the process of doing research, it's almost like trying to drive with the brakes on. _You can't do it!_ You're trying to do too many things at once. Just let each phase of the writing process have its own time to flourish. You can't grow corn overnight. It needs its different stages, the planting, the cultivation, the growing, and so on. That's what you should do with your writing assignments. Allow each of the five stages its time in the sun. 

Follow these recommendations and you'll be far more relaxed about your work, much more creative and productive. You'll also _like_ your work much more. 

— — _End of Session #6_

Yes, you can have a big winner. But if your mailing universe is only 10 million people, that's all you're going to mail to. The rollout and resulting sales will be limited to that universe. But I learned that Mike is a genius at finding much bigger universes than at first meet the eye. 

The way I research a product for copywriting, Mike researches lists and will often identify big lists that you'd never think could work but, after he recommends them, they do, sometimes for products that wouldn't seem to have an obvious connection. 

That's because there are many large lists that might not match up with your product's profile, at least in the way that traditional list brokers analyze them.
