In the next 10 minutes I'll have everybody right up to speed, so you don't need to spend the 20 years I've spent. 

For those of you who know these basics, please listen to them like you've never heard them before, and I think you'll gain some value from them. Then we'll look at the advanced course in lists and see what happens when you take the game to a higher level. Then I'll give you a potpourri of breakthrough strategies, other ways of thinking about lists, as well. 

Gary called this portion of the day something that's too laudatory for me to repeat _("Secrets of the World's Greatest List Broker")._ So that's why I've entitled these next 20 or 30 minutes simply as _"What Gary Just Said."_ 

277 - 

_-_ 

OK—Lists 101, the crash course. 

Here are 15 checkpoints to understanding a list... 

**==> picture [421 x 246] intentionally omitted <==**

_1. The source._ This is the first thing we want to know about a list. When we look at a list of subscribers to a newsletter or a magazine or people who've bought a particular product, there's a great deal of dimension and depth that we can examine to understand who's on that list, how it ticks, how it works. The ultimate goal, of course, for everybody—for me, for you as my client, for the writers in the room, everybody—is to mail as much mail as possible. And to sell as much product as possible. 

We want to understand lists as deeply and profoundly as we can and ultimately bring a lot of certainty to this process of list selection. The metaphor I like to use is that you're putting puppet strings on the marketplace. First you want to bring a level of certainty to how you're engaging people and speaking to them, which is what Gary's been talking about. Then you want to bring a tremendous degree of certainty to the way in which we decide who to contact. 

Do those two things right, and you bring that much more certainty to the process. In the case of a product that's mature and already validated to be working elsewhere, you'll assure yourself of a good result with the new list. On a product launch, this certainty will allow you to trust the results that come in and get a reliable barometer reading, so you'll know if you really have a hot product or not. 

So, number one, the source of a list. Where were the transactions generated for these people to get on the list? Was it a space ad, was it a direct mail piece, 

- 278 - 

was it an infomercial? If it was on TV, was it 30 minutes, two minutes, or one minute? You'll see very different behavior from people responding to television commercials of different lengths. That's why we want to know the source, everything we can know about the medium, which guides us to how the people on that list will likely behave. 

_**2. The offer.**_ In strict direct mail terms, the offer, of course, refers to the deal. Was it bill-me-later, was it three installments, was it cash with order? Was there a credit card option? If there was a premium, was it an editorial premium? A radio? An umbrella? All of these things help you gauge the level of commitment on the part of that person when he or she engaged in that transaction. 

_**3. The price or monetary commitment**_ What did they spend? How much money? Ultimately, in a sense, particularly in a bill-me environment, the tougher thing to get from people is not response but a commitment to pay. Copywriters might wince at that statement, but we really need to have a great sense of people's commitment. A commitment in a bill-me environment means that someone will be willing to pay the bill. We want to have a sense of what people have spent, by looking at the price or monetary value in conjunction with the offer. We have a sense of what they spent, what was the deal, and how they met their commitment. 

_**4. Mailing cycles.**_ We want to have a sense of how often the company who owns the list mails. If it's the Wisconsin Cheeseman and most of their business, probably more than 80/20, is holiday gift business, then of their million and a half transactions a year, maybe 90 percent are in the months of October and November. If that list works for us in a test at the end of the year, and we assume it will contribute to our campaigns in April, we're not being realistic. It's not going to happen, because the bulk of that business is in the fall. 

Some companies mail every single month and put up those transactions each month in what we call a "monthly hotline." And those names are available each month. That's what we mean by mailing cycles. 

_**5. Mail piece format.**_ I'll talk more about this in a few minutes in the advanced course. But we want to know, was it an envelope—either a #10, six-by-nine, a jumbo? We want to know if the package is what many people refer to as a component package, meaning it has a letter, a lift note, possibly a brochure, an order card, and a BRE. It has components inside the envelope, unlike a bookalog, magalog, or postcard mailing. Regardless of the subject matter you're selling, your price point, or anything else, the mail piece format and the way in which a list was generated have a lot to do with the behavior of that list going forward. We'll talk more about this in a minute, but for now, remember that the mail piece format is 

- 279 - 

very, very critical. 

_**6. A transaction versus update information.**_ If we're in May and we want to consider April's hotline update, did those transactions actually happen in April or are they several months old? When does a transaction actually hit the newest update? One of the key principles in direct mail is what we call "recency," the people that have brought most frequently are the ones that respond the best. Why? Because the buying event took place very, very recently. Someone who bought something last month is more likely to buy than someone who bought three months ago, six months ago, twelve months ago, etc. So that's recency. 

_7._ _**Frequency**_ is _how often_ someone buys, and we're going to talk about this a little bit more in a few minutes. Frequency is generally regarded as a good thing because the more someone buys, the better purchaser he or she is. If that person bought the Air and Space Ingots from the Franklin Mint and bought 82 of them, that's a pretty good direct mail buyer. However, I do have a concern about frequent buyers, which we'll talk about in a few minutes. 

_**8. The universe.**_ You always want to keep your eye on the size of the universe of the lists you're testing. If we test a one-month hotline or if we test a list of subscribers from the last six months or if we test a list of three-month credit card buyers or any of the permutations we can come up with and have reason to segment, how many names are available on the upside? 

What's the upside of that segmentation, and is it worth testing into? We devise a "select," or a segment, that we think will have value and productivity for what our mail piece looks like and what our goal is. If we test 6,000 and the entire segment comprises 12,000 people, we're investing in the mailing of 6,000 names with not a lot of payoff if that works. So we want to make sure we're testing into meaningful audiences. 

_**9. Service bureau.**_ The service bureau is the company that maintains the database or maintains the list that we're looking to order from. We just want to have a sense that there's integrity there, that it's a service bureau that we know, feel comfortable with, and can trust. We want to know that the fulfillment will be exactly what we specify, that we'll get the right segment, that we can count on it and not have a concern that they're running machines with rubber bands or something like that. 

_**10. Interest and affinity.**_ I know there are people here who publish books, newsletters, and magazines. There may also be people who sell sweaters. There are some selling educational programs. I know a lot of the conversation is about publishing, **but** I certainly acknowledge there's a range of things being sold here. In all cases, we want to have a sense that people on the lists we're considering are 

- 280 - 

interested in what we're selling. There are some topics like the ones we've been discussing today, particularly health and personal finance, which in a sense are of interest to everybody, or at least could be. 

So there's a universal nature to those subjects that enables the most powerful creative pieces in health and personal finance to be sold to people on lists very frequently that have nothing to do with health and personal finance. We'll talk about that in a little bit. 

If you're selling something about golf or fly-fishing or quilting, the odds are you need to go to somebody who has engaged in a transaction in those hobbies or interest areas before. People either fly-fish or they don't. And a mail piece won't talk someone into fly-fishing—not in the numbers you'd need to make it work in direct mail. But when you're talking about a product or a value proposition around health or personal finance, you can what I call "trigger" someone to take 

responsibility or interest in those areas because sooner or later everybody needs to deal with that. So we'll talk a little bit more about that too. But that's how you want to think about interest and affinity. 

_11. Demographics_ refers to the percentage of men and women, the average age, income, the composition and complexion of a list. Most of the time you have to have a sense of your market. The best marketers can put themselves in the shoes of the marketplace even if they themselves would never be a buyer of that product. 

In some cases, such as with magazines, the list owners will have done a lot of research in order to sell their ad space to advertisers. So they have a pretty good sense of who their readerships and constituents are. 

But most of the time, this is not the case. For most of the lists we look at, we need to infer who these people are. I need to infer who's bought from Neiman Marcus versus Fingerhut versus some nonbranded book promising health benefits. That's my inference to make because I'm getting very little research at all, or I'm not getting research I can rely on. So I need to infer who it is I'm going after, based on what the product was that they bought, the mail piece and copy that they responded to, and what they spent. 

_12. Usage_ refers to who else has used the list. In a sense, that's an area of proof for the productivity of the list. When people sell lists, they usually say that the following 28 companies have used it successfully. That helps. But I think in terms of the work I've done on Gary's packages, and certainly one of the very great ones for Rodale Press, now called Rodale Inc., you've got to go beyond this. 

Take, for example, Rodale's book _New Choices in Natural Healing._ In this instance—and in any situation, really—had we limited ourselves to following in 

- 281 - 

the footsteps of other mailers that we found using other lists, we would have mailed a small fraction of what we ultimately mailed. 

So using your intelligence and your knowledge of the marketplace and distinctions of the kind that Gary's been presenting to you, that'll take you a lot further than just following the competition. You want market leadership, you want category leadership, and following usage patterns won't enable that at all. But, that caveat aside, you do always want to look at usage. 

_**13. Response and back end.**_ You want to have a sense of how different kinds of lists respond, how catalog lists respond to publishing offers and vice versa. You want to know the relationship between your up-front orders and your payments if you're in a bill-me environment. 

_**14. List cost.**_ You want to have a sense of the cost of a list and whether or not the CPMs, the cost per thousands, are a match for your acquisition economics. That's the math and finance of direct mail. 

_15._ _**CPP metrics.**_ And then you have to have a great sense of your cost-per-order metrics, so that your mail piece, your list costs, and ultimately your overall CPM in the mail are workable for the product you're selling and your overall business model. 

Hopefully, everybody's caught up on list basics. I acknowledge we don't have time for questions, but I'm certainly available to support you if any of this wasn't clear. I'll be here afterward. Now let's move on to... **The Advanced Course in Lists— The Six-Level List Assessment** 

Here are six distinctions that I've come up with over the years in working with hundreds of books, newsletters, magazines, and other products. They enable me, so to speak, to pull the puppet strings on the marketplace, to take the great creative work that people like Gary produce and bring it to the marketplace with a great level of certainty. 

A lot of this is about the offer I'm mailing, and a lot of it also about the lists I'm mailing to. So you want to look for matches. 

_**1. Branding.**_ The first place you want to look for matches is in what I call branding. As an example, when I talk about branding, the context in which I refer to branding refers to the extent to which the name of the firm or the positioning on the mail piece is known to the recipient, whether or not that person has ever done business with you. 

- 282 - 

**==> picture [314 x 235] intentionally omitted <==**

If you're _Prevention_ or _Men s Health_ or _Bottom Line_ or _Dr. Julian Whitaker,_ one of my important considerations is, is there any equity, positive or negative, already existing in the marketplace? Is there resonance, reverence, familiarity with whoever is claiming credibility or whoever is the entity behind this mailing? 

In the health business, as an example, there are personalities like Whitaker and Chopra, whom Gary just mentioned. In the money business, there are Louis Rukeyser and Richard Band, whose mail pieces we've been looking at. 

There are also organizational and institutional brandings in the money business, like Morningstar or _The Motley Fool_ or the American Association of Individual Investors. In the health business, there are the Harvards, the Mayos, and others. These are faceless but highly revered institutional or organizational voices. 

There are commercial brands like _Prevention_ and _Men s Health._ There are consumer advocates like _Consumer Reports_ or Nutrition Action or Public Citizen and Ralph Nader. So we want to have a sense of which of these we are marketing with. In many cases, some of you may be marketing with no brand at all, in which case your jet fuel is your copy, your benefits, your value proposition. 

We want to look at whether we have a brand or any recognition in the marketplace. How would we characterize it? Or do we have no brand at all? 

As an example of the implications stemming from this, let's look at two health newsletters. A publication like the _Harvard Women s Health Watch_ could mail to a list like Neiman Marcus. Why would that be? Neiman Marcus buyers are people that appreciate prestigious brands and want to be associated with them. 

- 283 - 

Even though catalog buyers don't open envelopes very readily, and even though we certainly don't know whether Neiman Marcus buyers have any interest in health, they're getting an envelope from Harvard and that's kind of an 

## acknowledgment. Someone could think, "Whoa, Harvard! Harvard wrote to _me!"_

That's the dynamic that we're taking advantage of for the _Harvard Women's Health Watch._ By contrast, a personality letter written by a female doctor for women could never mail to the Neiman Marcus list. They're mailing low- and middle-end health lists and maybe some vitamin lists and maybe some lifestyle magazines like _Organic Style_ at the highest end of the demographic, but not any higher in terms of the demographics or socioeconomics. 

One of the things that we help clients to see as we're growing their business is that the people they think they're competing with, in many instances, they are not competing with at all. Even if the value proposition sounds very similar, such as with the two different newsletters I've just cited, both offering health advice for women. So any time we look at a list, we want to have a sense of whether it's a brand-sensitive list. 

As another example, let's look at women who've bought hosiery. Leggs has a presence in the retail market, so we could say that women who bought Leggs via direct mail are brand sensitive. 

Now, if we have a million of them, it may not be true in every case, but in the aggregate, when you're dealing with a million people, these kinds of theories work very well. So we could say that someone who bought from Leggs is much more brand sensitive than someone who bought from Hosiery Corporation of America, which nobody has heard of but does about half a million transactions a month based on other aspects of their value proposition, such as price and convenience and replenishing the supply every month and so forth. So we just want to have a feeling of brand sensitivity of people on the list we're mailing to, based on the transactions they've engaged in and the organizations that they bought from. 

_2._ _**Benefit and** experience._ This is something that Gary might have touched on and that we're aligned on. And that is, _any product can be distilled into its benefit or its experience._ 

Even though there are differences in the specific approaches you want to take when marketing books versus newsletters versus magazines, ultimately the thing I like to say to people that is always shocking yet good for a laugh is that _nobody wants this stuff!_ They just don't. Why would anyone want a book or a magazine or a newsletter sitting around unless it was pretty or provided some kind of pleasant experience? What they're really buying is the promise of the benefit and the 

284 - 

- 

solution, of course. 

So I always look at what's the benefit or solution or answer or assistance or experience that people are getting from the mailing. And then I say, OK, who would want that? _Who would want that?_ Take the case of Gary's package for the health book _New Choices for Natural Healing._ The mailing Gary wrote for that was a bookalog. It's about health, and everybody wants health. And if health is all things to all people, then who do we mail to? The answer is not everyone. The answer is, of course, _people who 've engaged in health transactions._ 

And then we look at this question—who wants this benefit? In this case, with our health book, who else would want to experience a benefit on the theme of health? Well, anyone over 65 is probably thinking about their health more than somebody who's 30 or 40. He or she may or may not already be dealing with some kind of degenerative condition, either minor or major. But if we can find somebody in that age group, with the right socioeconomic and demographic profile, and who has a brand reverence for the publisher, Rodale, and he or she has also engaged in a direct mail transactions, then even though we know nothing about that person's interest in health, we can round up all those other variables and infer that he or she could be an excellent prospect for our mailing. 

No, such a prospect may not have engaged in a health-related direct mail transaction that we can see, so we are missing a big qualifier. Yet we do know that the person is over 65. And we know that people over 65 in general are dealing with their health in one way, shape, or form, either to prevent what they don't want to get or to remedy things that they've already come down with. 

And that's how we turned Gary's winner for a health book into a much bigger winner, one that enjoyed very large mailings well into eight figures. 

And we were able to achieve such a large rollout in only our third mailing out from dipping our toe in the water. First we tested—dipped our toe. Then we validated our successes with a modified rollout. Then went rolled out in our third mailing to eight figures. And on this third mailing, probably 40 to 50 percent of our lists _had nothing to do with health._ We were able to do this because we maximized our chances for success by looking at all the other variables and aspects of transaction behavior, socioeconomic profiles, brand resonance, and so forth to make sure that we were going to the right people. 

In fact, we had just about eight or ten lists in the launch. I find that most companies way overspend on launches. In that launch, we had one or two lists that really popped when those lists don't normally pop. And I said to Rodale, "Guys, this is our magic moment." This is it, this list right here, which happened to have 

- 285 - 

been called Starcrest, a nonbranded general merchandise marketer that sells spoons and socks and all kinds of things like that, worked well. I said, "This is our magic moment." And then I told them, "We've got to take no prisoners." We probably put 80 or 90 tests in the next mailing and about 90 percent of them, maybe more, worked. So that certainly is the beauty and the mastery of Gary's vision for who would buy that product. And then it was looking at some of the other aspects of transaction behavior and knowing who to take that mail piece to. 

I'm going to move this along now because I know I'm flirting with 30 minutes. So that wraps up my "benefit and experience" list assessment tool. 

_**3. Editorial philosophy and product positioning.**_ Does the list we're considering have a conservative point of view, an alternative point of view, or no point of view at all? In health and money, you're almost always somewhere on that gradient, conservative or alternative. What's the level of opportunism? Do you want to be thin, rich, and happy _tomorrow?_ Or are you willing to do that in six months and get rich slowly? What's the level of opportunism of the people on the list? Very opportunistic people tend to respond high but commit low. They're not great in pay-up, they don't last long on the data base, and they don't, in many cases, buy a lot more things from you. 

_4. What's your product?_ A book, a newsletter, a magazine, a catalog, a sweater, a car? What's the product that we're selling? And what's the product that the people on the prospect list have bought? 

_**5. What is the mail piece format that the list responded to?**_ **Is it an envelope, is it** a magalog, is it something else? Mail sorting behavior is critical when you look at prospect lists. That's why the breakthrough in magalogs was huge, because, just like water is the universal solvent in many respects, magalogs are the universal format. People get their mail out of the mailbox, and magalogs get sorted with catalogs and magazines. They have a longer shelf life in the home, they get read 2.6 times over four days instead of the two seconds that envelopes are given. Direct mail envelopes, letters, and lift notes don't sit in the den or on the night table for three days, whereas a magalog might. 

The other thing is that magalog people do not readily migrate to envelopes. So if you had a magalog control and you've beaten it with an envelope, even though the lift might be 30 or 40 percent, you must still go list by list and analyze whether the people on each list will open an envelope. If it's a TV list or a catalog list, probably not. So the mail piece format that built any given list is critical. 

_6. Price and offer._ Of course, on whatever list you're considering, you want as close a match as possible between the _list's_ price and offer and _your_ price and 

- 286 - 

offer. Are they the same? At least similar? Very important. 

## **Leverage Points of Massive Value** 

And now I want to move ahead to what I call _leverage points of massive value,_ a few other things for you. 

First there's _categorization._ Categorizing lists can frequently conceal trends in a postanalysis of a campaign. That's because you can't lump all similar products together. Each has major differences. 

For example, all health newsletters are not the same. We have to ask about each, is it institutional, is it personality, is it a commercial brand, is it a consumer advocate? 

All apparel lists are not the same. Are they upscale or downscale? Are they sweaters—mohair or polyester? We need to understand how these markets segment. Are the boaters sailboat owners or motorboaters? Are the gardeners vegetable gardeners or flower gardeners? Are the craftspeople quilters, crocheters, or knitting enthusiasts? All these markets segment out, and unless you take great care to create strata, or line items, for the subsets of your categories, you're going to misfire. 

Mutual fund people aren't real estate people, aren't equities people, aren't futures people, aren't stock people. So do that work. Figure out who's who. Think about whether your value proposition can really cast the net into the entire investment category from the least opportunistic to the most opportunistic, from the beginners to the advanced. Get a sense of who you're really speaking to. 

I remember speaking with Bob King, who was one of the executives at Phillips years ago. Bob said, "Michael, I see you've recommended a list to our financial group called 'Big Money Pro Golf Secrets.' I want you to know we've mailed some golf lists and they really don't work." 

I said, "No problem—it's not a golf list. Think about who would buy a book called _Big Money Pro Golf Secrets._ I don't care if it's _Big Money Pro Flower Secrets._ Anybody who would respond to that language is somebody whose door we want to knock on. And we have a value proposition for an investment newsletter. Anybody who wants to know about big money and secrets, I want to talk to that person about an investment newsletter. We want to bring them that mail piece." So in that case, the categorization could have just been opportunistic book buyers. 

But be creative with your categorization so that you're not putting lists into categories based on an assumption that some category doesn't work. Look at the 

- 287 - 

context in which they bought, and get a sense of the tone, the context, the opportunism. Are they the kind of people who would listen to you? 

## **Yardstick Lists** 

Another leverage point of massive value is your _yardstick lists._ Know which lists in each category are your benchmarks. When you launch a new product, you want to have eight to 10 lists whose behavior is well known to you, so that if the mailing fails, nobody says, "Gee, maybe we picked the wrong lists." Your yardstick lists should be like litmus paper. They turn color or they don't; the water is acidic or it isn't. The product worked or it didn't. Each list should be a yardstick, a known past performer of significant volume for a rollout, one that's a tipping point or a domino in its category and representative of the behavior of other lists in the category. Those are your yardstick lists. Those are your category indicators. Those will be in your launch. 

## **Prospect Loyalty** 

We talked about frequency before. So let me mention this factor, understanding prospect loyalty. If somebody has bought from another company four times or has renewed a subscription with another company or has bought seven books, I would assert that if you can get them to buy from you, they'll be a good customer because they're obviously a repeat customer. But I would also assert, more important, that what you're observing is that they're very loyal in the camp where they are now. Getting them over to your party is going to be tough. So mailing to someone who's clearly loyal to another marketer makes it tougher to get a response. 

That's why mailing to someone _'s first-year_ subscriber or _first-time_ book buyer is generally the way to start with a list to maximize response, not mailing into loyalty but mailing, almost paradoxically, into disloyalty—not someone who bailed, but someone who has not yet forged their loyalty and their affinity with the company where you've found them. 

Those are some thoughts for you. I hope that's been helpful and clear, and I thank you so much for this privilege to be with you. 

## _(APPLAUSE)_ 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ Well, now you're seeing some of the reasons why he is called _the world's greatest list broker._ In fact, I would like to show you the 

- 288 

- 

example that Michael was taking about.

Much of what Michael has said relates to how you can get the broadest possible universe. That also applies when you're writing copy, and in giving a great list broker like Michael the material he can use to pull in many more lists. 

For example, I wrote the piece above to sell a book that had not done too well for Rodale, at least not until this piece was written. 

_MICHAEL FISHMAN:_ Yes, in the launch campaign for this book, there were two packages designed by different freelance creative teams. The launch had enough names to test two packages side by side. Both failed miserably. But you've heard Gary espouse here today that it's paramount to give the market what it wants. And Rodale had the research to know that this was a title that, with a compelling message, should get an overwhelming response in the marketplace. Gary's was the third package to be written for this book. And it was really a success of historic 

- 289 - 

proportions, not only in Rodale or in book publishing but really in all of direct marketing. 

_GARYBENCIVENGA:_ Thank you, Michael. Yes, remember earlier today, how important **I** told you it is to _**make your advertising itself valuable?**_ 

Well, the first two packages for this book were, as I recall, nine-by-twelve packages. Sindy, you can correct me if I'm wrong, but I think they were standard, conventional-looking direct mail packages that, in effect, instantly said to the person receiving it, _"This is a sales message."_ 

Whenever I have considered taking on a project for a client, I would always ask myself, "Do I see an opening here where I can make a difference?" 

If I don't, if I'm going up against a superstar copywriter, and he or she has nailed every single opportunity to sell this thing, you've got to be crazy to go up against it. It would almost certainly be a huge waste of time. I could very easily lose the contest, so I would tell my potential client that I'd just as soon decline the honor. 

But here I saw a big opening. 

First, I saw that the previous packages did not take advantage of the master principle I keep repeating, making the advertising itself look and actually be valuable. 

So in the piece I wrote, instead of a creating another direct mail package that instantly communicates "sales pitch," I designed it to look just like a paperback reference book. 

In fact, I went to a leading designer, Charles Bofinger—he's retired now—and said to him, "Charlie, I want this advertising piece to look valuable, just like a book you might pick up in your corner drugstore." 

Enhancing the perceived value, all the bullets that you see on the front cover are paid off inside this _"Doctors Vest-Pocket Sampler of Natural Remedies."_ They include free tips on how to lower your cholesterol, reverse heart disease, prevent cancer, live longer, look younger, have more energy, relieve arthritis, lose weight more quickly and easily, and so on. 

How could you throw such a valuable gift away? It promised remedies for almost every major health problem that a person could have. In other words, the piece was written to have the broadest possible appeal within the field of health, which was one reason why Michael was able to find so many lists that worked. 

In contrast to the previous two mailings that went out for this book, both of which screamed _"sales pitch,"_ this booklet is saying, "I want to give you lots of 

- 290 - 

major health secrets right here, right now. Just open the pages and you'll find them." 

The piece also uses the Trojan Horse Welcome Secret, as well. As soon as you open this book, the first piece of copy you notice on the first interior spread reads as follows... 

**==> picture [518 x 402] intentionally omitted <==**

When a person sees that "thank you," he or she has to wonder, "Did I send for something? Did I buy something I've forgotten about?" And that locks them into place. There's no way they can toss it out with that possibility open. 

And the "thank you" is very easy to pay off. The copy says, "Here's your free medical guide, and thank you...for taking a few minutes now to read it over." 

That "thank you" buys a further reading, and gets you past that critical moment of decision when a person is wondering what this is all about and is strongly tempted to toss it out, as it's probably junk mail. This gets you past that 

- 291 - 

hair-trigger reaction and keeps the person reading for a few more critical seconds, during which time my bullets are probably going to hook them. 

After this initial Trojan Horse flap in the first spread, and which also wrapped around in the printing to anchor the order card inside the back cover, there was also a letter. 

I always include a letter in a direct mail package, as direct mail is the medium of the personal letter. And even though this is not an envelope package, there's no reason to sacrifice the letter, which is so critical to direct mail response. 

**==> picture [445 x 398] intentionally omitted <==**

Now before I forget, I want to make a very interesting point about the front cover. Remember, I had asked Charlie Bofinger, the great designer, to make this front cover look valuable, like a real book. He said to me, "Gary, I've been to the drugstore. I looked at some of those reference books that they have on those racks that twirl around, and many of the reference books have a navy background." 

- 292 - 

I said, "Forget that, Charlie! If the background color of the cover is all navy, that means my type will have to be in reverse (white) type. And you can't use reverse type! David Ogilvy taught us, 'Never use reverse type.'" 

**==> picture [356 x 398] intentionally omitted <==**

Charlie responded, "I'm telling you, if you want your book to look valuable, in this case you must use reverse type because that's what many of the most authoritative and valuable-looking reference books look like in the drugstore racks and in Barnes & Noble." 

Reluctantly, I was persuaded, and I'm so glad I was. As Ogilvy himself said, "Rules were made to be broken, but you should at least know the rules first." 

And it's true. Sometimes you have to know when to break an old rule and to go for something new. Or perhaps a better description would be that there's a hierarchy of rules. And in this case, the more important rule— _**make your advertising itself valuable**_ —was of greater importance than the usual design rule 

- 293 - 

## of _always avoid reverse type in long copy blocks._

I had to make this tough decision in favor of Charlie's view because every time he tried to do it my way, it looked like an ad. It screamed _"salespitch!"_ But when he did it his way, putting in the navy background, the darned thing looked so valuable, just like one of those little real estate guides or interest table paperback books you can buy in Barnes & Noble. They look so important, they defy you to throw them away. So that's why he went with that color, contradicting the rule that says never use reverse type for a substantial amount of copy. Charlie was dead right about that, and this piece became a monster winner. 

## **It Even Worked on Men's Lists** 

_MICHAEL FISHMAN:_ Beyond the obvious lists in the health market that have clear affinity, this package actually mailed to donors to veterans' appeals, general merchandise catalogs, apparel catalogs, cooking magazines, cookbook buyers, craft book buyers. It even mailed to guys who subscribed to a home repair magazine. Think about that. We generally think about women as being the most vigilant about their health. Even with couples in their fifties, sixties, and seventies, it's usually the woman who is the caretaker of health not only for herself but for her husband or partner. But we had _men's lists_ responding to this, which really speaks to the power of this particular piece of copy. 

_GARYBENCIVENGA:_ Thank you, Michael. What I tried to do very intentionally throughout this piece was to use, very roughly, about a 50/50 ratio of giveaway information to sales copy. Use up to half of your real estate to give away great tips and the other half to sell. 

So on almost every page of this piece, you'll see boxes full of valuable information, and each one is described as coming from the book we're selling, _New Choices in Natural Healing,_ thus indicating that this wonderful little tidbit is just one of hundreds upon hundreds of health secrets you'll find in this book. 

By the way, it is especially important to give away something very valuable and interesting _in the centerfold_ of your bookalog or magalog. Why? Because when people sort through their mail, they'll scan each piece, to see if there's any possible reason to toss it quickly. And, remember, most people are right-handed. This means that when someone wants to give a little bookalog like this the old thumb test to leaf through it, they'll hold the piece in their right hand and, using the thumb of their left hand, quickly flip through the pages to get an overview of what the piece is about. 

## So make sure there's something really important on your back cover and also

- 294 - 

at the center, where a bookalog or magalog naturally breaks open, because there's usually a staple there. 

This means that your centerfold should have a good piece of valuable information. Not a sales pitch, but something that you're giving away that's really valuable and interesting. This way, when someone's quick initial scan alights on that page, their reaction will be, _"Aha, this looks like valuable information, too valuable to throw away."_ 

For example, here are some sample pages that ran throughout the _New Choices_ bookalog... 

**==> picture [463 x 281] intentionally omitted <==**

So on page 12 of this little bookalog, you'd find a box at the bottom that says, _"Discovered: The all-natural sleeping pill!"_ It then recommended a certain herb, called valerian, as a safe, effective alternative to prescription sleeping pills. 

If while flipping through, your gaze fell upon page 16, you'd encounter a box that said, _"For instant relief of a leg cramp, try a little apple cider vinegar."_ And then it tells you exactly how to use this wonderful home remedy. 

- 295 - 

**==> picture [466 x 269] intentionally omitted <==**

Here's another example, on page 17. _"Heal a burn_ — _with milk!"_ — On another page, _"Take the sting out of a bee bite with meat tenderizer!"_ 

Throughout the piece, it's almost impossible to land on any page without discovering a nifty little "home remedy" or health secret that's being given to you, and each is so valuable that you can't throw this piece away More important, each serves as an example, and thus a powerful demonstration, of how useful this book will be to you and your family for scores of everyday health problems and questions. 

Again, let's review what we first started talking about, how to open the sale. The only two things you have to know to be a great salesperson in print or multiplied salesmanship are (1) how to open the sale, which means making your advertising itself valuable, and (2) how to close the sale by hitting all those four points of _The Bencivenga Persuasion Equation®,_ the four points that everyone must be satisfied with before they'll agree to buy. 

Of those four closing points, the most important is proof. And the best proof you can give is examples of your information with great little tips that your reader will want to keep and remember. After all, now that you've seen this copy, you're going to remember, the next time you get a bee bite, to reach for the Al Steak Sauce, right? 

- 296 - 

## **A Funny Story About This Mailing and My Sweet Aunt Millie** 

This methodology I'm sharing with you is powerful stuff, and it works on just about everybody. In fact, let me give you a funny example, involving my sweet Aunt Millie, who is 85 years old. 

My Aunt Millie is a kindly little Italian lady who doesn't buy much through mail order, unless she really likes something. Several months after I wrote this bookalog for _New Choices,_ she received it in the mail, but didn't know that I had written it. 

But she knows I'm in advertising, so when Pauline, I, and a few of my cousins took Aunt Millie out to dinner to celebrate her birthday, she told us all about this wonderful mailing she received that was just like a little blue paperback book. It promised to relieve her arthritis, her failing vision, and several other ailments that bother her. 

I proudly told her and everyone else at the table that I wrote that piece. She was duly impressed, but then she started laughing. She has a delightful, mischievous sense of humor, but we had no idea why she kept laughing after I told her that I wrote this piece. 

"Aunt Millie, what's so funny." 

She replied, "You're gonna get in _t-r-o-u-b-l-e,"_ waggling her finger at me and elongating the word "trouble," as if she were tattling on someone. 

I asked, "Why?" 

Then she confessed, with difficulty because she mysteriously kept bursting into laughter, that, yes, she sent for this book, just as she sends for quite a few books from Rodale. "And, Gary, we have the same last name, Bencivenga, which is an unusual name, and at the company they'll probably guess that you're a relative of Mildred Bencivenga, and you're gonna get in trouble!" More laughter. 

"But, Aunt Millie, if you order lots of books from Rodale, that's a good thing. It means you're a good customer, so if they do happen to recognize that our last names are the same, that would be very nice." 

"No it won't, because I never pay for these books!" (My cousins, already giddy from wine, erupt in howls of laughter.) 

"You don't pay for them, Aunt Millie?" 

"No. You people who write this advertising make me so tempted, I can't help myself. I order the books, but I don't pay for them. I live on a small pension." 

- 297 - 

My cousins are now doubled over, slapping the table. 

"Aunt Millie," I protested, "you're _stealing_ all these books?" 

"No, I never steal." 

"But you order all these books and then you don't pay!" 

"Yes, but they come with a 21-day return guarantee. Here's what I do. When I send for a book, I hold on to the advertising. When the book comes, I look up each of the symptoms that the advertising talks about. Then I go to the senior center and photocopy the pages in the book that show the remedies I need. Then I send the book back in the box it came in." 

By now my cousins are cracking jokes about how I'll soon be fired by Rodale once the truth comes out. My cousin Arlene says, "Oh, yeah, Gary, you _are_ going to get in big trouble because of our juvenile delinquent aunt!" (More laughter.) It was a fun birthday party, as it always is when we get together. 

Anyway, I was so tickled by this episode that at the next lunch I had in New York with the marketing executives from Rodale, I related the whole story. They got a hearty laugh out of it too. Then one of them said, "Hey, Gary, with all the royalties we pay you, you can't spring for a couple of books for your poor Aunt Millie. She's on a pension, for heaven's sake! Shame on you!" I promised, _"All right, all right, I'll pay for her books. I promise. I'll pay for her books!"_ 

Actually, this was true for more people than Aunt Millie. For this mailing, the bullets were so intriguing that the returns were higher than normal, because even people who were not qualified to buy it felt they just _had_ to find out the remedies for the particular health issues that bothered them. And when they found out the relevant secrets, some, like my unrepentant Aunt Millie, would photocopy the pages and then send the book back. 

_MICHAEL FISHMAN:_ An important point we had to emphasize with Rodale was not to try to replicate the success of a mailing like this because it's so unusual. To try and replicate the success with other packages would be to go down a path that wouldn't work. We had a range and a variety of lists working for this package that certainly were not mailed before this package was written and likely have not been mailed since. So it really was a historic piece. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ Thank you very much, Michael, for that presentation. Thank you very much.

We've got a few minutes before we break today, so I'll take a question from the question box. OK, the question is... 

## **Gary, How and Why Did You Get into Copywriting in the First Place?** 

Good question. I'm going to ramble a bit here, because it's the end of the day, and I want to tell you a few things about myself, all of which will help answer this question. 

As I mentioned, I grew up in Brooklyn, on the other side of the East River. My parents were married during World War II. I was born in 1946, after the war ended. During those days there were very few apartments being built in New York, because of the war effort. 

After the war was over and new families were getting started, the baby boom was under way, and there were very few apartments available. So my parents considered themselves lucky to find a two-room apartment in Crown Heights, Brooklyn. That's not two _bedrooms._ That's two rooms for the whole apartment, a kitchen and a bedroom. And that's where I grew up, in this tiny little two-room apartment. 

All four of us slept in this one bedroom. And it didn't seem unusual to me, at least not until I grew up some and got to see how most other people lived. Once Pauline and I were married and were making a few bucks, whenever we'd look at a new home, I'd say to Pauline, "I want bedrooms, lots of bedrooms!" But a two-room apartment in my neighborhood at the time was not at all unusual. 

Something else _was_ quite unusual, though. I knew it because my friends, whenever they would visit, would comment that our apartment was strange. 

It was strange because as small as it was, it was crammed with all manner of merchandise, piled high against the walls and in the hallway by the front door. And this merchandise was always coming in, until my mother or father could find someone else in the family who could use it. But it was a regular, flowing river of new merchandise, moving in and moving out. 

The reason for this was because my dad was an amateur copywriter. During the thirties, in the Great Depression, when he had come of age, he had taken a secure job in the Parks Department of New York City. After all, this was the Great Depression, and a secure job was a rare treasure. He didn't make much money, and whatever he did earn he used to help support his mother, brother, and sisters. His father (my grandfather) had died in an accident in the early thirties, and my 

- 299- 

dad was the oldest son, which in an Italian family meant it became his job to support the household. 

So he considered himself very lucky to have landed a secure job working for the New York City Parks Department, where he eventually rose through management and introduced many creative innovations. He enjoyed his work and remained with the city for his whole working life. 

My dad, even though he had to leave high school to work, had always had a great facility with words. To supplement his income, he entered as many contests as he could. 

Back in those days, they didn't usually run sweepstakes. To get a lot of people to try a product, many companies would invite the public to write, in 50 words or less, why they loved this product. The best entries would win prizes. 

When you think about it, these contests were quite a clever and effective sales promotional tool. To enter, large numbers of people would go out, buy the product, try it, and figure out why they liked it. Result: distribution, often nationwide, plus people wracking their brains to figure out why this product was so wonderful and, in the process, selling themselves on it. 

In later years, after the 1960s, most people's lives had gotten so busy and their writing skills so poor that few people wanted to compose even 50 words or less. That's when sweepstakes took over. 

But in the heyday of the "50-words-or-less" era, my dad was brilliant at writing catchy slogans and jingles that captured the essence of why this or that product was terrific. 

He won a cash prize of $5,000—quite a fortune in the depths of the Great Depression—in a contest about the movies. But most of the prizes never amounted to much, just merchandise that clogged up our tiny apartment. Also, all entries became the property of the advertiser, so there never were any royalties. But he had an amazing knack for winning these contests. 

Some of his slogans are even being used to this day. The best-known one was also from the thirties, when Kellogg invited members of the public to "tell us in 50 words or less why you like this breakfast cereal." 

My dad wrote back, "I like Kellogg's Rice Krispies because it is the one cereal with that _SNAP, CRACKLE and POP."_ He typed this up on his ancient Royal typewriter and would always capitalize the words he wanted to emphasize. That was my dad's entry. He wrote that. I have no idea _what_ he won for it. If it was like the other prizes, it was probably 50 or a hundred bucks. The prizes were never that 

- 300 - 

grand, but at the time, he was making only about $50 a week from the city, and this extra income, which he won regularly from many different contests, helped a great deal. 

Now, it's important to note that he did _not_ create the cartoon characters who later adorned the Rice Krispies box and that made the slogan famous. The Kellogg Company handed my dad's winning entry to their ad agency, the Leo Burnett Agency, to see if they could make any use of it. The Leo Burnett Agency always believed in using cartoon characters to make the commercials more memorable and the packaging more noticeable on supermarket shelves. 

They are the same agency that created the Jolly Green Giant, Charlie the Tuna, Tony the Tiger, and many other famous cartoon characters that helped sell untold millions of dollars worth of goods. With my dad's winning entry, _"Snap, Crackle and Pop,"_ they created little cartoon characters to match his words. That was their idea. But my dad wrote the words and won a prize. And he was always winning these prizes. 

That's why, in this tiny two-room apartment, in Brooklyn, on the sixth floor of an apartment house—it had an elevator, thank goodness—we were always hip deep in merchandise. 

Ever since I can remember, I can recall seeing, along the walls, opened boxes of roller skates, new bicycles, Mixmasters, shiny new toasters, radios, furniture, suits of clothing, perfumes, jewelry, hats, vacuum cleaners, once even 20 pounds of Barricini Chocolates, all stacked in white bakery-style boxes, which I feasted on for two months. I ate so much of it, I can still taste that particular chocolate as if it were yesterday. 

I also recall a Hopalong Cassidy outfit, hanging for a while on the outside of my closet. It was too big for me, so my mom gave it to another family. The merchandise had to be moved, because the apartment was so small, and new stuff was always coming. 

He entered contests offering prizes from every kind of company, from local clothing stores to major national advertisers. Living with all this stuff was never unusual to me. But when my friends would visit on a rainy Saturday afternoon to play Monopoly or something, they would ask in wide-eyed wonder, _"Where does all this stuff come from? "_ 

He was so good at this that the companies who judged the entries—this function was usually farmed out to companies that specialized in running contests—came to recognize his name and felt that he was winning too often. He wasn't "blackballed" as such, but when they did recognize his name, they felt they 

- 301 - 

should allow other people to have a chance. He became known as _"The Slogan King of Brooklyn,"_ regarded as a "pro," and this hurt his chances of winning, since most of the companies wanted amateurs to win, as this generated more publicity. 

He kept winning anyway, as he would submit entries under the names of other family members with different last names, mostly his numerous brothers-in-law. He was so good that sometimes three different people in the family would win prizes in the same contest, all from my dad's entries. The family members would give him the money or whatever prize they won, and he would give them a commission for using their names. He was so proud of his ability that he would sometimes feel offended if he didn't win, under different names, at least two or three of the top prizes. 

Here are a few more among many examples I recall... 

## For Macy's department store, he named their in-house line of products _SupreMacy._

At a time when tobacco companies were denying any direct link between cigarettes and lung cancer, he wrote a slogan for one of the antismoking campaigns conducted, I believe, by the American Cancer Society: _"Cigarettes_ — _the smoking guns of cancer."_ 

## For Borden's packaged goods: _"If it's Borden s, it's got to be good."_

For a contest which posed the question, "Why has America gone gaga over Frank Sinatra?," he wrote, _"To be FRANK, he puts uS-IN-A-TRAnce."_ 

His friends told my dad that he should go into advertising, but he always felt ashamed of his lack of formal education. Also, he hated to work indoors. His first love was gardening, and he loved working in the parks, out in the fresh air and sunshine, creating oases of greenery and beauty in the crowded, concrete city. 

Once he relented and went in for an interview with a Manhattan ad agency, though I don't know which one. When they learned of the contests he'd won, they were skeptical but interested. The agency was preparing to pitch the Prince Albert Pipe Tobacco account. So they asked my dad, on the spot, for any ideas. 

He thought for a moment and came up with "Prince Albert never bites the tongue that asks for it." Also on the spot, he advised them to change their packaging, to get rid of their bright red can (too "hot") and replace it with a minty green color, for a less fiery and more cooling association. 

The agency guys were impressed and then asked if he had any ideas for another account, a maker of lingerie, that was about to introduce a new, sexier brassiere. I believe this was in the late 1940s. 

- 302 - 

They were having trouble coming up with a provocative name. He thought for another few moments and came up with the name, _Bra-vado._ Now they were really impressed and offered him a job as a copywriter. But after thinking it over for a few days, he declined. He loved his outdoor work too much. He loved breathing fresh air and didn't want to spend all day behind a desk. 

One time my dad decided to try to win a baseball glove for me. I wanted one for Christmas, and he said, "Let me see if I can win one for you. Why spend good money on a baseball glove?" He felt he could easily win one because the Brooklyn Dodgers were running a contest asking young boys my age to write, in 50 words or less, "why I'd like to be the Dodger batboy for a day." For him, that would be cake. 

This was before the Dodgers moved to L.A. They would award baseball gloves and bats to the best entries and would allow the boy who wrote the number one entry to suit up as the Dodger bat boy for a day, sit in the dugout for one game, and meet all the players. 

He wrote the letter just to win a glove, but it proved so good that they thought it was the best in the entire contest. They invited the two of us to downtown Brooklyn on Montague Street, where the brain trust for the Brooklyn Dodgers had their offices. Mind you, I had never even read the winning letter. My dad said, "Let me do the talking," and he figured he'd easily handle the situation. 

But after we sat in a waiting room for a while, a man in a suit came out and invited me in. Just me. My father stood up too, and the man said, "Mr. Bencivenga, why don't you wait outside because your son wrote the letter, and we want to talk with him." 

Dad said, "That's OK, I'll accompany him anyway..." And the man said firmly, "No, no, you wait here, outside." 

The truth of the matter was that although he'd written the best letter, the Dodger brass didn't think that an eight-year-old could write such a good letter. 

It was true. Heck, I hadn't even _read_ the darned letter, let alone written it! Oh, man, we were in trouble now! 

They brought me in, talked to me a while and, quickly deducing that I was no genius or prodigy, asked, "Do you think your dad may have helped you a little with this letter?" 

Wanting nothing more than to escape this extremely awkward situation, I responded, "I have to admit, yes, he did help a little." 

## They smiled and one of them said, "You're an honest kid. We're going to make

- 303 - 

you the _unofficial_ Dodger batboy for a night, because your letter is the best. But we can't let you be the official first-place winner, because your dad helped you, and that wouldn't be fair to the boy whose letter came in second. Does that sound fair to you?" 

I said, "Sure, why not?" 

_Fair?_ I was thrilled! I thought we were about to be led away in handcuffs and my mother would have to visit us in jail! 

In later years, we had many a good laugh over that predicament he got us into. I still have the original letter, as well as a baseball signed by all the Dodger stars. I treasure both, but especially the memory of that adventure. We felt like a Brooklyn version of Butch Cassidy and his Sundance Kid. 

The letter he wrote was so good—and way too mature for me to have written at eight years old. You see, the Dodgers' proudest achievement was that they were the first major league team to break the color barrier which, incredible as it seems today, had always barred African-Americans from playing in the major leagues. The Dodgers broke that color line by proudly putting Jackie Robinson on their team. 

My dad knew that a letter recognizing this landmark civil rights breakthrough would make the Dodgers beam with pride. So he drew a diamond on an 8'A by 11 sheet of paper, rolled it into his ancient Royal typewriter, and typed up a brief slogan at each of the nine positions on the field. 

For example, by the pitcher's mound, he typed in _"Pitcher: let's all pitch in to make democracy work"_ 

By the shortstop position, he wrote something like _"Let s all stop racial prejudice short in its tracks."_ 

For every position he wrote a little civil rights slogan, and, yeah, it was kind of sophisticated for an eight-year-old to have written. _(LAUGHTER)_ 

And that's how I became the _unofficial_ winner of the Dodger batboy contest. They did give us tickets for a night game and introduced us to all the players before the game. I shook hands with all my baseball heroes. I didn't wash that hand for a week. 

Now, finally, I'm getting around to answering your question, about how and why I got into copywriting. I wanted to be like my dad. I felt I had inherited some of his creative genes, but absolutely none of his love of gardening. So I wanted to be a copywriter and, at age 19, applied for and landed my first full-time job as a copywriter, while I went to college at night. 

- 304 - 

Two years later, I got my first copywriting job in direct marketing at Prentice Hall, a book publisher in Englewood Cliffs, New Jersey. My first assignment was to write a direct mail letter to sell a book on management techniques for top executives. I did _not_ do well. 

Why not? 

Instead of writing a typical sales letter, I wrote a catchy slogan for each of the chapters in the book. This was my weird notion of what a sales letter should do, inspired by—guess who? With my draft in his hand, my first copy chief called me into his office and asked, "What is this? What's with these slogans? You're writing a letter. Write like a normal person who writes to another normal person!" 

In truth, it took several months for my first copy chief to expunge this slogan gene from my system, but he eventually succeeded, and I haven't written another slogan since. And that's how I got into copywriting. 

Tomorrow we'll get into many more copywriting secrets, and I look forward to seeing all of you back here at 9 o'clock sharp. Thank you. 

## _(APPLAUSE)_ 

## — _End of Session_ #7—

I know that whenever I have participated in these contests, I never get as many right as other people in the audience. Consider yourself forewarned! Ready? Here we go...

_**Here's entry number one.**_ This is from Boardroom Books, offering _The Gary Null Complete Encyclopedia of Natural Healing._ The first headline is "He proved it to millions on PBS television." We're just going to look at the headlines and covers.

I am the best at picking winners after I have the results, of course. There's nobody better, I guarantee you that! Beforehand, I usually pick them wrong. - 310 - 

we get big lifts from just covers. I don't remember if this was just a cover test or not. But it gave us a decent lift, and it was a very tough one to beat once it became the control. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ I would like to add my own two cents, Brian, with 20/20 hindsight of course, that the winner, B, has more specificity in a number of ways. But also, proof is not always very strong unless you pair it with a big promise. 

Version A says, "He proved it to millions on PBS television." But I'm not sure what he proved. I have to read further to get a benefit. With Version B, the benefit hits me right up front. If I'm worried about clogging up my arteries or I already have such a problem, that really grabs me. Very often, the winning combination will be a big promise coupled with some type of strong proof. And Version B does a nice job of flowing right from the promise and into the proof. 

_BRIAN KURTZ:_ One funny anecdote on this, about the one on the right. I got a letter from another direct marketer, who's not in the room...

Everybody make their choice? 

The winner is A! I feel like the host of the Academy Awards. Version A won by a large percentage, 118 percent. 

Why did it win? My opinion is, the word "breakthrough" in the headline is always a good bet. I once heard Joe Sugarman, the brilliant mail order marketer, say that he couldn't recall ever having a losing ad that had the word "breakthrough" in the headline. Good word! After I heard Joe say that many years ago, I've used the word "breakthrough" in many of my ads, and I've never had a loser with it either. I'm sure it's helping here once again.

OK. Version A, "The real cause of digestive discomfort." 

Version B, "Eat all the foods you love without digestive discomfort!" Ed, was the interior copy the same, pretty much? 

320 - 

- 

## _ED ELLIOTT:_ Yes. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ So the only difference here is the cover, mainly the headline. 

By the way, David Ogilvy said that the headline is 80 percent of the sale, and that's been the conventional wisdom for all these many years, and rightfully so. 

But he was talking in that instance only about space advertising. In direct mail, he said, "Even more important than your headline is your format." 

Many of the packages we were looking at yesterday looked too much like sales letters. People saw them coming a block away. Generally, they do not do as well as formats that look more valuable _regardless of the differences in headlines._ 

In direct mail, it's often the _format_ that conveys the immediate perception of value and importance much more than just a headline can. A valuable-looking format will often carry the day even more than a great headline that instantly communicates that it's trying to sell something. 

Here the format is identical, so this is a pure test of headlines on the cover. 

OK, how many voted for A? 

How many for B? 

The winner is A. 

These were not landslide winners from what I understand, but A is the winner. _ED ELLIOTT:_ Both of these are by Kent Komae, who's here. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ Congratulations, Kent! 

_ED ELLIOTT:_ The copy on B was originally on the back cover of A. We all loved it, and we thought maybe that's why it's doing so well. Let's move it to the front. Didn't work. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ OK, let's move on to our next test, another one from Healthy Directions... 

- 321 - 

**==> picture [512 x 308] intentionally omitted <==**

Version A is "The Great Omega-3 Hoax: What You Need to Know About the New 'Miracle Food.'"

_GARYBENCIVENGA:_ Test B, "The Case Against Alternative Medicine."

How many voted for B? 

Well, you guys were paying attention yesterday. The winner was B, "The Case Against." 

This works especially well here because this is a newsletter about alternative healing. To have a champion of alternative healing saying "the case against what I've always stood for" is always an appealing proposition. 

Remember yesterday I said that this very sleepy-looking headline, "the case against," is just a powerhouse of a headline. I've written so many control packages that way. And I see my good friends at Healthy Directions are continuing in that direction, which I didn't even originate. It was not my headline; I borrowed it from somebody else when I got started. 

- 3 2 2 - 

_ED ELLIOTT:_ Oh, good, I was afraid we were going to owe you something! _(LAUGHTER)_ 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ No, you've paid me enough over the years. I have to admit that I have been compensating all these years for the small change that my dad got for those great slogans he wrote. _(LAUGHTER)_ I've gotten my revenge. 

Another one for _Health & Healing,_ this one a three-way split... 

**==> picture [524 x 210] intentionally omitted <==**

First, Version A, and you can read the headline under the _Health & Healing_ masthead—"A Texas Clinic Has Defeated Cancer." 

Version B features a much more prominent headline, "A Cure for Cancer." 

Finally, Version C, which is a message from Dr. Julian Whitaker, the editor of the newsletter—"Whitaker Rule #1, No One Is a Lost Cause." 

Everyone have their answer? How many voted for A? For B? For C? The winner is A. 

Now this is very interesting. This bears out a theory I have that if you're selling a newsletter, it doesn't hurt to let your advertising piece look like a newsletter. The other pieces don't. But yet, package C came in second. I would probably have guessed that package B, which looks so strong, would have come in at least second. "Cure for Cancer" is popped out so strongly there. I don't have any explanation for this. Maybe, Tom, you'd like to say something? 

_TOM CALLAHAN:_ The only comment I'd make on this is that we actually designed A to appeal more to our house file, to get back to those people who'd been with _Health & Healing_ in the past. And it also performed significantly better 

- 323 - 

to outside names than either of the others, which came as a surprise to us. 

_GARYBENCIVENGA:_ OK, now our last one. By now, everybody should have at least six right, just on the law of averages, but I don't think I would! 

## This is once more from the Healthy Directions company, selling their _Health & Healing Newsletter._

I have written none of the packages we're seeing this morning. They are all by others. And in this case, we have a pure design test. 

**==> picture [473 x 307] intentionally omitted <==**

The headline in Version A treats the lead story the way a magazine cover might. It looks very much like a magazine cover. 

- Version B is the same package we saw before. Which of the two is the winner? Everyone have their pick? How many voted for A? 

How many voted for B? 

The winner is B. 

Was this by a lot, Ed, or maybe 20 percent or so? 

_ED ELLIOTT:_ It wasn't by a lot, but this is one of my favorite tests. My background, as you know, was as a designer. I had been seeing designs for 

324 - 

- 

promotions that looked very much like magazine covers or like the magalogs of the early nineties, that were _not_ working to our market. 

I went to my favorite designer for something like this, Bill Bosler. I have a lot of favorite designers. But if I want something that's outside the box and is going to be a good rip-off of a particular look, Bill does that better than anybody. And I told him, "I want a magazine." And so the interior also reflects that difference. That was an excellent test. 

And it confirmed what I had suspected, that no way, at least three years ago when we tested this, is something that looks so much like a magazine going to beat something that is an easy, quick-reading newsletter. I have to say, I'm not sure that's the case today, because I think market conditions are shifting. 

_GARYBENCIVENGA:_ In what direction, Ed? What do you see, if you can tell us, maybe speaking generally and not just about your own products, but what are the hot formats, hot products being sold now? 

_ED ELLIOTT:_ In our category (health), I'm noticing that more visual formats are more likely to win now. They don't necessarily resemble magazines, but are just more visual, with more photography. I must say, though, I've not done any tests on this recently. So I don't know in terms of head-to-head results. It's just my own observation—I'm seeing more of that for us working in the mail. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ When you say "more visual," do you mean that these working packages are designed in a magazine style or rather in a newsletter lookalike style or even an envelope type of package? 

_ED ELLIOTT:_ I would say, still more in a newsletter style. But also things that are a bit more hybrid. Something that I've always tried to do is answer the question for people, _"What is this I got in the mail? "_ I want them to think, _"Oh, this is a magazine. Oh, this is a report."_ I want them to know what it is. If the reader doesn't know what the format is, then we don't benefit from the full value of masquerading as editorial. And, at least to our market, where we are mailing a hard offer for a newsletter, something that masquerades as a magazine still isn't working. But formats that are more visual _are_ working better than they did a few years ago. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ Interesting! I have the same observation that I'll share later when we talk about design. I'm also seeing a major comeback by #10 packages because so many advertisers have gone over to the magazine look-alike and the bookalog and so on. The old fashioned #10, as long as you still apply the Cracker Jack secret and have valuable information inside, can be quite strong. 

## You can make the envelope look editorial, not like an ad. I know that for

_Personal Finance,_ an outer teaser that says, "Your Current Issue Enclosed," is a big winner." 

Kim Krause is here this morning. Kim? Hi, Kim!

It's hard for information buyers to throw out an envelope that says, "Your Current Issue Enclosed." It seems like I've paid for this, so I better open it.

There's an advantage to that, but it's very risky because if the freshest thing is not as good as the older content, then the response can plummet when you roll out with it untested. _DOUG MIL:_ Yes, that's right.
