## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #1 Double Your Odds of Success by Creating Two Versions— One That "Plays the Probabilities" ...and a Second That's Boldly Original** 

_GARYBENCIVENGA:_ Here's where we ended our session yesterday, with streetsmart copywriting secrets. These are highly effective tips that, by and large, I didn't read in any book but gleaned from years of experience or that were shared with me by legendary copy chiefs. 

This first is a gem, especially if you want to win more control packages, establish your reputation as a gladiator who usually wins, and thus attract many more assignments. 

I alluded to it yesterday—how to double or even triple your odds of winning on an assignment. And that is to do two or three different versions of your package or space ad to be tested. 

We've seen how hard it is to predict a winner in a direct marketing horse race. But there's usually nothing stopping you from entering one or two more horses in the race. If, as you're writing your headlines and coming up with central concepts for your copy, you spot another great idea, don't toss it. Just create a second version around it. 

I always do this, and here's how... 

First, I want to be sure to create one version, my main version, in a way that plays all the probabilities. It takes advantage of as many secrets as we've been talking about as I can squeeze in. It will have a big benefit and strong proof in the headline/subhead combination. It will apply a traditional, benefit-driven approach to the whole front end of the copy. 

Second, because _most_ ads, including the ads I'm competing against, follow the same principles, that in itself could be a risk. It could wind up looking too much like everything else out there. So I will also look for another concept that's more boldly original, quite different from everything else that's been done in this product's field. 

Once you get in the habit of doing this, you'll often come up with a _third_ strong concept to test. If it's good enough, and if your client is wise enough to spend the money testing yet another version, you'll then have a third horse in the race. With all of these, your interior body copy can pretty much be the same, just as we saw in our Pick the Winner Contest. Sometimes there were huge differences in response, based solely on the headline and main concepts on page 1. So it pays 

- 331 - 

to test at least one extra and even two if you've got a third strong concept. 

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #2 Write at Least 20 to 25 Good Headlines** 

After your research stage, write at least 20 to 25 good headlines. I've always done that, and I've always hated doing it because it's a lot of work. The standard that I use is, it has to really be a good headline to be counted as one of the 25. 

You can't cheat here, because you'll only be cheating your client and yourself. You can't just go through the motions and come up with 20 to 25 mediocre headlines. You want to shoot for 20 to 25 good ones. I promise you, if you go through this exercise, your seventeenth or twenty-second will be a lot better than your first or second. 

No matter how good you think that first one is, don't fall in love it and think you've nailed it. For several days after you've written your first few headlines, keep trying to top them and come up with new combinations. Perhaps combine one good headline with a different idea or a different subhead. Then maybe switch, make the subhead idea the headline. You can get some great combinations going. 

## As I say, I promise that if you go through this exercise, you'll love the headlines at the end of the process much more than the headlines at the beginning. **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #3 Start Your Body Copy by Paying Off Your Headline** 

Not paying off the headline right away in body copy is a very common mistake, and it can be lethal to your copy's effectiveness. 

As a copy chief, I used to see this all the time from the writers in my department. The copywriter, the copy chief, the staff, and sometimes the client will all be involved in writing a brilliant headline. And once it's chosen, it's as if it's a separate job from the rest of the ad. And then the copywriter goes off and starts writing the body copy, forgetting about the first job that he or she just "finished." The result is that the body copy often goes off in a totally different direction that never references the headline! 

It's so common, and if you look at many ads you'll see it happen again and again. The reason is because the jobs are done separately. If it's your job to read over someone's copy, here's what you should do. 

First, and this is what I always do first, is to ask yourself, do I like the overall concept, the headline and visual combination, and the format? Does that first 

332 - 

- 

impression grab me? 

If so, that's good. After that, after "Dear Friend" or Dear Gardener" or whomever you're writing to, does this body copy pick up instantly from the headline? 

You don't want any speed bump, any dislocation. You want to imagine a straight railroad track between the headline and start of the body copy. Your headline is the locomotive coming down the track with a big head of steam. The track in your body copy must be straight and true. If that speeding locomotive rolls into the body copy and there's a total change of direction or if there is no track, you've got a train wreck. If you totally change directions at "Dear Reader," you are throwing a switch that derails your locomotive, the very locomotive that you spent so much time crafting, namely your headline. 

This is a very common mistake, and you must watch for it. 

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #4 Don't Address Your Readers as a Group, Such as "America"..."Friends"... "All of You"..."Folks"...Etc.** 

This is not as common as the problem above, but every now and then, you will catch yourself doing this or spot it in copy that someone has written for you. Get out your blue pencil and strike out the plurals! Yes, you may be sending this message to millions at once, _but each is read or heard by one person at a time._ 

Addressing your readers as a group is symptomatic of thinking as the marketer, like the Ford executives sitting inside the Ford building. "We're inside our building, writing to all the 'folks' out there." 

I remember mindlessly watching TV one evening and seeing a commercial for the Chevy Blazer. The announcer said, in a booming, energetic voice, "Come on America, discover the new Chevy Blazer!" 

I felt like peeking over my shoulder to check if the rest of America had mysteriously materialized in my living room. "Is _America_ here with me? Am / America? Who is he talking to?" Obviously not _me._ I don't think of myself as _"America."_ I'm just little ol' me. And he's not talking to little ol' me at all. 

Of course, inside the marketer's offices, few people might realize this mistake when reviewing the copy. It even makes sense in a certain myopic way. As the marketer, you're hell-bent to get the word out, to pump this message out across America or even the whole world. You want to make the whole country sit up and 

- 333 - 

take notice! 

But TV viewers all across America wind up feeling puzzled, as I did, as they sit half-conscious in front of their boob tubes, scratching an itch and suddenly being addressed as _"America"_ or—as if I've undergone a _really_ bizarre surgical procedure— _"Ladies and Gentlemen."_ 

"Not talking to _me!"_ is the inevitable conclusion—ironically, in _all_ of us. 

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #5 In Space Ads (as in Speeches), Acknowledge Your Specific Audience** 

Notwithstanding tip #4 above, when you're addressing your prospects in a _public_ arena, your headline should acknowledge their specific group, to make them feel more acknowledged. 

For example, you could write a headline in the _New York Times_ that says, "A Special Offer." 

How flat, unappealing, and "unspecial" is that? 

You'll trigger a much higher response, I guarantee, if you make just one simple change, by mentioning the name of the medium, as in "A Special Offer to Readers of the _New York Times."_ 

Wow, does that perk it up! You are acknowledging your readers' status, recognizing their personal affinity with their favorite newspaper, and making the "specialness" of your offer much more credible. 

And here's the real beauty of it—without the _New York Times'_ overt permission, you're getting something akin to a joint venture endorsement, as if the highly trusted _New York Times_ has vetted this offer and in some way is standing behind it. And in fact they are, by allowing you to run your ad in their pages. They don't allow just anyone to run ads in the _Times_ or in any decent newspaper or magazine. So this is a very nice little tip. I've never seen it fail to boost response. 

You can almost always do this in space, and you can do it in direct mail too. If the list is big enough that it warrants the extra expense of changing your outer envelope, you can say, "A special invitation to you as a subscriber of the _Wall Street Journal."_ 

You certainly should do this with your own house list. You almost always want to acknowledge whatever relationship you already have with your customers, because it flatters them. It shows that you recognize their importance and special status, rather than address them merely as unrecognized, unappreciated members 

334 - 

- 

of the great unwashed masses. 

Everyone likes to feel important. You should picture every human being you ever meet or write to as having this sentence tattooed in dark blue ink across their forehead: _"My greatest desire is to feel important!"_ 

Follow this tip and you will be far more persuasive and influential in all your dealings with others. 

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #6 Copywriters Love "Curiosity" Headlines, Such as...** 

**==> picture [342 x 464] intentionally omitted <==**

- 335 - 

I wrote my share of these until the marketplace beat it out of me. Very, very few "blind" or "curiosity" headlines ever work. 

When they do work, they can be blockbusters, but the odds are stacked heavily against you. 

This one is an ad for _Playboy_ magazine, encouraging businesses to advertise in _Playboy._ The headline is, "Cigar Man Catches Fire." It tells the story of a man who sells cigars and his business is taking off, thanks to the ad he placed in _Playboy._ 

It's a typical curiosity ad I came across in an advertising trade magazine. I have a collection of such ads, and most people, especially copy chiefs, copywriters, and marketing people, _love_ ads like this. They seem so cool, so clever, so interesting to read. 

But that usually means we are looking at it from the marketers' side of the fence. We already know where the story is going and assume it's easy to provoke our readers' curiosity to find out the whole story. Nothing could be further from the truth. Your readers don't have time to waste trying to figure out every ad or story in their newspaper or magazine, even one with an intriguing headline. They strongly prefer headlines that have an immediate relevance to a problem, interest, or desire in their own lives. 

## **- Street Smart Copy writing Secret #7 On Average, "Benefit" Headlines Outpull "Curiosity" Headlines Three Out of Every Four Times** 

One of the best copy chiefs I ever knew was meticulous about testing all manner of headlines and ads. He once told me that curiosity headlines have a very poor track record compared with benefit-driven headlines. _On average, benefit headlines outpull curiosity headlines three out of every four times._ 

So you automatically gain a 75 percent edge if there's an understandable benefit in your headline. You can buck those odds, and when you do, you can have a blockbuster. But just know that you're going against the odds by 75 percent when you test a curiosity headline. 

To see what does work so well, let's take a look at some strong benefit headlines I've culled from my ad collection... 

- 336 - 

**==> picture [484 x 550] intentionally omitted <==**

This is a classic benefit headline by a company that sells heating equipment for the home. With fuel costs so high, it would certainly capture the interest of many thousands of homeowners, especially those with an old or broken heating system they've got to replace. 

- 337 - 

Yes, this ad's design could be a lot better. Response would shoot higher, I'm sure, if it were designed to look like an editorial article instead of an ad. 

But just on the strength of this big-benefit headline, it deserves a tip of the hat. Almost anyone worried about rising fuel costs would be caught by that headline and read that ad. 

Here's another that would be hard to ignore by consumers in its market, in this case, avid bowlers... 

**==> picture [465 x 498] intentionally omitted <==**

- 338 - 

Another one, for thermal underwear. I have seen this ad running year after year after year. This ad has earned a place of honor in my "All Time Classics" file... 

**==> picture [402 x 572] intentionally omitted <==**

Here's another classic, this one for a children's reading course. This ad ran for many, many years... 

**==> picture [477 x 513] intentionally omitted <==**

I love the visual here. It shows a little girl on tippy-toes reaching up as high as she can, which of course is the whole message behind the course— _give your child the tools she needs to reach as high as she can in life._ I also love the specificity of this headline, "At _4'A_ she's reading _3rd grade_ books." 

340 - 

- 

The next one is also one of my favorite ads of all time, especially in the benefit category... 

**==> picture [436 x 369] intentionally omitted <==**

Look how economical and beautiful it is. "19 working days from now every person who sells for you can be up to 46 percent more likely to close." 

This is for a sales course. When Xerox was in the sales training business, this ad ran for many years in very expensive media like the _Wall Street Journal._ And it's so economical. The bottom line of the copy, actually the _only_ body copy, is also fantastic. Look at this superb proof element: "Thousands of companies, including 347 of the Fortune 500, use Xerox Learning Systems to help their people do a better job." 

Rarely have I seen such a strong, concise nugget of selling power. 

Those are some of the best "benefit" ads I've ever seen and that you can use as models.

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #8 For Strongest Results of All, Combine Benefits and Curiosity** 

Here's the best way to harness curiosity—surround it with benefit and proof. For example, here's a very successful package I wrote for Jeff Greenberg. It sells a newsletter about how to succeed in a corporation riddled with "office politics." In fact, we created a premium called _The Black Book of Office Politics._ 

**==> picture [485 x 517] intentionally omitted <==**

Here, the masthead, "Executive Strategies," identifies the market. The headline, visual, subhead, bullets, and caption all provoke curiosity. The bullets on the left, by their specificity, also serve as strong proof elements. If you're an executive in a corporation, many of these elements would prove irresistible. 

Next is one of the best packages I've ever written. It was for Jim Blanchard, who published a newsletter written by two women named the Aden sisters. 

These were two women who had traveled to Costa Rica and met a very wealthy, secretive investor there. He needed office help, and they agreed to work for him. They're very bright, and as they helped in his office, they absorbed his brilliant, secretive ways of making a lot of money from the markets. 

They later left his employ and, with the knowledge they gained, started writing their own investment newsletter. They took the financial world by storm. The media loved them. They were such an unlikely pair of financial gurus, as well as being attractive young women. Most of all, their predictions about the markets were uncannily correct and outrageously profitable. 

The major media were star-struck that these two young women could have the knowledge to be outperforming the top Wall Street analysts, yet they were. This package was a blockbuster because it rode the coattails of all this. It beat their previous copy, also written by a top copywriter, by a huge margin. Here's the front side of the package, promising lots of goodies inside the envelope...

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #9 To Defeat a "Killer" Objection, Confront It Right in Your Headline** 

This is another very useful secret, especially helpful when you have a product that elicits an immediate doubt in your prospect's mind. 

Remember Captain John Rade? 

He's a world-class professional fisherman, and as a pro, he's got to go out and catch fish even on days when they're not biting and even on stormy days when he doesn't particularly feel like fishing. It's his job to produce under almost all conditions, and this is what separates the pro from the amateur. 

Likewise, as a professional copywriter, sometimes you'll be given an assignment that's no fun. You can't believe your bad luck in getting such an assignment. You think, "How can I ever sell this? The objection to it is so strong." 

For example, how would you go about selling shoes by mail? Why would _anybody_ buy shoes by mail? You cannot try them on! Well, here's how one smart copywriter tackled this very problem, using the strategy I recommend, confronting 

- 3 4 4 - 

the killer objection right in the headline itself., 

## **Buy Shoes by Mail? You have to be Kidding!** 

Maybe you think buying pair is the finest quality. shoes by mail is just a lit- _-mm_ plus Americans say 11 tie crazy. Four million _M_ **AND MONEY SAVES TIME** you're wrong. This deft headline immediately communicates the proposition (buying shoes by mail), yet also confronts the killer objection head-on. 

The body goes on to say that buying shoes the "normal" way, in a shoe store, is such a risky proposition, because you can wear the shoes only for a minute or two. Anyone who's ever gotten stuck with uncomfortable shoes can relate to that. 

By contrast, the smart way to try shoes is to wear them indoors and outdoors for 10 days, so you can be sure you like them. And that's what you can do when you buy shoes by mail from this company If you don't like the shoes for any reason, just send them back. Shopping from your home is so convenient because it eliminates having to trudge from store to store. You also avoid hassles with traffic, parking, crowds at the mall, and salespeople exerting pressure to buy when you're trying to make up your mind. 

Suddenly, the killer objection has lost its sting. You can never dismiss or ignore a killer objection, or it will kill the sale. The smartest thing you can do is to turn the killer objection into _the single best reason for buying._ 

- 345 - 

Here's another example. 

**==> picture [530 x 581] intentionally omitted <==**

This is a classic old-time ad that illustrates the same principle. 

But remember, these headlines and approaches work best when used _appropriately,_ in a similar situation. You can't just cut and paste them if the situation is not parallel. 

For example, take this headline: "They Thought I Was Crazy to Ship LIVE MAINE LOBSTERS As Far as 1,800 Miles From The Ocean." 

Sometimes I'll see copywriters who unthinkingly cut and paste from these excellent models and completely miss the point. This "they thought I was crazy" strategy should be used when people _really think you may be crazy for doing something._ 

For example, I'll sometimes see a headline that says, "They thought I was crazy to offer a money-back guarantee!" 

_Uh...not really!_ A money-back guarantee is par for the course in direct marketing today, and everyone knows it. Such a headline is patently false, a wild exaggeration, utterly ridiculous and transparent. _Nobody thinks you 're crazy for offering a money-back guarantee in direct marketing today. It's what's expected of you!_ Cutting and pasting this kind of headline means you're being a lazy copywriter and not thinking it through. Your headline bears no resemblance to what your prospect is really thinking. 

As a reality check, when you're writing headlines, somewhere in the process, have the courage to ask yourself, in your effort to make the sale, "Is this true?" 

If it isn't, toss it, if only because other people will smell it as BS instantly. These formulas can work, but only if you use them in parallel situations and your copy and product can pay off your headline. **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #10 You Can Sell the Premium Instead of Your Main Product** 

This was the secret that I mentioned before, inspired Al Davis's defiant, macho football team, the Oakland Raiders. _"We don't take what the defense gives us. We take what we want."_ 

Copywriters, substitute the word "client" for "defense," and you've got a good approach on how you can help your client achieve more breakthroughs. _"We don't take what the client gives us, we take what we want"_ —for the benefit of the client, of course. If something can be improved upon, you owe it to your client to tell him or her how to improve it. 

- 347 - 

For example, this was a package I did not too long ago for the _Health & Healing_ newsletter, published by my good friends at Healthy Directions. 

I felt that just about all the packages in the health newsletter field were starting to look too similar, all touting the same benefits. I felt something new was needed. 

Since so many people are taking cholesterol-lowering drugs, I felt that a package on how to lower cholesterol with natural supplements could do well. But several other health newsletters were already making such claims, and I wanted our package to be different. 

I thought, let me send for every credible book at Amazon about lowering cholesterol and having a healthy heart and see if I can find a new way to convey this message. I ordered close to 40 books and read every one. 

Buried in one of them, I found a little item of research that bowled me over. It was from the Framingham Heart Study, the largest, longest-running, and most respected study of heart disease ever conducted in America. It was begun in Framingham, Massachusetts, in 1948 and is an ongoing study to this day. 

The researchers found that nobody in the entire history of this 50-year study— not a single person!—with a total cholesterol count of 150 or below ever died from a heart attack. Not one person from the thousands being studied! 

This means that if you can get your cholesterol below 150, you have as close to a guarantee as this world gives you that you are not going to die of a heart attack, the leading cause of death in America. 

I had stumbled upon such a powerful proof element that I made it my headline. 

Now the point of all this is that the editor of this newsletter had never written this! I followed the principle of _"go out and find something powerful even if your client isn 't handing it to you!"_ 

## Or, as Al Davis put it, _"We don't take what the defense gives us. We take what we want."_

Of course, when Dr. Whitaker read the copy, he was completely comfortable with it. He just never happened to write that headline or anything like it himself. But he vetted the research, and he said, yes, that is true. And this gave us a strong headline. It wasn't a long-term winner, but it did beat the former control. 

- 348 - 

**==> picture [498 x 620] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [559 x 657] intentionally omitted <==**

This next mailing also used curiosity, latching onto something that's interesting. I did this one for Boardroom, which is famous for having these little nuggets of curiosity-provoking bullets, or what they like to call "fascinations." The performance of this mailing was excellent. Brian, I think this helped to save this publication, right? 

_BRIAN KURTZ:_ You saved it a few times. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ I saved it a few times? Well, thank you. This one brought it back from the brink. 

_JOE POLISH:_ Gary, what is the reason women outlive men? 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ You know, I don't remember! I wrote this long ago. I'll have to look that one up, Joe. That's a good question, provoked by your curiosity. 

As I was mentioning yesterday, you can apply these ideas to any business. Don't just think you have to be a publisher. We've been very lucky to have many of the leading publishers donate the samples in our Pick the Winner Contest earlier today and share results that are normally confidential. 

But please don't think you have to be a publisher or a major advertiser to put these ideas into practice. They work for any business because our prospects are all alike. They want a promise that relates to a problem or desire they're experiencing, strong proof of that promise, and a proposition that spurs them to action. These universal truths cut across all product categories. 

Let me illustrate with this next secret... 

It, too, cuts across all categories and can be a lifesaver when you're not sure what strategy to use. By the way, all of these examples are still within our _"Street-_ — _Smart Copywriting Secret #10 You Can Sell the Premium Instead of Your Main Product."_ As you're beginning to see, this Secret #10 gives you enormous latitude and flexibility in coming up with breakthrough campaigns. 

Some people have asked me on occasion, "What can I do? I have a _commodity_ product." 

## I always answer, _"When in doubt, sell information."_

I believe there's no such thing as a "commodity product," or one that's interchangeable with every competitor's product, not if you know how to combine your product with the right information. Let me cite an example... 

When I worked at Ogilvy & Mather, Shell Oil was one of our biggest accounts. Now, what's more of a commodity product than gasoline, right? How could the agency help Shell Oil differentiate itself? 

- 351 - 

_When in doubt, sell information!_ 

## What the agency did was to produce these little booklets called _The Shell Answer Books._

This was an entire series of extremely helpful, no-nonsense booklets full of useful tips on a wide range of automotive topics—how to get better mileage, what to do if your car breaks down, how to avoid flat tires, etc. 

Space ads in major media would offer these great little booklets free. Just drive into your local Shell station and get yours. Since they were a series of booklets, once you got the first, you kept going back to get more as they were published. 

Naturally, when you visit the station, you don't want to feel like a moocher who just drives in, grabs a freebie, and then leaves. So you get in the habit of filling up while you're at the Shell station. 

Here are some examples of these booklets... 

**==> picture [502 x 253] intentionally omitted <==**

The first is _The Early Warning Book,_ revealing the telltale signs to look for before your brakes go, before your turn signals no longer work, and so on. 

Another is _The Breakdown Book,_ showing you how to be prepared for a breakdown. Very handy to keep in your glove compartment. Then there's _The_ 

## 3 5 2 -

_-_ 

_Gasoline Mileage Book,_ a very popular one on how to get more miles from every gallon of gas you buy, without having to purchase a new car. 

And there were many more in the series. 

How well did this strategy work? 

For this advertiser of a "commodity" product, this campaign brought in _900 million visitors_ into Shell stations across the country. 

900 million! That's astounding! There are, what, not even 300 million people in the entire United States. Yet, counting multiple visits from the same people who kept coming back into Shell stations to get their latest booklets while they filled up their tanks, 900 million of these were given away. 

The beautiful thing is, you can do this with any product. There is no excuse for settling for mediocre advertising because you have a "commodity" product, with nothing to differentiate you. _Information will differentiate you! Good, helpful information that your consumers would love to receive!_ 

You can always have a great informational booklet or article that you're ready to give people in your market, and this in itself differentiates you from your competition. The key is to fill it _not_ with vanity prose about how wonderful your company is, but with information your market will find extremely helpful in solving their problems and fulfilling their desires. Make it about your _prospects,_ not about _you!_ 

For example, you saw the ad I showed you yesterday, "Announcing a direct response advertising agency that will guarantee to outpull your best ad." 

By the way, several people have asked me which ad it was that prompted David Ogilvy to write me a complimentary letter. That was the ad. I still have David's letter and treasure it. 

Anyway, one of the companies that responded to that ad was a guard service, a company that supplies uniformed guards to department stores, charity events, and other functions. 

Guard services are a tough business because the guards don't make much money. Most of them earn close to minimum wage. There's a lot of turnover. And many of the companies who use guards wonder if they've hired a fox to guard the henhouse because you don't know how they are vetted. Also, people tend to think of them, as with gasoline companies, as a "commodity" service. All guard companies are, more or less, the same; at least that's the prevailing view. 

So we applied this strategy— _differentiation by information._ We studied the business and wrote a little booklet, as follows... 

- 353 - 

**==> picture [361 x 511] intentionally omitted <==**

Just as with Frank Perdue and his chickens, we built the campaign around the president of the company, Honest Ed Brandt. 

In the booklet, Honest Ed promised to tell it straight and take all the BS out of how guard services promote themselves. 

He frankly admitted some of the industry problems, such as where to find good men and women to guard your premises without stealing anything themselves. In the booklet, Ed described the great lengths the company goes 

354 - 

- 

through to make sure an employee has a solid history. He admitted turnover is always a problem, but is much lower at Royal Guard Services because the company pays slightly higher than the normal rate, which attracts the best people in the labor pool. And, as with Shell, this was the basis for all our ads, such as these two examples... 

**==> picture [468 x 371] intentionally omitted <==**

Another example: At an industry dinner one time, I found myself sitting next to the executive recruiter Hal Crandall, who specialized in the direct marketing industry for many years. 

Hal asked, "Gary, how can I get more clients?" I suggested that he create a salary guide for every position in the direct marketing field—what are the going salaries for copywriters, marketing managers, designers, copy chiefs, creative directors, account executives, and so on. He published the guide, and it became a red-hot item, as everyone in the industry wanted to know how _their_ salary compares with the industry norm for the same position in other companies. This gave him the best network of contacts in the direct marketing field. 

- 355 - 

Garden Way is a company that sells gardening equipment. To capture lots of names of gardeners, they offer a free spring planting guide. If you have a retirement counseling business, you can offer a free report on how to stay ahead of inflation in your retirement. You can come up with great information on anything! 

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #11 Always Aim for the Core of Your Market, the White-Hot Center** 

This is something that Richard Stanton-Jones and I came to realize. 

When we were sending out mailings on health, we weren't sure how to address the market. Should we talk to _everyone_ about their health, about how to prevent disease? Or should we aim for just those people with serious conditions? Should we go to just the people over 50? 

Over time, we found, after much testing, that it's always good to aim at the dead center of the market, unless that dead center is seven people. In other words, you want to aim your message at _the most passionate core of your market._ 

You don't want to intentionally exclude the people on the periphery, but it's almost like a political campaign. If you don't have your core, passionate troops enthusiastically engaged, you're not going to win. Your best strategy is make sure you're not sounding _too basic or simplistic_ to the core of your market. 

For example, in the food and wine newsletter I'm involved with, we want to be sure that we are aiming for diehard foodies, not "everybody" who likes a good meal now and then. We want to aim our message at those for whom good food and wine are an organizing passion in their lives, something that influences almost everything else, including their travel plans, choice of TV shows, etc. 

Naturally, if other people who are _not_ die-hard foodies get the notion from our mailing that it would be nice to discover the best foods and wines in the world, sure, they're welcome too. But we're not going to position the mailing mostly for them, as they are much less likely to respond. 

Very often you'll face this question: "Should I broaden this appeal and refrain from using the lingo of the most passionate core of this market, so we can be understandable to all? Or should I freely use the lingo and buzz words known to the passionate core and not worry that the broader market might not understand?" I've come to conclude that it's wise to go for the passionate core. 

Let's look at an example... 

- 356 - 

**==> picture [523 x 344] intentionally omitted <==**

This could have been another entry in our Pick the Winner Contest, and it's one that I flunked when I was asked about it back when I started work at Dan Rosenthal's agency. 

The product is a book by economist and historian Percy L. Greaves Jr. Which ad do you think won in a split-run test? 

How many people think the one on the left won? How many people think the winner is the one on the right? 

The one on the right is the winner. It won by a landslide margin. 

Maybe I gave you a clue there. I did not guess this correctly when first shown this pair. The one on the left seems more straightforward and easier to understand. 

This book being advertised was written to appeal to strong adherents of libertarian, laissez-faire economics. To the core of this market, the headline on the right is red meat. They _love_ the "Austrian School of Economics" and regard Ludwig von Mises, the author of the testimonial in the headline, as one of 

- 357 - 

history's great heroes. Of course, you wouldn't know this unless you did your research well, which is my whole point. You must research the core of your market and then aim your message directly at this core. 

## Forgive me, but this next ad always causes me to laugh. I don't know why

**==> picture [500 x 213] intentionally omitted <==**

Let me explain this one. 

Pauline and I are Catholic, and we have a disabled daughter. She is mentally retarded and is taken care of by one of the very fine community-based programs run by the Catholic Church. Our daughter lives in a nice group home not far from us. She lives there with 11 other individuals like herself, all of whom are known and welcome in their community and supervised by the Maryhaven Center of Hope. 

Thanks to Maryhaven, my daughter not only has a home, but also a job. She visits us every couple of weeks. When she does, it's just like with a son or daughter who's a college student. The feeling is, "It's nice to see you again, Mom and Dad, but I'd really like to get back to my gang." Her social life is more lively with her group of friends, and she loves the routine of her home. And we are thrilled about that. 

Naturally, Pauline and I are very grateful for this program. If you have a child like this and, heaven forbid, you're about to be involved in a collision on the highway, you don't want your last thought to be, "Oh my God, what will become of my daughter? Who will take care of her?" 

It gives us great comfort to know that she's in a place that's wonderful, full of love. She goes to her job every day, and she's one contented camper. 

- 358 - 

Because we're so grateful, I told the people who run this and other programs in our diocese that I'm in marketing. "If I can ever do something for you, to repay you in some small way for the wonderful life you've given my daughter, please let me know." 

The bishop of our diocese is the bishop for all of Long Island, in charge of numerous programs. One of his assistants contacted me and said that the bishop could in fact use my help. He would like to reach out and invite all the people of Long Island who are disabled in any way to a large meeting, where he would listen to all of their needs until the last person left the microphone. He wanted to help each one in any way he could. He was a great and saintly man. 

I told them, "If I write this, you're going to have a big response." They said, "That's what we want." 

So I wrote this piece, and it was distributed throughout the parishes of Long Island. Well, it was standing room only at a big auditorium. That poor bishop had no idea how many people were about to show up. But good to his word, he stayed until the last stragglers had their say, after hours of patiently listening to every disabled person who came to speak and ask him for all manner of assistance. 

It was a very touching day, even though the bishop was exhausted by the end of it. I shouldn't laugh every time I see that headline. It's just such an unusual juxtaposition of words in a headline that I somehow find it comical. Nevertheless, it does what I'm advising here. _Always aim for the core of the group you want to influence. Don't pussyfoot around or try to be everything to everybody. If you 're going for a very specific group, go right after them. If you 're trying to attract disabled Catholics, let your headline say, "Disabled and Catholic? "_ 

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #12 Never Try to Manufacture Desire— Far Better to Channel Existing Desires** 

Let me share a perfect example of a package that tried, unsuccessfully, to educate people into wanting a product. 

I wrote this for Phillips Publishing. Most of Phillips' lists in the early days of the company were built with names of male investors. Most investment newsletter subscribers, by far, are male. 

The good people at Phillips wondered, why not have a financial letter _just for women?_ They decided to launch such a newsletter and wanted me to write the advertising for it. At first I was puzzled. Is financial information _gender specific?_ 

## I can understand a _health_ letter for women, as women's health concerns are

- 359 - 

different from men's. But an investment letter? How would that be different for women? Why would a stock or bond be a better buy _for a woman or man?_ 

They said that their research showed that many women felt uncomfortable with existing financial advice, that much of it seemed geared to a man's needs, was too complex, and didn't address a woman's different roles in life. 

Phillips hired a female editor and stressed that no one can understand a woman's financial concerns as well as another woman, one highly qualified to help other women through the tough financial and investment decisions of life. 

I was still dubious, because there were very few lists of female investors. But they urged me to give it a shot, so I gave it my best try, with this package... 

**==> picture [492 x 224] intentionally omitted <==**

It flopped. We found that most of the lists were comprised of married couples, and in most of these families, the man handled the investment decisions. We even tried taking the names on Phillips' house list, and just changing "Mr. Jones" to "Mrs. Jones," even though we had no idea if there was a "Mrs. Jones" in the household. Our hope was that, in most cases, there would be, and that Mrs. Jones would open the envelope. 

Alas, even in those cases, since the man of the house usually handled the investing, when this mail arrived addressed to Mrs. Jones, she just handed it to him anyway. That was _his_ job in the house. 

We failed with this because we were trying to educate people into wanting something that they should have, rather than something they actually wanted. Big 

- 360- 

mistake. If you ever find yourself writing a letter where it seems necessary to educate the prospect into having a desire for the benefits you hope to deliver, beware. You're already in trouble. Far better to go with proven, preexisting desires, of which there are many. 

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #13 It's Easier to Sell to People Who Know You** 

When you send out direct mail, if you are unknown to the people on the list, you are at a great disadvantage. If you can joint venture with somebody who is known, you greatly improve your odds. 

For example, Lou Rukeyser has for decades been revered as America's leading financial journalist. TV viewers have thought of him for many years as Uncle Lou. 

If you can do a joint venture with Lou's financial newsletter, you get to ride on his coattails. You get right in the front door to sell your product, escorted by Uncle Lou, the family friend. Always look for people who can, either in a joint venture or as an editor of a product, give you an endorsement, either actual or implied. Failing that, you can also try to offer a premium, such as a book written by someone famous and trusted whom your marketplace knows and loves. Their "halo" effect will rub off on you and your offer. 

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #14 Avoid Marketing "Challenges"** 

Speaking of investing, we in advertising can learn a few things from the great investors. Here is one of the most important... 

Many people think that legendary investors like Warren Buffett and mutual fund genius Peter Lynch are fantastic risk takers. We tend to think, "What a world they live in! They make huge bets, sometimes billions of dollars, on different companies. What a high stakes game of risk!" 

But if you have ever listened to interviews with either of those two gentlemen, Warren Buffett or Peter Lynch, they say, no, we're not great risk takers at all. We're great risk _avoiders._ They emphasize that they will go to almost any lengths to squeeze out the risk from a proposition. If they see too much risk, they pass. When they find something that looks almost like a sure thing, they compare it to maybe a hundred other nearly sure things and of those choose only one or two that look like the surest. And that's how they build wealth—not by taking risk, but by carefully measuring and avoiding it when unnecessary. 

## So, too, in advertising. You may think there's a great machismo spirit of

- 361 - 

conquering where others have failed. You may feel flattered when someone asks you to sell what no one could sell, to persuade the most skeptical doubting Thomases, to convince Eskimos to buy ice and Saudis to lease sand. 

But you're most likely headed for the same failure as those before you. If something is a high-risk proposition, there's risk for a reason. If you want to compound your wealth and success in marketing the way Warren Buffett and Peter Lynch do in investing, avoid these pitfalls. Follow their lead and _squeeze out the risk._ Look for sure things. Your time is limited, and you want to invest it in projects _most likely to succeed big._ You don't want your signature on flops. 

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #15 To Write Great Headlines, Collect Great Headlines** 

If you want to collect great headlines, read the _Reader's Digest._ You'll quickly learn how they take a long and sometimes boring article, condense it, and make it very interesting, often capturing your attention with a compelling headline that makes the material relevant to your life. 

Same for the _National Enquirer._ People turn up their nose at the _National Enquirer._ I always read it. It gives me a chuckle. Their headline writers must make a lot of money because their headlines are hard to resist. 

In fact, I recommend having someone cover up with a piece of paper all of the headlines in either the _Reader s Digest_ or _National Enquirer_ or any newspaper that comes up with great headlines. 

Or, if you're not going to cheat, cover up the headline yourself. Read the story and see what headline you can come up with for the article. Then lift up the piece of paper and see how poorly you fared against the actual headlines. It's a humbling exercise. They're really, really good. I say this from experience, because I never seem able to come up with headlines as good as theirs. Maybe if I put in a lot more time I could. 

Let me give you an example... 

Here in New York we have a sensationalist newspaper, the _New York Post._ What I'm about to show you became a famous headline here in New York. 

A number of years ago, the New York City police received a report that there was a decapitated man in one of these sleazy Times Square strip clubs, one of the very places I saw some of the copywriters slinking toward last night (just kidding!). Sure enough, when the police arrived, they found a decapitated man in this sleazy strip joint, but they couldn't identify him because he had no head and 

- 362 - 

no wallet. So the headline the next morning in the _New York Post_ was... 

**==> picture [434 x 433] intentionally omitted <==**

When you compete against headlines like this, you're going to get good. 

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #16 To Create More and Better Headlines, You Should Come Up with Several on Each of the Four Pillars of The Persuasion Equation®** 

## By that, I mean come up with a batch of headlines in each one of these four

- 363 - 

categories—your prospect's most urgent problem, your unique promise, your unquestionable proof, and your user-friendly proposition. 

Let me give you some examples... 

**==> picture [399 x 182] intentionally omitted <==**

Here is a nice little headline illustrating _&_ _**problem**_ that many people have. Now, here's a typical headline built on a _promise..._ 

**==> picture [302 x 350] intentionally omitted <==**

- 364 - 

"Double your closet space in minutes." I see that ad repeated often. 

Or, three, if you have great proof elements, you might lead off your headline with your _proof._ This is _Money_ magazine, using their credentials for a special investment program that they have. 

**==> picture [344 x 192] intentionally omitted <==**

Your prospects mentally have to go through all four steps of _The Persuasion Equation®_ no matter what. They have to know that you're solving _& problem_ in their lives, that you're making _& promise,_ that you're _proving_ it, and that you're offering _a. proposition._ You have to begin the conversation somewhere, so you can try all four of those starting points to see what you come up with. 

And don't forget to try starting with the last of the four "Ps," the proposition, just to see what you can come up with. It's one of the most fertile yet overlooked hunting grounds for good headlines. Take this great little ad, for example... 

**==> picture [333 x 232] intentionally omitted <==**

- 365 - 

That last ad sold a lot of planes. 

I especially urge the people here from the traditional mass market publishing companies to try different offers. I think this is the least cultivated field for improving your results. 

As copywriters, especially those of us who work on royalties, we hardly ever look at improving our clients' offers, for two reasons. 

Number one, we don't want to take the risk that our copy is superior, but in changing the offer, we guessed wrong about it and then lost the test. This will have cost us months of work, down the drain, just because we tried a new offer and it didn't work. We don't like taking that risk unless you'll give us _two_ separate test panels, one to test our copy with the same old offer and a second to test our copy with the new and hopefully improved offer. Many clients, just like copywriters, don't explore the offer nearly enough as an avenue for testing. They're too rigid about this and therefore are missing out on some major breakthroughs. 

The other reason that we copywriters fail to test the offer enough is that there's no way to protect the idea as ours. Let's say I'm working for a client who's always testing different writers against each other. 

Let's say also that I'm one of those writers, and I go to great lengths, invest a lot of time, researching offers, and I come up with a brilliant one. Let's say further that you test the offer, and it's a monster breakthrough I've given you. 

What happens in the next round of tests? All the other copywriters competing against me on that product will just slap that offer on the back of their copy, and I've done a lot of work and research just to help their packages compete with mine. 

In other words, there's no incentive for royalty-based copywriters to work on your offers, because they are not protected against competition in that area. 

I'm not sure I have a solution for that. We'll talk about that later, about better ways that copywriters and clients can work together. 

All I know is that far too many direct marketers are paying far too little attention to testing their offers. If you look, you'll find lots of ways to dramatically improve your offer. It's one of the most volatile and leveraged ways to improve your response—come up with a more enticing offer. And that doesn't always mean a lower price. It could be a higher price. Your prospects use price as a key proof element, and surprising as it may seem, I've often seen a higher price yield a higher gross response and therefore a _much_ higher profit. But you must test new prices and new offers to find these breakthroughs. 

- 366- 

In fact, Gary Halbert mentioned a great offer to me last night. Let's get the mike over to Gary Halbert, in the center of the hall, the gentleman with the beret. 

Last night, Gary Halbert told me about a breakthrough he had with an offer. Last night, I had a sip of ginger ale with some friends, and Gary Halbert was there. 

By the way, thank you all for inviting me to different places. Later today, after our last session, we'll have a chance to get together and party. Yesterday I mostly had to keep within myself and turn down many invitations to be with many of you, just to make sure I'm on top of everything I want you to experience at the seminar. I want to give you the best seminar I can, so I've got to stay focused. I didn't make it over to that place in Times Square last night. 

Anyway, let's listen to Gary Halbert's idea. Go ahead, Gary. 

_GARY HALBERT:_ What was the question? 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ You had a comment about an offer that can triple response. _GARY HALBERT:_ Oh, yeah. I'm kind of afraid to stand up here because I forgot my name tag and Pauline may throw me out! _(LAUGHTER)_ 

I don't know if this idea would apply to a lot of you, and maybe it would wear out, but it's been proven over and over and over, and I think I ought to give a seminar on how to get Bencivenga-like results by cheating with different techniques and tricks. This is one of those tricks. 

Guthy-Renker, for example, is the world's biggest infomercial commercial producer. They had one with Victoria Principal where they could only make it break even. I told them all you have to do is put on a _**30-day hold.**_ Tell the audience that you won't cash the check for 30 days or that you won't process the credit card for 30 days. They took my advice, and it ran for seven years. 

I worked with another guy who was a publisher. He published a book on how to become a mail order millionaire. But he didn't know how to become a mail order millionaire, because he mostly sold his books through retail outlets. So he asked me to change the ad. Well, I did rewrite the ad, but the main thing that I put into the ad was the 30-day hold. 

This was at the time where there were very few credit card orders; there were mostly check orders. I remember standing in his office, and he was almost apoplectic. He pulled a bunch of checks out of a drawer and said, "Look at all these bad checks I got with your crazy idea!" 

I said to him, "Mel, did the ads that produced those bad checks make a profit?" And he said, "Oh, yeah!" 

- 367 - 

I said, "You should wish you had a barn full of them in that case!" 

Now this is not etched in stone, but it's fairly reliable. You've talked a lot about making people feel comfortable with the purchase, taking the fear out of it. If you say you're not going to cash the check or you're not going to process the credit card, on many offers that will triple the sales and it will double the refund rate. Let me illustrate that. 

Let's say that you have something—I'm going to greatly simplify this—that takes in $100 and you have a 10 percent refund rate. Then you have a $90 contribution to overhead. 

Let's say you add the 30-day hold. Then you have $300 income, but that 10 percent refund rate jumps to 20 percent. Twenty percent of $300 is $60, and in my way of thinking it is better to have a $240 contribution to overhead than $90. And all you have to do is test that. 

For many mailers, there's a lot of psychological resistance to doing that. They feel, "I'm going to get cheated if I just test it." 

I have never known a single person who tested this who came back and found that it didn't make more money— _significantly_ more money—than not adding that 30-day hold. And these are some pretty good-sized advertisers. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ Thank you, Gary. And the main point is, even if that offer doesn't seem right for you, do a lot of testing of alternate offers—lower the price, raise the price, change the term, whatever. As I mentioned, sometimes a higher price will bring in more orders and much more dollar volume because the price is a proof element itself that something is much more valuable. 

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #17 Use the "If...Then" Technique** 

Another idea to try in your headline is the _"If...Then "_ technique. 

## Some of you have seen this idea mentioned in the ezine I write, the _Bencivenga Bullets._

It's a very effective way to write a headline. 

For example, let's take a look at two direct mail packages—both magalogs— that I wrote for the financial guru Charles J. Givens... 

- 368 - 

**==> picture [498 x 335] intentionally omitted <==**

Between these two ads, the one on the left was the runaway winner. We have someone in the audience who came up to me yesterday and said that after reading about this technique in the _Bullets,_ he wrote a similar headline for the DVD real estate course he sells. In his ad, the only change he made was to use this headline, and his response soared by 200 percent and sold more than a million courses in 24 hours. 

That's how effective this headline can be because of the psychology it harnesses. 

Instead of using the classic type of headline from John Caples, "Give me blank, blank, blank, and I'll give you a blah, blah, blah," this involves the reader more. 

It holds up a conditional finger that seems to be a magical ingredient in boosting response. It says, in effect, "If you can meet this requirement, you can experience the benefit." In other words, you've got to qualify in some way, which for some reason, makes the reader _want_ to qualify and experience the benefit. What's so interesting is that you can make the condition fairly easy, yet it 

- 369 - 

nevertheless usually adds tremendous propulsion to the proposition. 

The reader, or viewer if it's an infomercial, seems to respond, "Wait a minute, if I can meet this condition, I can get the benefit? Well, heck yes! I can meet that condition!" And this makes the person happy to qualify, that he or she can be one of those allowed to experience the benefit. He knew the "password," he met the qualification and was allowed entry. 

This package for Givens was so successful that the publisher called me, and even though I didn't write the copy for Richard Simmons on this next piece, he asked if he could borrow the same headline. 

I said sure, go ahead, no problem, and thanks for asking. 

**==> picture [410 x 359] intentionally omitted <==**

Once again, in this case against a very strong control package, this new front cover for Richard Simmons proved to be another blockbuster. 

Isn't that a cute outfit on him? 

## Richard's publisher saw a big boost in response just by testing this new

370 - 

- 

headline. 

And you can use this as a formula for almost anything... 

_"Ifyou've got (fill in the blank), then I guarantee to (blah, blah, blah)."_ 

This headline formula was such a breakthrough, I'd like to tell you how I _knew_ it would work even before it was tested for Charles J. Givens. This next technique is one of the most original and valuable I have to share with you, because it reliably gives you an advance reading of the success _of anything_ you're thinking of testing... 

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #18 Use the "Translation Technique"** 

When I first came up with that headline for Givens _"If you 've got 20 minutes a month, I guarantee to work a financial miracle in your life,"_ I had no idea if it was any good. 

But I knew I'd find out within seconds. Here's how. 

Very often, for the past 40 years now, I've been chasing Pauline around the house asking, _"Do you like this headline? "_ 

Very often she'll say, "I don't know," and doesn't want to guess in case it influences me in a wrong direction. 

So we came up with a simple tool that anybody can use, and it's very effective in knowing if a headline is good. I named it _"The Translation Technique."_ 

For example, when I first came up with that headline for Givens, I really wasn't sure about it. 

I didn't know if the qualification was strong enough. Twenty minutes a month to see a financial miracle in your life? Is that believable? Is "financial miracle" too vague? I worry about such things. 

So I asked Pauline, "Can you read this for me?" She's my first circle of editing help. She said, "I think it's good, but it's not a product I'm terribly interested in. I would never buy a financial newsletter, so I'm not a good prospect. I don't want my lack of interest in the subject to misguide you." 

She knew what was coming next. 

## I said, as I always do, "OK, _let's go to translation!"_

That means that if Pauline is not very interested in this subject, I _translate_ my headline into a subject she _is_ very interested in. 

## For example, Pauline is a great cook, and she loves to learn more about Italian

- 371 - 

cooking. So I said, "Forget that it's Charles J. Givens on the cover. 

"Let's imagine instead that it's a photo of a famous Italian chef, let's say, Lidia Bastianich from TV [Lidia teaches on PBS how to make great Italian food, and is someone Pauline watches often.] 

"So, Pauline, let's say it's Lidia on the front cover, and she's saying, _'If you 've got 20 minutes a month, I guarantee to turn you into a master Italian cook.'_ 

"Now, would _that_ interest you?" 

Pauline's face now lit up instantly with interest, and she said, "I'd buy that in minute!" 

At that moment—without focus groups, without a three-month testing cycle, without anything further at all—I knew we had a big winner for Givens. 

It's as simple and reliable as that. 

You can use this idea any time you're not sure how your prospects will react to a headline or piece of body copy, either because you're too close to it or you have no natural passion for this type of product yourself yet must sell it to those who do. 

_**Just substitute your headline into afield that you do feel passionately about.**_ It's so simple! 

For example, to verify Pauline's instant reaction of excitement when I translated this _"If...then "_ Givens headline into something she enjoys learning about, I did the same thing for myself. 

I'm very interested in persuasion. I'll buy just about any book or course about how to be more persuasive. It's my passion. 

So, although I could not tell if the Givens headline was any good, since I was so close to it, I used the translation technique on myself. 

I imagined that instead of Charles J. Givens on the front cover, it's Gerry Spence, the colorful cowboy lawyer who's so skilled at persuasion, he's never lost a criminal case and hasn't lost a civil case since 1969. Gerry has won more multimillion-dollar verdicts without an intervening loss than any other lawyer in America. 

You've probably seen Gerry on TV, wearing his trademark fringed suede cowboy jacket, as if he's just back from the frontier and has hitched his horse to the parking meter outside. He's a colorful, remarkably persuasive human being. 

So, to know immediately if I found my Givens headline strong or not, I just translated it into a subject I am passionate about. 

- 372 - 

I thought, how would I react if Gerry Spence was on the front cover, with his amazing credentials under his photo and a headline that says, _"If you 've got 20 minutes a month, I guarantee to make you a master of persuasion."_ 

"Wow," I thought. "I'd jump on _that_ in a New York minute!" I'd open up that magalog with just one question in mind— _"Where's that order form!"_ 

Even if I had never heard of Gerry Spence but was interested in learning about persuasion, I'd still be sold by the cover, as long as his proof elements were there under his photo. This again shows you how critical proof elements are. 

Let's say I never knew Gerry but am very interested in persuasion. 

I see a headline that promises, _"If you 've got 20 minutes a month, I guarantee to make you a master of persuasion."_ 

My thought process would be "Master of persuasion? Oh, yeah, I'd love to be that! But who is this guy anyway? Who is he to make such a promise?" 

Then I'd look under his photo and read his credentials, whereupon I'd see, "Gerry Spence, the famous cowboy lawyer who's so skilled at persuasion, he's never lost a criminal case and hasn't lost a civil case since 1969." 

Even if I never heard of Gerry until that moment, I'd open that magalog with just one question on my mind— _"Where s that order form? "_ 

Do you see how easily this works? Just translate whatever headline you're thinking of using into a subject you're passionate about, and you'll know instantly how strong or weak it is. 

In many cases, you'll find that the headline is disappointingly weak and you've got to do better. But it's much smarter to find this out early, before you mail, when you can fix it, rather than after a losing test, when there's egg on your face and a failure on your track record. 

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #19 Useful Phrases for Starting Your Body Copy** 

Here are four great phrases I've given copywriters over the years who've worked with me in my copy department, at least back when I worked at agencies, before I went out on my own. 

These are very useful phrases for starting your body copy when you're not sure how to begin. 

The first is "Picture this..." After those two words, you just describe a typical situation that your prospect might find himself or herself in, in need of a great solution. 

- 373 - 

If you're ever wondering how to start your body copy, setting it up this way is very easy. 

"Picture this"... and then paint a word picture of the prospect experiencing the problem or the solution. 

For example, and I'm literally just making this up off the top of my head, let's say you're selling a burglar alarm system. 

You can start the copy with "Dear Homeowner, Picture this. You've just returned home from a night out with friends. You're tired but pleased at how well the evening went. But as soon as you turn the key and open your front door, your heart sinks. Your house has been robbed. Ransacked. Every drawer in every room has been pulled out, your precious personal belongings are strewn about everywhere, as if they were trash. You feel so utterly violated. But your rising anger quickly turns to fear as you wonder, _'What's missing? What did they take?"_ 

You just paint the scene, and you can probably do it much better than I'm doing off the top of my head, of what a horror it's like to come home and find your house ransacked by thieves. Then you transition into some facts about how often this happens in America. But it need never happen to you and your family when you're protected by..." 

That's a simple example you can apply to just about anything you're selling. 

Another handy leadoff sentence is _Has this ever happened to you?_ Then just talk about an experience that many people have had, so they can immediately relate to your promise. 

If your product has many attractive benefits, you can start off by summarizing them. Say simply, _Now you can...and_ then just fill in a whole string of benefits. That's another very good way to start your body copy. 

## Or you can just use the word _Imagine..._

And you can take that anywhere you want to go—a description of the problem; the promise; a sensuous, emotionally satisfying experience of the benefits; a description of a hard-to-believe offer that really is on the level—whatever seems natural for you to talk about, given your product or offer. 

For example, this was an ad I did for Ted Nicholas. Ted, as we all know, is a brilliant copywriter, a legend. But every now and then he likes to bring in other copywriters to see if they can take a slant that he might not have thought of. This was an ad I wrote for him on how to incorporate yourself and discover America's most powerful tax shelter. This ad ran in numerous, numerous magazines for many years... 

- 374 - 

**==> picture [508 x 594] intentionally omitted <==**

## Take a look at the body copy. I'm going to read it aloud. It summarizes all the

- 375 - 

benefits of becoming your own corporation... 

**Imagine having all your family's medical and dental bills paid for tax-free. Imagine owning two family cars tax-free. Imagine having your hobby expenses paid for tax-free. And how about tax-free life, health, and income-protection insurance? And would you enjoy tax-free trips...a tax-free boat, plane, or hunting lodgc.or tax-free meals at fine restaurants? These are just some of the remarkable tax advantages and fringe benefits that can be yours** — **through the most powerful tax shelter in America** _**owning your** own corporation._ 

You can see how effective it is to start off with "Imagine" as a word that links a whole series of glorious benefits. 

The copy in this ad goes on to say that, to encourage small business, Congress has filled the tax laws with loopholes that richly reward those who form small corporations. As a result, more than 30,000 people start corporations _each month._ 98 percent are small businesses, often one-person operations. These people have discovered that it's _remarkably easy_ to start your own corporation...(the copy then describes how the reader can do it too). 

## **- Street Smart Copvwriting Secret #20 Use the "Hard to Believe" Question** 

This is another excellent technique that most writers have never heard of. I've dubbed it the "hard to believe" question. 

I love to use this right after presenting a whole string of great benefits that may sound too good to be true. Use this piece of copy exactly as it's written here. 

**Hard to believe? Perhaps. But I'm not asking you to believe anything just yet, until you see the evidence for yourself. All I ask is that you** _**refrain from disbelieving**_ **while I show you my proof. It will take just a few minutes, yet the rewards can be enormous because...(go back to benefits).** 

What you do with this piece of copy is say to someone, "Look, I acknowledge that this may seem hard to believe. But I'm not asking you to believe anything yet. [In other words, you're selling them _not_ on the product, but on giving you a fair hearing, a critically important difference.] 

"So, sure, don't believe me yet. I wouldn't expect you to. But please don't _disbelieve_ me yet either, because you haven't seen what I want to show you." And that's a reasonable proposition. 

- 376 - 

Most of your readers, the overwhelming majority, are too busy to want to bother with your message right now, so they're looking for a fast, easy excuse to toss it. 

If you seem too unbelievable, they're gone. Right before they're about to reach that point, after you've presented a series of marvelous benefits, right at that point is where you want to freeze them in place with this eminently reasonable proposition—don't believe me yet, all I ask is that you _refrain from disbelieving_ for a few minutes while I explain how this works. 

Most people like to think of themselves as fair-minded, even in small matters. This appeals to that sense of self. It's a very fair proposition that goes a long way toward winning you a hearing. 

Your foot is in the door, and you can now start to tell your whole story. _You ve successfully sold them not on your product, but on the chance to make your case._ It's then the job of your sales presentation to sell the product. But your first order of business is always to _sell the sales presentation, not the product._ If you don't, if you try to jump right into selling the product, you may well have the door slammed in your face. _"I'm too busy now, and you 're too unbelievable."_ 

A funny thing about this technique—right after you use that "hard to believe" paragraph, you can go right back to selling the benefits of your product or service. At least for a while, the doubting Thomas in your prospect's mind has been quieted, put on hold. 

He'll show up again later, you can be sure, so you should always keep layering on your proof elements. But for now, you can feel free to start selling and getting your prospect really interested in the benefits of what you offer. 

Here's why this "hard to believe" paragraph works so well... 

All of us have different "personalities" inside us. Each of us has an emotional inner child who wants to buy everything right away. He or she has no selfdiscipline. We also have an inner adult who watches and protects our inner child. 

When a new opportunity knocks on the door of our consciousness, especially from a stranger, our inner child may answer the doorbell (i.e., start reading the ad), but our inner adult quickly marches to the front door and takes over. He or she is _**not**_ about to let any salesperson into the house—unless _proof_ is presented. Proof of legitimacy. Proof of reasonableness. Proof that this is not a scam. Proof that the inner child will not be harmed. 

So you must, early on in your copy, acknowledge this doubting Thomas, this guardian of the inner child. You must quickly assuage this defensive demand for 

- 377 - 

legitimacy. 

Many copywriters don't understand how important this is. And there's a reason for that. In the earlier days of advertising, before everyone was so overmarketed, people were more relaxed about letting a salesperson, in person or in print, have their shot. They'd sit still longer. 

And that's why many copywriters and salespeople from earlier times espoused appealing directly to emotion, not worrying too much about proof. _"Emotion is what sells,"_ they proclaimed. And many, many copywriters are unfortunately emulating that approach even now, not realizing how different our world has become. 

Yes, it's absolutely true that emotion sells. But it's also true that, in today's overmarketed world, _you '11 rarely get the chance_ to sell on pure emotion unless you first show up at the front door wearing a big badge of proof, which serves as your "ID pass" that you're legitimate, that you're not just a fast-talking hype artist. Without that "ID badge" of proof showing very visibly when the front door opens, you'll seldom get the chance to come inside and sell. 

But if, in the first critical minute of your advertising, you win over that monitor, that doubting Thomas, _then_ you will gain entry and can bring in all the emotion you want, because by then you're past the guardian at the door. The sentry has been satisfied, has retreated to the background and invited you in. 

And you don't have to spend a lot of time proving everything up-front. What this technique lets you do is simply say, "Look, I know you want proof, and I've got it aplenty. It's coming. Just bear with me a bit, and I will certainly respect and honor the doubting Thomas of the house. I'm not like all the others who try to do an end run around Mr. or Ms. Doubting Thomas. Unlike such hype artists, I will provide all the proof you require if you'll just allow me a few moments to tell my story." 

That works wonders because Mr. or Ms. Doubting Thomas can then relax and grant you entry. You've shown the right "ID pass"—the ID pass of proof or at least the promise of proof to come—and are now welcomed in. 

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #21 Never Let a Negative Statement Stand Alone... Follow It Up with a Positive Statement, Plus Proof** 

Just a quick example. When showing you a house, a real estate agent might say, "This house costs $350,000," and leave it at that. 

## In selling and in copywriting you don't want to leave it at that. You might say

- 378 - 

instead, "This house costs $350,000, and that's a good buy in this neighborhood." That's a step up. You didn't just leave a possible negative (the price) out there, but followed it up with a positive. However, your positive is still just a claim. 

Even better would be to say, "This house costs $350,000, and that's a good buy in this neighborhood. We know that because our online appraisal services show that the average sale price within a four-block radius is $427,000. So at $350,000, this is an excellent buy. I'll show you the research and print out a copy for you back at the office." 

See what a big difference proof makes in creating desire and conviction? 

## **- Street Smart Convwriting Secret #22 Use an Outline to Organize Your Copy in a Logical Flow** 

This is something I've always told young copywriters. Sometimes I'd have a young copywriter come to work for me when I was a copy chief and tell me, "I don't like outlines. I like to go with my flow of consciousness." 

No way, dude. You _are_ going to use an outline because our readers may not be in sync with _your_ unique "flow of consciousness." 

I'm not saying that you have to write every ad the same way: describe the problem, elaborate your promise, emphasize your proof, etc. As I mentioned about headlines before, you can try an entry point anywhere along the spectrum of _The Persuasion Equation®._ 

Just be sure that each paragraph flows naturally and logically from the one before. 

My test is, can I start reading the ad somewhere in the middle and feel _driven back_ to the start of the ad? 

In other words, it should be written tightly enough so that if you break into the ad in the middle, you'll feel you've got to start higher up in the copy to understand the story. 

## **- Street Smart Copywriting Secret #23 Enlist the Bucket Brigade to Keep Your Paragraphs Flowing** 

To help your copy flow smoothly and tightly, enlist the bucket brigade, stationing "linkage" phrases at the beginning of your paragraphs: moreover...not only that, but...what's more...and we're just getting started...this is why...in addition 

- 379 - 

...because of these factors...etc. Use lots of phrases like these that keep forcing the reader north until he or she concludes, "I better read this from the top to get the full story." 

Also, you want to arrange your flow of thoughts to match what your prospect is likely feeling at any moment. If you make a strong claim that invites skepticism, right there is where you want to say, "I know this may sound hard to believe, but let me show you the eye-opening proof, as recently published in the major trade magazine, _XYZ Gazette..."_ 

Or if your gut instinct tells you that the selling point you've just made will likely evoke an objection such as "I just can't afford something like this," right there is where you should answer the objection. "At first, you may think that the cost of such a widget with all these benefits would be out of reach. But thanks to our breakthrough production process, it is now more reasonably priced than you might ever suspect..." 

Write as if you're having a conversation between _you_ as the writer _and you_ as the reader. All of these little tips will help you snare the impatient reader who merely wants to skim and get away or, more likely, find a quick excuse to throw your piece away so he can get back to his life. Don't let him. When he tries to cut into the letter halfway through or at the back, you want him to conclude immediately, "Oops, I've missed something. Better start reading higher up." If you do this throughout your copy, you'll force him all the way north to your headline. 

Now I'd like to talk about some psychological hot buttons that persuade others... 

## **Psychological Hot Button #1 People Desperately Want to Be Consistent** 

This is one of my favorite "hot buttons" to use when persuading people. 

It was described by Dr. Cialdini in his great book on influence. But I was using it long before I read his book, simply because I'd discovered pragmatically how well it works. 

For example, I just heard a TV commercial the other day that said something like "You wouldn't think of buying a car without taking a test drive. And that's why we invite you to accept a free trial of..." 

The appeal to consistent behavior sounds so logical, reasonable, even reassuring. The offer sucks me right in because I want to be consistent. We all do. 

Recently I heard a leading Democrat on the news using this very psychology. She said about the Bush administration, "They're spending $87 billion to rebuild 

380 - 

- 

Iraq, but refuse to spend $1 billion to rebuild New Orleans." 

Another good example was in 1975 here in New York. We had a feisty little mayor named Abe Beam. Normally, New York City sends many more tax dollars to Washington and, by extension, the rest of the country, than we ever get back. 

But in 1975, the city had a fiscal crisis, and there was even the potential for New York City to declare bankruptcy. Our mayor, Abe Beam, pleaded with President Gerald Ford for emergency assistance, just to get over the hump, and then New York would be fine. We'd pay it back very soon. 

President Ford said no. Maybe it was politics, since Beam was a Democrat and Ford a Republican. Or it could have just been than Ford didn't want to open a Pandora's box and encourage all the other big cities to start coming to him, hats in hand, which is certainly understandable. His response to New York was, "You've got to keep your own financial house in order. No bailout." 

Abe Beam was furious. He researched the federal budget, then went before the microphones, and said, "Can you believe this? Ford approves sending billions in foreign aid to Moscow, but not one thin dime to New York City in our hour of need!" His argument was deadly effective and was reflected in this headline on the cover of the _New York Daily News..._ 

**==> picture [314 x 331] intentionally omitted <==**

- 381 -
