President Ford, in the next presidential election, lost New York State and was never reelected president. He later said that he lost the election because of that headline in the _Daily News._ 

## **Psychological Hot Button #2 Fear of Loss Is a Stronger Motivator Than Desire for Gain** 

This is an interesting and very telling bit of psychology. _Fear of loss is a much stronger motivator than desire for gain._ 

I learned this from a master salesman. I'll explain it this way... 

Imagine that you and I are neighbors. I call you up at two in the morning one night and, full of enthusiasm, say, "Hey, neighbor, it's Gary from across the street. How ya doin'?" 

Through the fog of sleep, you mumble, "Gary, what's up? Is something wrong?" 

I say, "Heck no! I just had to tell you about this. I just heard that down at the auto center in town, they're having one of these midnight madness sales, and it's going all night long. Listen to this—if you scoot over there now, they'll give you four brand-new snow tires! No strings attached! Isn't that unbelievable? Yes, I know it's mid-July, but that's an awesome giveaway, don't you think?" 

Your response would probably be something like, "Gary, I know you really love this marketing stuff, but you've got to get yourself a life, man. Do you realize you're calling me at two in the morning to tell me about a stupid tire promotion? I don't care how good it is. I'm going back to sleep, and please don't ever again wake me up at this time of night to tell me about a tire promotion, OK?" 

Now imagine this scenario instead... 

I call you up at two in the morning and say, "Hey, neighbor, it's Gary from across the street." 

You say, "Gary, what's up? Is something wrong?" 

I say, "Listen, you must have left your garage open last night. Do you know those four snow tires you've got stacked up in your garage? Well, two teenagers are stealing them! I can see them from my bedroom window. They're rolling them out of your garage right now and putting them in the trunk of their car!" 

What's your response this time? 

You bolt out of bed, fly down the stairs, possibly risking your life, to save your four tires. 

- 382 - 

## Just think about this: _it's the same four snow tires in each case!_

But in the first example—the desire for gain—you couldn't care less. 

In the second—the fear of loss—you're willing to risk your life. 

Believe me, your writing will be far more persuasive if you remember this simple truth. You can harness it in almost any selling situation—by emphasizing what can be lost by not taking action, as well as what's to be gained. 

Let me show you a sophisticated example of how to use this psychology... 

**==> picture [365 x 271] intentionally omitted <==**

Remember yesterday, I described a campaign for a financial newsletter where we tested two different ads. One was "The Case Against Tax Exempts," which talked about the reasons to be wary of tax exempt bonds...versus "7 South African Golds Poised to Soar." 

"The Case Against Tax Exempts" won in a landslide. 

Why? 

Because the bond market is nine times bigger than the stock market. Tens of millions of investors, many of them retired, own tax exempt bonds and depend on them for income. And here we are saying, in effect, "Watch out! There's something terribly wrong with tax exempt bonds, very possibly _yours"_ 

## Well, if teenagers stealing your snow tires would make you bolt out of bed, can

- 383 - 

you imagine how engaged you'd likely become if you see a headline saying that a big piece of your life savings, the source of your income in retirement, may be in some sort of jeopardy? 

So that "Case Against Tax Exempts" doesn't look like a powerhouse headline on the surface, but because it attacks something investors already own lots of, it gets a lot of attention and interest. 

"7 South African Golds Poised to Soar," on the other hand, tries to tap into the desire for gain, but just as with the opportunity to get four free tires in the middle of the night, it's not nearly as motivating _as protecting what's already yours._ 

By the way, "The Case Against" ad was so successful as a space ad, we took it into direct mail, as follows... 

**==> picture [341 x 432] intentionally omitted <==**

- 384 - 

Many years later, I applied the same psychology with a magalog for a mutual fund letter published by Phillips. Instead of telling readers about great _new_ funds to buy, I applied the "teenagers-stealing-your-tires" psychology to mutual funds. 

We asked the editor of the newsletter, Jay Schabacker, to name all the large mutual funds that had serious problems. He named almost all the large ones, meaning that most mutual fund investors were in funds with hidden risks. 

This very successful mailing was the result. Again, if you're a mutual fund investor and you see this magalog, you have to check if your fund is listed. If it _is,_ you've got to keep reading to discover _"What's wrong with my fund? " A_ warning has been issued by a very astute fund analyst about the fund you own! You _must_ investigate, just as you surely had to prevent the teenagers from stealing your snow tires. You have no choice. When something you own is threatened, you can't ignore the threat, and this instinctive reaction is much stronger than the desire for gain. 

**==> picture [449 x 417] intentionally omitted <==**

**385** 

## **Psychological Hot Button #3 Build "Takeaways" into Your Offers** 

Always remember that _**price and scarcity are the two most important factors** in perceived value._ A higher price, in and of itself, is normally a proof element of higher value. And scarcity is a strong incentive to take action _now._ 

We've talked about price, so let me now address scarcity. 

There is a deeply rooted desire in people not to lose what _is_ or _could be_ theirs, and you can use this to great advantage in your offers. 

## Have you ever noticed how people want something much more _if it suddenly may be taken away?_

Have you ever been house hunting and found a new house you think you may like, but just aren't sure? You plan to take your sweet time and think it over, right? After all, this is a big decision. You want to make it rationally, and there's no need to rush. 

Then what happened? You saw another couple getting serious about the same house, and now it's a battle to the death. _We saw it first! That's our house! Let's make our best offer now!_ 

Have you ever noticed how shoppers at a bargain counter will always try to seize the same item the moment another shopper makes a move for it? 

Have you ever observed that you never want to buy into a mutual fund as much as when it's just been closed to new investors because the performance has been so hot and so much money has poured in that they had to shut the doors? 

If you're thinking of buying a one-of-a-kind _anything_ —an antique chest, modern sculpture, classic car, custom jazz guitar, three-masted schooner, cottage by your favorite lake, _anything_ —you will never want it more than when another buyer threatens to snatch it from your grasp. 

Such is human nature, so recognize it in your offers! Let your prospects feel the pressure of a possible takeaway, that the offer will be gone if action is not taken promptly. 

You can do this with offers that feature a time limit, a time-sensitive premium, a bonus for prompt response by a certain deadline, a limited supply, a limited number of participants, anything that is truthful and will take the opportunity away if the prospect dawdles too long. 

And be sure you follow through on your threat! Actually pull the offer, the premium, or whatever incentive you offered, so that your market learns you mean 

- 386 - 

business and will respect your deadline the next time. 

## **Psychological Hot Button #4 These Nine Words Work Like Magic** 

The nine words are _"I have a problem, and I need your help."_ I've seen many letters begin that way, "Dear So-and-So, I have a problem, and I need your help." Or "Dear So-and-So, I'm writing to ask a small favor..." 

Let me cite a super-successful example... 

After I wrote _The Little Black Book of Secrets_ mailing, it was so strong that no one could beat it for a long while. 

Marty Edelston and Brian Kurtz at Boardroom always like to be testing new packages to beat the control, as they wisely never want to have a control die on them with no strong replacement ready to step into the breach. 

Well, there was only one way I could figure out to beat such a strong package, and that's with a variation on those nine magic words. 

Boardroom has a great offer for its flagship publication, _Bottom Line/Personal._ Send no money now. Try six issues free—totally on us. If you don't like it, tell us to cancel, and pay nothing, owe nothing. If you do like it, pay the invoice and keep getting it. Either way, we'll send you a terrific premium full of great secrets just for giving us a try. 

So it's a no-cash offer. Pay up later only if you like it. 

The problem is, it seems almost too good to be true. It's akin to seeing someone on a New York street corner offering you a free $10 bill. You don't take it, because you suspect there's got to be a catch somewhere. 

I realized I could change this psychology by asking a simple favor of prospects, and then, if they did us this favor, reward them with six free issues of the publication. It was the same exact offer as always, except now I was writing to ask a small favor first, and if they granted my wish, we'd send them six free issues of a terrific publication, one they could continue receiving if they liked it. 

Here is the package I created... 

387 - 

- 

**==> picture [459 x 255] intentionally omitted <==**

The outer envelope and letter were personalized, giving credibility to the personal request of a favor. The corner card said, "Office of President," showing that the request was coming from the head of a company. 

The address label said, "Confidential Survey and Gift Certificate Enclosed for..." (followed by the recipient's name and address). 

   - And on the lower left, it described the enclosures: (1) a private survey and 

- (2) a gift certificate of appreciation. And here's what the letter inside looked like... 

- 388 - 

**==> picture [515 x 502] intentionally omitted <==**

As you can see, the whole psychology of this offer now changes. Instead of trying to push "six free issues" at people, just like the guy on the corner unable to give away $10 bills, we are now asking for the favor of someone's opinion and giving away six free issues of a great magazine as the reward, with a generous option to keep receiving it if they so desire. 

This offer lowered the guard of our prospects who now saw the six free issues as a bona fide reward, not just another "hypey" subscription pitch. It changed 

- 389 - 

everything, and this offer quickly became a control that lasted for years. 

Here's another interesting trick of the trade, as illustrated by this magalog... 

**==> picture [498 x 477] intentionally omitted <==**

## **Psychological Hot Button #5 When Your Creativity Fails and All Seems Lost, Create an Enemy!** 

When you attack an enemy—be it big government, conservatives, liberals, the Supreme Court, the medical establishment— _anybody_ that gets your market peeved, you issue a clarion call that fires up the troops as few techniques can. 

- 390 - 

You change the dynamic of your pitch from _marketer-vs.-prospect_ to _marketerand-prospect-on-the-same-team vs. the enemy._ 

The "Get Rich Slowly" magalog is a great example. I wrote this for the Hume _Successful Investing and Money Management_ self-study course. 

Instead of sounding like just another get-rich-quick scheme, this copy changes the psychology by making get-rich-quick schemes the enemy that both the 

publisher and the prospect know are worthless. The subhead on the cover says, "The surest, safest way to get rich is _gradually,_ easily, day-by-day, and _never_ by risking everything you've ever worked for." This copy rang true for this market and was an unbeatable control for many years. 

## **Psychological Hot Button #6 If You're Losing the Battle, Switch the Battlefield** 

For example, the Classics Book Club found it hard to attract new members. Books by Plato, Aristotle, and Marcus Aurelius are not what most book buyers think of as action-packed best sellers. 

So the Classics Book Club changed the battlefield. One of their copywriters, I don't know who, wrote this ad, which became a great control for many years. _Instead of selling "books," it sold both "wisdom " and, more subtly, "home decor."_ 

The copy sold "wisdom" by asking the reader, in the first paragraph, 

## **"Do you have room in your home for three wise men? They are Plato, Aristotle, and Marcus Aurelius...three of the wisest, wittiest, and most stimulating minds that ever lived."** 

This changes the battlefield, the whole positioning. Instead of being asked to buy mere "books," you are now invited to let three wise men into your home to enlighten your life, add polish to your educational background, and give wise guidance to you and your family for the rest of your lives. 

- 391 - 

**==> picture [515 x 586] intentionally omitted <==**

"The selections themselves are remarkable values. They're carefully printed on expensive paper stock. They're hard-bound in matched, wheat-colored buckram, worked and stamped in crimson, black, and 23-karat gold..." 

And then the offer—even for a Classics Book Club!—seems hard to resist... 

"And through direct-to-the-public distribution, we are able to offer our members these deluxe editions for only $4.95 each, plus shipping. 

"Interested? We'll send you the first three selections, Plato, Aristotle, and Marcus Aurelius—all three for only $1 plus shipping. 

"We know what charmers these three wise men are. We're betting that you'll be so taken by them that you'll want to stay in the Club and meet some of their friends, including the greatest storytellers, philosophers, poets, and historians the world has ever known..." 

## **Psychological Hot Button #7 Multiple Benefit Headlines Are Extremely Persuasive, Often the Most Useful and Easiest to Write** 

For example... 

**==> picture [472 x 200] intentionally omitted <==**

This is not just another desperation technique. The multiple benefit headline is often the most persuasive, useful, and easiest to write of any type of headline. Another example, one I wrote for an investment letter... 

**==> picture [442 x 211] intentionally omitted <==**

This piece, a very big winner, shows many of the principles we've been talking about. The format looks very valuable, as this was printed on the cover of a stand-alone "report" look-alike. The advertising itself looks valuable and is valuable, because it contains this company's top investment recommendation for the coming year, one that embodies all these terrific benefits. It's hard to read that list of benefits and not want to find out what the investment is, which you can do by getting involved with the mailing. 

Here's another example, this one offering a commodity managed account service... 

**==> picture [428 x 190] intentionally omitted <==**

- 394- 

I like the multiple benefit headline so much, first, because it works so well. I can't recall a single case where I've used it and had it fail. People just love reading about products that combine a lot of benefits. 

More than that, it's such a handy copywriting tool. That's because it can be very hard sometimes to sum up in one tight headline what the main benefit is. The good news is, you don't have to! You don't have to feel straightjacketed by that "tight, one-sentence mold" for your headlines. This type of headline construction opens up your canvas to a whole new world of possibilities. 

All you have to do is create a list of your product's best benefits and announce to the world, "At last, a blank that combines all these advantages..." This and variations of this idea give you great flexibility. 

## **Psychological Hot Button #8 Proven "Formula" Headlines of Last Resort** 

You probably already recognize these... 

_"Do you make these mistakes in ?"_ 

_"Seven nightmares of "_ 

_"Five minutes to_ " 

_"They laughed when I_ " 

These are classic headlines you can consider when everything else fails you. However, I strongly advise you to consider them only as a last resort, and only when the marketing profile of your product or offer allows you to pay off what the headline promises. For example, don't use a "five minutes to (a big benefit)" headline if there's no way your product can deliver the benefit in five minutes. 

Do _not_ flee to these headlines as your first refuge! Look at them only after you've exhausted all other possibilities of writing headlines that reflect your product's unique strengths. Running to these formula headlines first is lazy copywriting. It means you're not even trying to think through what unique strengths can be captured for your specific product and offer at hand. 

This is especially true if you're aiming to play in the major leagues, with clients like Brian Kurtz of Boardroom or the good people at Healthy Directions and Rodale. For these people and their products, these headlines won't cut it. 

They'll know you've lazily slapped on an old and probably inappropriate "formula" headline, because they've seen them all before. Such headlines will not be unique or strong enough in fields where the marketing is very competitive and skilled. Your headlines will need a sharper focus in such hotly competitive 

- 395 - 

markets. 

But if you're selling a product in a field that is not heavily marketed, where most of the advertising is ineffective and amateurish, these old chestnuts can give you an easy success, because the competition is so weak and the market has not seen these approaches before. 

So consider them a handy tool for quickly turning out "B-level" copy, not "Alevel" copy, and only in virgin direct response markets. Even there, though, your first order of business is to write 20—25 good headlines that embody what is different and special about your product and offer. 

## **"Writing Is Rewriting." Strong Recommendation—Establish Your Own "Crit System"** 

"Crit" is shorthand for "critique." 

If you're an advertiser, I strongly recommend the following... 

Teach everyone in your operation the simple _Persuasion Equation®_ I've described. Everyone who has anything to do with your marketing, and even those who don't, can quickly and easily learn to ask four simple questions of any copy you're considering. 

## Does this address our prospect's _urgent problem (want, desire, or need)?_

Does it make a _unique promise_ to satisfy that desire? 

Does it present _unquestionable proof_ 'side by side with the promise? 

Does it make a _user-friendly_ offer? 

Make these questions available to all the people you work with, and ask them to review, or "crit," whatever copy you're thinking of running. 

Never be afraid to solicit other people's opinions, because you'll be amazed at what good comments just about anyone will come up with for improving the copy. 

## **But Beware of These Pitfalls...** 

Establishing a "crit" system like this will work wonders in your company. But it has to be done right. 

First, most copywriters will initially feel defensive, fearing they must now design horses "by committee" and wind up with camels. They fear they will now suffer the most dreaded of all fates, _death by nitpick._ 

## Also, who's got the time to keep running this copy past everybody and

- 396 - 

responding to everyone's little questions and concerns? 

Finally, how can we ever produce great copy when we've got to please everybody who's jumping into the critique act now? So often, different people make diametrically opposed, totally incompatible suggestions for the same piece of copy! This is a formula for insanity! 

OK, hold on. Copywriters, please don't jump out of the building just yet. Come in off the ledge and let's talk. Here are the rules of the road for establishing a system like this and for making every piece of copy far better, which benefits everyone, including the copywriter, especially if the copywriter wants to build a portfolio of big winners and, even more so, is on royalties and will financially benefit from any improvements in the pull of the copy. 

First, the purpose of this exercise, of distributing the copy widely and having everyone critique it, is _**not**_ to please everyone. It's to make sure that large mistakes are not being made, that a major benefit, copy point, ordering option, great testimonial, sales argument, frequent customer objection, etc., has not been overlooked. 

It's also to be sure that the copy reads smoothly. The writer already knows what he or she is trying to communicate. Do others? This in-house "dry run" tells you right away. 

Also, the purpose of having lots of people read your copy is to get everyone else's viewpoints _before_ you go out with the copy. This way, the copywriter and copy chief get some quick feedback on what's working and what doesn't seem to be working. 

So here are the rules of the road that I have found to make such a system work. 

_**1. Constructive criticism.**_ First, everybody, including the copywriter, is told that we're going to review copy this way and that everybody should contribute in a fair and positive way. 

_**2. Clear-cut responsibilities.**_ Second, to put the writer's mind at ease, it will still be up to the writer, copy chief, and any other key marketing people who would _normally_ approve the copy to continue doing so. It will be up to the writer to decide which critiques make sense and which can safely be ignored. Those who were making "final approval" decisions before are still in charge now. 

_**3. No need for consensus.** Nobody, especially not the writer or copy chief, is looking for universal consensus._ Sometimes you want to stretch with copy that does make everyone edgy, and that's a good thing. Remember before how Brian 

- 397 - 

Kurtz was saying that some of their biggest recent winners made the staff quite uncomfortable before they were tested, such as "animated arteries" and, with a weight-loss headline, a cover photo of a heavy woman grabbing food. 

The point is, they went ahead and tested these groundbreaking ideas anyway, despite the initial discomfort of staffers. Just be sure you also have, as did Boardroom, other, more traditional, high-probability packages in the mix as "insurance" in case the boldly original idea fails. 

_**4. Everyone applies**_ **The Persuasion Equation®,** so that all have a consistent framework from which to judge and compare reactions to the copy and identify problem spots. Of course, any additional observations for any reason are also welcomed and encouraged. 

_**5. Intellectual honesty.**_ Everyone is expected to act with integrity and be intellectually honest when critiquing somebody else's copy. If you're in a cranky mood that morning, don't take it out on the copywriter's draft. Have the intellectual discipline to give it a good, fair-minded reading regardless of your mood or feeling about the writer's personality. Some of the most intolerably obnoxious writers I've known have produced some of the best writing. 

As you review the copy, honor Leo Burnett's admonition: _"Any fool can write a bad ad, but it takes a real genius to keep his hands off a good one."_ 

Remember that the copy represents a lot of hard work that someone sweated and bled to produce. Be fair and considerate. Critique thoroughly and express your concerns straightforwardly, without pettiness or meanness. 

The way I do that with copywriters, some of whom are sensitive plants, especially younger copywriters, is to first point out what's good, even if it's hard to find something I like, and then go into things that could use improvement. You don't want to sugarcoat your critique, pussyfoot around, or conceal any concerns. But neither are you out to crush people either, as you may drive some very talented people away for good if you make the process too uncomfortable. 

## **The Benefits Are Enormous** 

Don't be put off by the concerns I've raised above. Writers and everyone else, including people who were suspicious at first, quickly adapt and come to love the _results_ of the "crit" system, if not the process. That's because the improvement it makes in the responsiveness of your ads is enormous. 

Think about it! What I'm suggesting here is a relatively easy way for everyone involved to pitch in and make your ads better. You can get everybody in the company or, if it's a large company, the entire circle of people that you're involved 

- 398 - 

with, all trying their hardest to make every piece of copy better. This elevates great copy, and an awareness of good marketing, to the highest level among your key people. They'll talk about it over lunch. They'll think about it on a Sunday. And that's as it should be. 

You'll also find that critiques from many different people often have a remarkable consistency. 

Most important, you'll invariably boost your response, sometimes by as much as 50 to 100 percent, when you go through this critiquing process—as long as the writer and copy chief are the main ones who get to decide whose comments are listened to. 

You get this boost in response because you'll be combining, from many sources, scores of "1 percent" improvements that make the copy flow more smoothly, the benefits clearer, the offer more powerfully expressed. 

And sometimes you'll catch a whopper that would have been a disaster if unnoticed. 

I committed one a month ago. I still keep my hand in copywriting for a few special projects I'm a partner in. 

A month ago I received a call from my partner, someone who's always so polite and respectful when sharing his comments about my copy. He said, "Hey, Gary, the copy is great. But do you think, uh, would it be OK, if, at least somewhere, uh, maybe on the order form perhaps...that we could mention, uh, the price?" 

"The price? I forgot to put in the price? Sure, that wouldn't be a bad idea!" _GARYHALBERT:_ I ran a full ad in one of the big New York City dailies, saying you could respond only by phone. And I left out the phone number! _GARYBENCIVENGA:_ Welcome to the club, Gary! 

Now I'm going to describe some good habits for more successful copywriting. All of you designers and marketers can extrapolate these ideas for your own use, and I'm sure you'll find them helpful, as well. 

## **Good Copywriting Habit #1 Make Your Ads as Interesting as Articles, With Great Headlines, Visuals, and Facts** 

Make your copy as good as a _Reader's Digest_ article. I mention this to underscore two points. 

## First, a well-written article, full of facts and interesting information, and

- 399 - 

## presented in an editorial design, makes your advertising _look too valuable to throw away._

Second, it's not just that your advertising is _disguised_ as valuable editorial matter, by virtue of the layout alone. Remember that value is in the eye of the beholder. You must make your copy actually _be_ valuable to the marketplace _as it exists right now._ 

In other words, we have to stop thinking like fishermen, out to catch fish with lures that look good to _us._ We must use lures that look good _to the fish_ we aim to catch! 

That is likely to be different today than it was six months ago. The bait must be fresh. Don't think that just because the format, the look of your piece, is editorial, you've done your job. Your content, the information you hope is of interest to your prospects, has to be in sync with the market's current interests. 

This total picture—both your layout that looks valuable _and_ your content that is current and interesting, will determine whether or not your prospect perceives your ad as valuable information. 

## **Good Copywriting Habit #2 Go the Extra Mile for Your Clients... Make "Beat the Control" Your Mantra** 

In your work for clients, always go the extra mile. Make "beat the control" your mantra, your battle cry, because this is what your client needs most of all from you. 

Do the things that lazy copywriters don't feel like doing. By lazy copywriters, I mean those who don't write 20—25 headlines before choosing the best, because that's too hard. Or they take easy ways out, by always relying on old formula headlines instead of thinking about what makes this offer or product unique and writing a headline that dramatically captures that uniqueness. Or they take shortcuts with the research and wind up with weak bullets. 

Do all the things I've talked about and you will, once you make these habits second nature, easily beat most of the lazy copywriters you're competing against. Do it long enough and you'll virtually always beat them, unless they happen to get really lucky one time. But that's OK, let them have the occasional winner. You will always have all the clients you can handle if, on average, you are the winner, and you'll almost always win if you use these techniques. 

As part of this, remember that there are no small roles, only small actors. There are no small copywriting assignments. Sometimes, for example, what looks 

- 4 0 0 - 

like a small, sideline product becomes, with the right copy, the company's biggest success. 

For example, I remember when Phillips launched _Health & Healing,_ the company's first health newsletter. No one, except perhaps Tom Phillips, ever expected it to even hold its own against the stable of Phillips' big investment newsletters. Nobody had any idea of how successful it would become. 

But that never bothered the copywriter who was given the assignment, Clayton Makepeace. Clayton went all out on that new, "less important" newsletter. He worked his butt off and socked a grand slam out of the park. That newsletter, and the supplement business it spawned, became not only Phillips' most successful business, but one of the greatest, most profitable successes in direct marketing history. 

A lesser writer than Clayton might have thought of this as just another new product, not worth too much effort. But Clayton "left everything on the court," as athletes say, and his effort created a gigantic product success. It didn't hurt Clayton's royalty checks either, of that you can be sure! 

## **Good Copywriting Habit #3 Keep Your Eye on the Best Seller Lists** 

Best seller lists are a happy hunting ground for copy concepts and premium ideas. For example, if I get a book or newsletter to sell on health, I will go online to see the best sellers in that and all related areas, such as weight loss. 

Among the current health best sellers, I may well find a great concept for a mailing. And among the current weight-loss best sellers, I may well find a great idea for a premium, one that my client could easily put together on the same theme but with a new twist. 

Amazon can do this for you very easily. Just surf through the various categories at Amazon to identify the top-50 best-selling books in any one category, and you'll see what the public is buying now. Best sellers are your best straws in the wind of what the marketplace wants right now. This tip will work for any premium you want to create, for any product you want to sell. 

## **Good Copywriting Habit #4 Commit Yourself to the 1 Percent Rule** 

We've covered so many ideas and secrets today for boosting your response. Don't feel as if you must absorb all of this overnight. I've been in the business 40 years, and I'm still learning so much. From many of you, I want to learn still 

- 401 - 

## more. 

But neither should you take all these tips and just let them sit on the shelf. You will be very happy, you will strike the right balance, if you follow my simple 1 percent rule, that is, commit yourself to 1 percent improvement per week. 

That's easily doable. Anybody can improve 1 percent a week. If you do, you'll be compounding your advances in our business very, very rapidly. With just a 1 percent improvement compounded each week, you will be a star in our industry. If you're a junior writer now, you'll be a star very soon. 

## **Good Copywriting Habit #5 Be Ruthless About Getting Your Three Hours a Day** 

## You might think, "Three hours a day? Cake! With the hours I put in, hey, three hours is _nothing."_

Not so fast! I'm talking about three solid hours of writing, researching, or in some way working on your main project, the one that will make you the most money. 

If you're an entrepreneur and not a writer, the same holds for you. You want to harness that 80/20 formula and put three hours daily into the 20 percent of your activities that yield 80 percent of your income. 

If you try to follow this rule, you'll see how hard it can be to cordon off three hours a day, even if you work 10 hours a day. Between all the people, events, emails, phone messages, and miscellaneous administrative piranha fish that constantly nibble at your time, it can be hard to get three quiet hours to yourself to do whatever is job number one. 

No matter. You've got to make it your top priority every day to invest at least three hours in your most important priority. And some days, you'll miss, which is why I aim for my three hours seven days a week, every week of the year except when I'm on vacation. 

Even if you miss a day here or there or sometimes four or five days in a row because of something unavoidable, don't fret. It happens. Just get back on track as soon as you can. If you can log more than three hours in a day, consider it a gift. **Good Copywriting Habit #6 Write Like a Crazed Arsonist... Edit Like a Supreme Court Justice** 

## Early in my career, this took me a long time to figure out. I tried to write and

- 402 - 

edit at the same time, and that's like trying to drive with the brakes on. You must _separate_ these two activities for maximum productivity. 

When it's time to write, write like a crazed arsonist, caring nothing about what anyone will think about your words, even yourself. Write with passion, gusto, and abandon, lighting bonfires of emotion on every topic you touch. 

Then, in a day or two or three, after your copy has cooled down to smoldering embers, don the robes of a deliberative Supreme Court justice. Coolly weigh the sense of every argument, the believability of every claim, the impact of every word, the logical flow of the paragraphs. And, this is the most helpful comment of all, _pretend that the copy was written by someone else, preferably your closest competitor or the upstart copywriter you don't like down the hall._ This will make you much more critical and your copy much stronger. 

## **Good Copywriting Habit #7 Read an Ad a Day** 

Consider it your one-a-day marketing vitamin. It's so important because you'll get more ideas from doing this than almost anything else, even your own in-house testing, assuming you do this exercise as I describe below. 

By doing this every day, you'll see more good advertising than your competitors and develop much better instincts for what will work and what won't. 

You'll get a wide range of insights on how other people are tackling the same creative problems you face. You'll see what the whole marketplace is testing and what is being repeated, which means that these ads are working and perhaps worth adapting—in a creative, thoughtful way. It's as if you're putting legions of copywriters and designers to work for _you free._ 

Some of these ads, such as the longer magalogs, may run to 80 pages. You won't have time to read so much copy every day. That's OK. Just read a piece of it, a couple of pages every day, preferably at the same time every day, as part of your daily routine, perhaps with your morning coffee or whatever. 

To vary your marketing diet, read a classic mail order ad one day, followed the next day by a current control in your own field, followed by a space ad in a magazine you'd never normally look at, followed by a Web site of an Internet guru—look everywhere for ideas. 

Whenever you see a great ad, save it. Start your own collection of great ads. Read and reread them, especially the best ones. Instead of reading every ad you come across in a magazine, shoot to have a collection of 100 great ads that you keep reading over and over and over, so they seep into your consciousness. You'll 

- 403 - 

find that the next time you wrestle with a tough marketing problem, they will come galloping to your aid like a loyal, charging cavalry. 

Of course, you must define these ads as "great" not because you like them or think they're clever. Such ads don't count. You can count only those ads you see repeated again and again, because these are the ads that are working, whatever warts and pimples you may think they have. 

Finally, and this is the most important part of this exercise, no matter how strong the ad may be, always ask yourself, _"How might I beat this? "_ 

This will always be a depressing question every time you see a great ad, because it is so strong. And sometimes, you won't be able to beat it. Try as I might, I don't think I could ever write a better newspaper headline about that **decapitated man than** _**"Cops Find Headless Man in Topless Bar"...**_ or when President Gerald Ford stiffed New York— _**"Ford to City: Drop Dead"..** .ox_ this one, announcing the death of Chinese Communist leader Deng Xiaoping— _**"Head Red Dead."**_ 

Headlines like that are admittedly untouchable. But it never hurts to try. And, thank goodness, the overwhelming majority—98 percent—are not nearly as unbeatable. You'll see that if you don't let yourself feel defeated from the outset, you _will_ come up with worthy and, at times, superior ideas, either in the headline, the offer, _somewhere_ in the ad. Certainly you will come up with ideas worthy of testing. 

And this will give you great confidence in your headline and copywriting ability. It's just as in sports. If you want to play better tennis, play every day against _better opponents._ 

If you can't think of any improvements, keep trying. Consider it a brainteaser or a puzzle and make a game out of it. Say to yourself, "If they will haul me in front of a firing squad tomorrow at dawn if I don't beat this ad, what would I insist they try?" A firing squad concentrates the mind wonderfully. You'll be surprised at the ideas you start coming up with. 

In this way, you will let these great ads become your best teachers. 

## **Good Copywriting Habit #8 Retain a "Mental Board of Directors" And Stack It with the Best** 

On my "mental board of directors," I have appointed Claude Hopkins, Albert Lasker, John E. Kennedy, John Caples, David Ogilvy, Rosser Reeves, Max Sackheim, Victor Schwab, Gene Schwartz, and a few others. 

- 404 - 

Since I've read and reread their books, I know how they think. 

Whenever I'm stuck on an assignment, I'll kick off my shoes and lay down on my couch with a pad and pen at the ready. I close my eyes and call an emergency meeting of my board of directors. 

I see myself walking into a fancy boardroom with a long mahogany desk surrounded by my elite team of advertising immortals. 

Then I go around the room. I'll say, "Claude Hopkins, you were so good with demonstrations and offers. How can you help me here? How would you make this a blockbuster success?" And I wait for him to give me his ideas. 

I may turn next to David Ogilvy and ask, "Mr. Ogilvy, you wrote such powerful and informative ads that were always as interesting as articles. What do you recommend in that vein for me?" 

And so on. "Mr. Caples, you wrote so many winning headlines. Do you have a good one for me?" 

Do this, and in no time you'll have more great ideas than you can possibly handle. 

This works because, once you know their books and pose questions to these great advertising minds in this way, your subconscious searches for the answers in your own mental database of knowledge. 

Of course, that's the scientific explanation. There may be another. 

This sounds a little Shirley MacLaine-ish, I agree, but—who knows?—maybe these guys are out there somewhere, floating on clouds, playing little harps, thinking "I'm so bored. I wish I could help some poor copywriter down there. I would whisper such great ideas in their ears, if only they would ask!" 

I'm picturing these people like Clarence, the angel, _in It's a Wonderful Life,_ who had to earn his wings by helping Jimmy Stewart. 

Maybe Gene Schwartz is up there right now, whiling away eternity, distractedly plinking on his harp, just wishing for a new assignment, yearning for somebody here on earth to ask for his help with a headline, or at least a bullet, for heaven's sake! You never know! 

One last thought on this matter of advertising books. My friend David Deutsch, who is here, had a perceptive observation about how to invest your time wisely with marketing books. He observed that there may be 100 new marketing and advertising books published every year. You could invest a lot of time trying to keep up with them all, with very little return on your investment. That's because most of them are worthless! They teach nothing. Worse than that, they advise the 

- 4 0 5 - 

_wrong_ things to do. 

David said that instead of trying to read 100 advertising books, either in a year or a lifetime, your time would be much better spent reading the 10 _best_ advertising books _10 times each._ 

How true, how true! What are the top 10? Any books written by the people I mentioned on my "mental board of directors." 

And that's the perfect segue into my last tip for copywriters... 

## **Good Copywriting Habit #9 Always Be Learning** 

We're engaged in perhaps the most fascinating and rewarding business on earth. The day you think you know it all and can relax or stop learning is the day you're fooling yourself. 

Every day, I try to learn a little something, to fit another piece of this gigantic, beautiful, unimaginably large puzzle into place. It's a lifelong pursuit of the treasure of knowledge. 

Whether it's reading a new ad or rereading a book by an advertising immortal, this is the most important habit of all: _don't lay your head on your pillow at night until you 've learned something new and valuable about our business._ 

That's the main habit to establish—learn a little something new every day. No matter what else happened that day, this will allow you to feel, as your head hits the pillow, _"Today was not a wasted day. Today I learned something."_ 

**==> picture [176 x 16] intentionally omitted <==**

- 406 - 

**==> picture [476 x 269] intentionally omitted <==**

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ Please bear with me as I fiddle with this computer. Even though I have Meaghen and PowerPoint helping me, I sometimes screw up. 

I'm not alone in such ineptitude. About two years ago, Pauline and I were coming back from Europe on the _Queen Elizabeth II_ We don't like to fly and had always wanted to sail on the _Queen Elizabeth_ ship. 

The transatlantic crossing takes five days, so the ship offers many interesting programs and lectures to fill the time. 

One of the presentations was by the top flight engineer and pilot of the Concorde, that beautiful plane that no longer flies. He invited everyone on the ship who was interested to come see his PowerPoint presentation on how the Concorde was flown, why it's the most technically difficult plane in the world to fly, and what happened with the terrible accident when it crashed in France. It sounded like an interesting presentation. 

Well, this world-class engineer and top pilot who trains other pilots on how to fly the most complex plane in the world _could not get his PowerPoint to work!_ He was repeatedly frustrated, and the audience found this much more amusing than his original presentation. So I am in good company. 

It may seem rather odd for me to be recommending direct response design tips, but I have learned from the best, including people like Ed Eliot when he was doing design, the terrific designer Rob Davis, and a few others. 

So now I'm going to teach you what little I know about design, which was shared with me by these excellent designers, so the credit goes to them. 

If you think about it, many of the breakthroughs I've been involved with were primarily breakthroughs in design. For example, when magalogs first came along, they created much more success than any other format. That had less to do with copy than design. 

As David Ogilvy said, in space, if you make it look like an editorial article, you'll automatically get 500 percent higher readership. 

And in John Caples' tests, his carefully tracked results proved that an editoriallooking ad pulled 81 percent higher in actual sales compared with the same exact headline and copy designed as a typical ad. 

Those improvements stem not from copy but design. If you're a copywriter, you have to bear these results in mind, even though you're not the designer, because the assignment starts with you and so, therefore, does the design. 

This means, for example, that when you're writing your headlines, you've got to envision the eventual design of your ad because, if it's an editorial-looking 

- 411 - 

space ad or a magalog, the format itself will start to dictate the style of your headline. Headlines of real articles don't begin with phrases such as "Act Today to Take Advantage of This Amazing Offer!" 

That headline screams "sales pitch" and would not be an effective way to create an editorial-looking space ad or magalog. So even though you're the writer, you always have to be a little bit of a designer as well, at least to take advantage of these editorial and value-laden formats and ads. 

Let me give you some examples, starting with our first rule that you're familiar with by now... 

## **Design Tip #1: Make Your Advertising Itself Look Valuable... Follow the "ABA Rule"** 

We've covered this, but it's worth revisiting here. 

As you recall, the top-of-the-line execution of the way to make your direct mail radiate value and not be tossed away is to use a FedEx package if you can. If you can't, because of economics, you can work your way down the direct mail food chain—personal letter with a first-class stamp, magalog, bookalog, letter that _looks_ like it's a personal letter, and so on. 

No matter what format you use, apply the "ABA Rule." This is something I came up with to guide Bob King when he was running Phillips Publishing. That is _"Your advertising should look like Anything But Advertising,"_ because an "advertising" look automatically triggers a rejection response among many if not most readers. In a split second, the look of the ad itself makes the prospect think, "Not now. I'm too busy to read advertising right now." You must try your best to bypass that automatic reaction. 

## **Design Tip #2: Test a Magalog That Looks Like a Real Magazine** 

One popular way to apply the "ABA" rule is with a magalog, so let me give some advice on how to create magalogs the right way. 

The natural tendency of most of us copywriters is to cram lots of bullets on our outer envelopes and covers of magalogs. As a result, many of the magalogs have gravitated from "magazine look-alikes," which was the original concept of a magalog, into hybrids that no longer resemble magazine covers, but instead look like four-color brochures in the shape of a magazine. 

## This happens because most of us copywriters can't resist the urge to start

- 412 - 

selling harder, even when an editorial format requires more restraint. 

With an editorial format, you don't want to "spill your candy in the lobby." By that I mean, if your main strategy is to graphically resemble valuable editorial, such as a magazine, you want it to look at least remotely like the cover of a real magazine, which would not normally be filled with pushy bullets and offers. 

When we pile on so many benefits, we spill our candy in the lobby. We say too much too soon and can actually lose sales, because we red-flag the prospect that this is a "sales pitch," not valuable information on a subject of his or her passion. 

When it first appeared on the scene, the magalog format outpulled, by wide margins, every other format for every mailer I know who tested it, because it looked a lot more valuable than "junk mail." Response jumped much higher and still does whenever the format is introduced to a market that hasn't yet seen it. 

But as it became more familiar to consumers, since they were inundated with so many magalogs, we had to become smarter. Let me show you the evolution we've tested our way into, starting with the first magalog I ever did... 

- 413 - 

**==> picture [374 x 411] intentionally omitted <==**

This piece was intended to look something like _Time_ magazine. But it's got a lot more copy than _Time_ would put on its cover. At this early stage, we had no idea of the right amount of sell copy and bullets to put on the cover. I couldn't resist putting five bullets on the cover, and this was a blockbuster success. 

Eventually it started to fatigue, as all control mailings do, and I had to come up with something else. Here is a later example I created for the same product, and it, too, did very well. It, too, had a lot of copy on the cover, but we also tried to keep a "magazine" feel to it. 

- 4 1 4 - 

**==> picture [430 x 463] intentionally omitted <==**

There were some magalogs I wrote for publications whose actual issues _do_ have lots of sell copy on their front covers, and these were the best match of all between promotional and actual editorial front covers. For example, this was a winner I wrote for _Ladies' Home Journal..._ 

**==> picture [474 x 455] intentionally omitted <==**

I pulled out the best articles from the past several years and summarized their advice on the interior pages of the magalog: "Lose 10 Pounds". .."25 Foods under 100 Calories"..."Age Erasers That Wipe 5-10 Years off Your Looks"...etc. 

All these great stories were featured inside as great examples of this magazine's coverage. 

You might wonder, "How could you afford to pay a big star like Kathie Lee to pose on your ad?" The answer is, _it cost nothing!_ That's because when _Ladies' Home Journal_ and other magazines do a story or interview a star like Kathie Lee, they secure the rights to use in advertising any photos they shoot for the article. Something to bear in mind if you're working with a major star. 

- 416 - 

**==> picture [466 x 478] intentionally omitted <==**

Here's one I did that was _not_ a success. Dr. Joyce Brothers wanted to do a newsletter on emotional health and healing. 

This was a product launched by publisher Jeff Greenberg. Of the three writers involved in the test, I came in number one, and my pride was intact even though the product went down the tubes. I was happy that I was the winner, though unhappy that my client's product didn't fly. 

We learned that you can't always take a well-known personality and assume that her publication will sell. In hindsight, I believe Dr. Brothers' information wasn't specialized enough to justify a newsletter subscription. There's so much psychological advice available free on the radio or inexpensively in magazines. You must ask yourself, _"Where are we adding the extra value for the extra price? "_ 

_- All -_ 

At the time I did this piece for Dr. Joyce Brothers, I was still using a lot of bullets on the front cover. So, too, with the following pieces, which successfully sold books for Rodale. 

**==> picture [463 x 267] intentionally omitted <==**

But here's a very interesting test. This was a very successful package for a Rodale book on how to look younger... 

**==> picture [340 x 279] intentionally omitted <==**

- 4 1 8 - 

This was a book with lots of tips on how to look younger. I wanted to demonstrate this, so I talked with the good people at Rodale, and we decided to do something interesting along the lines of the "Al Davis" approach I mentioned earlier—"Let's not accept what we've been given here. Let's take what we want." 

I said, "We should put out a call to modeling agencies and ask for models who look much younger than they really are. We'll use them in our promotion under one condition—they must be willing to reveal their real age in print." 

Also, since there were by now so many magalogs, all crammed with bullets on their front covers flooding the mail, I said, "Let's make this front cover really look like a magazine. We'll cut the bullets! We'll go with just one strong cover story, just like many magazines." Here are the front and back covers of this magalog, which blew the doors off the control and show the point I'm making. Sometimes less is more. Here, we had much stronger proof elements on the cover—actual women revealing their surprising ages, yet fewer bullets, and response soared over and above an already very strong piece. 

**==> picture [519 x 275] intentionally omitted <==**

Many of the women we showed throughout this magalog were not only gorgeous but also proud of their ages. For example, the woman on the front cover is 44 years young. The woman on the back cover is 48 years young. 

The pages throughout the piece featured women in their forties, fifties, sixties, and seventies, all of them beautiful. They were fantastic proof elements. We even asked them to help match their techniques for looking so good with the editorial in 

- 419 - 

the book we were selling. And we featured some of their tips right in the advertising. Our copy also acknowledged that, yes, it helps to have good genes. But nobody gets to _stay_ this good-looking throughout life without some expert help, and this book features the best, most ingenious tips for looking younger from beautiful women themselves, as well as from Hollywood cosmetic artists, dermatologists to the stars, top hair stylists, renowned image and clothing consultants, famous plastic surgeons, and so on. 

There are four principles underscored by this great success. 

First, we made the advertising itself valuable by featuring lots of great beauty tips throughout its pages, explaining that these were but a few examples from the hundreds to be found in the book. 

Second, we didn't merely accept what the product people handed to us. We asked, "What can we go out and get to make this campaign a blockbuster?" And this led to... 

Our third factor, hiring beautiful models who look many years younger than their actual ages _and requiring them to state their real names and ages,_ which served as powerful proof elements. 

In the book we were selling, there were hundreds of great beauty tips, but very few women who exemplified them. Yes, that was a shortcoming. But there's no excuse to settle for that. If you do, you are importing and compounding a product weakness into your advertising. You must be proactive. 

We said, "Let's go out and get the youngest looking women we can find in New York and invite them to be our models if they'll reveal their real names and ages," which these women did, showing how it is indeed possible to look much younger than your real age. 

Ideally, you then want to revise the product to make it as strong as you can. You always want to allow the marketing people to help make your product be as strong as it can be. Build the proof elements into the product from the beginning. The product people should ask the marketing people at the start, "What should we put into this book, this course, this car to give you the strongest possible advertising advantage?" And especially, "What proof elements would you love to feature in the advertising?" 

Finally, let me mention the fourth factor that contributed to this success: a truly editorial-looking cover. 

We cut all the overcrowded bullets from the control and went with just one strong benefit: _**"Look Younger** Now"_ with the proof element of a young, vibrant- 

- 4 2 0 - 

looking 44-year-old woman. 

I almost shed tears when I had to cut those original cover bullets like "Erase Wrinkles and Crow's-Feet, Like Magic!"..."Breeze Through Menopause and Cool Off Hot Flashes"..."Clothing Trick—Look 10-20 Pounds Thinner Instantly!" 

But that was the test, and it had to be done. And the cuts worked. Sometimes one big idea that looks more editorial will outsell a whole lineup of selling points that sacrifices the appearance of value and slides into a "salesy" look. 

Here's another example of the same principle. This was the first winner I wrote for a book on foods that are healthy for you.... 

**==> picture [414 x 318] intentionally omitted <==**

This looked like an issue of _Prevention_ and worked very well. 

But for our next test, I said, "Let's try turning it into a bookalog, with a front cover that looks more like a real paperback book cover you might see in Barnes & Noble. Again, let's cut back on the 'salesy' bullets and look more valuable, just like a paperback book." 

That was my instruction to the brilliant designer Rob Davis, who, using stock photos off the Internet, quickly came back with the following redesign, which walloped an already-strong control... 

- 4 2 1 - 

**==> picture [341 x 327] intentionally omitted <==**

So this is what I'm recommending here—if you want your formats to look valuable, just remember it's not just the size or the masthead that makes them look valuable. It's the whole visual treatment. If you're a copywriter, trust the designer to carry more of the burden to bring this about. 

After this package was mailed in the many millions, it, too, fatigued. The good people at Rodale then used their own in-house design talent to create the next winner. For this design, I felt we could harmlessly sneak a few bullets back in, without hurting the perception of value. 

So that's what I did, under the main title. Also, since people have gotten to know these bookalogs _are_ offers of something or other, I also wanted to add a hint about the offer, at the bottom of the cover... 

- 4 2 2 - 

**==> picture [215 x 301] intentionally omitted <==**

In all, we went from the first magalog that looked _mostly_ like a magazine or book cover but with lots of bullets...to featuring fewer bullets...back to sneaking a few bullets back in and adding a hint of the offer as well, which has been our most successful mix as of now. 

This shows the evolution of our thinking, which I'm sure will keep changing as the marketplace changes. There are no "final" answers save one: _"Always keep testing to see what the market responds to now."_ 

## **Design Tip #3: What to Avoid?** 

## **(What Didn't Work?)** 

It would feel great to show you only success stories. But I want you to see my clunkers too. 

Once I saw what a difference it makes to make your advertising look valuable, I experimented with a whole variety of ways to execute that idea. 

For example, for the _Personal Finance_ newsletter, I thought of creating a selfcontained course in successful investing that would also sell newsletter subscriptions... 

- 4 2 3 - 

**==> picture [235 x 301] intentionally omitted <==**

We designed this to look like a school notebook. It bombed. Maybe a "notebook" feeling made readers recall those dreary days in class with their greenor black-speckled notebooks. Not a happy remembrance. Also, the headline offers no immediate benefit. 

## Here's another test, this one designed to look like the _National Enquirer._

I figured that the _National Enquirer_ is America's largest-selling newspaper. I'd bet we'd enjoy unprecedented readership if we'd emulate its sensational style. 

I don't know how much readership we triggered, but the response was terrible. In hindsight, the match between substance and style was all wrong. The _Enquirer_ does not have a lot of credibility as an investment publication. So this, too, went down the tubes... 

- 4 2 4 - 

The Billionaire's Simple Secret of _**4**_ **Investors losing $$$$** Successful Investing **to telephone con men** 

**==> picture [226 x 34] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>

## **How To Achieve The 5 Biggest Financial Goals Of Your Life**

**==> picture [237 x 66] intentionally omitted <==**

I tried to make this next one for _Personal Finance_ look like a Sunday supplement, like _Parade_ magazine, with a big, wide format... 

**==> picture [260 x 144] intentionally omitted <==**

**----- Start of picture text -----**<br>

**==> picture [239 x 13] intentionally omitted <==**

**==> picture [252 x 131] intentionally omitted <==**

**-** 425 

The headline is "Who Will Feed CHINA?" Maybe a lot of readers thought, _"Who cares? "_ or _"Not_ rav. _problem!"_ Again, there's no big benefit in the headline, which is often a danger sign. Also, I suspect that the _Parade_ format was another mismatch between message and medium, as people don't look to _Parade_ for investment advice. 

**INSIDE:** Actual foreign currency made worthless by severe runaway inflation... plus a warning by seven prominent economists that the U.S. dollar may now be headed, inevitably, for the same fate... 

**==> picture [183 x 53] intentionally omitted <==**

This is one that disappointed us. When I worked with Dan Rosenthal, we had a newsletter that told you how to profit from inflation. And this is the envelope we sent out to promote this newsletter, exactly as you see it above. 

In other words, there was not an order form showing out through the window, which is normally the case. We addressed this mailing on the _back_ of the envelope. 

We used stock window envelopes, off the shelf, so they weren't expensive. Neither was it expensive to buy Brazilian five-cruzeiro notes, because they had just been through runaway inflation. You could buy truckloads for very small amounts of money. So we bought enough to test this mailing, and we knew there would be enough to roll out, if this was successful. 

We mailed this at a time when U.S. inflation was getting very scary, back in the 1970s, when President Jimmy Carter was in the White House. Inflation was running at 17 or 18 percent per year. That means your money is losing almost 20 percent of its value per year, and if that continues very long, it's devastating to your savings, your pension, your Social Security benefits, your cost of living— almost every area of your financial life. If the inflation rate _doubles_ from that point, you're in Brazil territory, and that means economic and social chaos. 

- 4 2 6 - 

**==> picture [405 x 175] intentionally omitted <==**

So this was, we thought, a very arresting visual—real money made virtually worthless by inflation. 

And it was a beautiful certificate, very fancy engraved printing. We picked them up very cheaply, for even less money, as I recall, than it would have cost us to print them ourselves. And this of course had once been legal tender of a major country. 

The copy in the letter said something like "Take this Brazilian five-cruzeiro note in your hand and realize that a few years ago it would have bought you a steak dinner in one of the world's great beef-producing nations. Today it won't even buy you a cup of coffee from a street-corner vendor in Rio. It's become worthless. Of course nothing like that could ever happen in America to our money. Or could it?..." 

It went on from there to say that while we're not in extreme danger now, if inflation in America keeps growing worse, your life savings could be destroyed, unless you take some smart steps now to protect yourself and even profit from the great inflationary trend of our time. 

The control package that this tested against was just a straight newsletter sell, something like "7 investments that will profit from inflation." 

And the control beat this currency note package handily. 

This was so disappointing. I think the problem was that nobody really believed that America was going to have runaway inflation or maybe didn't want to think about it. Or, since we had real money showing through the envelope, it was seen by every employee who handled these letters throughout the postal system. They may well have been too tempting and visible a prize. Perhaps they looked so valuable that they somehow disappeared from the post office before they were 

- 427 - 

even delivered. That's not totally out of the realm of possibility. 

## Design Tip #4: 

## Once Your Foot Is in the Door, Copy Drives the Sale 

As important as design is in getting the prospects to read your messages, don't rely on good design to outsell good copy. I've always agreed with Rosser Reeves' assessment that "an ad agency is an assembly line for a good piece of copy." That's the attitude I've always had working in a copy department. It starts with the copy. 

The designer can play a very important role in bringing out what the copywriter is trying to sell. But it usually doesn't work in reverse, where the designer comes up with the visuals and then the copywriter writes around them. Once a prospect is into an ad or direct mail package, it's the copy that will close the sale or not. 

## Design Tip #5: 

## Always Work with a Good Direct Response Designer 

On one test we did about a year ago, we worked with a very talented designer. She designs many book covers and is very creative. But she never really did a direct mail package before. 

She did give us a very nice design. We all liked it. But there were so many little things she didn't get right about the technical aspects of direct mail. Right up until the last minute before it went to press, we were catching things that were not right for the post office, not right for the printer, not good for readership. 

There were so many headaches that we caught and corrected, and we're not even sure we caught them all. You really need a designer who knows direct response and has specialized in direct response. The best one I know doing freelance work today is Rob Davis. 

## Design Tip #6: 

**==> picture [229 x 35] intentionally omitted <==**

For example, the eye is used to reading serif typefaces. (The text you are reading now is in serif type, with small "ligatures" or lines at the ends of letters, which serve as a "railroad track for the eye," making reading easier. **By contrast,** this is "sans serif type. "Sans" means "without," so "sans serif means 

- 428 - 

## "without serifs," meaning such fonts have no ligatures to guide the eye, thus reducing readability of long copy blocks.) 

This is why almost all newspapers and books, including the school textbooks we all grew up reading, are printed in a serif type. This lifetime familiarity alone makes the eye much more comfortable reading serif type. Sans serif fonts are fine for headlines or subheads containing just a few words. But anything more lengthy, and especially long copy blocks, should always be in serif. 

I'll tell you an interesting story. For one of the newsletters that we advertised once, we would run space ads in the _New York Times._ One week, we were right up against the deadline when our newsletter editor phoned to say, "Because of recent developments in the news, I've got to change my recommendation this week. In fact, my new report is so timely, I definitely want to run it in this Sunday's _New York Times."_ 

So we quickly changed the ad copy. But this was in about 1972 BC (before computers), and we had no way to set the type in time to make the deadline for Sunday. 

We said to the _Times,_ "Here's our ad for this Sunday, but your production department will have to set the type, since we can't do it in time. And please be sure you set the body copy in serif, as we know this is more readable and will result in higher response." At least that's what we had always read in the writings of the masters, such as Ogilvy and Caples, though we had never tested this ourselves. 

We sent in our raw, typewriter-written copy. In the rush, the _Times_ production department goofed and set the type in sans serif face. We screamed to the _Times_ about it on Monday when we got back to the office. They said, "Yes, it was our fault. We'll give you a free run of the same ad this coming Sunday, and this time we'll set it right." 

What do you think happened? Did the age-old admonition to use serif in body copy make any difference? 

The following Sunday they ran the ad as it was supposed to be, and there was an 86 percent increase in pull just from changing the type of the body copy. We had always read that serif makes a difference, but found it hard to believe it made _such_ a difference. In fact, normally there is a sharp falloff in response, usually about 40 percent, the second time you run a large ad so soon in the same newspaper. But the serif type boosted response by more than 80 percent on a second run in the same newspaper. 

## Everything else was exactly the same—same headline, same position, same

- 429 - 

coupon, same offer, same everything. Only the setting of the type was different, from sans serif to serif. 

You could argue that this wasn't a pure split-run test, that there was a week's difference between the two ads. Maybe the marketplace changed somewhat in our favor, who knows? That's possible, but we didn't think so. We couldn't identify any factor like that when we analyzed the results. 

Whether those same results would be duplicated in a split-run test, I don't know. Somehow I doubt it, because I still find those results so hard to believe. But as mindful as we were _before_ this experience to set body copy in serif type, we became absolutely fanatical about it afterwards. Based on this one experience, we saw that this factor alone could make or break an ad's success. 

That's how powerful this principle can be. You want serif type only in body copy. For headlines, fine, san serif is perfectly acceptable. But in body copy, always insist on serif. People quickly tire of reading sans serif type. **Design Tip #7: Don't Force Your Readers to Fight Against "Reading Gravity"** 

If you put your headline above the illustration you force readers to fight against reading gravity. For example, this is an ad that has a lot of problems... 

**==> picture [203 x 278] intentionally omitted <==**

- 4 3 0 - 

First off, the headline says nothing of benefit or relevance to the reader. If you were judging this with _The Persuasion Equation®,_ you'd spot that immediately. You'd ask, "What problem are we solving for our readers?" 

Second, the body copy is set in reverse type, which is very hard to read, and in sans serif no less. Just a comedy of errors. 

Chief among them is that the headline is above the photo. Always remember that visual elements attract the eye first. Therefore, you want your visual normally to be at the top of the page in a space ad, to attract your reader's eye to start at the top of the ad. Then put your headline under it and your body copy under that, to allow the eye to proceed naturally and effortlessly down the page, which is the normal flow your eye wants to follow. 

_The eye habitually reads from left to right and from top to bottom._ So place the major components of your ad to facilitate this habitual reading flow. 

In the ad above, for example, the eye sees the computer screen first. When it does, and assuming the reader wants to know more, it would normally want to follow usual "reading gravity" and head _down_ from where it starts. 

But this headline is placed _above_ the visual starting point in the ad, forcing the eye to go north, to the top of the page, and then back down to the body copy. This makes no sense. It makes the reader fight against "reading gravity" by having to go up the page to get to the second-most-important element. The eye does not like to do this and will resist. 

Simple rule: if you want to use a strong visual in your ad, put it at the top of the page and your headline under it or, in some cases, to the right of it. "Visual on top, headline under" is the classic "Ogilvy" space ad design. 

## **Design Tip #8:** 

## **We Notice Elements Out of Place** 

That's why handwritten notes in the margin are almost always read. 

Another technique is to place a black bar over someone's eyes in an ad. It always arrests attention, and you can use this to your advantage. 

A simple example: let's say you have a book on how to cut taxes, written by a former IRS agent, which is a great proof element, by the way. You could have, as I remember seeing in one ad, a visual of a man in a business suit with his eyes blacked out. The headline was something like "Renegade IRS auditor reveals the best ways to slash your taxes and your chances of an audit." 

## Another example is Ogilvy's famous ad _The Man in the Hathaway Shirt._

_-_ 431 - 

He used a distinguished-looking gentleman modeling a Hathaway shirt. There was some downtime during the shoot, and while everyone was waiting for the photographer to get ready, Ogilvy noticed that somebody was horsing around with an eye patch. 

He said, "Put that eye patch on my model." They did, and _The Man in the Hathaway Shirt_ now stood there with a beautiful shirt and an eye patch. The eye patch gave the fellow a swashbuckling look that drew much more attention to the ad. Look for those opportunities. 

## **Design Tip #9:** 

## **Make Your Graphics Suit Your Product's Personality** 

This is obvious. If you're going to a high-end market, like investors, you should not make your graphics look like the _National Enquirer._ This rule was written after that test. 

## **Design Tip #10: Watch Out for Wide Columns** 

A rule of thumb I follow in ads is _don't make your columns wider than 40 to 60 characters._ Different designers prefer different widths, but if you stay within this range, you'll be fine. 

Use a traditional newspaper as a guide, such as the _Wall Street Journal_ or the _New York Times._ From reading newspapers, people are more accustomed to reading relatively narrow columns. Even in books, the columns don't stretch on forever, because the eye feels as if it's unnatural to travel too wide a measure. It yearns to return home, to hit its own internal "carriage return" and go back to the start of the line again. 

## **Design Tip #11: Photos Outpull Illustrations** 

This has been tested and proven many times. Photos have more realism and thus more credibility. If you're graphically portraying something, if you're showing a product such as a copy machine, camera, whatever, it's much better to use a real photo. Better still is a photo of the product in action or in some way contributing to the benefit that the reader wants to experience. 

- 432 - 

**Design Tip #12:** 

## **Never Let a Photo or Illustration Interrupt Your Coupon Rule** 

Once you know to look for this mistake, you'll be amazed at how many direct marketers, even smart ones, commit it. Here's a good example... 

**==> picture [324 x 367] intentionally omitted <==**

This ad sells a book by one of the greatest golfers of all time, Sammy Snead, who reveals his golfing tips in his book. The headline isn't all that great, but there's a mortal sin being committed in the coupon. 

That's because any golfer who would love to respond to this ad has to cut Sammy's legs off. And they don't want to do that! Believe me, this might seem like a minor matter, but it's not. For most readers, it may not even be a conscious thing. Almost anyone will have strong subconscious resistance to cutting off a part of the human body. 

## I saw strong evidence of this myself once. I was taught this principle by a

- 433 - 

designer at Ogilvy & Mather, that a coupon rule should never be interrupted by any visual, not a photo of a salesperson, a picture of the premium, not a product shot, nothing! A coupon rule is meant to be cut with scissors and not have any image obstacle in its way that would, in effect, have to be severed by the act of cutting out the coupon. 

When we went to visit one of our clients, they were giving us a tour of the building and wanted to show us the mail room, where all the orders come in. 

They said, "Come see the responses." They retained a couple of different ad agencies, always pitting one against the other. Another ad agency they used had designed an ad with a little man standing in the coupon. And his head and shoulders came above the coupon rule. In case after case, as we leafed through the coupon returns, we saw that almost everyone who had sent in the coupon had taken great pains to cut around his head. Nobody wanted to decapitate the smiling salesman. 

You can understand that. It's a mildly unnerving thing to have to do. And while it may sound funny, for every person who took pains to go get scissors, so as to not rip the man's head off, there was probably somebody else who didn't have scissors handy and went looking for one. And then perhaps the phone rang or he was interrupted by something else while looking for his scissors, and the sale was lost. I'm telling you, a mistake like this will really depress response. You will depress response whenever you make someone have to do _any_ extra work in cutting out your coupon. 

Of course, many general advertisers who've never visited a mail room have no idea about this. Likewise for some direct response designers. And they love to get creative with their placement of images. They loathe "static" layouts. They sometimes feel creatively restrained to keep all the ordering information in the tight, cramped quarters of the coupon. So what do they do? They bust out! They enlarge some elements so that the coupon rule is interrupted, and they now can feel creative. But they've just made a terrible mistake that will depress response unless you correct it. 

## **Design Tips #13H¥29** 

Here, in rapid succession, are a bunch of additional design tips to bear in mind whenever you create direct response advertising... 

_**Design Tip** #13:_ In space ads, it's best to imitate the design of the medium in which you're running. If they insist, let them slug in "Advertisement" in small letters at the top of your ad. That's harmless, as most people don't notice it or, if they do, are already hooked by the editorial look and content of your ad. 

- 434 - 

Unless it's disruptive to the editorial look you're emulating, it helps readership to start your body copy with a drop initial, one of those oversize letters at the beginning of text. This is a device used by many magazines to direct the eye to the start of the copy. 

_Design_ _**Tip** #14:_ 1 mentioned this before when reviewing the eye's readership "habits," so it's worth including in this checklist—serif type outpulls san serif type. 

_Design_ _**Tip** #15:_ In body copy, avoid reverse type—white ink on black or other dark backgrounds. It depresses response. You'll see a lot of people who should know better making this mistake, as well. This begs people to dismiss your copy. Too hard to read. 

_Design_ _**Tip** #16:_ Long copy generally outpulls short copy. But make it readable. Use lots of subheads to break up long pieces of copy, just as I'm doing here. Keep sentences fairly short. If you notice one that's overly long, break it into two sentences. You can usually pick this up by reading your copy out loud. Wherever you stumble, your readers will too. 

_Design_ _**Tip** #17:_ This is one I learned recently. I never knew it, but it makes so much sense. _Never put a period after a headline._ It depresses response. And someone actually did tests of this. He found that putting a period at the end of the headline stops the reader from moving forward because a period says "STOP!" Why would you want your reader to stop at the end of your headline? You want him to _go_ —straight into the rest of your copy!
