I can't explain this, and I'm not sure anyone can, but writing your goal down seems to make a contract between you and your subconscious mind. It seems to harness your untapped powers in a way that just doesn't happen when you don't write it down. It affirms your seriousness, your commitment, your degree of desire to make this happen. Writing it down is a giant step forward in bringing your goal into reality, and it also makes it much easier to stay on course. It's a serious contract with yourself. 

Always think big, big enough to get yourself really excited about what you will do with your life. 

Now let me show you how I do this... 

**==> picture [342 x 254] intentionally omitted <==**

I take a little index card. I call it my "Manifestation Index Card." 

On it I write what I choose to manifest. Let's say I want to beat the control by 100 percent. Maybe I'll fail, but remember, that's OK. I have banished the fear of disappointment from my process of goal setting. Yes, maybe I'll "fail" and beat the control by only 30 percent. But it's still a tremendous success, and that's part of my plan! I want to shoot for something really big. If I miss, more often than not I'll still have a huge success. And often I will succeed beyond all reasonable 

- 473 - 

expectations. 

Whatever the major goal I have in my life at the time, I'll create a card or sheet of paper like this, or you can even put it on your computer, whatever works for you. Just be sure to do it, to write it down. 

For example, it could be that I want to sell a home, buy a home, or take a trip around the world within the next two years. Whatever it might be, I will create a little card like this. The first thing I write down is exactly what I am choosing to manifest in my life. 

Then, down below, I list my visualizations. How do I see myself luxuriating in the experience of this goal that I want? Vividly imagine these scenes as if you are already living them, and you will bring them to pass much more reliably. This process also lets your inner genie (your subconscious mind) know in vivid, sensory terms what you want him or her to go out in the world and get for you. 

**==> picture [340 x 252] intentionally omitted <==**

On the back of my card, I list the reasons why I want this. For example, if I want to be among the world's greatest copywriters, that's because I'll have a good income, I'll be able to work anywhere I want in the world, I'll enjoy great respect in my profession, and so on. The more reasons the better. These reasons renew your motivation when you review them every day. 

Then I think about the next actions that I'm going to take. You don't even have to fill that out here. You can put those steps on your to-do list, because these will always be changing. 

- 4 7 4 - 

In reviewing your goal, let's say it's to get to San Francisco, you have to assess what you have to do next to flesh out your plan for getting there. What is your next action to take to get to your destination? And that's it—you're on your way. 

Every day, you reaffirm your clear destination, you reexamine your plan getting there, and you take actions to get there. That's all you need to get to any destination, whether it's something that you want to experience in your life or your arrival in San Francisco. 

## **The Most Important Tip of All...** 

The most important thing of all is to review this little card or your sheet of paper, whatever you use, every day for each major goal you want to experience. But don't make this an overwhelming project that you never get around to doing. If you have no specific goals written down, just pick your most important one now and start with that. You can add others later. 

Write it down like this and review it every day. Just like the airplane heading to San Francisco, you're going to run into a storm and you want to self-correct as soon as possible. And reading that card every day will do that for you. Just make it a little ritual. Do it with your morning coffee or before you go to bed, whatever you're comfortable with and will keep doing consistently. That's the key. Do it every day. 

That way, when the inevitable storms and gusts of wind blow you off course, you'll get back on course quickly and be re-excited about where you're going. 

The wisdom of doing this is expressed in an age-old truth. It's often expressed **in** this way— _"You become what you think about."_ 

That's not exactly how I view it, because by that definition, I would have become a girl by about age fourteen. 

## _(LAUGHTER)_ 

I like to put it this way... 

## _**You will manifest a desire if you envision it daily as already achieved... with vivid mental imagery and emotion...and without fear of disappointment.**_ 

Again, you're saying to yourself, "If this happens, great; if not, _no loss._ Just having made the daring attempt will enrich me beyond measure and take me further than if I never even try for fear of disappointment." 

It's like taking a three-point shot when you haven't played basketball in a while. If it goes in, great; if it doesn't, there's no harm to my self-image 

- 4 7 5 - 

whatsoever. 

I'm now envisioning lunch, and I'll bet you anything it will now materialize! Let's go have some in the next room and be back here in one hour!

## **Wealth Is an Income Stream** 

Now on to our final session of this seminar. This is a short session, but it's such an important one for both marketers and copywriters who want to increase their income and annuitize their income. By annuitize, I mean create a regular, ever-flowing river of money coming into your life, year in and year out. 

One of the best people in marketing that I ever had the privilege to work with was Bob King at Phillips Publishing. 

Bob King was so strong on this principle of annuitizing, which means that when you're marketing something, you want to set things up so that your prospects buy from you not just once, but become regular customers who keep buying from you year in and year out for many years to come. That's the smartest way to lock in an automatic revenue stream so that you don't have to reinvent the marketing wheel every year. You can begin each year secure in the knowledge that a big portion of your income is already locked in for the year because the lion's share of your customers keep coming back to buy more. 

If you can accomplish that, it also means that your cost of acquiring that customer gets amortized over many years' worth of purchases, driving your 

- 483 - 

average cost per sale way, way down. 

So this is one of the main goals you want to have in winning a new customer. You don't want to be happy with just a onetime transaction, but you want to aim instead to create a lifetime relationship with that customer. You're not looking for a one-night stand but a marriage where you're going to be doing good things with and for our customers for many, many years to come. 

Bob King and Phillips Publishing sold newsletters, but he placed such importance on this principle, here's how he told us all to think. Bob said... 

## **"We Are Not in the Newsletter Business... We Are in the Renewal Business!"** 

Bob never tired of explaining to us, and correcting our thinking when we put all our emphasis on getting new subscribers, that most profits in any business come from repeat sales. This is true in virtually any business. Whenever you're thinking of how to serve your customers, never think "one shot." Always think of the long-term relationship. Indeed, many direct marketers are happy just to break even on the first sale if it means they've won a customer likely to give them repeat sales long into the future. 

## **The King Gillette Story** 

Probably the first and best marketer to demonstrate this principle was a man named King Gillette. He was a marketing genius and a peculiar, eccentric man. 

He became a success rather late in life. He was working as a traveling salesman for the Crown Cork and Seal Company, which had marketed bottle caps, the first major disposable product in America. 

But he wanted to be more than a salesman. He dreamed of having his own business empire. 

In the 1890s, Gillette hit upon an idea. He saw what a great success his employer was enjoying by selling disposable bottle caps. He was a traveling salesman and had noticed that when he and other salesmen needed to shave on long train trips—using the typical long, sharp straight razor—it was quite risky, often resulting in cuts because the blade was so large and unprotected and the train ride was so unsteady. 

## He wondered if he could produce a safety razor, one that would be _disposable._

- 484 - 

**==> picture [312 x 231] intentionally omitted <==**

This was his ingenious idea. There already were some safety razor blades at the time, but they weren't too sharp. He dedicated himself to the idea of creating a very sharp safety razor blade, which he eventually did. 

But the real marketing brilliance of what he did was this—he decided to _give away_ the razor handle...and then sell the blades that fit into it. He realized that you can sell a razor handle only once. But you could sell razor blades to the same customer every week for the rest of his life! 

So in very creative marketing campaigns, he gave away these razor handles or sometimes sold them very cheaply, depending on his latest promotion. When most men were given a free razor handle, they were thrilled. What a nice gift! And of course they then had to buy—and keep buying—Gillette razor blades, which were the only blades that would fit in their free razor handle. 

By the simple act of giving away free razor handles, he created legions of customers for life and built one of the world's great corporations. 

## **This Is What You Want to Set Up with Your Major Products and Services— Endless Rivers of Revenue** 

He also created one of history's greatest marketing models, which many of us can use to create _endless rivers of revenue._ 

Mobile phone companies use the same strategy. They either give you, or sell you very cheaply, a cell phone, but then make money on every phone call. 

- 4 8 5 - 

When we bought our first laser printer many years ago, it was very expensive, several thousand dollars. Now the printers are cheap, but when you must frequently reorder the toner cartridges, you realize they have stolen a page from King Gillette. _The repeat sales are where the real money is._ You buy a printer only once in a rare while, but use toner every day. 

Look at the Apple iPod. It's a great piece of technology, but its real purpose is to sell you the songs for 990 apiece through the iTunes connection with the Apple music store. That's really where they make their back-end money, and they've already sold over a _billion_ songs at 990 a pop! 

Ezines are very similar. They're a great way to engage someone in a long-term relationship, with frequent opportunities to buy your products along the way. 

Never think in terms of one-shot opportunities to make money. Always think, "How can I leverage a onetime transaction into a permanent, self-renewing river of revenue from the same customer?" This will make a tremendous difference in the wealth you acquire over your business life. 

## **Wealth-Building Secrets for Copywriters and Marketers** 

The King Gillette example leads us naturally into this subject—how copywriters and marketers can make more money. Let me share a few tips I've observed during my career. 

## **Wealth-Building Tip #1: Find Work You Love.** 

## **"Work Is More Fun Than Fun"—** _**Noel Coward**_ 

My father taught me many things, but this was his most important lesson, something he mentioned to me again and again when I was growing _up: find the work you love._ 

He did what he loved in life. He was a big, strong Italian man, smart, courageous, very creative, built like a bull, and strong as an ox. Yet his love in life was tending delicate little flowers. 

He believed that since you spend so much of your life at work, your work should be your play, that you should look for whatever work naturally gives you a sense of fulfillment and pleasure and not worry so much about the financial consequences. 

For example, though he grew up on the tough streets of Williamsburg, 

- 486 - 

Brooklyn, a neighborhood that produced more than its share of gangsters, including Al Capone, he found that he loved growing beautiful flowers on his family's tenement rooftop. 

During the Great Depression, he landed a secure job—a great prize in those days—with the New York City Parks Department. This allowed him to work with the trees, plants, and flowers he loved, creating oases of beauty and serenity in this city of concrete, asphalt, grime, and bustle. 

When I was a senior in high school, hoping to go to college, he told me that our family didn't have enough money to send me to college. If I wanted a college education, I'd have to pay for it myself at night school, which is what I wound up doing. He added that education is to be highly prized, especially when it leads you to the work you naturally love to do. 

He told me that he didn't care what I did with my life, as long as it was an honest living and, most important of all, _that I enjoyed whatever work I chose._ 

Even now, I can hear him repeating his familiar refrain. "You will be working for most of your life, and if you don't enjoy your work, most of your life will be unhappy. But if you love your work, you will look forward to arising each day to try some new experiment, to learn some surprising and exciting new secret of how to do what you love even better. Over time, this will make you among the best in your field, because you will always enjoy thinking about your chosen work, even on holidays and weekends when others try to escape the work they dislike. The work you love will be your best friend through thick and thin. When emotional storms and other hardships hit, as they inevitably will, your work will always be there, giving you a safe, secure harbor of satisfaction, no matter what else is happening in your life." 

At one point, during my last few months of high school, he added, "I'm not going to give you a big speech about what I expect of you. You were not born to fulfill my expectations or your mother's or anyone else's. You were born to discover what you were meant to do in this life. That's your only job during these years of your education—to find out what you naturally enjoy doing." 

I consider that advice my father's greatest gift. I promptly informed him, only half jokingly, that I had every intention to be the shooting guard for the New York Knicks. But, given my suspect jump shot, slowness of foot, and inability to jump more than a few inches, he said I should have a plan B, something else I also enjoyed that could be more likely to meet with demand for my services. 

It took me a few years, while I was going to college at night and working fulltime at an insurance company during the day, to find what I really enjoyed doing. 

- 4 8 7 - 

But once I discovered copywriting, I knew I had found something I naturally enjoyed doing. 

To become successful at it, I needed to learn a great deal, and my first few years were difficult, until I managed to learn the right way to write advertising. These early years were actually a sad, dark time in my life. For a couple of years, I felt like a failure, that I was not able to write copy because I hadn't learned the correct techniques, and it all seemed so hard. 

At the time, I was trying to hold down a full-time job as an advertising writer and going to school full-time in evening classes, and I also had a young family, including a medically involved disabled daughter who needed a great deal of diagnostic, therapeutic, and other intervention. 

It was hard for me to concentrate on anything, to have enough time to focus on doing any one thing right for very long. All of this, including the thought that I was failing at work and, if I got fired, would lose the medical coverage that we desperately needed for my disabled daughter, weighed heavily on me. I also thought I was failing at the only thing my father had asked of me, which was to find a field I enjoyed. It was a lot of responsibility for someone only 21 years old. 

So, for a few years, I certainly wasn't enjoying copywriting. It was disheartening in the extreme to see again and again that my copy stank, that it would always return to my in-box covered with blue pencil marks from my copy chiefs. I was beginning to think that I had chosen the wrong way to make a living. 

Since I needed the income for my young family and schooling at night, all I could do was hang in there and keep trying. As very often happens in life, perseverance, all by itself, can carry you, even when you are incompetent. 

I would tell myself back then, "Gary, when all else fails, try courage." Sometimes the only thing that would get me back to work in the morning was this mantra, to just have the courage to try another day and put up with the humiliation of being incompetent and having everyone in the office know it. I felt that every time anyone in my office even looked at me, what they saw was incompetence personified, and I hated feeling this way. I yearned for respect, not daily humiliation, but I didn't know how to earn it. 

I think that the only thing that kept me from being fired was the mere fact that I was willing to keep trying, that I still wanted to succeed despite draft after draft being covered with corrections, blunt exhortations, and negative comments. 

I remember once overhearing my boss, the copy chief, having a conversation with his head art director. I was outside the copy chief's office, waiting to speak with him. His door was open, and I overheard him say, "Steve, we've got to get rid 

- 488 - 

of Gary. He's just not cutting it. I need someone who can bat this stuff out. He can't. I have to spend so much time correcting and rewriting his stuff myself. I don't have the time for this." 

I turned around and walked back to my little cubicle. I went home that night feeling more dejected than ever. 

Luckily the axe didn't fall any time soon. My perseverance seemed to give my exasperated copy chiefs more patience, to cut me yet a little more slack, until I could get the hang of writing copy that sells. 

After a couple of years of this in various jobs, I did gain a minimum level of competence, and I again felt happy and hopeful about my future, that I had in fact found work I naturally enjoyed doing. At last, my efforts seemed to be meeting with success in the big, tough world out there. 

From then on, I kept trying to get better and better, and more and more success followed. Once that train was moving down the track, once I started experiencing even a little success, I really did love going to work every day, as my father had predicted. But I won't deny it was tough at first, which is why I have such empathy for anyone trying to make it in a new field and feeling unsure of themselves, having so little self-confidence. Sometimes it's very hard to find one's place in this world, but once you do, life can be glorious. Until it clicks for you, you must just hang in there and keep trying, as I had to. 

## **Wealth-Building Tip #2: "We Are All in Business for Ourselves** 

Another good point to remember in your career, whether you're working for yourself or a large corporation, is that _we are all in business for ourselves._ This philosophy has kept me in good stead, even when I worked at large corporations and large ad agencies. 

It's all too easy for employees to take their employers for granted, to assume that their job and income are secure and always will be. _Not so,_ not in today's fastchanging world. 

Always regard your employer as your client—your _only_ client, which makes him or her even more deserving of your utmost efforts. 

I realized that if I had my own business, I'd have a variety of clients and would be more secure, because the dissatisfaction of one client would not be fatal to my income. But as an employee, I have but one client, once source of income, so I had better treat that client right! 

- 4 8 9 - 

Sometimes others have sought my advice on whether they should go into business for themselves. I have always responded, "You are _already_ in business for yourself. No matter who you may work for, there's somebody you must keep happy. If you forget that, you can too easily start taking people—and your paycheck—for granted." 

## **Wealth-Building Tip #3:** 

## **At Least 10 Percent of All You Earn Is Yours to Keep** 

This is a great little tip I learned from the book _The Richest Man in Babylon._ At least 10 percent of all you earn is yours to keep, and that's 10 percent of your gross income. 

No matter how little or much you're earning, you should be able to salt away at least 10 percent of your gross income every year, even when you're young. If you do that, you'll start to harness the miracle of compounding, hailed by Albert Einstein as "the eighth wonder of the world," because it allows each of the dollars you save to have lots of "baby dollars," which in turn spawn lots of other "baby dollars," and so on—countless generations of new dollars to be born within your lifetime once you get the process started. 

A famous financial advisor I know, Richard Young, who has counseled and become friends with many fabulously wealthy people in his career, once told me that some of the richest people alive walk around with the same little chart in their pockets—the compound interest chart—which shows how quickly your savings double, then quadruple, then grow eightfold, and so on, all through the almostmagical power of compound interest. This is especially true in an IRA program or pension plan, where your money doubles, then quadruples, and so on _tax-free._ 

So start putting this astonishing, wealth-building power of compound interest to work for you. Start salting away at least 10 percent of your gross annual income as early as you can in life, but certainly starting this year, no matter what your age. You'll be amazed at how much money this can grow into, just given the time to multiply. 

If you can salt away 20 percent or even more of your gross income, that's really fantastic. But 10 percent is your bare minimum. _Anybody_ who earns almost any amount of money at all should be putting away at least 10 percent for the future. Looked at another way, you can always cut 10 percent of your expenses and still survive. So be sure to _pay yourself first._ Before you spend all your cash on the scores of other things that demand your money each month, put the first 10 percent away for _you,_ and let it start compounding, preferably tax-free. 

- 4 9 0 - 

## **Wealth-Building Tip #4:** 

## **If You're a Freelancer, Don't Let One Client Become More Than 50 Percent of Your Income** 

One of the agencies I worked for let this happen. I saw it coming but couldn't convince my partners to take appropriate action. Over the span of a few years, one of our clients—a generous, well-heeled, very successful client—became more and more of our business. 

At first this client represented maybe 25 percent of our agency's income. Then, because our work was succeeding for them, their growth exploded. We wanted to remain their exclusive agency, so we asked to handle all of their advertising and marketing needs, and they happily obliged. And soon they were more than 50 percent of our business, then 75 percent and still wanting more and more of our agency's resources. 

We tried to increase our "supply"—our production capacity—by hiring and training more creative people as quickly as we could. We were making a lot of money, hiring lots of people, and racing to train and organize everyone just to keep up with our fast-growing client. But in this mad, hectic race to keep up, our top management was blinded to the danger of letting this one company monopolize our income. 

Then the inevitable struck. The company grew so large, so fast, that it brought in several layers of new management people—new "brooms" who had their own ideas, who wanted to hire their own familiar suppliers and sweep us out of the picture, even though our work had been so successful. 

In time, we took a devastating hit. When they severely cut their expenditures with us, we had to fire a small army of loyal employees. These were good people with whom we shared many a laugh, many a crisis, many a long night and weekend of extra work at no extra pay. Yet they left our offices that black day without a job, several of them in tears, worried about how they would pay their mortgage and other bills, terribly anxious that it might take them a long time to land a new job, and so on. It was a brutal experience for all concerned. We simply no longer had the income to pay them. 

Managing a successful client can be a tough problem, especially if you're a one-person band. My advice is to prevent any one client from becoming more than 50 percent of your income for very long. Rigorously enforce a limit of about 50 percent of your time on any one client, even if they're growing by leaps and bounds and filling your coffers with gold. You must build up other sources of revenue because any client can suddenly stop using you for any of a dozen valid 

- 4 9 1 - 

reasons. 

Keep the lid at about 50 percent, even if it means that your only client for the other 50 percent is _you,_ as you develop more of your marketing efforts to fill in your schedule with new clients. 

If you ignore this rule and grow too dependent on one client, if you find yourself receiving 90, 95, or even 100 percent of your income from just one client and then that client sells the business or takes the work in-house, you will plunge from 100 percent income to 0 percent. That's quite jolting, especially if you've never taken time to build up a marketing "machine" that can bring in new clients when you need them. To manage your practice as a freelancer or your small business properly, you cannot let this happen. To prevent it, you cannot let any one client be more than 50 percent of your income, in my view. 

## **Wealth-Building Tip #5:** 

## **In Your Negotiations, Treat All Clients Fairly and Equally. (If You Don't, They'll Find Out Because They Talk with Each Other!)** 

I've always tried to treat all my clients equally because they talk with one another. Never believe it when a client says, "I need a special deal, and if you give it to me, I promise to keep it a secret." 

Even with the best of intentions, he or she will cheerfully forget this solemn oath before lunch. Eventually one of your other clients or prospects will hear about it and insist on the same deal. And you'll have no one to blame but yourself. 

Don't forget, too, that people change jobs. What will you do when the person at company A moves to company B, who is also your client? Company B will now want the same favored treatment and may rightfully feel resentful that you've been charging them more than company A all these years for the same work. 

Make your pricing consistent for all concerned and stick by your guns. Don't let your pricing be hammered down by the toughest negotiators. Do that, and you will be rewarding the penny-pinching hagglers at the expense of your best-paying clients, the ones you want to cherish, treat fairly, and keep as clients for your entire career. When you teach your clients to hammer you down on price by rewarding such behavior, you will never see an end to such haggling. 

## **Beware the "Ouch!" Technique** 

As part of this, beware that shrewd negotiators will usually use the _"Ouch!"_ technique. 

- 4 9 2 - 

No matter what price you quote, they will respond with some form of _"Ouch!"_ at which moment you will be sorely tempted to offer a better deal. If you have set your price fairly, resist this urge. You are the victim of the highly effective _"Ouch!"_ technique that some negotiators like to use. They know that most vendors feel anxious and even guilty when quoting a price, and this technique maximizes your guilt, sometimes so much so that you immediately offer an onthe-spot reduction, since you want to win this new client. 

If you yield to this temptation, the haggler feels that he or she must have to _keep_ haggling, because you're obviously not sure of what to charge. If you keep yielding, you will ruin all chances of this client hiring you. Remember that clients don't want the cheapest supplier. They want the best they can afford, especially in creative work. The most expensive copy is copy that fails. 

The best antidote to this is to prepare well and give your best, fairest, marketbased price at the outset and hold firm. Just figure out what fair price you deserve by your level of success and demand in the marketplace, and stick with it. Don't reward endless haggling. 

## **Advice from the Tough Negotiator, George Meany** 

In any negotiation, remember the advice of George Meany, the former, toughas-nails head of the AFL-CIO union. He recommended, "Never sit down at the negotiating table unless you're also willing to get up and walk away." When you're in negotiations with anyone, remember that you don't _have_ to come to an agreement if the proposed deal doesn't make sense for you. 

## **Wealth-Building Tip #6: A Great Negotiating Technique from "George George"** 

No, that's not a typo. I once had a client with the unlikely name of George George. I remember him so well because he was such a pleasant man. You hardly knew that he was negotiating with you, which may be the smartest negotiating technique of all. 

George George was an executive—maybe he still is—at Doubleday Books. For many years, Doubleday was based in Garden City, New York, where I live. I got a call one day from George George. He said, "Gary, we're neighbors, and I need a copywriter to help us on a few projects. Why don't you come over and visit me some afternoon, just to see what we're doing, or even just to say hello?" 

So one afternoon when I wanted a break, I went over to see George George. He said, "We'd love you to knock out a couple of these brochures every month, 

- 4 9 3 - 

little inserts about our best-selling books. You could write them in your sleep, they're so easy—just the title and picture of the book, a strong headline, some glowing reviews that we'll supply, and a quick summary you can cut and paste from the book jacket. They're so easy." 

As he was delivering this little spiel, I was mentally rehearsing my response. I would gently inform him that I never work for less than my standard, nonnegotiable fee, which for many years has been a $25,000 nonrefundable upfront advance and a 50-per-piece royalty on every piece mailed. 

This of course was for a full-scale direct mail package. No one had ever asked me to write small inserts like this, so I'd have to figure out a proportional rate, which would be much lower, but still expensive. 

But before I could even open my mouth, he beat me to the punch—and totally outnegotiated me! 

He said, "The most I can pay you is $400 per piece to do each of these little inserts. As you can see, you can knock them out so fast. What do you say?" 

And, before I realized it, I said, "Sure, why not?" 

I could see how easy they'd be to write—maybe two or three hours tops. But what I found even more appealing was that they were so easy. Normally, I had to wrack my brains wrestling with some of the toughest assignments, against the strongest competition, in the direct response industry. What a relief to be able to turn to a no-brainer now and then to give my overworked brain a rest. 

These would be easy-as-pie "in-between" assignments after I'd finished a major project and needed a breather before plunging in on the next superchallenging one. 

So here I was, with a two-year-long waiting list of people willing to pay me $25,000 plus royalties, saying yes to George George, letting him go right to the front of the line, after he had effortlessly sweet-talked me into working for him at $400 per project. 

I was astonished at how quickly he resolved all doubt and negotiating postures I was all set to assume, simply by informing me, _"This is the most the job can pay. Can you do it? "_ 

I have since found this ingenious little ploy very effective in all manner of negotiations whenever hiring somebody to do something. State what you're willing to pay _before_ your hopeful vendor gets carried away with visions of sugarplums emerging from your deep pockets. 

## Ted Nicholas used the same technique with media. If you know Ted, you've

- 494 - 

probably heard him talk about this. Ted is one of the most brilliant mail order advertisers who's ever lived, probably the foremost space ad marketer of the last several decades. He's sold tons of books and merchandise via mail order, most of it through fantastic space ads in magazines and newspapers. 

He's also a sharp negotiator. He would send his ad to different media. He would include a note that said something like this... 

"I know your rate card says it's $8,000 to run a full-page ad in your fine publication. I'm a mail order advertiser. I can only afford $4,000. Here's my check for $4,000. If you can run the ad for $4,000, cash my check and run the enclosed ad. If you can't, please send my check back." 

He discovered it was a mortal sin in the religion of magazine publishers to return any check. They would, almost always, reluctantly run the ad on his terms. 

After they did agree to run the ad on his terms, he'd call back and say something like, "Of course, you understand that I also need a right-hand page and far-forward position?" And they'd often give him that too. 

## **Wealth-Building Tip #7: What About Working for Competing Clients?** 

This is a ticklish subject. I know that some of the direct marketers in this room are always confronting this issue. They give an assignment to a copywriter, sharing confidential information. The copywriter works on the project and hits a home run. Because of this success, competitors start to call the same copywriter, offering similar assignments. Like the lame and the halt who crowd the stage at revival meetings, clients surge to the stage to have their products blessed and healed by the new miracle worker. 

What's the copywriter's proper response? 

I think that this whole matter can be equated to marriage versus playing the field. If the client wants an exclusive relationship with the copywriter, I believe the client should offer exclusivity too. The client should allow the same copywriter or agency to handle all the work and refrain from calling in three other copywriters the moment the first copywriter has a winner. 

In this arrangement, as in marriage, both parties exclusively live with each other, free from the threat and jealousy caused by ever-present competition. It means that the client is not allowed to "play the field" with other copywriters and the copywriter is not allowed to jump into bed with competitive products. Most direct marketers will recoil at such a notion, even as they desire and 

- 4 9 5 - 

sometimes expect their copywriters to vow fidelity and love only them. As in marriage, one-sided monogamy is neither fair nor realistic for long-term happiness. When a client likes to boogie with every copywriter at the dance, it induces every copywriter to keep an eye open for other attractive partners, as well. A few more thoughts to remember... 

First, as David Ogilvy put it, "Successful polygamy depends upon pretending to each spouse that she is the only pebble on your beach." If you do work for competitive clients, don't rub it in. It instantly enflames professional jealousy. 

## Second, remember what I said earlier— _control packages are the coin of the realm._

If you have succeeded in knocking out control packages, and especially if you can do it consistently, you gain great leverage, especially with those clients for whom you currently have the current control. They will probably resent your work for competitive clients but not let it become a make-or-break issue. Your proven performance for them is too valuable a prize. Indeed, if they do force a break over the issue, they will lose 100 percent of your attention to their competitors, which would be even more irritating. 

Second, with informational products such as newsletters, magazines, and books, there are few if any _truly_ competitive products. The purchase of one newsletter, unlike the purchase of a car, does not diminish the likelihood of another purchase just like it. To the contrary, the purchase of one newsletter will often stimulate sales for other newsletters from other publishers as the reader's passion for the subject grows. 

Sometimes, when making this point and hearing my potential client still upset over the matter, I'd say, "You know I'm right about this. After all, you rent your lists, your most precious asset, to the same other people I'm working for. So how 'competitive' can they be if you're willing to rent them your own house names? And the bigger that their publication grows, the more names they can rent to you." 

Third, explain to your client that there are great advantages to your specializing in one field. I told my clients who questioned me on this that I could help them to a much greater degree because I had solved so many similar marketing problems for products just like theirs. I knew the offers, formats, and premiums that were working best. Most of all, I knew, from hundreds and, in some cases, thousands of tests of headlines, offers, copy appeals, and lists, what would almost always work and what would almost always fail in their marketplace. 

Fourth and most important, assuage your client's apprehensions by acknowledging their concerns over confidentiality and stating your firm policy 

- 4 9 6 - 

never to breach it. Then live up to it. 

As long as I kept my clients' trust and never spoke to one client about another's private business, my clients felt secure that I would be just as closemouthed about their secrets. 

The _worst_ thing you can do is to share the secrets of one client with another in a misguided attempt to curry favor. They will assume that when the shoe is on the other foot, you will be equally indiscreet with their secrets. Sometimes a client will test you. Dan Rosenthal, the head of the agency where I worked many years ago, liked to test new job candidates in this way. 

Whenever we had a major job opening, he would interview people from other ad agencies and client companies. He would attempt to pry confidential information out of them. He would conduct a cordial, friendly interview for a while and then express curiosity about confidential matters at the person's present employer. 

He would probe for information in different ways—politely; charmingly; and, failing that, with impatience that signaled that a job offer could hinge on being a little more cooperative about this. 

Dan couldn't have cared less about the secrets of these other companies, most of whom had zero relevance to our clients. It was all a test. If the candidate held firm or diplomatically changed the subject or forthrightly said, "I really can't talk about that, as it would be unfair to my present employer," his stock soared. If he folded like a chap card table and spilled the beans, he was immediately eliminated from consideration. 

## **What I've Learned About Building Wealth with Royalties** 

I may have been either the first or one of the first copywriters to forge royalty arrangements with the clients I was working with. Interestingly, I did this not so much to make more money. I did it as a defensive measure. 

More and more good copywriters were coming into the field, just as today. That's one of the reasons I'm retiring—too many of you are so good! I saw that if I could put more time into the research and into the preparation of my advertising, the stronger it would be. And if I could have a much bigger payout for winning a control, I'd be able to serve the clients a lot better, by lavishing upon their project much, much more time and work than other writers were normally willing to do under a flat-fee-per-project basis. 

- 4 9 7 - 

That was how I started with royalties. My clients were perfectly comfortable with it, right from the first. They didn't mind paying more money, as long as it came from the additional profits my work would be generating. And that's what royalties do—they align your interest with the client's. The better your copy works for your client, the more you can make. 

Once I had royalty arrangements in place, I was able to put in two or three months on a single direct mail project instead of the usual two or three weeks. And this made an enormous difference. I now had the time to research the market and product much more thoroughly, to create much longer and more effective copy, and to polish everything I wrote to a gleaming sparkle. 

And the deeper I would go into the research, the more I would discover what was working in other fields and bring that knowledge to bear on each client's assignment. 

I've always been one of those people who, even in school, was never the head of the class on brains alone. But I learned that I could usually outwork somebody. If I was persistent and just worked a little harder, put in more time and dedication on a project, I would almost always win. And royalties gave me the means to do this, because they meant a much bigger payout if my copy would win. 

And that's how copywriting royalties started, at least for me. Most of my initial royalty arrangements were with clients who already had prior flat-fee arrangements with me. I told them that this is how I'd be working from now on. I didn't ask if they'd pay me royalties. I bit the bullet and simply informed my clients that this is how it had to be, and they all accepted it. 

This gets back to the leverage you get once you know how to produce control packages consistently. It gives you a lot of sway with clients who, more than anything, don't want to lose you because you are their top salesperson. And in reality, this new arrangement would help them in the long run, too, because they'd be getting much more powerful campaigns, as I proved with my work. The betterresearched, better-written packages I was able to produce beat virtually all the work of writers still on the flat-fee basis and putting in much less time. 

Through the years, other writers, some of them in this room, asked me, "Gary, I'd like to go on royalties too. Can you tell me what your contract is like?" 

They'd be astonished to learn that I had no contract. As I say, I had been with these clients for many years on a fee-per-project basis, which required no contract. When I informed them of my new way of working and they agreed to it, we had a long history of trust, and there was no need, at least in my view, to write everything up in a contract. 

- 498 - 

I've found that a letter of understanding was the best way to communicate what my arrangement was. From a couple of unpleasant experiences, I found that once you get lawyers in between the parties, each lawyer wants to protect his client like a pit bull. Before long, each lawyer is coming up with highly unlikely scenarios for which contractual protections are needed, often leading to ill will, counter demands, and more reasons than you'd ever imagine for killing a perfectly fine deal. 

Don't get me wrong. Attorneys are absolutely necessary in some situations, such as when you're buying a home or forging a complex partnership agreement or going into business with someone for the first time. 

But in my copywriting relationships with my clients, I didn't feel the need for an extensive contract, just a letter of understanding describing when royalties are payable, when they're not, and the expectations of both parties. This was more for clarification rather than legal enforcement. 

Don't forget, my arrangement was a $25,000 advance against royalties, which would be earned at the rate of 50 for each direct mail piece mailed. 

I figured that if a client screwed me on the royalties, I at least had been paid something for my time, and I'd never work for that client again. The $25,000 upfront fee cushioned the risk. Half of this deposit was due on my acceptance of the assignment, and the balance after approval of the copy. And this was spelled out in my letter of agreement. 

And this is how I worked with great entrepreneurs who welcomed the idea of paying someone more for outstanding performance. This is the agreement I had with Tom Phillips, Bob King, Marty Edelston, and other great direct marketers. 

Sometimes questions would come up about whether something was covered by a royalty or not, such as whether the client could adapt my direct mail copy to an in-house insert or use it in a space ad. And we always worked out something fair. 

Some companies, as a matter of policy, do prefer to have contracts with all their vendors, such as Rodale, and in those situations, it was fine. But the main point is, I always had to trust my client, even in cases where there was a contract at the client's request. 

One of the best attorneys I ever spoke with said that any client you _don't_ trust, even if you have an ironclad contract, will find some way to screw you, despite the contract. If you're thinking of going with royalties, the character of the person you're dealing with is the most important factor. 

## There's another factor at work, which I call the "decency factor." And that is, if

- 499 - 

you're working with a large enough company where there are several people involved with the calculation and payment of your royalties, and most seem trustworthy to you, they will almost certainly honor their agreement with you. That's because most people in business _are_ trustworthy. You have to be to earn a good reputation. If someone does have a good reputation, it means they probably honor their word. 

In such cases, where you can check around about someone's reputation, even if there were someone untrustworthy at the firm, he or she would have to conspire and persuade the decent people at the firm to screw you, and this is highly unlikely. 

In other words, if you know the people that you're dealing with at every level at a company like Phillips, and you can't imagine them deviously conspiring to cheat you, it would be equally unlikely to imagine that someone you don't know at that company could deviously persuade them to cheat you, which would be necessary, as royalties are calculated, reviewed, and paid by several different people in different departments of the same company. 

For example, at Phillips Publishing, I worked with Tom Phillips and also with people like Bob King, Richard Stanton-Jones, and many others, all of whom were involved in the marketing. I couldn't imagine any of them bringing up at one of their meetings a scheme to cheat the copywriters. Their corporate culture, the key people involved, were so honorable that I just couldn't imagine this happening. 

Your best defense against being cheated in a performance-based agreement is to work with decent people who are committed to the notion of higher rewards for higher performance. They are the smartest people in the business, as they place no limits on what they will pay, as long as you make a lot more money for them. 

The converse is also true. Even if you have an ironclad contract with somebody who's not trustworthy, believe me, you're going to get beat out of your royalties at some point, probably sooner rather than later. 

And sometimes you lose your royalty arrangement with a good client for some unforeseen and perfectly legitimate reason. 

For example, and this happens much more often in the sales field, a company will structure a commission system with big rewards for outsize performance. This really motivates the sales force, and some dedicated salespeople work their hearts out to generate the higher sales and resulting commissions. 

Sometimes they'll succeed so spectacularly that, even though they made a lot more money for their companies than themselves and every penny was fairly earned, it ticks off some of the bureaucrats at the home office. 

- 500 - 

For instance, the company or division president might say, "What the heck is this—someone in _sales_ making more money than me, _el presidents_ No way, Jose!" 

And then they'll welsh on the star performers who worked their hearts out to figure out a new way to sell more than anyone previously sold. The company changes the rules when someone succeeds "too much." They'll change the commission structure to knock them back down to a mediocre level. This happens. 

In our business, it sometimes occurs that you'll be working for a highly entrepreneurial person or team who _likes_ to shower big rewards for outsize performance. But then the division or company is sold to someone with a different mind-set. They take over and tell you that they'd love to continue the relationship with you, but this "overly generous" royalty arrangement has to go. Our new corporate team won't allow it, so here's what we offer instead... _blah, blah, blah._ 

This happens, and it's a risk you're always taking in business. I would never let such people renegotiate my arrangement, as that could let them jeopardize my royalty arrangements with other clients. If my other clients got wind that I was giving a better deal to this new management, they, too, would want and expect a lower rate, and I would have allowed this new penny-pincher to ruin my other client relationships. I would sooner walk away from any such client than let them renegotiate and put my other arrangements in jeopardy. Always imagine that whatever you agree to do for one client will be in the newspaper tomorrow, for all your other clients to read. Are you still willing to do it? If not, walk away. 

Remember, too, that any company can be sold, any management can change, and that's why I recommend that you have to spread your income across a base of several clients, because you never know which client may be gone a year from now. But if you keep your eye on the ball, _if you focus your attention on doing everything you can to keep producing control packages, you never need to worry. Wonderful new clients will always be coming into your life._ 

## **How to Make a Ton of Money ($1,000,000 or More) from the Same Piece of Copy** 

Here is an interesting idea that came to me because I wanted to set a really big goal for myself. Remember before, I was saying how you should try to envision what you'd like to experience and really push the envelope in expanding your limits when you do this? 

## One day, I decided to have a big goal, one that would really excite me. I asked

- 501 - 

myself, "OK, Gary—Mr. Goal Guy—if this goal stuff really works, how could I make a million dollars from a single piece of copy? Wouldn't that be cool?" 

Not unexpectedly, and this will always happen when you first push the envelope of what you ask for in life, my initial reaction was, _"You 've got to be kidding! No way will anyone pay you a million dollars for a single piece of copy."_ 

That's your defense mechanism kicking in, trying to protect you by keeping you safe against hurt and future disappointment. But now you know how to get around this automatic self-protecting reaction that keeps you in the same old rut. 

Now you know the critical "missing step" that allows your subconscious mind to go all out to help you in an utterly fearless way. Now you know that your proper response is to reassure your subconscious mind, your inner "bashful genie," not to worry at all, because this is just a playful exercise. This is fun. _Life_ is fun when lived this way! 

Now you know to establish a solemn agreement with your bashful genie that there's no risk here whatsoever. It's just an imagination exercise, and there's absolutely no harm, no disappointment, no downside of any kind just for wondering how achieving such a goal could be possible, when you really want to think big. 

Here is what I said to myself, to my subconscious mind... 

"No matter what happens, Bashful Genie, we will still love and accept one another just as much as before, if not more. So there's no downside here, no fear of failure, no fear of anything. We pledge that neither of us will blame the other if this doesn't work. _The mere attempt is our success and our fun together._ So let's have some fun with this. I need your help because you're so imaginative. Since we're going to love each other just as much as before—or more—let's make life interesting and have some fun together, imagining how we could actually pull off such an outrageous goal that would make us so much money, be incredibly fulfilling, and wow everyone who watches us do it! Wouldn't that be fun?" 

This is what I said to my subconscious mind when I first wondered, "How could I make a million dollars from a single piece of copy?" 

I added, "Ok, Bashful Genie, we're going to forget the word 'impossible' here. This goal is certainly possible. Perhaps very unlikely, but possible. Who knows? Let's take the idea for a spin and see what we shall see. Bashful Genie, if the both of us were going to be hauled out and _shot_ tomorrow at dawn unless we come up with a potentially viable way to earn a million dollars from a single piece of copy, what would you come up with? Think about it and let me know sometime today, OK?" 

- 502 - 

In no time at all, I had an idea. I (or my bashful genie!) realized that to earn a million dollars for a single piece of copy, I wouldn't have to get a million dollars _from just one client._ 

What if I sold the same piece of copy for $ 1,000 to 1,000 different people? Well, there's my million dollars for a single piece of copy. What an idea! Thanks, Bashful Genie. I always knew you're a genius (smiles all around). 

Then the question became who are the 1,000 people? What market would allow me to sell the same piece of copy 1,000 times over to 1,000 different people? 

Over the years, I had been very successful in selling financial newsletters. I came to know the financial market well. 

I noticed that most brokerage houses have hopelessly incompetent advertising. I know that they are hamstrung by all kinds of SEC requirements. Nevertheless, the advertising is not direct response style and is pathetically weak. It still is today. 

Imagine if I were to sell to stockbrokers a campaign that they could use to generate leads. There are tens of thousands of stockbrokers and financial planners across the country, all of them in need of sound marketing. 

Imagine if I could create a simple campaign for them and license it to them. I could sell it to many different people around the country, perhaps a $3,000 direct mail campaign, which is very reasonable, and sell it to 1,000 different people. My goodness, $3,000 times 1,000 brokers is...$3,000,000, or three times what my original goal was! 

Bashful Genie, you _are_ a genius! 

I ran the following ad in _Registered Representative,_ a magazine for stockbrokers... 

- 503 - 

**==> picture [428 x 524] intentionally omitted <==**

When this ad hit the streets, all hell broke loose in our tiny office. As Pauline remembers, this was when we first were starting out and had just a single business phone line. 

For the first few days, we could not answer the phone fast enough. When one of us was finished talking to a broker and went to hang up, as soon as the phone receiver hit the cradle, the phone would start ringing again. We couldn't even imagine how many callers heard nothing but a busy signal. 

## I'd be on the phone with a broker in San Diego who urgently wanted more

- 504 - 

information. Put the phone down— _ring!,_ a broker from Miami was hot to trot. And this went on for days. Hundreds and hundreds of brokers—and eventually thousands—called me about this offer. We had to hire temporary help just to cover the phone line. 

I never made $3,000,000 on this campaign, or $1,000,000, or even $100,000. It didn't work, even though it made so much sense. I was so young, I didn't realize that this campaign would be taking on the brokerage house establishment. Most of the brokers, the guys and gals in the offices, said, sure, they'd exercise their own authority in approving my ad. And, even though I had the prototype cleared by one of Wall Street's top SEC attorneys who gave it his complete blessing, when I reached a deal with a given broker, he or she invariably would say, "Well, just to be safe, I'm going to run this by my office manager." 

And that's where the idea would blow up. It crashed into a bureaucratic wall of resistance at every single brokerage house. The office manager would say to the eager-beaver broker, "What? You want to run _your own_ ad? What's wrong with _our_ advertising? Not good enough for you? Look, I'm not sticking my neck out running an ad from some maverick copywriter in New York. What if the home office calls me on the carpet for this? They've already picked the firm's ad agency, and those are the ads we're going to run." 

In a couple of smaller companies, some office managers did agree to let the broker do this, but they nevertheless wanted to add their "input," resulting in too many cooks crowding over the pot. 

So this didn't work. True to my agreement with my bashful genie, we celebrated a victory anyway, the victory of having made the attempt with no fear of failure and actually coming up with a concept that can earn $1,000,000 from a single piece of copy. 

Indeed, I had just picked the wrong market for this. This concept can be a very viable way to make $ 1,000,000 from a single piece of copy. If I had pursued this idea, I would have refined it to work with a less bureaucratic, more entrepreneurial market, one where the person who _wants_ the ad has the full _authority_ to approve it. And there are plenty of those markets around. 

In fact, Jeff Paul later succeeded with the same notion, selling turnkey marketing campaigns to financial planners and insurance agents. 

Today, Joe Polish, who is in the room, does this with the carpet cleaning market. Tom Orent does it with dentists. Others are doing it with restaurants, pizza parlors, chiropractors, and real estate offices. There are many other localized markets awash in incompetent advertising where you can come in, write a piece of 

- 505 - 

copy that mops the floor with the competition, generate thrilled testimonials from your first customers, allowing you to roll it out—sell it over and over again all around the country. 

In doing so, you'll build a cash cow, a yearly annuity where your $1,000,000 or $3,000,000 or more comes back to you and even grows each year, because each person in your growing customer base will want changes to the campaign each year. Over the years, you can make a great deal of money from essentially the same piece of copy, and that was my original thought. 

Ironically, and perhaps mysteriously and wonderfully, soon after I pursued this method of making $ 1,000,000 from a single piece of copy through this "licensing" approach, which did not work, another method did work. 

Thanks to my royalty agreements, I simultaneously started making that kind of money, and substantially more, on individual mailings I created for major mailers. So my goal was fulfilled, as often happens when your bashful genie is busily working behind the scenes, in a grander although slightly different way than you originally thought would happen. 

The specific road to the top of the mountain doesn't matter. Your bashful genie will often spot a shortcut that takes you there faster, without any conscious planning on your part— _once you 've freed your bashful genie to participate fully, which happens when you pledge, as I mapped out above, that there is nothing to fear, there is no disappointment to protect against, and the mere attempt at such a grand, outrageous goal is a success and fun in and of itself._ 

## **How Copywriters and Clients Can Be Better Partners and Both Grow Richer Together** 

I'm going to stop talking for a while and throw some questions out. We'll need some microphones around the room. 

We have an amazing gathering of talent here today, including superstar copywriters who work on royalties and lots of other kinds of incentive arrangements. Likewise, we have many advertisers who pay those very generous royalties and other incentive arrangements. 

A while back, the introduction of royalties revolutionized direct mail for a lot of the major marketers. It inspired copywriters like myself, Pat Garrard, Gary Halbert, and several others to work harder than ever to invent new ways to boost response and invest amounts of time they never would have put in if they were just getting $5,000, $10,000 for a new ad. The stakes got really high, and therefore, the 

- 506- 

creativity rose take advantage of it. 

Maybe we can do that again. Maybe we can take it to the next level now. To start the dialog, I'm going to throw out some questions. 

Each of these questions can lead to a rambling discussion, I know, so if I see that we've gotten enough opinions on one subject, I'll introduce the next question. 

Of course, I realize, too, that the clients here are not going to be revealing their bottom-line negotiating positions, and neither will the writers and designers. But let's see if we can come to a meeting of the minds at least on some of the principles on how both can work better with each other. 

For example, let me throw out this first question... 

For many years, writers have been making a lot of money in direct mail with a royalty of so many cents per piece mailed. It's a very simple calculation. But that doesn't work on the Internet, where there are no "pieces mailed." 

I, like many people in this room, suspect that the Internet is going to be the dominant marketing force for years to come, probably sooner than later. Obviously, it will never kill direct mail. Direct mail will always be there. It's a beautiful medium. But every day more people are coming online, buying more things online. 

So the question is how can writers who normally work on royalties "per direct mail piece mailed," now be compensated online, where there is no per-piece mail? What would be a fair arrangement? Are we talking about percentage of sales? Anyone have any thoughts on that? I'm going to open the floor, and we'll pass mikes around. Christian Godefroy, our superstar copywriter and marketer from Europe... 

_CHRISTIAN GODEFROY:_ I give 10 percent of the gross sales to the copywriter for my nine products. 

_GARYBENCIVENGA:_ And how does that work, Christian? 

_CHRISTIAN GODEFROY:_ It's just automatic. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ Before you leave, you may be approached by some copywriters in the room. 

_CHRISTIAN GODEFROY:_ I ask the copywriter to open an account. Every month he gets 10 percent of the sales, and all the returns and the refunds are deducted from that. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ And what do you sell, Christian? 

- 507 - 

_CHRISTIAN GODEFROY:_ I sell self-improvement products and a copywriting course. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ That's a very simple arrangement, 10 percent of gross sales. OK, any other thoughts? Perry Marshall... 

_PERRY MARSHALL:_ I don't approach a copywriting assignment in isolation. I'll charge a client to do a Google AdWords campaign, a landing page, an autoresponder series, and a sales letter, and I'll get paid a fee for doing all those things plus a percentage of sales. Since I've created all that stuff, I have four different things I can tweak instead of one to make the sales work. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ Say those four again. And, by the way, Perry Marshall is the best wizard I know on Google ads. What is it, Perry, 80 percent of all searches online or something like that go through Google? 

_PERRY MARSHALL:_ More than half. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ Where do the other half come from? 

_PERRY MARSHALL:_ They go through Overture, Find What, Yahoo, and all these others. But, yeah, Google is definitely the biggest pay-per-click. So first I do a Google ad, then an opt-in page, so I capture the person's e-mail address, then an autoresponder series that does what you talked about earlier, which is building value, educating and warming them up to you, which could be anywhere from a few days to several weeks. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ Is that like a short course in whatever the subject is? 

_PERRY MARSHALL:_ Right. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ They're not sales messages so much. 

_PERRY MARSHALL:_ Right. And [I also create] a sales letter that the autoresponder series keeps sending the person back to. It's not that much more work to do all four than it is to do one, but now you've got complete control of the traffic. It's consistent traffic. You can split-test a whole bunch of things. I charge a fee. I'll never do anything just on upside, which you don't either, but now that I have complete control of the sales process, now I know what all the numbers are. In some cases, [my arrangement is] pay-per-lead. In some cases, it's pay-per-sale. In some cases, the company bonuses me in stock. So all of those can work. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ Great. Any other comments? Yanik Silver... 

_YANIK SILVER:_ There's a very easy mechanism, actually, for tracking all of that, 

- 508 - 

and it's great. It's software, called affiliate software, that will track everything. 

Trust me, I'm not technical. But what you do is set up your copywriter as a "subaffiliate." So let's say you've got four copywriters working for you, or five copywriters. Those copywriters can become your "subaffiliates," and they automatically get assigned a certain percentage. It's all automatic. There's e-mail notification of it, and it's very clear. 

Whatever becomes the winner, it's automatically tracked. There are so many ways of generating income online, and it's all driven to the same page now that this top copywriter created it, and it's all tracked that way. 

_GARYBENCIVENGA:_ So in other words, even though they're not technically affiliates who are selling your product, you use the affiliate software to keep track of royalties. 

_YANIK SILVER:_ Exactly. And we use that for all kinds of things, as well as advertising, so we can know specifically to the penny where income came from and where the sales came from. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ Excellent point. Thank you. Anybody else? 

_BRAD FALLON:_ My name is Brad Fallon. I specialize in organic search engine marketing, and I thought I might throw out an idea. I work with, for example, a lot of DUI lawyers around the country, which is a kind of consumer-oriented type of attorney. And so we get them up in the search engines. And we'd constantly struggle with the idea that we'd do all this work, and they'd be number one on Google for "Florida DUI lawyer" or "Georgia DUI lawyer," something like that. How do you make sure that you get paid? 

So we came up with this idea. It worked pretty well, and I'll contribute this idea. On the Internet, what we would do is buy the domain name and own the Web site. And our guarantee was 100 percent. We said you can stop paying us any time you'd like, and we'll just go sell that traffic to someone else. And that actually worked pretty well. The guarantee was very strong, the clients were very happy with it, and they pay _much_ more than other firms. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ So you'd own the copyright to the Web page? 

_BRAD FALLON:_ Yeah, we would just build the Web page for them and do the things that Perry was talking about. We'd get them up in the organic rankings, [which means] you do most of your work at the beginning, and then, if they were to quit and stop paying you, they'd still be up in the rankings getting all the benefits. So we would just own the Web site and just leave it up to them to keep 

- 509 - 

paying or not. 

_GARYBENCIVENGA:_ That's very interesting. Getting back to Dan Rosenthal at that first agency that I was talking about, Dan's philosophy was, when we had an incentive agreement, he wanted to own the copyright, or actually our ad agency would own the copyright, which does the same thing offline as you were just discussing. As he put it, thisjyas a "bone in the throat of many clients," having us own the advertising, but he stuck to his guns. It gives you tremendous leverage. You will pull in fewer clients, especially if you want an up-front fee, but it gives you enormous bargaining power if they do start playing games with what's rightfully your end of the bargain. 

Maybe one more on this subject.

I've learned a lot from Perry about advertising on Google. Once you get hooked on it, it's amazing. When you test offline, you've got to wait six or eight weeks to get your results. Online with Google, you can test six different ads and the next day or in a couple of hours, you'll see how differently they're pulling. It 

- 516 - 

becomes addictive. 

I've already mentioned Ken McCarthy. 

Joe Polish has a _Genius Network Newsletter,_ a newsletter on a CD every month. Joe, how can they find out more? You've generously given two examples of your CD interviews. They're in the goody bags. 

_JOE POLISH:_ The way I met you, Gary, is through Marty Edelston. I interviewed Marty and Brian Kurtz, who is awesome and a handsome fellow too! So, yeah, I sent Gary interviews for the gift packet so that everyone can hear the Marty Edelston and Brian Kurtz one, and I don't know what the other one is, maybe Joe Sugarman or something.

Thirty-day free trial. So order it, copy it, send it back, you won't have to pay anything for it!

Yanik has taught me many things through his books, little tricks on how to boost your response online. _YANIK SILVER:_ I'm at SurefireMarketing.com, and I specialize [in helping] info marketers who want to sell online, also affiliate programs, and I also have a copywriter course just for Internet marketers, just selling on the Web. _GARY BENCIVENGA:_ Thank you. Any other comments on any of the subjects that we talked about? Anybody want to comment on how copywriters and marketers can make more money together? Jenny Thompson? 

_JENNY THOMPSON:_ This is one of our favorite ways of thinking like the fish and not the fisherman, so I just figured I'd share it with everybody. Internet chat rooms are a great way to eavesdrop on your customer base. Just go to wherever they are. If you're trying to sell a weight-loss product, there are all these weight-loss chat 

- 517 - 

rooms, and just listen to what they're saying. You're going to find out their vernacular, you're going to find out what matters to them. It's a great way to get really intelligent marketing information without spending a dime. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ How do you find those, Jenny? What if you wanted to find out about people interested in weight loss—how do you find those chat rooms? What do you do? 

_JENNY THOMPSON:_ A lot of times we'll Google it, or we'll start on WebMD for the health side, because we know that's a very popular place for a lot of our people. Or we'll go to our own Internet chat and find out where else they're going and follow them there. We try really hard to stay out of the conversation so that we don't direct it. It's just a great way to hear what your fish are thinking, so I just wanted to share that. 

## _GARY BENCIVENGA:_ Excellent! 

_JENNY THOMPSON:_ The only other thing is, earlier in the week—it hasn't been that long, but it feels like there's been so much information, I've taken more notes than I've taken all year—but Gary mentioned that people should look at the Agora letters. And if you want to do that, you can go to Agora-inc.com. You can sign up for eight of our e-letters there, but if you want more than eight, you can just ask anyone at Agora, and we'll be happy to opt you into everything. 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ How many e-letters—did you say 80? 

_JENNY THOMPSON:_ No, eight. We probably have close to 20 or 30 right now, but there are eight that you can opt into through that Web site. 

## **"Time to Wrap This Up..."** 

_GARY BENCIVENGA:_ I think it's time for me to wrap this up. 

I mentioned before what I felt was my father's greatest advice to me, and I'd like to close with some thoughts about him because I love him. 

He passed away about eight years ago, and I love him so much. I love him so much because, as with my mother, my dad accepted me for whatever I was going to be and told me to find what I really love in life and pursue it. 

And I was able to do that. Sometimes that can take a long time to do. It took me maybe several years to figure out _what do I want to do with my life?_ 

That doesn't happen overnight. Once you find what you were meant to do, life can be beautiful. I thank him for that gift, that gift of acceptance, of not trying to 

- 518 - 

push me into a direction that I may not have been comfortable with. I thank him for the wisdom to tell _mQ,find the work you love._ 

I remember when he was ill and about to pass away, I visited him in the hospital. I was holding his hand, and he said, "It won't be long now." 

I said, "Oh, come on, Dad, you've beaten worse things than this. You and Mom will be back at our house enjoying Pauline's good cooking before you know it." 

He said, "No," and then added, with a weak smile and a twinkle in his eye, "It's all right. I've sung, I've danced, I've made love. I'm ready for my next adventure." 

And this had a profound impact on me. I don't know if he'd thought this up beforehand or, being the great slogan writer that he was, this just popped out of his mouth. But it made a profound impression on me. Not long after that, he did pass away, but with a deep serenity and peacefulness, and even an eagerness to get on with his next adventure. 

And it was such a life lesson because I realized that if you get to that fateful moment of your life and you _have_ pursued what you love, you will not have regrets about having stifled your dreams for your whole life. Set yourself free to live, and you will be free to die. You will be free to go on to your next adventure without looking back and saying, "I wish." I wish I could have done this. How I wish I had done more of that. 

We've come full circle now. I mentioned at the beginning of the seminar what Somerset Maugham said. _"It's a funny thing about life. If you refuse to accept anything but the best, you very often get it."_ 

And that applies to your work, your copy, your design, the products we bring to the market, and also the things that you want to experience in life. 

I want to read a poem that has influenced me. I won't read it as well as my friend Richard, of course, but it means a lot to me. 

It's called "My Wage," and it's by Jessie B. Rittenhouse. 

## I came across this in the book _Think and Grow Rich..._

- 5 1 9 - 

**/** _**bargained with Life for a penny, And Life would pay no more, However I begged at evening, When I counted my scanty store.**_ 

_**For Life is a just employer. He gives you what you ask. But once you have set the wages, Why, you must bear the task.**_ 

_**I worked for a menial's hire, Only to learn, dismayed, That any wage I had asked of Life, Life would have willingly paid.**_ 

I commend that to you. Don't you ever dare bargain with life for a penny. Find your heart's desire, the work you love, and when your fateful moment comes, you can say, _"I've sung, I've danced, I've made love. I'm ready for my next adventure."_ 

Thank you very much. 

_(STANDING OVATION)_ 

## — — _End of Seminar_
